HEADLINE: House Committee Approves A Package of Tax Changes

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 25

 BODY:
The House Ways and Means Committee endorsed a package of tax changes today including breaks to help blighted areas and the repeal of luxury taxes on expensive yachts, furs and jewels.
The bill, which the House will probably vote on next week, would cut a variety of miscellaneous taxes by around $14.4 billion over six years and raise others by the same amount. 
 The single biggest money raiser in the bill would hit workers who have to move because of a job change, and also affect employers who pay those costs. The measure would impose a $5,000 limit on tax-deductible moving expenses. Since many moves cost far more than that, the restricted deductions would bring the Government an extra $3.5 billion.
The largest tax cut -- the $2.5 billion centerpiece of the bill -- would go to investors and businesses that put their money and efforts into 50 "enterprise zones" in depressed, poverty-stricken inner cities and rural areas.
Through income-tax rebates, the Government would in effect pay part of the wages of workers hired in enterprise zones, up to maximum of $3,000 a worker. Businesses would be allowed to quickly write off equipment purchases. Investors would pay no capital-gains tax on profits from the sale of investments in enterprise zones so long as they plowed the gain back into a zone.
The committee agreed to repeal, retroactive to Jan. 1, the luxury taxes on expensive yachts, private planes, furs and jewels. The 10 percent tax on expensive cars would be retained but the $30,000 car price, after which the tax is imposed, would be adjusted yearly to offset inflation.
Republicans on the committee fought unsuccessfully for President Bush's enterprise-zone plan, which would have excused enterprise-zone profits from capital-gains taxes even if they were taken out and pocketed or invested elsewhere. The G.O.P. considers a zero capital-gains tax essential to attract investors into risky neighborhoods.
The Ways and Means bill would extend a dozen special tax breaks that expire June 30. They include incentives for investment in low-income rental housing; assistance for moderate-income families buying a first home, and a credit for businesses that increase research spending.
The bill would relax a 1986 law on "passive losses" to permit builders and real estate managers to use losses on real estate activities to shield wages and other income from taxes. The provision would apply only to property placed in service before Wednesday.
That tax cut would be financed by a tax increase on investors who own nonresidential property. The 31 1/2-year period over which such property could be fully depreciated for tax purposes would be raised to 40 years.
The bill would eliminate the tax deduction now allowed for dues paid to athletic, business or social clubs.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Subpar-Growth Signs Help Prices

BYLINE: BY KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Additional signs of subpar economic growth drove up prices of Treasury notes and bonds yesterday and caused long-term interest rates to fall to their lowest level since early February.
An upward revision by the Commerce Department in first quarter economic growth to 2.7 percent from 2.4 percent was discounted by traders as old news, even though the rate of expansion was the strongest since the fourth quarter of 1988. 
 But credit market participants began to bid up prices almost immediately after the Labor Department reported that initial claims for unemployment insurance rose by 16,000 in the week ended June 13, to 422,000.
Economists said they were particularly troubled by the fact that the weekly claims numbers remained above the 400,000 level. Unless claims move lower, employment growth will remain weak, the analysts said.
 
'Still Flopping Along'
 The initial unemployment claims figures "provide further confirmation that we have failed to establish self-sustaining growth conditions we need to have for a good recovery," said David H. Resler, chief economist at Nomura Securities International. "We are still flopping along at about 2 percent growth, and that is not encouraging."
In other signs of weakness welcome in the bond market, the National Association of Realtors said sales of existing homes fell by 1.7 percent in May, the second straight monthly decline. And the Federal Reserve Board reported that all three measures of the nation's money supply fell during the week ended June 15.
The economic and money supply reports only served to fuel speculation that the Fed might feel it needs to lower short-term interest rates again soon, and rates on short-term securities outstanding moved lower as the session progressed.
The prospect of a Fed easing also helped demand at an auction of one-year Treasury bills.
The new bills were sold at an average discount rate of 3.93 percent, down from the 4.07 percent average rate at the last one-year bill auction on May 28. It is the lowest rate on a one-year bill since the auction on Jan. 9, when the rate was 3.84 percent.
 
Benchmark Issue Yield
 In the secondary market for Treasury securities, the 8 percent 29 1/2-year bonds of 2021 were offered late yesterday at a price of 102 5/8, up 19/32, to yield 7.77 percent, compared with 7.82 percent late Wednesday. Yields on the benchmark issue have not been this low in nearly six months.
Among outstanding Treasury note issues, the 7 1/2 percent 10-year notes were offered at 102 18/32, up 13/32, to yield 7.13 percent. And the 5 7/8 percent three-year notes were offered at 101 13/32, up 5/32, to yield 5.34 percent.
The new two-year and five-year Treasury notes auctioned earlier this week continued to perform well.
The 5 percent two-year notes were offered on a when-issued basis late yesterday at a price to yield 4.88 percent, compared with 4.95 percent late Wednesday. And the 6 3/8 percent five-year notes were offered in when-issued trading late yesterday at a price to yield 6.31 percent, compared with 6.40 percent late Wednesday.
In other developments, new-issue activity in the tax-exempt municipal bond market was heavy yesterday, as roughly $2.5 billion of short- and long-term debt securities were priced.
 
Big New York Offer
 By far the largest issuer was New York City, which offered a total of $1.4 billion worth of short-term tax and revenue anticipation notes at competitive bid.
A bid from a group led by the First Boston Corporation won the bulk of the $700 million worth of tax anticipation notes.
The securities, which mature April 14, 1993, carry a coupon of 3 1/4 percent and were priced to yield 2.40 percent.
A bid from a group led by Goldman, Sachs & Company won $500 million of the $700 million revenue anticipation notes sold by the city.
These securities mature June 30, 1993, carry a coupon of 3.50 percent and were priced to yield 2.80 percent.
The tax anticipation notes, which are secured by city property taxes, are rated MIG-1 by Moody's Investors Service and SP1+ by the Standard & Poor's Corporation.
The revenue anticipation notes are secured by certain New York State aid payments. These securities are rated MIG-1 by Moody's and SP1 by Standard & Poor's.
Demand for the city notes was extremely strong, as more than $11 billion worth of bids were received for the securities sold, according to a news release from City Comptroller Elizabeth Holtzman.
 
Spiegel Bonds Offered
 In the investment-grade corporate bond market, Spiegel Inc. offered $250 million of certificates backed by credit card receipts through an underwriting group led by the First Boston Corporation.
The 6.95 percent notes, which have an average life of 5.08 years, were priced at $99.359 to yield 7.141 percent, 80 basis points, or one-hundredths of a percentage point, over the prevailing yield on Treasury securities of comparable maturity.
The issue is rated AAA by both Moody's and Standard & Poor's.
In the secondary market for investment-grade corporate bonds, traders said that prices of most issues rose by about 1/8 point.
Demand for outstanding securities in the tax-exempt municipal bond market was good despite the flood of new issues. Traders said that prices of most widely quoted bonds rose by about 1/4 point.

HEADLINE: G.D.P. Revised Upward But Other Indicators Lag

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 25

 BODY:
The economy grew at an annual rate of 2.7 percent in the first quarter, the briskest pace of the Bush Presidency, the Commerce Department said today.
But while the Administration insisted that the economy was clearly in a period of "modest growth," some private analysts were more cautious. 
 The number of Americans filing first-time claims for unemployment benefits shot up 3.8 percent, to 422,000, for the week ended June 13, the Labor Department said. The unexpectedly sharp increase, 16,000, from the previous week put the number of newly laid-off workers seeking jobless benefits at its highest level in six weeks.
 
Other Signs of Weakness
 Another sign of weakness: Sales of existing homes fell for the second straight month, falling 1.7 percent, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 3.43 million units in May. The National Association of Realtors attributed the setbacks to rising mortgage rates.
Economists said the weakness in the housing sector and the labor market, coupled with a report Wednesday showing that orders for durable goods dropped sharply, could be pointing to an economy that is slowing down rather than revving up.
"The first quarter was a good starter, but the question is, can we maintain it, and the answer right now is that we are going to have trouble," said Robert Dederick, chief economist at the Northern Trust Company of Chicago.
But Michael Boskin, chairman of the President's Council of Economic Advisers, told reporters that he was not overly concerned to see some economic indicators falling while others were rising.
"The economy is improving, but you can't expect every indicator to be up," he said. "Clearly, the economy is in a period of modest growth."
The Administration, worried about rising unemployment rates during an election year, has been keeping up pressure on the Federal Reserve to cut interest rates further. Earlier this week, President Bush publicly called on the Fed to ease credit.
On that point, Mr. Boskin said, "It would be perhaps wise to realize the risks are greater on the sluggish growth side than they are on the reaccelerating inflation side."
 
Second-Quarter Pessimism
 The increase in the gross domestic product, the country's total output of goods and services, was revised up from a 2.4 percent estimate made a month ago. It represented the fastest growth since 1988's fourth quarter, when the economy expanded at an annual rate of 3.9 percent. Since that time, the economy has been virtually stagnant or in outright recession.
The Bush Administration had forecast 2.9 percent growth for this year. In its semiannual economic review, the Paris-based Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development today projected growth in the United States for 1992 at 2.1 percent, with 3.6 percent for next year.
The upward revision to the first-quarter G.D.P. report reflected new-found strength in housing construction, business investment and the country's trade performance.
Analysts said, however, that in the second quarter all those areas have weakened. And of even more concern, the biggest source of strength in the first quarter, a 5 percent rise in consumer spending, appears to have faltered in the current quarter, as Americans, worried about job losses, cut spending.
 
Economy Is 'Pretty Lethargic'
 Kevin Logan, an economist at the Swiss Bank Corporation, said the recovery still had a pulse beat, but "it's pretty lethargic right now."
The G.D.P. report did show that an inflation index that measures the prices paid by United States residents increased at a modest rate of 3 percent in the first three months of 1992. And an accompanying report showed that after-tax profits of American corporations jumped 11.3 percent in the first quarter, the biggest increase in nearly five years.
The various changes left G.D.P. at $4.9 trillion in the first three months of the year, after removing the effects of inflation.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Off in Heavy Selling Amid Rumors of Fed Move

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Heavy selling drove the dollar down sharply against major foreign currencies yesterday as traders reacted to rumors of central bank activity and a gloomy employment report.
Gold prices rose slightly. Gold for current delivery was quoted at $343.20 an ounce, up 40 cents from Wednesday's closing quote. A late bid at the Republic National Bank in New York quoted gold at $342.80 an ounce, up 15 cents. 
 Several currency traders said the dollar plunged after rumors swept the market that the Federal Reserve was checking price levels of the Japanese yen. If true, that would indicate that the central bank was poised to intervene in the market to support the dollar.
Andrew Hodge, vice president at Bank Brussels Lambert in New York, said he had received no confirmation that the Fed actually was checking yen prices, let alone intervening on behalf of the dollar.
Mr. Hodge said the reports had hurt the dollar, as well as a Labor Department survey showing first-time applications for unemployment benefits rose by 16,000 in mid-June, an indication of growing economic weakness.
The second day of a national railroad shutdown was also cited by currency traders as a problem for the economy, which would weaken the dollar.
Jack Stapleton, vice president, Standard Chartered Bank in New York, said predictions that the economy would suffer even further weighed on many traders' minds.
In Tokyo, the dollar fell to a closing 126.62 Japanese yen from 127.03 yen at Wednesday's close. Later, in London, it fell to 125.35 yen. At closing in New York, the dollar was 125.15, down from 126.45 late Wednesday.
In London, the British pound rose to $1.8910, from $1.8780 late Wednesday. The pound was quoted at $1.8970 at closing in New York, more expensive than $1.8795 late Wednesday.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Wednesday's rates, included: 1.5375 German marks, down from 1.5500; 1.3830 Swiss francs, down from 1.4005; 5.1750 French francs, down from 5.2185; 1,163.00 Italian lire, down from 1,173.00, and 1.19175 Canadian dollars, down from 1.19565.

HEADLINE: The Wild Calls. Anybody Listening?

BYLINE: By ANDREW H. MALCOLM

 BODY:
WITH an uncanny precision that perhaps comes from knowing that no one else is counting, New York City officials can at a moment's notice announce precisely how many gallons of water currently reside in their complex system of regional reservoirs. Having automatic access to that water is essential to the city's well being. And such volume figures are most helpful when necessary to flush guilt through the modern consumer's civic psyche.
What have never been tracked so closely, however, are the city's equally vital reservoirs of serenity, those sirenless, automobile-alarm-free areas where urban residents of even modest means can fleetingly flee the city life they are paying so much to endure. In these rural refuges for humans where only visitors lock their cars, city people can pay large sums of money to accommodating local citizens to allow them to sleep, eat, hike, swim, canoe, read, acquire souvenirs and generally relax the way real people do routinely all over this country. 
 Judging by the volume of automobile, railroad, bus and airplane traffic attempting to exit New York on these summer Fridays, New Yorkers have a high serenity deficiency. For many generations, a favorite destination to mine repose has been the Adirondacks, organized since 1892 into the Adirondack State Park, that immense, troubled, much-romanticized upstate area where animals, lush scenery and good times abound and the air is invisible.
These six million acres -- the largest state park in the lower 48 states -- are actually a political patchwork of timberlands, public wilderness and private holdings whose incongruous juxtaposition and conflicting priorities guarantee, in perpetuity, almost as many disputes as trees.
Wild, at times, the Adirondacks certainly are. Beautiful, definitely. A colorful countryside contrasting starkly with the city's straight lines, numbered streets and manmade grays, but of course. However, only an Eastern urbanite accustomed to trees in concrete pots would call the Adirondacks a wilderness; for anyone else, the presence of 45 state campgrounds, 3,000 miles of biking and horse trails, countless canoes, 130,000 year-round residents, 250,000 more summer residents, potentially 400,000 additional homesites and nine million visiting wallets a year would very likely nudge the area out of the wilderness category.
But in many ways that region, its history and its stubborn problems, which receive only episodic attention in the polluted but politically powerful large television markets, are apt symbols of the rural-urban tensions bubbling at varying temperatures across the country.
A helpful backgrounder or fond reminiscence for any New Yorkers who have yet to flee this weekend is "Forever Wild: The Adirondack Experience," a current exhibition at the New-York Historical Society in Manhattan.
Organized by the Katonah Museum of Art for the park's centennial this year, the exhibition is an intriguing three-room collection of oil paintings, watercolors, photographs, postcards and Adirondack-style wooden furniture. Even the walls are trimmed in birchbark, the region's unregistered trademark.
The show effectively captures the flavor of the Adirondacks in the New York mind, which is the same as any American urban mind, only more so. "The weather is just lovely here," Annie wrote on a 1943 postcard to Loretta Williamson over on Madison Avenue. Scores of cards are imaginatively displayed within plastic windows affording views of both sides. "Having a fine time," Wilson wrote to Dr. A. B. Wright at his city home. "Go rowing, swimming and fishing about every day. Weather is cool and fine."
The exhibition also captures the romance of the place, lush oil landscapes caressing the beautiful unfenced countryside, outdoor life flowing on freely before human regulation was deemed necessary. A prominent theme of the 19th-century oils is tiny humans dwarfed by the immense landscape, Lake George reaching on toward the horizon where even a steamboat seems like a toy or a storm boiling up over the same scenic waters. William J. Stillman's canvas titled "The Philosophers' Camp in the Adirondacks" (1858) shows a group of men in their Adirondack camp looking off into the dark, forbidding woods that surround them.
Rustic romanticism hangs everywhere like moss, although Thomas Cole's 1827 portrayal of an impending Indian sacrifice may only be romantic as long as you're not the maiden at the cliff's edge.
There's a Winslow Homer of 1889 depicting a delicate flycast from a canoe. There's a snowy Frederick Remington oil from 1904 showing a blue-gray barn scene after those early winter sunsets. The light and warmth seeping from a nearby cabin door make viewers want to blow into their cupped hands. And to keep that flavor of the Adirondacks (actually a disparaging term meaning "bark eaters" used by the Iroquois to describe the hunting ability of their Algonquin enemies), the Adirondack exhibition also has a gruesome moose-leg lamp that almost certainly looked better on the moose.
Thanks to 19th-century photographic protocol, which forbade smiling on camera, Adirondack-users seem a pretty dour crowd, although this didn't stop them from clearcutting vast areas of virgin timber. Two centuries ago this year New York State, desperate for cash, started the resource scramble by selling off (the state did not call it privatizing) 3.6 million acres of wilderness to developers.
 
Syrup, Soaps, T-Shirts
 This being the Adirondacks, the show also has a souvenir shop offering everything from maple syrup, soaps, T-shirts and bird books to puppets, bug bottles, china and a jigsaw puzzle of backwoods outhouses. But save for one Pop Art watercolor juxtaposing a waterfall with a Hershey bar and tennis shoe, there is nothing to suggest the modern conflicts enveloping the Adirondacks and similar rural refuges with the misfortune to be so handy to so many.
From the Smokies to the Olympics, millions of presumably well-meaning city folks want to preserve these places for their pleasant escapes, their comforting thoughts and their hunting expeditions. Even on the crowded, cacophonous corners of filthy streets with worn curbs, there is a reassuring psychic charge to be had from thoughts of soft, needle-strewn paths, dainty does and fluoride-free water not far away. Naturally, these occasional visitors are pro-preservation, leading to a new definition for conservationists: Some people who built their summer home last year.
 
How Much Is Too Much?
 Many rural residents, year-round custodians of these surrounded sanctuaries of green, also want to preserve their lush life style. This is hard without work. Development might mean cutting trees for lumber, or approving new sites for septic tanks or for new attractions to get those wandering wallets to stop again. But how much development is too much for whom? And how do you find the balance in a divided legislature in an inattentive land that devotes live television time instead to learning the results of the Elvis stamp contest?
Answers don't seem to come easily on many emotional issues in a modern America adrift in its unappreciated natural bounty. What seems in short supply, besides domestic jobs and foreign enemies, is an old-fashioned willingness for reasoned discourse and careful listening. And therein lies the value of a thoughtful exhibition like this.
Yes, it wallows in the rural romanticism of urban ignorance: the vast Kodacolor vistas, the quiet fear-free paths, the treasured moments of shared leisure that linger long in family memory. No, the exhibition does not portray the mosquitoes that also reside there. There are no acid rains in this indoor Adirondacks, no vandals spraying graffiti onto ancient rocks, no carbon-copy condos with tax-deductible mortgages, no economic refugees fleeing to other regions, no McDonald's blasting out the odor of hot fat, no septic tanks seeping sewage into overloaded soil.
So what? The exhibition, indeed the Adirondack-type memory for many, is like a ferocious wild animal, a beguiling wet stone or an innocent lightning bug. In their natural habitat -- the fabled forest, the dappled depths of a clear brook or the cool dusk darkness of a bush -- these things are wild and beautiful, even quietly inspiring.
The instinct of humans, however, is to seize these wild sights with cameras, with postcards, with rifles and souvenirs, even with museum exhibitions, as if grabbing, possessing and transporting a piece of wild somehow enhances us and preserves it. This explains the existence of taxidermists (and trees in pots).
But the moment you plop a burly bear into an ersatz cave in some concrete-laden city park, the moment the colorful river stone dries to a drab brown on the car's backseat or the 10th time that intriguing, illuminative insect falls back down inside the glass jar to wiggle helplessly on its back, you begin to realize that wild is wild and captured is captured. And captured wild cannot be.
 
Where and When
 "Forever Wild: The Adirondack Experience," an exhibition of artworks, photographs and furniture, will run through July 12 at the New-York Historical Society, 170 Central Park West, at 77th Street. Hours are Tuesdays through Fridays, 11 A.M. to 5 P.M., and Thursdays, 11 A.M. to 8 P.M.; closed Mondays and Saturdays. Admission: $4.50 ($3 for students and the elderly; $1 for children under 12). On Tuesdays, it is pay-what-you-wish. Information: (212) 873-3400.

LENGTH: 457 words

HEADLINE: Doing Right by Russia

 BODY:
Finally, Boris Yeltsin has won a victory he has richly earned: economic help from the West. For six months the Russian President has implemented astonishingly bold market reforms, pushed his country to democracy and, last week, proffered momentous nuclear cuts. The West, after having given him many warm words and little cold cash, finally has its priorities straight.
The International Monetary Fund has decided to lend Russia $1 billion before working out the details of economic reform. It's a big win for all sides. Under the arrangement, Russia gets cash that will preserve reform, and democracy. And the I.M.F. gets time to hammer out a long-term plan satisfactory to Western creditors. 
 Until now, the I.M.F. balked at putting up money before it was completely satisfied with Russian policies. It deserves credit for reversing course. President Bush deserves applause as well, for delivering on his promise to press the I.M.F. to accept a sound, even if not perfect, agreement.
In April the West promised Russia $24 billion in 1992 to bridge the vulnerable period between breakdown of centralized controls and the rise of a functioning market. The money was to import desperately needed food and raw materials and to create a fund to stabilize the ruble, a prerequisite for international trade. The catch was that no money would be released until Russia adopted a plan acceptable to the monetary fund.
Russia needed no prodding. It had already begun decontrolling prices, cutting its deficit in half. This month, the Parliament passed a sweeping plan to privatize state enterprises. The pace of reform has been astonishing.
Yet the I.M.F. insisted on imposing a series of crippling conditions. Russia agreed to stop paying for its deficit by printing rubles and thereby accelerating inflation. But the fund insisted that Russia balance its budget without Western aid -- even though the aid would substitute for printing rubles. The fund wanted Russia to begin immediately to pay off debt of the other republics, even while its own economy was collapsing. In the name of market reform, the fund became an obstacle to reform.
The fund has reason to worry, for example, about recent decisions to back away from decontrolling oil prices and to reinstate subsidies to failing state enterprises, decisions that will jack up the deficit. It frets, justifiably, that Russia can't control excessive printing of rubles as long as central banks in the other republics do as they please.
But Russia acknowledges these problems. They are matters of execution, not principle. Mr. Yeltsin has proved, by bold deeds, that he's prepared to make reform work. He needs and deserves the West's help. He has earned this I.M.F. yes.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Reagan Can't Shift the Blame to Congress

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In "Bush Leads the Way" (Op-Ed, June 10), Ronald Reagan makes the assertion that Congress "has shown little restraint in spending" and therefore Congress alone is responsible for our debt-burdened condition. The former President also accuses unnamed "pundits" of playing "the blame game" by critizing the White House for this mountain of debt.
It is true that only Congress can pass a budget (and therefore must shoulder some of the responsibility for the fiscal disaster of the 1980's), but it is the President who offers a budget to Congress. Mr. Reagan's claim that Congress is solely to blame for the massive budget deficits is similar to A blaming B because B did not prevent A from committing a crime -- that is, it is patently false. 
 During his Presidency, Mr. Reagan never once offered anything even resembling a balanced budget -- instead, deficits upward of $300 billion became normal.
We must also keep in mind that in the eight years when Mr. Reagan was President, more debt piled up than under all previous Presidents, spanning two centuries, combined.
The huge debt amassed under the Reagan Administration was not, as Mr. Reagan charges, the result of "entitlement" programs, like Social Security and Medicare. It was the result of large increases in the military budget, combined with large tax cuts for the wealthiest of Americans.
Mr. Reagan's goal of saving money in Government operations, while it was surely worthwhile and laudable, in no way extended to military spending. Indeed, during his Administrations tens of billions of taxpayer dollars were simply thrown away on vast amounts of waste, fraud and abuse, not to mention weapons systems that never worked.
Another assertion of Mr. Reagan's, that it is unfair of Congress to inflict "new burdens on state and local government without providing the money to finance them," is also shifting the blame. It was the Reagan Administration, not Congress, that introduced budgets calling for massive cuts in state and local aid.
It is wrong of Mr. Reagan to blame Congress for the economic calamity that he left behind as his legacy. Then again, when we consider the many deceptions that were offered to the people of the United States during his Administration, we should not at all be surprised.
 
MARK D. SHUCHAT
Port Washington, L.I., June 10, 1992

HEADLINE: Serbs React With Anguish As the U.N. Sanctions Bite

BYLINE: By MICHAEL T. KAUFMAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BELGRADE, Yugoslavia, June 25

 BODY:
The war in Bosnia has come home to Serbia.
Though it has not experienced any fighting on its soil or lost a single inch of territory, this largest and most populous part of fragmented Yugoslavia is reeling under United Nations sanctions imposed to punish its leaders for provoking and sustaining fighting across its borders. 
 With the economy all but capsized and Serbian politics turning increasingly stormy, it is no longer possible, as it was a month ago, for people in this capital to view the fighting in Bosnia as remote from their daily lives. Now, as the sanctions' bite grows more painful each day, waiters and cabdrivers routinely but apprehensively ask foreign customers when America will bomb Belgrade.
 
Inflations Soars Daily
 It has been slightly more than three weeks since the Security Council moved to bar all trade with Serbia and its allied republic of Montenegro, except for humanitarian aid, and to cut air links. Since then the economy has gone from eccentric to fully crazed. Inflation has zoomed to the point where, measured by the prevailing rates for foreign currency, it is climbing at more than 10 percent a day. The important textile industry has shut down. Most construction projects have been suspended.
Tens of thousands of people have been given paid leaves financed by sending the already high-speed printing of dinars into inflationary overdrive. Next week tens of thousands more are scheduled to lose their jobs.
Three weeks ago a 5,000 dinar note, the largest denomination available, had a value of about $550. Today, both banks and the legions of street hustlers exchanging money are delighted to trade a 5,000 dinar note for $2.70.
 
Hard on Pensioners
 For pensioners the effect has been particularly devastating. A woman in her 60's who worked for 25 years in a chemical plant said the other day that her entire monthly payment of 35,000 dinars would buy no more than 44 loaves of bread, or three-quarters of a tank of gas for her son-in-law's car if he gave her the right ration coupons.
"Only the criminals can live on what they earn now," said Milos Vlasic, an editor at a news magazine. "The rest of us are living on the foreign money we have kept in our mattresses. When those dollars and Deutsche marks are gone, I can't imagine what people will do."
Under such volatile conditions, the facade of aloofness from war that Serbia's leader, Slobodan Milosevic, was able to project here, even as he was encouraging Serbs to fight for territory beyond the republic's borders, is tearing sharply. So is the tight control he has maintained over political debate and dissent.
For the last 11 days, students occupying the university, where the enrollment is 80,000, are demanding that Mr. Milosevic "step down and go away."
The bishops of the Serbian Orthodox Church, which is sometimes described as "more Serbian than Christian," have also called for Mr. Milosevic to resign in favor of a "Government of National Salvation."
 
A Rally Is Planned
 The more formal political opposition, long stymied by Mr. Milosevic's control of television and by the wide appeal of his image as a uniter of all Serbs, is organizing a rally for this Sunday that its leaders say is sure to stagger the Government, if not yet bring it down. The main figure in this opposition, Vuk Draskovic, a 44-year-old writer and lawyer, said the protest, which was originally scheduled for last week, was delayed until St. Vitas Day, the most significant date in Serbian history.
Not only does it mark Serbia's nation-building defeat in the battle of Kosovo in 1389 and the beginning of what was to be 500 years of occupation by the Turks, but it is also the day chosen by a Serbian nationalist named Gavrilo Princip to assassinate Archduke Francis Ferdinand, the heir to the Austro-Hungarian throne, while he was visiting Sarajevo in 1914. The shooting ignited World War I.
"Every Serb is aware of history," said Mr. Draskovic, a bearded and long-haired man who, dressed in floral Hawaiian shirts and with a retinue of bodyguards, looks like a rock star. Asked about the potential for violence at the demonstration, he said it was taking place on the only day of the year when it was "unthinkable for Serbs to kill Serbs."
"We expect our rally will mark the beginning of the end of Communist rule and a new and better time for Serbs, for Serbia and for Europe," he said.
Adding still more yeast to the suddenly heady brew of Serbia's politics, the son of Yugoslavia's last King is scheduled to arrive here this weekend intent on eventually restoring the monarchy that ended when his father, King Peter, fled the throne as Nazi forces invaded. Although he speaks Serbo-Croatian only haltingly, the pretender, Alexander Karajorjevic, has generated some support among young people, who seem to regard him less for his royal lineage than for the understanding and respect for democracy they assume he acquired by living in Britain. For some others, the monarchy is revered as a link to the pre-Communist past.
 
'Anything Can Happen'
 "We can see for him a role like that of Juan Carlos in Spain," said Voja Kalic, a 25-year-old philosophy student taking part in the university protest. "He may be the only person in this country who can attract enthusiasm from people like us and also appeal to the people in the countryide who are supporting Milosevic because they think he will bring them their dreams of Greater Serbia."
A few days ago a local newspaper used the headline "Anything Can Happen" to describe the political situation that has developed in the wake of sanctions. In the article and in seemingly never-ending discussions in every restaurant and coffeehouse, the possibilities suggested range from the toppling of Mr. Milosevic by the kind of peaceful protests that succeeded in Czechoslovakia to dire warnings of civil war in Serbia or an even wider conflict that would once again directly involve Serbs and Croats.
Fears are being expressed that students at the university might be attacked by workers, the police or any of several paramilitary groups to inhibit attendance at the Sunday rally. When a delegation planning the demonstration week asked for Government assurances that the army would not intervene, they were told by Dobrica Cosic, the nationalist writer chosen by Mr. Milosevic to head a new Yugoslav Federation that links just Serbia and Montenegro, that he could not offer them such a pledge.
A view widely held among Mr. Milosevic's critics is that he has in the past vaulted over problems in Serbia by aggressively defending Serbs and Serbian interests in Slovenia, Croatia and Bosnia. "Whenever he runs into trouble at home he extends the field of battle," said Miodrag Perolic, a mathematician who publishes on opposition monthly, Monitor, in Montenegro.
 
A Diversion Is Suspected
 Such analysis, on the first anniversary of Croatia's and Slovenia's secession from Yugoslavia, has led some of Mr. Milosevic's opponents to suspect that the Government may try to divert attention from its difficulties by raising the nationalist banner over Kosovo. Although two million ethnic Albanians form 90 percent of the population of Kosovo, it lies dear to the hearts of all Serbs as the site of their defeat by the Turks. A few of the critics said their suspicions grew after Serbian forces surrounded an Islamic school in Pristina on Wednesday to prevent the Kosovo Albanians from convening a parliament they had chosen in clandestine balloting.
But there is mounting evidence to suggest that however much Mr. Milosevic might be tempted to confront the students, the political opposition or the Albanians, he is constrained from doing so by the sanctions.
On the outskirts of Vukovar, a Croatian city of 43,000 that Serbian guns blasted into a stark wasteland during three months last fall, ethnic Serbs complained that the region they had carved out of Croatia and renamed Krinea was in danger of being sold out by Belgrade.
Even more indicative of Mr. Milosevic's reduced options has been the silence of the Government-controlled news media about recent attacks by Croats against ethnic Serbian units across a six-month-old cease-fire line. The television and radio here have played down the news, presumably to keep the public from either demanding military action, which could only bring about greater isolation and international reprisals, or from asking whether in the light of Croatian gains and their own current sacrifices, Government policies had been worthwhile.
In the absence of a military option, it appears the Government continues to pin its hopes on some miraculous political stratagem that will lead to a reduction of sanctions without forcing the resignation of the leadership here.
In this vein, there were reports today that Mr. Cosic, who has not yet appointed a Yugoslav Prime Minister, as he was constitutionally obliged to do two days ago, is continuing discussions with Milan Panic, a naturalized American and a millionaire entrepreneur from California, in hopes of persuading him to take the post. Mr. Panic had earlier refused to consider such an offer.

CORRECTION-DATE: June 27, 1992, Saturday

 CORRECTION:
Because of a computer transmission error, an article yesterday about effects felt in Serbia from the war in Bosnia and Herzegovina described the drop in the value of the currency incorrectly. While inflation has sometimes climbed at a rate of more than 10 percent a day, in the three weeks since sanctions were imposed the value of a 5,000-dinar note dropped to $2.70 from about $5.50, not $550.

HEADLINE: U.S. Sends Mobutu a Message By Welcoming a Cleric-Critic

BYLINE: By BARBARA CROSSETTE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 26

 BODY:
With negotiations to bring democracy to Zaire entering a critical phase in Kinshasa, the Bush Administration has given an unusually high-level welcome to a Roman Catholic archbishop who is leading the movement for change in the Central African country.
The cleric, Laurent Monsengwo Pasinya, the Archbishop of Kisangani, said today that he had come to ask for the moral support of the United States as he begins negotiations with President Mobutu Sese Seko, Zaire's dictator for 27 years, to install a new prime minister and open the way to more representative government. 
 Archbishop Monsengwo predicted that if the effort fails, there will be "an apocalypse" in Zaire, where nearly a year of turmoil and the loss of foreign aid has created an economic and social crisis.
The Administration, sending a strong message to President Mobutu, arranged meetings for the Archbishop with Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d and other high State Department officials; with Brent Scowcroft, the President's national security adviser, and with Karl Jackson, Vice President Dan Quayle's foreign policy adviser.
 
McNamara Turns Up
 At the International Monetary Fund, which like the World Bank has suspended help to Zaire because of President Mobutu's repressive policies, the Archbishop was welcomed by Michel Camdessus, the managing director. He also met with Robert S. McNamara, a former Defense Secretary and a former head of the World Bank.
The Senate Foreign Relations Committee held a reception for the Archbishop, who was brought to the United States by the Africa Faith and Justice Network, an association supported by Christian religious orders. Two committee members, Senators Nancy Landon Kassebaum, Republican of Kansas, and Paul Simon, Democrat of Illinois, have pressed the Administration to cut ties to President Mobutu. For them, the message from the Administration was not strong enough.
"I'm pleased at the general reception for the Archbishop," Senator Simon said in an interview today. "But I think we have to convey more clearly that we are on the side of the people of Zaire. My own reading is that the State Department would like to shift on the policy, but the White House does not want a break with Mobutu because President Bush feels that Mobutu was with us during the cold war, and we shouldn't turn our backs on our friends.
 
National Effort
 "Past friendship alone should not determine foreign policy," Senator Simon said.
Archbishop Monsengwo, 52 years old, is president of a national council that has been trying for a year to draft a constitution and form a transitional democratic government for Zaire leading to elections.
President Mobutu has put pressure on the conference, often with troops, and had repeatedly sought to have it abolished. He has drawn strong messages of condemnation from the United States for his actions. But the Bush Administration has stopped short of openly forcing the removal of the Zairian leader, who allowed his country to be used as a base for rebels fighting Angola's Marxist Government in the 1970's and 1980's.
Archbishop Monsengwo said today that he believed that President Mobutu would have to be consulted and cajoled into accepting change to avoid civil conflict in Zaire, where soldiers have mutinied for lack of pay and citizens are running out of patience as hardships increase.
In Zaire, as in Kenya, Malawi and Zambia, churches have played a leading role in the democracy movement. On Feb. 16, Zairian churches organized a march in Kinshasa, which was attacked by policemen. At least 32 people were killed.
Early this month, the Zairian bishops issued a declaration outlining a program against "injustice, egoism and untruth." The document assailed a range of what it saw as economic, social and moral shortcomings on the part of the Government, mentioning "nepotism and clientage," a disastrous education system and a moribund health service.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

International
An angry American steel industry has decided to seek tariffs totaling billions of dollars on imports from Japan, the European Community, Canada and more than a dozen third world countries. The companies plan to file nearly 100 suits on Tuesday -- the day Prime Minister Kiichi Miyazawa of Japan is scheduled to visit President Bush and before the economic summit meeting [Page 37.]
 
The Economy
Personal income grew just three-tenths of 1 percent and consumer spending rose five-tenths of 1 percent in May, but analysts said the increases would not be enough to spur a dull economic recovery. [37.] 
 When the rail strike began on Thursday, traffic managers like Doug Ely of Kransco, a toymaker based in San Francisco, moved from the corporate background to become the most important people at their companies, finding alternative shippers. [39.]
 
Markets
Globex got under way at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange with more sound than fury, and that was as its planners intended. The goal for the first night -- and even the first few months -- of the new 24-hour electronic futures trading system was simply to make sure that it worked. [37.]
A summer lull settled over Wall Street, with the Dow Jones industrial average slipping 1.60 points, to 3,282.41, and volume shrinking. [40.]
Prices of Treasury securities were basically unchanged in very slow trading. Dealers closed their books and waited for a series of important economic reports next week. [40.]
The dollar was mixed against leading foreign currencies. Gold prices also fell. [46.] Cocoa futures prices rose to a three-and-a-half-week high, but analysts disagreed on the reasons and were reluctant to declare an end to the downward spiral that brought prices to a 19-year low earlier in the week. [46.]
 
Companies
Digital Equipment said it would spend more than $300 million over the next three years to buy 10 percent of Ing. C. Olivetti & Company, the troubled Italian computer maker. [39.]
A court victory was won against two Olympia & York affiliates by one of the nation's largest pension funds, Teachers Insurance and Annuity Association of America. [37.]

Rodale Press is the fountainhead of an all-natural mainstream, but some critics contend that the company is more willing to consult customer data bases than its conscience. [37.]
Intel declines to name its powerful new microprocessor the 586, putting aside the "86" moniker it has used for 14 years. The new chip, which has the code name P-5, has more than three million transistors and will be introduced in September. [39.]

The I.R.S. dismissed Beverley P. Addison as a revenue agent in 1985 and later sent her a bill for back taxes. HEADLINE: Big U.S. Steelmakers to Seek Billions in Tariffs

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 26

 BODY:
An angry American steel industry has decided to seek billions of dollars in tariffs on imports from Japan, the European Community, Canada and more than a dozen third world countries.
The six large American integrated steel producers plan to file nearly 100 trade complaints on Tuesday seeking protection from foreign competition -- on the day Prime Minister Kiichi Miyazawa of Japan is scheduled to arrive here on an official visit and a week before President Bush meets with the leaders of the seven leading industrial democracies in Munich, Government and steel industry officials said. 
 These complaints amount to "a declaration of economic warfare," one European Community official said.
The Bush Administration has quietly tried to persuade the steel companies to postpone their filings at least until after the Munich summit meeting to prevent harm to America's diplomatic relations. Steel trade cases, like auto cases, tend to result in diplomatic frictions because steel companies employ hundreds of thousands of people around the world and are often regarded as important for national security.
The Japanese Government has joined with Japanese steel companies, which own large stakes or have joint ventures with all six big American steel companies, in an aggressive but unsuccessful campaign to persuade American steelmakers to leave Japan out of the trade cases.
But the United States steel industry is planning to go ahead with the filings anyway for several reasons, five people involved in the cases say.
For one thing, the companies are furious at the Bush Administration for refusing to file the cases itself, which would have spared American executives the embarrassment of confronting Japanese business partners, whom they increasingly rely on for investment and technology.
For another thing, top steel executives feel they are legally obliged, by the risk of shareholder lawsuits, to take every opportunity under American law to minimize competition. Steel imports totaled $7.8 billion last year, or 17.8 percent of the market.
The steel companies plan a news conference on Tuesday, to be attended by senators and representatives from steel-producing states as well as the heads of most of the big steel companies. People involved in the case insisted that Tuesday had been chosen because all the officials would be in Washington, not because of the arrival of Mr. Miyazawa.
Like the Japanese officials, European, Mexican and Canadian officials have expressed alarm to the Bush Administration. A senior European Community official said that as soon as the cases were filed, the community would immediately seek to block them by appealing to the Geneva-based secretariat of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade, the international arbitration panel for trade disputes.
A senior Japanese official said his Government was irritated at the prospect of the steel complaints, noting that Tokyo warned on June 7 that it might file its first GATT cases against the United States.
Mexican and Canadian officials have been seeking modifications to United States trade laws as part of a nearly completed free-trade pact the three nations are negotiating. These officials have expressed concern to their American counterparts that the trade cases could harm the negotiations and result in retaliatory actions by their domestic industries.
 
No Pact on Steel Exports
 The United States has not had a lot of steel-trade cases since 1984, when it imposed import restrictions that expired on March 31 of this year. International negotiations in Geneva have failed to produce an agreement limiting countries from subsiziding their steel exports and barring industries from selling their steel below cost in foreign markets.
The steel companies face a difficult legal battle in which the strength of their cases will depend partly on how many countries they include, said Gary C. Hufbauer, a trade policy specialist at the Institute of International Economics, a nonpartisan research group in Washington. Each additional country means the inclusion of more of the American market, which makes it easier to prove before the International Trade Commission, an independent Federal agency, that the imports are the source of the American industry's troubles.
The United States steel industry has decided to include as many countries as possible in the complaints because of concerns about a change in direction at the commission.
 
What Damages an Industry
 With three new commissioners on the six-member panel, the commission has been looking more closely at the causes of an industry's troubles and less at its overall financial condition. In particular, the commission is looking at whether imports have claimed a large enough market share to have an appreciable effect on the industry.
The Commerce Department can only impose tariffs after a ruling by the commission that imports are hurting the domestic industry seeking protection. The commission shocked the steel industry on June 10 when it ruled in an early test case involving steel rails that imports from Britain and Luxembourg but not imports from Japan were hurting the United States .
This new perspective at the commission is forcing the American steel industry to include as many countries as possible in its filings, especially Japan, so that their combined market shares add up to a large enough percentage to appear threatening to the domestic industry.
The six American producers -- Bethlehem Steel, USX, LTV, Inland Steel, Armco and National Steel -- are seeking tariffs on imported steel that would allow them to raise the price of domestically produced steel. The domestic industry sought quotas on the tonnage of imported steel in the early 1980's when it was still an oligopoly with fairly little price competition, and could make a profit on each ton of steel sold.
But the import quotas are no longer providing much protection because the tonnage of imports has fallen and the quotas are no longer being filled.
In the last decade, the large integrated steel companies, which make steel from raw iron ore, have also been ravaged by price competition from small steel mills that recycle scrap steel. At the same time, the fall of the dollar and the increasing competitiveness of domestic mills have meant that the tonnage of imported steel has fallen. With the exception of some specialty steel products, import quotas are not being filled, so importers are not bidding up a scarce supply of foreign steel and prices have fallen.
The main question then for the American steel industry is whether the International Trade Commission will attribute the industry's problems to competition from dwindling imports rather than from the new small mills. The small mills, led by Nucor, are not joining the trade cases but will also benefit if prices rise.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Voices -- Atlanta;
On Political Expediency and the Price of War

 BODY:
Political experts, local officials and ordinary voters in several cities were asked to appraise President Bush's handling of the Persian Gulf war.
 
Charles W. Scott
60 years old; retired Army colonel who is now a consultant and lecturer on the Middle East; one of the American hostages in Iran in 1979-80
In the broadest sense, the Persian Gulf war was a tremendous military victory in terms of logistics, tactics and strategy. 
 There have been some very positive things that have come out of the war. One is the realization that American technology and American power is real. The Vietnam syndrome has been set aside. On the negative side, we've learned that we can't afford to do that very often.
It's an oversimplification to say General Schwarzkopf should just have gone all the way to Baghdad. If he had done that, the United Nations coalition that supported this operation certainly would have fallen apart. And by the time he got to Baghdad, Norm probably would have gotten a phone call from Saddam sitting in Tripoli or Algeria.
 
Kenneth W. Stein
46 years old; associate professor of Middle Eastern history and political science at Emory University and director of Middle Eastern Studies at the Carter Presidential Center
During the 1980's, the United States' attitude toward Iraq was characteristic of the way we shaped our foreign policy in the Middle East -- in a stealth fashion, essentially by the executive branch.
We developed our attitude toward Iraq out of political expediency: "The enemy of my enemy is my friend." And since Iraq was Iran's enemy, therefore Iraq was our friend. We did not want to know more than we needed to know about Saddam's domestic behavior. It was not so much a policy as a stopgap of political expediency.
After the war, the Bush Administration took the attitude that one of two things would happen: either there would be a coup within the military that would oust Saddam, or that economic sanctions would cause internal turmoil that would bring him to his knees. But Saddam is the quintessential survivor. Saddam lives to fight another day. And the irony is that he may well outlast in power the man who defeated him militarily.
 
 Daniel S. Papp
40 years old; professor of international affairs at the Georgia Institute of Technology
It's been said that United States policy in the Persian Gulf has been inconsistent over the past few years. But, in fact, the United States policy for the last 50 years in the headwaters of Persian Gulf has been very consistent: to guarantee, to the greatest extent, that the area not fall under the control of a hegemonic power who may have intentions adverse to American interests.
I would argue that the Persian Gulf war was worth the price we paid because it did have two positive outcomes aside from getting Saddam Hussein out of Kuwait. First, it illustrated the American capability to put together a very diverse coalition. It was one of the most adroit diplomatic, political maneuvers of recent years. Second, it was a crucial factor in bringing the Israelis and the Arabs to the peace table.
The Persian Gulf war was not a war fought purely for oil. For one thing, it was appropriate to demonstrate that in the 1990's invading one's neighbor is not the way to solve one's problems. The international community did come together in strong support of getting Saddam out of Kuwait. At the same time, had we left Saddam there, he would have had control of something like 38 percent of the world's known oil reserves and he would have been sitting there menacing Saudi Arabia. On that basis, it had to be done.

GRAPHIC: Photos of Charles W. Scott, Jenneth W. Stein and Daniel S. Papp.

HEADLINE: Albany Session Limps to an Unproductive Close

BYLINE: By SARAH LYALL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, June 26

 BODY:
A legislative session that once held great promise now appears to be dwindling to an anticlimactic close, with state lawmakers failing so far to agree on many of the most critical issues facing the state, from economic development to Medicaid to environmental protection.
With the Senate and Assembly planning to recess within days, legislative aides and Cuomo administration officials said today that a proposal to have the state take over local Medicaid costs was close to death. The same was true of a bill intended to discourage adolescent smoking by limiting vending machines and requiring antismoking advertising in public places where cigarette ads are displayed. 
 Officials in the Republican-controlled State Senate also said that Gov. Mario M. Cuomo's two primary economic development initiatives were in serious trouble. One bill would authorize a November referendum on an $800 million bond act that would create jobs through infrastructure projects. The other would greatly expand the power of the State Thruway Authority, using it to finance construction projects along the Thruway corridor and giving it control of the state canal system.
And two environmental bills, one that would create an environmental trust fund with the state's bottle and can deposits and another that would strengthen wilderness protections in the Adirondacks, appear unlikely to go anywhere.
"You are now on the verge of an extremely disappointing session," said Mr. Cuomo, acknowledging in a news conference that many of his pet initiatives were in danger. "There's still time to make different judgments, but as of this moment this could be a disastrously nonproductive session."
The last days of the legislative session are always tricky, with long stretches of inaction pierced by frenzied activity in which dozens of bills are traded for one another and passed at the last minute. Legislative aides are saying that two things might break the logjam this year: the Republican Senate's desire to allow Nassau and Suffolk Counties to impose new taxes and incur new debt to close budget gaps, and the Democratic Assembly's need to alter its legislative redistricting plan to conform to a Justice Department order.
"The only way I think some of these things could be resurrected are in the bigger matrix of things, if something broke and all of a sudden things started getting traded," said one Senate aide, who requested anonymity. "You have to look at all this as part of a mix where you say, 'We'll give them that thing,' and they say, 'We'll give you this thing.' "
James A. Yates, the counsel to Assembly Speaker Saul Weprin, said that it would be a mistake to conclude that the Legislature had done nothing. He said that many important bills, such as an overhaul of the state's trust and estates law, had drawn little attention because they were less glamorous than some other issues.
"The items that make a leader's list or attract media attention are just the tip of the iceberg," he said. "There's a mass of legislation we've done that never makes the front page."
Much of the legislative inaction on the more controversial pieces of legislation can be attributed to classic Albany inertia, in which lawmakers in the divided Legislature try to put off tough decisions until some hazy point in the future. But this is an unusual year, a time when the Legislature has also been obsessed with its own redistricting problems and with fears of what tetchy voters might do in the November elections.
 
New Maps Take Time
 Mr. Cuomo said today that the Legislature's preoccupation with redistricting -- in addition to drawing its own lines, it was charged with drawing new lines for New York's Congressional delegation -- had "consumed" the Legislature and served as a "retardant" on the session.
The issue drags on still. On Monday, New York State's highest court, the Court of Appeals, will hear the Legislature's appeal of a lower court ruling that overturned the new state lines. If the court upholds the decision, the Legislature may have to return and refashion its plan.
In addition to focusing on redistricting, lawmakers are particularly worried about the fall elections. "The Legislature is trying hard not to take action on measures that would alienate important constituencies," said Robert Jaffe, who lobbies the legislature on health and other issues.
A couple of months ago the session seemed particularly promising, because for the first time in years the state budget was passed just after its deadline, April 1, giving lawmakers three whole months to deal with the rest of their agenda. But on some matters, such as an overhaul of the state's health insurance system, negotiators for the Governor and the Legislature seem stuck on the same issues that divided them when they began talking.
The session was actually supposed to end today, and all week long legislative leaders had promised their members that they would close on time. But the deadline faded away as the Assembly debated for more than five hours about a bill that would outlaw the practice of selling babies through surrogate parent arrangements, and finally passed the measure. The Senate had passed the bill with no debate earlier in the week.
Legislative leaders also agreed today on a bill to sell property at the Aqueduct Race Track to the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey for $50 million to raise money to balance the state's budget.
On Thursday, the Assembly debated for five and a half hours and then finally passed a bill that would establish universal health care in New York State. Today, with great fanfare, the Senate passed a bill that would allow voters to decide in a referendum whether juries should be allowed to impose the death penalty in first-degree murder cases.
But both actions were essentially symbolic, because neither measure has any chance of getting through the other chamber in its current form.
At a news conference today, Governor Cuomo predicted that some of the unresolved issues would return in the fall to haunt legislators.
Mr. Cuomo has promised to make a particular campaign issue out of the Senate's apparent failure to agree to his Medicaid proposal, a complex and expensive plan in which the state would gradually assume Medicaid costs currently being paid by New York City and the state's counties. In exchange, the state would be allowed to keep the revenue generated by the city's personal income tax and by the sales taxes in the state's 57 counties.
Mr. Cuomo estimated that the plan would save $1.6 billion annually for local governments by the year 2000. The Assembly Democrats supported the plan, but the Senate Republicans said they were concerned that it would burden the state with huge new costs that would not be offset by an economic recovery or by cuts in Medicaid programs.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Although the New York legislative session is drawing to a close with many issues unresolved, the Senate did pass a bill that would set a referendum on whether juries could impose the death penalty for murder. With Senator Dale M. Volker, right, the bill's sponsor, was his counsel, Kenneth J. Connolly, left, and Ted Hallman, director of the Codes Committee. HEADLINE: U.S. Presses Ousted Haitian Chief To Negotiate a Return From Exile

BYLINE: By HOWARD W. FRENCH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MIAMI, June 26

 BODY:
In a push to end a nine-month political standoff, the United States has begun urging Haiti's deposed President, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide, to meet with the country's army-backed Prime Minister to negotiate his return from exile and the lifting of an international embargo.
While the new approach falls short of outright recognition for the recently formed Government of Prime Minister Marc L. Bazin, the pressure on Father Aristide to negotiate represents a strong shift from recent statements supporting the exiled President's right to choose his own prime minister. 
 In recent weeks, American diplomats have spoken caustically of Mr. Bazin, a former World Bank official who they said had struggled for months to block an internationally brokered settlement of the crisis that began with the violent overthrow of Father Aristide last September.
More recently, diplomats had also complained that Mr. Bazin's nomination as Prime Minister was secured with the support of less than the required majority of Haitian senators.
 
Pledge for Negotiations
 Since his inauguration a week ago, however, Mr. Bazin has repeatedly pledged his willingness to negotiate with Father Aristide.
Calling Mr. Bazin a political force to be reckoned with, a senior State Department official credited the de facto Prime Minister with securing the removal of Haiti's provisional president, in theory clearing the way for Father Aristide's return. "Clearly that is important movement if you are trying to resolve this situation," the official said.
Diplomats explaining the new approach also spoke of a growing disenchantment with Father Aristide, who they said had recently failed to play a constructive role, preferring instead, as one official said, to "make speeches and tour the Haitian diaspora."
Several diplomats said Father Aristide had adopted an unacceptable strategy of simply waiting for hemisphere-wide economic sanctions imposed on Haiti last October to generate serious political unrest, which they said he believes represents his best hope of returning to power.
 
U.S. Approach Criticized
 Haitian officials loyal to Father Aristide have rejected those assertions and strongly criticized what they see as a reversal of Washington's approach to the crisis.
"Mr. Bazin represents the best form of disenfranchisement of the Haitian people," said Jean Casimir, Father Aristide's Ambassador to Washington. "The person who was resoundingly defeated is now being put forward through all sorts of devious means. They are asking us to spit on our constitution. We are being told that democracy is for rich, white countries and that black, poor countries should be practical."
Father Aristide, a populist priest, won Haiti's fist democratic elections in December 1990 with over two-thirds of the vote. Mr. Bazin, a conservative rival, placed a distant second.
Since the immediate aftermath of his overthrow in a military coup Father Aristide has called for a purging of the army, starting with the commander, Lieut. Gen. Raoul Cedras. General Cedras and other senior army officers have repeatedly ruled out Father Aristide's return as President.
 
Stronger Embargo Urged
 With signs that Haiti's small elite is now seriously chafing under economic sanctions, aides to Father Aristide have called for a more vigorous enforcement of the embargo and the cancellation of American visas for senior political and military figures in Haiti. Already, Washington has canceled more than a score of visas.
"The military's selection of Bazin is to me an indication of their awareness that they need to do some things," said Michael Barnes, a former American Congressman who is advising the Aristide government-in-exile. "If Washington is worried about where we go to from here, they are right. We're worried too. But this is a case where the old medicine may still work."
Diplomats at the Organization of American States, which has sponsored sporadic discussions between Father Aristide and political leaders in Haiti, have expressed mixed opinions about the efforts to urge Father Aristide to work with Mr. Bazin.
Citing fast deteriorating economic conditions in the country and widespread human rights abuses, some diplomats backed negotiations with Mr. Bazin. Others, however, expressed skepticism that a lasting political settlement could be built around the new Prime Minister, a bitter enemy of Father Aristide, whom they blame for scuttling past negotiating efforts.
One Latin Ambassador whose views were echoed by several other O.A.S. officials said Father Aristide would be committing "political suicide" to accept Mr. Bazin, a politician whose years in Washington have given him the image of an internationally imposed candidate. On the other hand, he said, whatever Mr. Bazin's sincerity, he could never prevail upon the army to accept the President's return.
"To many of us, this seems like a Band-Aid that is tempting because it may keep things quiet as long as it sticks," the diplomat said. "But like all Band-Aids, it seems destined to fall off."

HEADLINE: Uncharted Territory Ahead as Trains Roll Again

BYLINE: By ROBERTO SURO, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 26

 BODY:
Railroads around the country moved quickly to resume normal service today, ending the two-day chokehold on industries that rely on the rails and the threat of no service for tens of thousands of commuters in the Northeast.
Just after 1 A.M. today, President Bush signed into law binding arbitration that was approved by Congress late Thursday night. 
 But neither side involved in the dispute seemed particularly pleased by the outcome. Union leaders said they resented the binding arbitration, and the railroads gave it only a slightly warmer reception, saying that it was uncharted territory at best.
"We've been negotiating for four years, and when we get to the point where the law allows us to exercise our right to strike, Congress intervenes in a way that is going to make it very difficult for us to win legitimate wages and working conditions," said Jed Dodd, chief negotiator for the Brotherhood of Maintenance of Way Employees.
 
'This Is Brand New'
 Edwin L. Harper, president of the Association of American Railroads, which represents freight lines, said the new law applies only to the current situation and not to future disputes.
"I wouldn't draw the implication that this is the way it is going to be forever or that it impinges on the right of unions to strike," Mr. Harper said. "This is brand new. I think the Congress is as curious as we are on how this is going to work."
Whatever its prospects, the law did immediately set trains back in motion. Farmers, travelers, manufacturers and even a group of circus performers all expressed relief after experiencing inconveniences and worrying about the prospects of major economic losses.
A unit of the Ringling Brothers and Barnum & Bailey Circus, which had been stranded in Lafayette, La., loaded up and moved out this morning to Jackson, Miss..
 
Busier by the Hour
 The American Association of Railroads reported that 70 percent of all freight traffic was back to normal by 8 A.M. today, and it expected that the rest would be running by the end of the day.
This morning, the "Lakeshore Limited" set off from Boston to Chicago by way of New York, and some other long-distance Amtrak trains began normal runs. Amtrak, the federally subsidized passenger carrier, expected to have the rest of its trains back on track over the weekend.
The law enacted early this morning marked the 18th interventionin a rail labor dispute since 1963. All the parties involved agreed that there needs to be serious updating of the Railway Labor Act of 1926, whose arcane provisions govern the way contracts are negotiated.
Clearly frustrated with repeatedly sorting out messy controversies, Congress this time tried a novel approach. It imposed a 20-day cooling-off period during which the parties are supposed to negotiate. If there is no agreement, each of the parties then submits a "last best offer" to a neutral arbitrator who picks one or the other to serve as the new contract.
The procedure has been used by several state governments with unions representing police officers and firefighters, but the "last best offer" provision has become best known for its application to salary disputes between major league baseball players and their teams.
For a short time on Thursday night, the lawmakers who devised the measure thought it might prove the key to revising the old, painful rail contract negotiating procedures.
Representative Dennis Eckart, a Democrat of Ohio who was chief sponsor of the bill in the House, said, "It will drive the parties to negotiate seriously and present realistic offers, because they'll know that at the end of the process it is an all-or-nothing deal."
The Secretary of Transportation, Andrew H. Card, said, "The solution represented in this legislation might well become a model that could be used in the future."
 
Won't Lament Passing
 But the angry reaction of the unions and the indifference of the railroads today suggested that the solution was unlikely to become regular fixture in rail labor talks.
"This is a one-shot deal that we will be happy to be rid of," said Mr. Dodd.
George Kourpias, international president of the machinists union, said "We're getting calls from hundreds of members who are upset that their collective-bargaining rights were taken away from them even when we decided not to create a national crisis, and then the carriers went ahead and shut down the country."
When a negotiating deadline passed at midnight Wednesday, the machinists called a strike against a single railroad, CSX Transport, the railroads then responded with a lockout on 38 railroads, effectively halting freight traffic nationwide.
As of this afternoon, neither the machinists nor the freight railroads had scheduled their first negotiating session. Talks between Amtrak and the locomotive engineers and the maintenance-of-way employees continued even while Congress was deliberating and will go on over the weekend.
Under the new law, the railroads and the unions have until no later than Wednesday to choose one person apiece from a list of Government-approved arbitrators, and they in turn will select a third person to serve as the arbitrator in the process.
After picking between the proposals presented by the railroads and the unions, the arbitrator must submit the choice to the President, and it becomes a matter of law unless the President disapproves of it within three days. If he disapproves, the unions would then be free to strike again, and the railroads could conduct lockouts.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Commuters from San Diego and Orange County, Calif., arriving yesterday morning at Union Station in Los Angeles as railroads moved to try to resume normal service following the two-day shutdown. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: Religion Notes

BYLINE: By Ari L. Goldman

 BODY:

Switching Allegiances
Americans are a changeable people. They switch homes, cars and spouses with alacrity. A new national poll shows that religion is no exception.
One out of four Americans has switched faiths or denominations at least once from the religion in which they were born, according to the Princeton Religious Research Center, an affiliate of the Gallup Organization. 
 Of those who switch, 81 percent end up in Protestants denominations and 9 percent in the Roman Catholic church. Fewer than 1 percent become either Eastern Orthodox Christians, Mormons or Jews.
Before anyone jumps to the conclusion that all this switching comes from a deep spiritual search, one should consider the reasons that the respondents cited for the switch. The most often cited explanation, at 24 percent, was marriage to a person of another faith or denomination. Fourteen percent said they were motivated by a positive religious feeling toward their new church and 7 percent said that they were motivated by a negative feeling about their old church. And 11 percent changed for convenience, as in a move to a new neighborhood. The remaining explanations varied greatly.
The findings were based on telephone interviews with 1,002 adults conducted April 9 to 12. The margin of sampling error was plus or minus three percentage points. The center did not collect data on the respondents' original church.
 
Clerk for a Day
 It was a great mystery. Twenty-four hours after the Rev. W. Clark Chamberlain of Houston was elected stated clerk of the Presbyterian Church (U.S.A.), he resigned citing "private reasons" but offering no details.
This week, the tale began to unravel when the Texas clergyman explained that he had stepped down after he learned that he had been accused of sexual harassment by an employee of the national church organization.
"I could not have functioned as head of the church under a cloud of suspicion," he said Wednesday. "Certainly it could not help the church."
Mr. Chamberlain's election and sudden resignation took place June 10 at the annual meeting of the 2.8 million member church in Milwaukee. In a telephone interview with The Associated Press, Mr. Chamberlain said that he had been unaware that the charge had been lodged against him until a day after his election. He said that he still did not know the details.
"I may never know," he said, noting that under church disciplinary procedures such charges are kept confidential and are released only if an investigating committee finds there is basis for a trial before a judiciary committee.
At the Milwaukee convention, the 45-year-old Mr. Chamberlain, a philosophy professor and lawyer, defeated the incumbent, the Rev. James E. Andrews, for the position of stated clerk, who shares leadership responsibilities with the denomination's executive director. After the resignation, the convention returned Mr. Andrews to the position.
 
Second Renaming
 What's in a name?
Well, Second Cumberland Presbyterian Church, a small Southern denomination, felt that its name connoted a second-class status and so announced recently that it was dropping the troublesome word.
The church's General Assembly, meeting in Huntsville, Ala., announced June 12 that the 25,000-member denomination would henceforth be known simply as the Cumberland Presbyterian Church in America.
"We felt that 'second' gave a message we did not feel good about," said the Rev. R. Stanley Wood, the general secretary and stated clerk of the denomination. "We did not want to say that because we are African-Americans we are second to anyone."
The denomination was formed in 1874 as the Colored Cumberland Presbyterian Church by blacks who had left the predominantly white Cumberland Presbyterian Church, which today has a membership of over 100,000. In 1958, the breakaway group dropped the word "Colored" and replaced it with "Second."
The white and black Cumberlands have been negotiating for years about the possibility of reunion. Dr. Wood said the action of the convention might well prove a step in that direction. "We at least have one thing out of the way: the name."
