HEADLINE: Credit Market Awaits New Economic Data

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities were basically unchanged in very slow trading yesterday, as dealers chose to close their books and wait for a series of important economic reports next week.
Selling pressure arose early in the session, as some traders chose to take profits after the market's solid price gains earlier in the week. 
 But those declines were reversed later, and participants said market psychology remained upbeat on expectations that the Federal Reserve Board would move soon to cut short-term interest rates again.
"On balance, I think the Fed would like to wait as long as it can" before cutting rates, one government-bond trader said. "But if the employment numbers are weak, that may force its hand to move quickly."
 
Several Reports Due
 June employment data are scheduled to be released by the Labor Department on Thursday. A number of other reports on the economy, including home sales data for May and the monthly survey of manufacturing companies by the National Association of Purchasing Managers, are also expected next week.
"Every day next week there are important numbers," said Michael J. Moran, chief economist at Daiwa Securities America Inc.
"Right now, the economic evidence is not decisive enough to get the Fed to lower interest rates," Mr. Moran added. "But they would react if the data coming out next week are weak."
In the secondary market for Treasury securities, the 8 percent 29 1/2-year benchmark bonds of 2021 were offered at a price of 10218/32 , unchanged on the day, to yield 7.77 percent, the same as late Thursday but down from 7.83 percent on June 19.
Among Treasury note issues outstanding, the 7 1/2 percent 10-year notes were offered late yesterday at 10217/32 down 1/32 , to yield 7.13 percent. And the 7 percent seven-year notes were offered at 101 1/2, down 1/32 , to yield 6.71 percent.
Among the new issues auctioned earlier this week, the 5 percent two-year notes were offered on a when-issued basis at a price to yield 4.88 percent, unchanged from late Thursday.
Short-term Treasury bill rates inched higher.
Three-month bills were offered at a discount rate of 3.62 percent late yesterday, up one basis point, or one one-hundredth of a percentage point. Six-month bill rates rose by two basis points, to a late offered rate of 3.71 percent. And one-year bills were offered at 3.93 percent, up two basis points.
In the corporate bond market, Merrill Lynch & Company acted as lead manager on the first public bond sale for a bankrupt company.
The transaction was for Mid State Homes, the financing subsidiary of Jim Walter Homes, the modular home builder that filed for protection from its creditors under Chapter 11 of the Federal Bankruptcy Code in December 1989.
The financing is part of the reorganization plan designed to bring Jim Walter, which is owned by Kohlberg, Kravis, Roberts & Company, out of bankruptcy.
The $249.86 million offering of securities backed by mortgages on Jim Walter homes is fully insured by Financial Security Assurance.
The 7 5/8 percent securities, which have an average life of 5.7 years, were priced at $99.375 to yield 7.83 percent, 1.31 percentage points over the prevailing yield on Treasury securities of comparable maturity.
The issue is rated AAA by both Moody's Investor's Service and the Standard & Poor's Corporation.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Mixed Results for Dollar; Gold Prices Move Lower

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar was mixed yesterday against leading foreign currencies in trading largely pegged to sour economic news earlier in the week.
Gold prices also fell. Gold for current delivery at the Commodity Exchange in New York was quoted at $342.50, down 70 cents from late Thursday. At the Republic National Bank in New York, the late bid for gold was $342.50 an ounce, off 30 cents. 
 After heavy selling in the last two sessions, a technical correction propped up the dollar slightly yesterday. But investors were still reluctant to buy the dollar ahead of the weekend, causing its downward drift.
There had been a sense early in the session that traders who had sold short, or bet that the dollar would decline, would be doing some buying to reduce their exposure ahead of the weekend, said Mike Faust, senior currency strategist with MMS International. 'But when it became obvious that wasn't going to happen, the dollar sold off again," he said.
There was little in the way of good news for the dollar this week and a sense that many of the negatives contributing to the losses will not disappear, Mr. Faust said.
Figures released yesterday showing personal income rose three-tenths of 1 percent in May were in line with expectations and did little to motivate the dollar, he said.
Uncertainty over the Presidential election and fears that the Federal Reserve might ease interest rates were also not in the dollar's favor, Mr. Faust said.
Lower interest rates make dollar-denominated securities less valuable to investors, thereby decreasing need for dollars.
In Tokyo, the dollar fell to 125.60 Japanese yen, from 126.62 yen at Thursday's close. Later, in London, it rose to 125.70 yen. At the close in New York, the dollar was 125.70 yen, up from 125.15 late Thursday.
In London, the British pound rose to $1.8925, from $1.8910 late Thursday. Sterling was quoted at $1.8945 in New York, down from $1.8970 late Thursday.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Thursday's rates, included: 1.5355 German marks, down from 1.5375.

HEADLINE: Modest Rise In Spending And Income

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 26

 BODY:
Personal income and consumer spending rose modestly in May, the Government reported today, but analysts said the increases would not be enough to spur a dull economic recovery.
The Commerce Department said income grew just three-tenths of 1 percent, to $5.01 trillion at a seasonally adjusted annual rate. Income in April totaled $4.99 trillion, a gain of a tenth of a percent. 
 The report said consumer spending rose five-tenths of 1 percent in May, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $4.06 trillion. Consumer spending, which represents two-thirds of the nation's economic activity, fueled much of the economy's first-quarter growth of 2.7 percent at an annual rate. That rate is about half the average growth for previous recoveries.
 
A Downward Revision
 The department revised its April spending increase to three-tenths of a percent, or $4.04 trillion, from the original estimate of four-tenths of a percent. The March spending calculation, earlier reported as unchanged, was revised to a decline of a tenth of a percent, the first since October.
"Growth in income is very modest," said Sandra Shaber, an economist with the Futures Group in Washington. "Luckily, inflation is so low that it's permitting at least small improvements in purchasing power."
Edward Yardeni and Deborah Johnson, economists with C. J. Lawrence in New York, said the modest income gains supported forecasts that the recovery would be far less robust than rebounds from other recessions since World War II.
"It's hard to see much of a gain in consumer spending in the second quarter," Ms. Shaber said. "That removes one of the important props to the economy that we had in the first quarter."
Elliott Platt and Christine Schneider, economists at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette Securities in New York, concurred, saying the April and May spending figures mean growth in the second quarter was just 1.8 percent.
Although analysts described the income growth as meager, it was the first four-month string of advances since March-June 1991.
 
The Savings Rate Drops
 Disposable income -- income after taxes -- rose three-tenths of 1 percent after being unchanged in April.
The fact that spending growth exceeded income gains meant that Americans' savings rate dropped to 5.3 percent in May from 5.4 percent. It had been 5.8 percent in March.
Wages and salaries totaled $2.89 trillion, up from $2.88 trillion a month earlier.
The income and spending figures were not adjusted for inflation. When adjusted, disposable incomes inched up one-tenth of 1 percent, after falling two-tenths of a percent in April.
Consumer spending, when adjusted for inflation, was up just three-tenths of a percent after increasing two-tenths of a percent the previous month.

HEADLINE: Your Taxes;
I.R.S. Pursues One of Its Own

BYLINE: By John H. Cushman Jr.

 BODY:
BEVERLEY P. ADDISON'S problems with the Internal Revenue Service began when the agency dismissed her as a revenue agent in 1985, supposedly for unacceptable job performance. Only later did the I.R.S. send her a bill for back taxes.
She challenged the agency on both matters, with varying degrees of success. The Merit Systems Protection Board, which protects civil servants from unfair dismissal, quickly gave her back her I.R.S. job. And last week the United States Tax Court said some of the tax deductions that the I.R.S. had challenged were proper. On some other counts the court upheld the agency. 
 The court's decision shows, among other things, how important it can be to keep good records, like detailed logs of automobile mileage.
In August 1984, according to court papers, Mrs. Addison bought a Cadillac, which she used mainly to drive from one tax audit to another. Because the car was essential for her job, she took an investment tax credit in the year she bought it, and she claimed deductions for business use of the car in 1984 and 1985.
But her dismissal in May 1985 disqualified Mrs. Addison from using some of those tax breaks, the I.R.S. argued.
The agency reasoned that she had stopped using the car for business within one year of placing it in service, and under the rules this meant that the 1984 investment tax credit had to be paid back.
Furthermore, during the trial the agency raised the ante and challenged Mrs. Addison's deduction for business use of the automobile in 1984, saying that like the tax credit, this deduction too must be revoked since the car was not used mainly for business in 1985.
The I.R.S. introduced as evidence the mileage vouchers she filed while employed by the I.R.S.
Mrs. Addison countered that the I.R.S. had been at fault for improperly dismissing her, as the Civil Service board declared despite repeated appeals by the I.R.S. In any event, she said, she continued to use the car for business purposes throughout 1985, since she used it extensively to drive to union meetings and Civil Service hearings as she fought successfully to hold onto her job.
In Tax Court, petitioners like Mrs. Addison bear the burden of proof when they are appealing I.R.S. decisions. But the I.R.S. must prove any new claims that it raises in Tax Court arguments. And because of this legal technicality, Mrs. Addison, who had not kept good mileage records in 1985, lost part of her case and won another part.
Without logs showing how far she drove her Cadillac as she fought her dismissal, she could not prove that she continued to use the car mainly for business purposes for more than a year after buying it, even after she was dismissed. So on this point the I.R.S.'s claim against her was upheld. Unfair as it might seem, the court said, the law forced her to give up the investment tax credit she had claimed in 1984.
But the I.R.S., not Mrs. Addison, bore the burden of proof when it raised new questions during the trial about her other deductions for the Cadillac. Testifying that she drove to meetings and hearings while fighting her dismissal, she told the court that she had been so upset at the time that she did not keep records of those miles. "We believe her," the court said, and it allowed the deductions since the I.R.S. could not prove her story wrong.
The court went on to decide several unrelated issues in her case, including whether she properly filed as the head of a household and whether her unemployment benefits should be taxed. In some matters the court sided with Mrs. Addison and in others with the I.R.S.
Finally, the court had to decide whether Mrs. Addison owed penalties for negligence in those instances when she filed incorrectly. The court ruled that her errors were nothing more than those an ordinary person might have made under the same circumstances, the standard the law sets for determining negligence.
"We arrive at this conclusion notwithstanding the fact that petitioner is an Internal Revenue agent, presumably with more than general knowledge of provisions of the code," the court remarked.
 
Survey of Businesses
 With state and local taxes steadily increasing, the accounting firm of Coopers & Lybrand recently surveyed 348 companies about their tax burdens.
Based on the survey, the company estimates that state and local governments now take 46 percent of business taxes. The Federal Goverment gets 48 percent of the total, and international taxes make up the rest.
But the average respondent assigns markedly fewer people to handling state and local tax issues than to handling Federal tax issues.
And they mistakenly believe that state corporate income taxes are the biggest burden, when according to the Bureau of Census, property taxes and sales and use taxes are actually greater.
The accounting firm says most companies fight taxes in two main ways: by lobbying governments against increases and by challenging property tax assessments. It recommended that they also emphasize state and local tax considerations whenever they plan to reorganize, expand or acquire new properties.

HEADLINE: County Chiefs in Albany Seek Approval to Borrow

BYLINE: By JOHN T. McQUISTON

 BODY:
A delegation of government leaders from Nassau and Suffolk counties went to Albany yesterday to lobby for borrowing plans to balance their budgets and to save Long Island homeowners from property-tax increases.
As lawmakers raced toward adjournment, County Executives Robert Gaffney of Suffolk and Thomas S. Gulotta of Nassau, both Republicans, worked the corridors of the State Capitol, accompanied by local legislative leaders. 
 Both delegations said that they were being received well and that they were optimistic that the Legislature would approve their requests, possibly as soon as this weekend.
"The feedback has been positive," said David Vieser, a spokesman for Mr. Gulotta. "We anticipate approval."
A spokesman for Mr. Gaffney, Tim Ryan, said, "Indications are there will be no last-minute hangups."
 
Suffolk Sales Tax Would Rise
 Mr. Gaffney, armed with strong bipartisan support, sought approval of up to $91 million in borrowing to help close a deficit in Suffolk's $1.3 billion budget that is estimated at between $90 million and $110 million. The fiscal year in both counties ends Dec. 31.
Mr. Gaffney is also seeking approval of a half percentage point increase in Suffolk's sales tax, to 8.5 percent, which would bring in an estimated $23 million this year and at least $50 million next year. Mr. Gulotta, carrying home-rule measures adopted by the Nassau County Board of Supervisors shortly before midnight on Thursday, sought Albany's approval for $100 million in borrowing. He also sought backing for a doubling of the mortgage tax in the county, to 2 percent, which is expected to raise $213 million by the end of 1997.
The Nassau plan is intended to close an estimated $159 million deficit in the county's current $1.8 billion budget and eliminate the threat of a property tax increase for two years.
 
Critics Call It a Bailout
 Opponents of the borrowing measures in both counties argued again yesterday in Albany that the borrowing was, in effect, a tax increase, since taxpayers would ultimately have to repay the loans, with interest.
Ben Zwirn, a Democrat and the Supervisor of the Town of North Hempstead in Nassau, told the Albany lawmakers that "so long as Nassau has no fiscal plan in place, there is no reason for the state to bail it out once again."
He recalled that six months ago the State Legislature approved a half-cent increase in Nassau's sales tax, to 8.5 percent. He said the additional income was supposed to "solve all of Nassau's problems."
"And here were are, no less than six months later, with hat in hand, asking for another bailout," Mr. Zwirn said.
Mr. Gulotta and Mr. Gaffney met with Ralph J. Marino, the Senate majority leader and a Republican from Long Island, who has indicated support for the borrowing measures, and with Saul Weprin, the Speaker of the Assembly and a Democrat, who has indicated he opposes further borrowing unless local authorities agree to state oversight of their finances.
Critics of Mr. Weprin's view argued that since dozens of municipalities and counties were also seeking home-rule measures to borrow money, oversight of all of them by the state would create an administrative nightmare.
Both Nassau and Suffolk have been hit hard by the recession. Sales-tax revenues have dropped off sharply while the cost of social-welfare programs and other county services have increased.
Among those affected by the hard times are several hundred former Nassau County workers who were laid off this year to cut costs. The outcry from civil-service unions and the effect of the layoffs on county services caused Mr. Gulotta and county lawmakers to rethink plans for additional layoffs and for wage cuts.
A similar outcry in Suffolk brought legislators to a near standstill on measures to cut costs. Mr. Gaffney broke the stalemate on Thursday by announcing the formation of a new bi-partisan coalition, which quickly approved the borrowing and sales-tax measures.
A bipartisan effort was also needed in Nassau, where Lewis Yevoli, the Democratic Supervisor of the Town of Oyster Bay, broke with Mr. Zwern and another fellow Democrat, Bruce Nyman, Supervisor of the City of Long Beach. His shift allowed the borrowing and mortgage-tax measures to pass on a 4-to-2 vote.

HEADLINE: Albany's Parting Shots

 BODY:
The New York State Legislature is, as usual, bogged down in partisan politicking as lawmakers move toward summer recess. Negotiations over important bills are further complicated by the need to agree on a reapportionment plan ordered by the Justice Department.
Here, in any case, are measures that deserve last-minute attention. 
 Police Plan. New York City can save $21 million in pension costs by pushing the start-up date for the next class of police recruits into fiscal 1993, a three-day delay. But meddling legislators won't go along unless they can impose tight controls over the city's police program and the $21 million. The Legislature has neither the knowledge nor the authority to run city government. That's the job of people elected to city office by city voters who can hold them accountable. Prompt approval of the city's request is in order.
Bond Act Authorization. Gov. Mario Cuomo's $800 million bond proposal could be an economic stimulus or a boondoggle, depending on how and where the money is spent. The Governor and legislative leaders pledge to provide detailed spending objectives before a November referendum. In the meantime, it makes sense to approve a pending bill that authorizes a November vote. There's enough time after that to determine if the act itself deserves support.
Medicaid. The State Senate is balking at Mr. Cuomo's proposal for a larger state role in Medicaid. Under the Governor's plan, local governments, instead of paying 25 percent of Medicaid bills, would give the state part of their tax revenues. The senators worry about saddling the state with high Medicaid costs, especially New York City's, but the measure would give Albany a better grip on expenses and the system in general.
Environmental Trust Fund. This fund would earmark certain revenues, like taxes on soda and beer containers, for land acquisition, recycling and sewage treatment. The Assembly has approved the fund, which is environmentally desirable and fiscally sound. The Senate balks.
Gay Rights. An overdue measure to bar discrimination based on sexual orientation stands a chance in the Assembly. But Speaker Saul Weprin, eager to avoid a tough issue in an election year, refuses to schedule a vote on the bill. Avoiding debate doesn't advance gay rights; it adds to the stigma. Mr. Weprin's proper role is to lead, not duck.
Child Health Plus. This program provides primary and preventive health insurance for children of the working poor. It has already signed up 25,000 youngsters. But because the program started late, only a small fraction of its $20 million annual budget was spent in 1991 and not all will be spent in 1992. What's needed is authority to roll over unused funds to open the program to new enrollees.

SECTION: Section 1; Page 22; Column 1; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 380 words

HEADLINE: The Enduring Cost of Rent Control

 BODY:
An "enduring monument to economic illiteracy," Vice President Quayle called rent control in his latest speech bashing New York. On this subject, at least, he's got it right. Rent control remains one of New York City's more galling and costly policies.
It has been in effect ever since World War II -- and hasn't made sense for most of that time. It purports to help the poor afford housing. Instead, it does serious damage, speeds the destruction of older housing, reduces the city's property tax revenues and discourages the construction of new rental apartments. That hurts the poor and middle class by keeping housing at a costly premium. 
 Moreover, as a study by the Citizens Budget Commission reiterated last year, rent control helps a small number of low-income New Yorkers but mainly benefits wealthier people who pay artificially low rents -- rents in effect subsidized by the taxpayer.
Nobody who understands the economic realities of New York City and its housing stock can justify the perpetuation of rent control. It was an emergency measure enacted during World War II. But it has become an icon in New York City, protected by politicians who should know better.
They do know better. But because real estate is so expensive, rent control has strong support from tenant groups, and no elected official in a position to revise the law will touch it.
Yet there are reasonable ways to release this stranglehold on the housing market. The simplest is called vacancy decontrol, under which apartment rents are freed only when they change hands.
Right now, a reasonable bill to decontrol luxury apartments is stalled in the State Legislature. The measure would decontrol rents of anyone making more than $100,000 a year, and decontrol apartments renting for more than $2,000 a month once the current tenant moves out. The Republican State Senate approved the bill, but it can't even get out of committee in the Assembly, where Democrats from New York City are in charge.
The repeated failure of New York City officials to revise a policy that is so fundamentally unfair and damaging remains a disappointment. Now it is also an embarrassment that it should take Dan Quayle, who scorns New York at every opportunity, to speak the truth about rent control.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Sudan Points Up the World Hunger Crisis

 BODY:
To the Editor:
 I write to express the combined voice of the world's largest Roman Catholic, Protestant and Jewish overseas relief and development organizations in response to "Trek to Misery by 12,000 Pawns in Sudan War" (front page, June 10), about the young refugees who have made a 1,000-mile, four-year, barefoot odyssey from the Sudan to Kenya.
Your article provides just a glimpse of the structural violence, both endemic and epidemic, that threatens the world's poorest citizens. It is endemic, as every night more than one billion people throughout the third world go to bed hungry, most of them women and children. It is epidemic, as the worst drought of the century puts some 30 million lives at risk in South and Southeast Africa this year alone. 
 The agencies of the Interfaith Hunger Appeal -- Catholic Relief Services, Church World Service, Lutheran World Relief and The American Jewish Joint Distribution Committee -- urge that the United States take the lead in providing immediate assistance to the famine-threatened people of the third world.
Sub-Saharan Africa remains the world's most serious development challenge: more than 450 million people with an annual per capita income of $323 translates into a high incidence of chronic malnutrition and periodic famine. In the last 40 years, United States foreign assistance has played a constructive role in the social and economic progress achieved in many developing countries.
It is time to recommit the United States to this humanitarian task and to coordinate its aid with multilateral institutions so that people who suffer chronic poverty and hunger might have an opportunity to achieve self-reliance.
 
(Msgr.) ROBERT J. COLL
Executive Director
Interfaith Hunger Appeal
New York, June 17, 1992

HEADLINE: South Bronx Begins to Shed Image of a Land Without Hope

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"A Bank Shows It Can Profit and Follow a Social Agenda," your May 24 front-page article on Chicago's South Shore Bank, sheds light on one model for revitalizing economic opportunity in blighted neighborhoods.
However, having been involved in economic development in the South Bronx since 1971, I was dismayed to read that South Shore's strategy "would not work in the South Bronx, or other areas so distressed that neighborhoods have gone to demolition." This statement unfortunately perpetuates a persistent stereotype of the South Bronx as beyond hope, which clearly contradicts the progress being made here. 
 Several groups -- including the South Bronx Overall Economic Development Corporation -- are pursuing similar goals as South Shore, albeit with slightly different means. Acting as an intermediary between capital and communities, we have developed an approach to community economic development that involves helping business to expand; easing the way for commercial revitalization and neighborhood development and training youth and the unemployed for jobs.
And the strategy has worked: since 1971, more than $100 million in new capital has come into this community, more than 20,000 jobs have been created, and 19,000 jobs that might have been lost have been retained.
The challenge for the South Shore and for our organization alike is to harness capital for the continuing revitalization of neighborhoods such as the South Bronx. As the events in Los Angeles indicate, there may be no more pressing task in the United States today.
 
JOHN PATTERSON JR.
President, South Bronx Overall Economic Development Corporation
Bronx, June 9, 1992

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Hardly Laissez-Faire;
Perot Says Strong Government Action Could Restore Industries to Strength

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 26

 BODY:
In his muscular rhetoric about making America's economy stand tall again, Ross Perot sounds more activist about the economy than many liberal Democrats, promising a far more interventionist role for the Government to make American industry more competitive.
"I don't want to live in a country that can't make its own television sets," he told The New Republic recently. "It's just bizarre that we can't make consumer electronics. Those are industries of the future. If we forfeit whole industries from this country, we will have a third-world-nation standard of living." 
 On economics, as in other areas, Mr. Perot is long on criticisms but short on specific solutions. But while he has never issued a detailed program, he has conveyed his general approach in interviews and television appearances.
One thing he has made clear is that he supports an industrial policy that would have government focus on and help the growth areas of tomorrow.
"There are industries we've got to keep in the country, and we're losing them right and left," he said in a speech at the National Press Club in April. "We've got to target them. They will create millions of jobs. We've got to make sure that we're first and best. In Japan, that's called MITI: study it, analyze it, improve on it."
While Mr. Perot says he does not want to create an American version of Japan's Ministry of International Trade and Industry, which played a crucial role in helping industry rebuild after World War II, he does want to develop strategy, industry by industry. "We think 10 minutes ahead," he said. "Japan thinks 10 years ahead. I suggest we start thinking 15 years ahead and ace her."
Mr. Perot's approach is in stark contrast to the laissez-faire attitude of President Bush, whose Administration argues that American industry is on the mend and that it would be wrong for a ham-handed Government bureaucracy to interfere.
"I do not want to see the Government pick winners and losers," Mr. Bush said in an interview this week.
The Perot approach is also different from that of Mr. Clinton, who bemoans the decline of American manufacturing. In the plan he released last Sunday, the likely Democratic nominee said he would concentrate on spending on roads, railroads and other public works and on improving the skills of the work force.
The Arkansas Governor supports creating a Federal agency to coordinate research on new civilian technologies, having a broad investment tax credit and requiring employers to spend a certain percentage of their revenues on job training.
In contrast, Mr. Perot talks of action to help companies directly.
It might "make sense from time to time to assist" financially in developing "some leading-edge technology," the Dallas billionaire told The New Republic, particularly "if corporate America is bled so white it doesn't have the capital to do it."
Mr. Perot also suggests easing antitrust laws so that large corporations could share the cost of developing products to enable them to vie more effectively against the Japanese. He wants to change banking regulations to pressure banks to lend more to small business, and he wants to enable banks to invest directly in new companies.
Like Governor Clinton, he favors spurring entrepreneurs by reducing capital-gains taxes for investments in new businesses. In contrast, President Bush has called for cutting capital-gains taxes for almost all investments.
All in all, Mr. Perot's activist approach to industry makes many conservatives uneasy. "He seems to be a kind of corporate state planner type," said Edward Hudgins, an economist with the Heritage Foundation. "He is pro-business in that he wants government to intervene to favor specific industries and companies, but the unspoken secret is that this will be at the expense of others."
Viewing trade policy as a potent tool for strengthening industry, Mr. Perot sounds far more protectionist than Mr. Bush or Mr. Clinton. While President Bush and Governor Clinton support the proposed North American Free Trade Agreement, Mr. Perot is strongly opposed, saying it would mean a massive loss of industrial jobs to Mexico.
Mr. Perot told The Los Angeles Times that he would adjust the "tilted deck" on trade policy with Japan. He said he would make the Japanese say, "We'll take the same deal on cars we've given you." This, he said, would slash the number of imported cars.
Mr. Perot talks vigorously about the importance of slashing the deficit, condemning Congress and the past two Presidents for letting Federal debt mushroom to $4 trillion. He has given few specifics on how he would balance the budget, but he has suggested that wealthy retirees forgo Social Security payments, and that Germany and Japan contribute billions more for American troops overseas. Mr. Perot has said he would slash $180 billion in fraud and waste, but he has not said how.
Richard G. Darman, the Bush Administration's budget director, said on Wednesday that Mr. Perot's proposals were not serious and would save just a fraction of what he suggests.
The Perot campaign has promised to issue a more detailed economic plan, but has not said when it would do so.
Mr. Perot sounds like President Bush in backing a line-item budget veto. He also sounds Republican in forswearing tax increases. "Raising taxes is like taking dope for politicians," he said on the "Today" program on NBC.
Some critics say he has changed his tack on taxes for this year's campaign; they note that in October 1987 he wrote in The Washington Post that taxes needed to be raised to trim the deficit.
"There are some positions where he would be closer to an activist Democrat, and some where he would be close to very conservative Republicans, which is why I think he appeals to a lot of people," said Felix Rohatyn, the New York investment banker, who recently met with Mr. Perot to discuss economic policy.
Far more than President Bush or Governor Clinton, Mr. Perot warns that the nation will have to tighten its belt. If "you can't stand to trip across the desert with limited water, we're not going to straighten this country out," he said on the "Today" program. "If you just want Lawrence Welk music, I'm not your man."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN;
Bush's Greatest Glory Fades As Questions on Iraq Persist

SERIES: The Bush Record -- Third article of a series

BYLINE: By ELAINE SCIOLINO with MICHAEL WINES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 26

 BODY:
President Bush may have reached the pinnacle of his political career on March 7, 1991, when he addressed a joint session of Congress and basked in a three-minute standing ovation from lawmakers who waved small American flags and even chanted his name in admiration of his handling of the Persian Gulf war.
He had skillfully forged an international diplomatic and military coalition that had driven the Iraqi Army from Kuwait. His approval rating had soared to nearly 90 percent in several polls and even the Democratic Speaker of the House, Representative Thomas S. Foley, introduced Mr. Bush that night by giving him "our warmest congratulations on the brilliant victory of the Desert Storm operation." 
 So it is not surprising that Mr. Bush's friends call him frustrated, even angry, that as the afterglow of the war fades, Democrats in Congress are trying to transform what was once regarded as his greatest triumph as President into one of his most significant political liabilities.
Genuine concerns about the policy failure before the invasion are mingled with election year politics; Democrats in Congress are asserting that the Administration's policy of supporting Iraq before the war was not only misguided, but criminal, and even some of Mr. Bush's staunchest backers wonder why Saddam Hussein is still in power in Baghdad.
The stark but unanswerable question behind the politics is this: Could Mr. Bush have avoided the war if he had moved to restrain rather than reward the Iraqi leader earlier?
Mr. Bush and his aides insist the answer is no. Still, no other foreign policy issue is likely to so dog Mr. Bush through the campaign. Ross Perot has reserved some of his most scathing criticism for Mr. Bush's Iraq policy, portraying the President as a sissy-turned-swashbuckler who sent emissaries to "burp, diaper and pamper" Mr. Hussein just before the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait.
Mr. Bush and his aides were urged to rethink Persian Gulf policy from the moment they took office. Shortly after Mr. Bush won the Presidency in November 1988, a State Department strategist drafted a paper for the President-elect urging that the United States take a fresh approach to the region.
The Reagan White House steadfastly believed that the great menace to peace in the gulf was Iran. But Zalmay Khalilzad, an official in the office of Policy Planning, asserted that the more dangerous threat came from Iraq, which had replaced Iran as the strategic regional power and was now poised to dominate the gulf.
Mr. Khalilzad advised in the paper that America's new policy should concentrate on strengthening Iran and containing Iraq. The paper was included in the State Department Policy Planning Staff's official "transition book," which reviewed all the foreign policy issues the new President would soon have to confront. But after Mr. Bush took office, the State Department staff's arguments were either overlooked or rejected.
The Administration knew, however, that Saddam Hussein spent $10 billion -- 40 percent of Iraq's oil revenues -- in 1988 and 1989 on military equipment and personnel.
It also knew that mounting evidence suggested he was pressing ahead with the developement of nuclear and biological weapons, enhancing his chemical weapons and working on his own long-range ballistic missiles.
 
Staying a Policy Course
 When President Bush finally issued his policy on Iraq in October, 1989, the decision was to stay Ronald Reagan's course, which evolved at the height of the Iran-Iraq war. Iran should be isolated, and Iraq should be extended political and economic favors as a way of moderating its behavior and improving its relationship with Washington.
In the nine months after the Bush Administration settled on its policy -- as Iraq grew steadily more belligerent and, eventually, massed 100,000 troops on Kuwait's northern border -- the Bush Administration provided Baghdad with $500 million in agricultural sales credits and pushed for $500 million more. It overrode Congressional objections to continuing Federal financing for commercial transactions with Iraq and sold Baghdad high-technology equipment that could be useful in Iraq's military programs.
It provided Iraq with intelligence on Iran's political and military situation -- until the Iraqis themselves cut off the so-called intelligence-sharing meetings in February 1990. It fought Congress's attempts to impose economic sanctions on Baghdad and to brand it a supporter of terrorism. And it played down the importance of what it acknowledged was Iraq's "abysmal" human rights record.
 
A $40 Million Explanation
 Now, some Democrats say, it is difficult to explain to the American people why the Central Intelligence Agency must spend $40 million in taxpayers' money next year to try to overthrow a dictator whose Presidential Palace is protected by an American-made communications system -- particularly after the United States and its allies spent $61 billion to fight the war.
But Mr. Bush's unwillingness to come up with a coherent defense of his policy has frustrated even his Republican supporters. "I consider this an Administration of generally good judgment, but it is totally bereft of philosophy," said Representative Jim Leach. Although the Iowa Republican said he is "99 percent sure" that the Administration committed no crimes, "The more ham-handed the defense, the more the public will suspect a cover-up."
While some Congressmen say that all this suggests a large-scale cover-up that can be investigated only by an independent counsel, the White House has denied any wrongdoing. Mr. Bush admits that his policy failed while also continuing to argue that the gulf war victory was one of the greatest achievements of his Presidency.
 
Bush Smells Politics
 He said that the move to appoint an independent counsel "smells political to me." Referring to Congressional inquires about the Iraq policy and accusations that the Reagan campaign in 1980 tried to delay the release of American hostages in Iran, he added: "Why the Congress keeps spending the taxpayers' money on these witch hunts, I do not know. And I'm a little sick of it, but there's not a heck of a lot I can do about it except to express a continual and somewhat mounting frustration as I see now another attack. Our policy was well known. We tried to bring Saddam Hussein into the family of nations. That policy was not successful."
For some lawmakers and analysts, Iraq is a showcase of Mr. Bush at his best and at his worst. At his best, Mr. Bush built an international coalition to push the Iraqis out of Kuwait and strip Baghdad of its weapons of mass destruction. At his worst, Mr. Bush allowed the policy to drift as soon as peacetime set in -- just as he failed to modify it in the months before Iraq's invasion of Kuwait.
"He showed great skills in ad hoc policymaking, and his performance during the war was impressive," said Representative Lee H. Hamilton, the Indiana Democrat who is the leading contender to head the House Foreign Affairs Committee this fall. "But his policies prior to the war failed, and the follow-through afterward has been disappointing. He is not thinking ahead."
Charles William Maynes, the editor of the magazine Foreign Policy, put it more bluntly: "Triumph has become ashes in the mouth of the victor," he said.
 
Hazy Memories
Pursuing Peace; A Personal Note
 Among the thousands of pages of classified and declassified papers that have come to light since the Iraq war, the one that perhaps best captures Mr. Bush's attitude toward Iraq was his personal message to Mr. Hussein on July 28, 1990, a few days before Iraq's invasion of Kuwait.
In it, Mr. Bush noted that both countries "have a strong interest in preserving the peace and stability of the Middle East." The President also said he welcomed a statement Mr. Hussein made a few days earlier to the United States Ambassador, April C. Glaspie. "Iraq desires friendship with the United States," the Iraqi leader had said, and Mr. Bush assured him that Washington felt the same way. "My Administration continues to desire better relations with Iraq," he said.
Mr. Bush did not mention that Iraq had already massed tens of thousands of troops on its border with Kuwait.
To this day, Mr. Bush's aides defend that message by pointing out that King Fahd of Saudi Arabia, President Hosni Mubarak of Egypt and King Hussein of Jordan all told Mr. Bush that the Iraqi leader was bluffing, and that United States pressure would only worsen the crisis.
"The President's statement was intended to give the sense that the peak of the crisis had passed," one official said. "The President didn't want to be the American bull in the Middle Eastern bazaar."
When Mr. Bush was elected President, United States policy toward Iraq was a marginal issue; he and his senior advisers were consumed with the developments in the Soviet Union and Eastern Europe as the Communist bloc disintegrated, and in the Middle East.
Iraq remained a back-burner issue, even by the spring of 1990, when the signs of Mr. Hussein's confrontational behavior seemed unmistakable to many.
In December 1989 he had launched his first long-range missile. Two months later, he demanded the removal of United States forces in the gulf. In March, United States intelligence detected the construction of six fixed launchers for long-range Scud missiles at an Iraqi base near Jordan, putting the weapons within range of Israel.
In April, Mr. Hussein threatened to "burn half of Israel" with chemical weapons if attacked and during the same period of time, British Customs seized part of a nearly 200-foot "supergun," and a joint American-British sting operation intercepted nuclear-weapon triggers intended for Baghdad.
Mr. Bush noticed the ominous developments, but according to his aides, he was puzzled, not alarmed. Although the Administration publicly and privately criticized Mr. Hussein's words and actions, it did nothing to alter its policy.
"Bush was going around and asking, 'What gives with this guy?' " recalled one senior adviser. "He was saying, 'Gee, we are trying to be reasonable, yet this guy says and does these crazy things.' "
By June 1990, Iraq's behavior had become so worrisome that some in Congress began to call for economic sanctions. In response, Mr. Bush dispatched to Baghdad Richard N. Haass, the chief White House official dealing with the Middle East, who said the Administration wanted to avoid sanctions by working with Baghdad.
"The jury is out on the future of the relationship, and it will not be possible to sustain it unless your behavior improves," he told Iraq's Foreign Minister, Tariq Aziz, according to a senior Administration official.
Mr. Aziz, the official said, replied: "What are you going to take away from us? You don't sell us anything anyway."
 
Selective Memories
Intelligence Gaps; Advice Not Taken
 At a Congressional hearing last year, Robert M. Kimmitt, then the Under Secretary of State for Political Affairs, asserted that American weapons had never been sold to Mr. Hussein. "If other countries had followed the same policy, Iraq perhaps would not have become the force for instability that it became in the region," he testified.
But throughout the 1980's, when the United States wanted Iraq to win its war with Iran, the Government encouraged America's allies to sell Iraq conventional arms as well as equipment that helped Saddam Hussein's efforts to build nuclear, chemical and biological weapons. And from 1985 to 1990, the United States approved 771 licenses to export $1.5 billion in advanced equipment to Iraq -- much of it useful for both civilian and military functions.
Throughout the Reagan and Bush Administrations, the State Department led a campaign to transfer millions of dollars worth of high-technology equipment to Iraq, at first to help Baghdad in its war against Teheran, and later as part of its policy of conciliation.
Similarly, White House officials have said that foreign policy concerns did not play a role in the Bush Administration's decision to extend agricultural credits to Iraq. But both the Reagan and Bush Administrations used the Commodity Credit Corporation, an Agriculture Department agency that promotes farm exports by arranging Federally guaranteed loans to foreign nations, as a key element of its policy to curry favor with Baghdad, pumping out $5 billion in credits that built Iraq into America's ninth-largest agricultural customer and a top buyer of rice, wheat and corn. When Iraq invaded Kuwait in August 1990 and defaulted on its foreign debts, the Commodity Credit Corporation was liable for nearly $2 billion in bad Iraqi loans -- one quarter of its portfolio.
Almost two years later, most of those debts have been repaid with American tax dollars, even as investigations suggest that Iraq's "farm" loans were widely diverted to kickbacks and arms-buying.
Some call that mismanagement. Others raise a more serious charge: that the White House knowingly pushed the credit corporation into foolhardy fiscal adventures in its courtship of Saddam Hussein.
Since the war, extensive evidence uncovered by United Nations inspectors on Iraq's programs to build weapons of mass destruction has illustrated the gaps in the Administration's knowledge of Mr. Hussein's war machine. But there were exceptions, isolated warnings that Iraq had embarked on a major effort to build a nuclear bomb. These warnings, however, were disregarded.
 
Short Memories
Gloria Fugit; Saddam Remains
 Before Mr. Bush appeared before the National-American Wholesale Grocers' Association in March, the audience was shown a film about the Persian Gulf war that was filled with images of the President reviewing and honoring American troops. When the film ended, the audience responded with total silence. It was only when Mr. Bush greeted the crowd that he was cheered on -- driving home the painful point that even among staunch Bush supporters, the war is no longer a career-sustaining victory.
Sixteen months after the war, the man Mr. Bush called "worse than Hitler" is still firmly in power in Baghdad.
Even though much of his military arsenal was destroyed, Mr. Hussein still controls the largest army in the region. According to testimony before Congress by Robert M. Gates, the Director of Central Intelligence, Mr. Hussein could rebuild Iraq's ability to make nuclear, chemical and biological weapons within a few years and he is still believed to be hiding hundreds of Scud missiles.
Although Mr. Hussein rarely appears in public and reportedly spends much of his time moving from bunker to bunker, the problem for Mr. Bush is that Saddam Hussein is still there at all.
Aides who advised Mr. Bush during the Persian Gulf crisis strongly defend his decision not to attempt to overthrow Mr. Hussein as part of the gulf war.
"There was a sense that Saddam was not going to sit on the veranda and wait for us to find him," said Mr. Gates, who was deputy national security adviser throughout the gulf crisis. "We would have had to occupy all of Iraq. We would still be there."
But out of that comes a dark possibility that no one in the White House wants to contemplate: That President Hussein may hold on to his job longer than President Bush.

HEADLINE: ARCHITECTURE VIEW;
Did Los Angeles Change the Rules of the Game?

BYLINE: By Herbert Muschamp

 BODY:
To put it bluntly, the Regional Plan Association is not known as a bunch of live wires. An organization of professional urban planners from New York, New Jersey and Connecticut, it enjoys no legal power and hasn't lately rocked the world with visionary fervor. The association was established in 1929 on the sound idea that the future growth of New York City would require rational management of the entire metropolitan region. But in recent years, it has come to seem like a relic of an earlier time, when people actually believed they could shape the urban environment.
So the lively mood at this year's annual meeting, which drew about 700 of its 1,300 members to the New York Sheraton Hotel last month, came as a pleasant shock. The event percolated with energy and ideas.
The ideas came from speakers like Rena Cusma, executive director of the Metropolitan Service District of Portland, Ore. Sound dull? Not to planners who for years have wrestled unsuccessfully with the steady drift of economic vitality from the inner cities to the suburbs. 
 As head of the nation's only directly elected regional government, Ms. Cusma has been a champion wrestler. Her agency -- Metro for short -- was created 13 years ago by the Oregon Legislature to manage the "regional growth boundary," the border between urban development and rural areas. Metro has the muscle to override local governments when necessary -- to carry out its vision of compact urban centers, to prevent development from eating up open land. It has already produced conspicuous results: since the 1970's, the number of jobs in downtown Portland has increased by half, while the level of incoming automobile traffic has remained constant.
But most of the energy at the conference came from the audience. The kinetic mood was evident in their reactions to the speakers, in the substance and urgency of their questions. For once, economic priorities were relegated to the back seat while social issues rode up front. When the subject of regional transportation came up, this audience wasn't satisfied with being told how many people could be moved by public transportation. What people, the planners wanted to know. From what income levels? Moved from what kind of housing to what kind of jobs?
The planners wanted to remind themselves that urban forms have social costs, and that these costs can be measured. For example, average commuting times for working mothers (two hours, say, to get from a low-cost apartment in the city to a low-level job in the suburbs) can be correlated with other local statistics: reading scores, incidence of drug use and juvenile crime.
There was no mystery about the catalyst behind the Regional Plan Association's sudden surge of vigor. South Central Los Angeles had burst into flame just days before. Headlines, editorials, magazine covers, talk-show slots had been invaded by The City, the group's field of expertise. An opportunity had opened up for planners. How could they take advantage of it? How could they claim a place at the table the media had spread for the nation to graze on during the crisis of the week?
It was by no means certain that a place had been set for planners, or, for that matter, for architects and urban designers. The table was already packed, what with all the economists, politicians and religious leaders who expected to be seated, not to mention the police chiefs, the National Guard and even the Marines. If any planners were invited, we didn't hear about it on the news. We didn't even hear lip service paid to the idea that the quality of urban life is linked to urban form. If the subject of planning came up at all, we heard only echoes of familiar complaints. Aren't planners those awful people who rammed the expressway through the old neighborhood, demolishing buildings, uprooting lives? Or aren't they irrelevancies, mere onlookers at an urban spectacle created by forces that lie beyond the control of even national governments?
The Los Angeles riots had provoked the feeling that the rules of the urban game had changed. But perhaps what had changed was only the feeling: indifference gave way to raised hopes that might soon be dashed again. That depressing possibility is being faced now by everyone engaged in the physical shaping of cities, from regional planners to urban designers to the architects who seek to integrate individual buildings into the larger urban picture.
The training these professionals undergo, their titles, licenses and job descriptions are all based on the assumption that reason can influence events. That is a hard idea to hold onto in cities, which so often appear governed not by reason, or even money, but by chance. Francesc Torres suggests as much in his "Assyrian Paradigm," a city of cards ruled over by a pair of dice, now on view at the Whitney Museum's downtown branch.
The sense of urgency at the planners' convention arose from something more than distress at the condition of the cities. It was also prompted by an awareness of the public's short attention span. Soon The City would be yesterday's stale crisis; who could say when there would be another opportunity even to speak of change, much less effect it?
Of course, the conditions that produced turmoil in Los Angeles have not vanished. And even without political clout, architects and planners could begin to exploit more fully the power of ideas. Their expertise lies in the molding of ideas as well as in the shaping of physical forms. They could recognize that they have considerable power not only to formulate new urban concepts but to deny intellectual respectability to ideas that have become bankrupt. They could actively question concepts that have received far more attention than they are worth: Festival marketplaces. Public art. Edge cities. Contextual design guidelines. Mid-block arcades. Skywalks. "Neo-traditional" suburban centers. And that is just a short list.
Some of these concepts may have their uses; many will endure through sheer inertia, still others should be buried with all possible haste. And surely one of the urgent tasks now is to go patiently down the list and inquire of each of these ideas: What have you done for us lately?

GRAPHIC: Photos: "Assyrian Paradigm," a work in which a house of cards is ruled by a pair of dice -- In art, as in life, chance seems to govern. (Rebecca Cooney for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: 'SESAME STREET';
High Grades For Big Bird

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"Getting Big Bird off the Dole" [June 14] is Laurence Jarvik's most partisan attack to date on "Sesame Street." While he pretends that he and his fellow conservative Republicans are supporters of Big Bird, this latest essay is just another in a seemingly endless chain by Mr. Jarvik attacking the program, its producer, Children's Television Workshop -- and now Senate Democrats. In each attack, Mr. Jarvik has selectively used and misconstrued the facts to serve his ultimate goal: the defeat of public television. 
 In his most recent round of accusations, Mr. Jarvik questions "Sesame Street's" educational impact. I'd like to set the record straight.
Research has proved that "Sesame Street" is educational and that it reaches an ethnically, racially and economically diverse audience. For example, a 1989 Yankelovich, Skelly and White/Clancy Schulman study conducted in 25 cities across the United States found that nearly two in three youngsters in poverty-level families watched "Sesame Street" daily. In the same study, 75 percent of the mothers named "Sesame Street" as their children's favorite show.
Studies conducted by the Educational Testing Service after the program's first two seasons indicated that "Sesame Street" viewers demonstrated significant gains in basic skills, and that frequent viewers of "Sesame Street," regardless of racial background or socioeconomic status, made greater cognitive gains than infrequent viewers or nonviewers.
"Sesame Street's" reach is broad. But it will take more than a widely available television program to narrow the gap that continues to exist between disadvantaged and advantaged youngsters in our society.
Mr. Jarvik chides Congress for worrying about the continued existence of "Sesame Street." Yet it was he and his colleagues who led the charge to privatize the Public Broadcasting Service, a proposal sure to eliminate broadcast of "Sesame Street" and other educational television programs for children.
 
DAVID V. B. BRITT
New York
 
The writer is president and chief executive officer of the Children's Television Workshop.

HEADLINE: Talking: Bankruptcy;
Can Filing Protect The Home?

BYLINE: By ANDREE BROOKS

 BODY:
THE explosion of personal bankruptcies has created confusion about the fate of a debtor's residence. Is bankruptcy a magic wand that can save a home from the onslaught of creditors, including the mortgage holder? Or is it more likely to clean out a homeowner (and even a renter) of everything -- including the home and all its furnishings?
The reason so much misinformation is circulated, lawyers say, is that so many variables can affect the outcome. Much will depend on the kind of debts burdening the person filing for bankruptcy, the chapter of the bankruptcy code being used and the state in which the home is situated. 
 Even so, some general rules hold everywhere. If bankruptcy is being contemplated because the person can no longer afford the monthly mortgage payments or rent, then the home will ultimately be lost -- irrespective of which state it is in.
"But it could delay that day for as long as a year," said Barbara McEntyre, a lawyer in San Raphael, Calif., who specializes in bankruptcy. She said she recently watched a family hang onto its home for a full year as a result of a bankruptcy filing.
If the person can still afford the mortgage payments and rent but is sagging under an overload of other debt, such as high credit-card balances, business losses that were personally guaranteed or damages from a suit, then the home may well be saved.
First, some background reading can help make sense of what is clearly a complex situation. An exceptionally clear account can be found in the latest edition of "How to File for Bankruptcy" (Nolo Press, Berkeley, Calif., $24.95, obtainable at bookstores or by calling Nolo at (800) 992-6656).
A consultation with a bankruptcy lawyer before problems reach crisis level can also help save the debtor from two classic errors: falling behind on mortgage payments or rent while catching up on other debt, or taking out a large equity loan to pay off other debts and then falling behind on equity payments.
"More people lose their homes because of the size of their equity loans than for any other reason," said Jeffrey Sapir, a lawyer in White Plains who also specializes in bankruptcy law. This is because an equity loan is a secured debt and even under bankruptcy the courts normally let the lender go ahead with a forced sale to recoup all or part of his outstanding balance.
One of the first decisions the debtor must make is whether to file under Chapter 7 or Chapter 13. If the debtor is unemployed and has no alternative source of steady income, it may be necessary to file under Chapter 7.
Chapter 13 is reserved for people with a regular income, less than $350,000 of secured debt and under $100,000 of unsecured debt. The aim is to give any debtor with an income a regulated method of paying off the overload of debt. It does not normally eliminate those debts.
Chapter 13 normally affords both the owner and the renter the best chance of saving the home because the court can impose a repayment schedule on the lender or landlord, said Peter J. Broege, a bankruptcy lawyer in Manasquan, N.J.
And this will immediately put the brakes on any efforts to reclaim the premises, provided the debtor can also meet ongoing payments or rent.
Chapter 13 can also lower the overall level of debt level on a home.
Consider the experience of Jimmy and Cynthia Bellamy of Bridgeport, Conn. The couple had a mortgage balance, including arrears and penalties, of $151,000, when they filed for bankruptcy in 1991. But the value of their home at the time of filing was only $127,000. The judge therefore reduced the debt to $127,000, arguing that a secured claim cannot exceed the value of the security.
While the ruling has not lowered the couple's monthly payments (the court did not have the right to change the terms of the original mortgage contract), it means that should they sell the house in the future the lender cannot demand more than $127,000.
ALTHOUGH this is not an unusual ruling, says Richard Zeisler, a lawyer in Bridgeport who handles many bankruptcies, it may not be the view of all bankruptcy courts.
Chapter 7 requires the debtor to liquidate all assets to help pay off creditors since the debts cannot be paid from income. Afterwards, any debts that cannot be satisfied from the proceeds are wiped clean.
But under Chapter 7 certain categories of assets, such as a personal residence, may be exempt from the sale and placed beyond the reach of creditors. When and how this can be done will hinge on how much equity is locked in the home and the laws of the state where the debtor lives. However, it is important to remember that such an exemption can never stop the forced sale of a home needed to pay off a secured creditor such as the holder of the mortgage or equity loan.
That is why most people should try to keep their mortgages or equity loan payments current even if they have to raise the money with a cash advance on a credit card, said Robin Leonard, the lawyer who was a co-author of the book from Nolo Press.
In Texas, Florida, Kansas, Iowa, Minnesota, South Dakota and Oklahoma, none of the equity in the home can be used to pay off unsecured creditors (Texans, incidentally, are also permitted to keep two guns and a horse).
"That's how we became known as a debtors' haven," said Arthur Menor, a lawyer in Palm Beach, Fla., referring to a recent incident in which a developer from New Orleans hurriedly bought an oceanfront mansion in Palm Beach when faced with business debts totaling several million dollars.
Other states, such as New York, Connecticut and New Jersey, have less generous homestead exemptions. And these may save only $10,000 of equity in the home, not the home itself. However, the filing normally delays any pending foreclosure or forced sale because the bankruptcy court must review and approve such action.
Ms. Leonard said that renters using Chapter 7 can often buy time by tapping their "wild card" exemption. In most states the wild card allows the debtor to select any asset, such as a car or jewelry, up to a certain limit -- normally $4,000 -- and hang onto the cash it might generate for any purpose.
"You could put that money aside for future rent and or even use it to make the arrears," she said.
Finally, the contents of a home are rarely scooped up into a forced sale, said Ms. Leonard, because the cash that second-hand furnishings can generate is normally too paltry.

HEADLINE: $2.3 Billion Plan On the Horizon for Hunters Point

BYLINE: By DAVID W. DUNLAP

 BODY:
ALTHOUGH the economy has played havoc with grand development visions, the public sponsors of the $2.3 billion Queens West project in Hunters Point believe that its time is at hand.
In part, that is because an experienced and influential group of developers and financiers already controls a key site in the 74.5-acre project area along the East River. This group may be able to solve the almost insurmountable problem of financing by building middle-income co-ops with tax-exempt bonds and the help of labor unions, either as partners or lenders. 
 "Right now, finding developers with financing ain't so easy," said Vincent Tese, chairman and president of the New York State Urban Development Corporation, one of the public sponsors. "If, in fact, the group that owns this site has the financing in place and is ready to go, it is going to look very attractive."
"As a general premise, there is a desire to get a shovel into the ground," he said, as a way of triggering the whole project.
The ground in question is known as the Gateway Residential Area, a 10.5-acre development site immediately south of the Pepsi-Cola bottling plant. Under the general project plan for Queens West, 1,450 apartments could be built in this area.
Most of the Gateway site is owned or under option to a partnership called M.O. Associates, which includes the Dreyfus Corporation, through a subsidiary called the Trotwood Corporation; an affiliate of the James D. Wolfensohn investment banking house and affiliates of the Zeckendorf Company and the Japanese construction and real estate concerns of Tekken and Tobishima.
"It demonstrates that there's serious private interest in the redevelopment of this area and that it's not simply an abstract fantasy of some bureaucrats," said Carl Weisbrod, president of the New York City Economic Development Corporation, another public sponsor.
In theory, the state could condemn this property and award it to another developer. But Mr. Tese said the choice of M.O. Associates "might be judicious."
Herbert J. Sturz, a former chairman of the City Planning Commission and the president of Trotwood, said that if M.O. Associates were designated as the developer of the Gateway site, construction might begin by the end of 1993. That would surely enhance the credibility of the overall project.
THE Queens West Development Corporation, a new subsidiary of the Urban Development Corporation, has also made a bid for Unicef headquarters, which is now across the East River in Manhattan.
"We're hopeful they'll show a strong interest," said Vincent M. Albanese, chairman of the Queens West corporation. "If they do, it will accelerate the start of the project."
Although Unicef's director of administrative management dismissed the Queens West overture as not being nearly specific enough, it is conceivable that other United Nations agencies might be lured over the river.
Borough President Claire Shulman of Queens is also talking about a biomedical research complex with a 400-bed hospital at the site, which is across the water from numerous major medical centers.
"Lots of people are starting to look at the site," said Anthony E. Shorris, deputy executive director of the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey, the other public sponsor of Queens West. "We are ready, as soon as the market is ready, to move ahead."
There are those familiar with Hunters Point who still believe that time is distant. "I think it's many years off before we see brick upon brick at that site," said Councilman Walter L. McCaffrey. "We've had no work done on the infrastructure. None of the street work, none of the sewer work, has been started, let alone completed."
But a middle-income housing project may break the logjam because "it's a product no one else is offering," said Frederick S. Harris, the vice president of Trotwood and former director of real estate for the Metropolitan Transportation Authority.
"We're looking at a limited-profit potential," Mr. Sturz said. "We want to be part of something where we're making the statement that you can build in New York, reach a middle-income population and keep the union members who are here working."
M.O. Associates is talking with the Joint Industry Board of the Electrical Industry, a labor-management organization that administers the benefit plans of Local 3 of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers. The board sponsored and was a developer of Electchester, a middle-income co-op project in Flushing, Queens.
"We try to see where we can invest money back into the community, as long as it's a prudent investment that has a market rate of return," said Larry Jacobson, assistant to the chairman of the joint board and its director of investments.
"What intrigued us about Queens West is that, for the first time, they're willing to do waterfront housing for middle-income people," he said. "A lot of our members are looking to the suburbs. This might be another alternative. We also felt it was a good time to get involved because we are a construction union and a lot of our members are unemployed. This is a way to spur job creation at the same time as housing."
Mr. Jacobson said the joint board would "reach out to the whole trade-union movement in the city to see if they would also participate." However, he also stressed that Queens West was not envisioned as an enclave for union members but intended for "working people and their families, whether they belong to the unions or not."
As conceived, two-thirds of the units in the first building, at least, would be available only to those whose household income fell below a certain level, currently $60,000. "We want to have a down payment low enough that it's realistic for people in that income range," Mr. Harris said, which would suggest something like $10,000 to $15,000. The other units would not be subject to an income limit.
MS. SHULMAN withheld any judgment of the scenario outlined by Mr. Sturz and his associates. But she said she would like Queens West to be "a cohesive project instead of an ad hoc, acre-by-acre thing." After all, she said, "this is one of the last large pieces in the city."
The Hunters Point waterfront runs a mile, from the Anable (or 11th Street) Basin to Newtown Creek -- or, to use more familiar landmarks, from the Pepsi-Cola sign, past the Tennisport tennis bubble and Norval cement silos, to the abandoned Newspoint printing plant, which was once used by The Daily News. The south half of the site is bounded on the east by Second Street; the north half by Fifth Street.
During Mr. Sturz's term as director of the City Planning Department, in 1984, the agency suggested that the Hunters Point waterfront be redeveloped in its entirety, "with a mix of community facilities, residential, commercial and residential uses."
That year, the Port Authority was empowered by the New York State Legislature to finance and implement waterfront projects and it undertook Hunters Point in tandem with a development in Hoboken, N.J.
In 1985, the authority acquired 22 acres at the south end of Hunters Point, paying $15.5 million to the Tribune Company of Chicago for Newspoint, which closed in 1982, and $9.8 million to buy out the lease that the Norval cement company had from the Tribune.
Master planning was undertaken by the architects Jordan L. Gruzen of Gruzen Samton Steinglass and John H. Beyer of Beyer Blinder Belle, both based in Manhattan. Ted D. Kleiner was named manager of the Hunters Point development at the Port Authority.
For a time, the project had 10 million square feet, divided evenly between offices and apartments, with about eight acres of public space on decks over the river. But this approach was abandoned and the public space was moved inland, thereby shrinking the overall development area. Also, the commercial-residential ratio was changed.
The final master plan called for 6.385 million square feet of residential space, or some 6,385 units; 2 million square feet of office space; a 350,000-square-foot hotel (about 350 rooms); 225,000 square feet of retail space; an elementary school for up to 650 pupils and a community center with a swimming pool.
Several key planning principles emerged. The existing shoreline, with its variety of coves and inlets, is to be retained, as are the old gantries on the water's edge. This is to be the site of a continuous esplanade.
The entire project is to be organized along a new north-south thoroughfare, called Center Boulevard, which would bend and shift to match the site geography and enhance vistas of the waterfront from inland sites.
EAST-WEST streets are to follow the established grid, both to integrate the project with its surroundings and to preserve view corridors from Long Island City.
In 1990, the Board of Estimate approved the project. Last September, Gov. Mario M. Cuomo announced that he would create the Queens West corporation to "buy up land at depressed recession prices and help set the stage to develop the area."
But the Governor was speaking in only general terms, Mr. Tese said, "I don't think he had any specific parcels in mind."
The new corporation was also meant to untangle the somewhat confused situation that resulted from three public sponsors.
In April, Mr. Cuomo appointed Mr. Albanese as chairman. He is the president of the Albanese Development Corporation and a partner in the law firm of Albanese, Albanese & Fiore, both of Garden City, L.I.
Albanese Development has built offices, houses and apartments in Queens, Nassau and Suffolk Counties but may be best known for 100 United Nations Plaza, a 52-story apartment tower at First Avenue and 48th Street, whose triangular top is a striking skyline feature as seen from Hunters Point.
Now, the corporation is looking for a president. Mr. Albanese expects one might be selected by summer's end.
The overall development plan divides the Hunters Point site into four areas.
SOUTHERN RESIDENTIAL AREA
Running from Newtown Creek to 54th Avenue, this area would have four development parcels and a waterside park. Buildings would range in height from 210 to 390 feet (about 21 to 39 stories) and would have from 453 to 656 units.
This is the former Newspoint site and is owned by the Port Authority.
COMMERCIAL CORE
Straddling Borden Avenue, this district would run from 54th Avenue to 50th Avenue and have four development parcels. Three of these would get office buildings, ranging from 180 to 400 feet. Two sites would have 800,000-square-foot buildings, one site would have a 400,000-square-foot-building and one site would get the hotel.
The south end of the area, the former Norval site, is owned by the Port Authority.
The north end is Tennisport, owned by Fred Botur, which has 16 indoor courts and 13 outdoor courts. Mr. Botur has a lawsuit pending in the Appellate Division of state Supreme Court that challenges the environmental analysis performed for Queens West, said his lawyer, Donald H. Elliott.
GATEWAY RESIDENTIAL AREA
Running from 50th Avenue to 47th Road would be four development parcels for apartment buildings ranging from 104 to 495 units and from 130 to 390 feet in height (13 to 39 stories). Floor-area ratios, the relation between lot size and floor space, would range from 2.98 to 8.37.
M.O. Associates owns most of the two southern development parcels outright, accounting for 58 percent of the Gateway area. The group says it has an option to buy most of the two northern parcels from Pamela Rankin-Smith, thereby bringing 93 percent of Gateway under their control.
Half of M.O. (it stands for Manhattan Overlook) is owned by T.T. Associates, a partnership of affiliates of the Tekken Construction Company and the Tobishima Corporation, both of Tokyo.
The other half is divided among six partners. Besides Dreyfus, Wolfensohn and Zeckendorf, they are World-wide Holdings, headed by Victor Elmaleh, a frequent partner in Zeckendorf projects; the M. J. Raynes Company and the Resolution Trust Company, as successor to the CorEast Financial Group.
NORTHERN RESIDENTIAL AREA
Running from the Anable Basin to 47th Road, this district would have seven development parcels, a landscaped waterfront park and a recreational park on Fifth Street. Apartment buildings would have from 212 to 745 units and range from 170 to 390 feet in height (17 to 39 stories).
This 21-acre property is owned by Pepsico and leased to the Pepsi-Cola Botting Company of New York. Pepsico has an agreement with an affiliate of M.O. Associates granting a "preferential right to purchase that site or develop it as a joint venture, if Pepsi decides to sell or develop it," said Anne Ward, a spokeswoman for Pepsi. She added that "there are no plans to make any changes right now."
