HEADLINE: THE VIEW FROM: THE FOOTE SCHOOL IN NEW HAVEN;
'Don't Have So Many Baby Sitters. Enjoy Your Children.'

BYLINE: By MARCIA CHAMBERS

 BODY:
FRANK PERRINE was doing what he does best, moving in and out of classrooms, getting the feel of things. The fourth graders were in high gear, having performed in a Mayan festival the night before. "Were you nervous," asked Mr. Perrine of one. "Yes," confided the smiling child, "I said symbols instead of syllables."
Other children clamored to be heard. At one point he sat on a small chair and the students gathered round. "What's your theory as to why the Mayan civilization ended?" And the answers poured out. "But we really don't know, do we?"
And letting the question hang in the air, he was out the door. "This is what I like to do," he said. "Get out and around, make sure the kids still got the spunk and the spirit in them."
 
Savoring Every Moment
 Earlier this month he found himself savoring every moment in the final days in what has been a remarkable career as headmaster at the Foote School in New Haven. Most heads of schools do not last a decade; Mr. Perrine has been headmaster for 25 years. He has won the praise and affection of the staff, faculty and parents. His retirement, a year in the planning, has generated a library, a scholarship fund, a sculpture, a tree, a chair, a book, a biographical video, even an ode -- all in his honor.
Mr. Perrine's leaving the Foote school was as thoughtfully planned as Benno C. Schmidt's leaving Yale University was abrupt. While Mr. Schmidt's departure made headlines, Mr. Perrine's has been less visible yet of great significance to the same community. The Yale president and the Foote headmaster are more closely related than one might imagine, for many Foote parents teach at Yale and they care deeply about the private day school founded 75 years ago by Martha Babcock Foote, a Yale faculty wife and parent as well as a graduate of Bryn Mawr.
Foote has been home to many connected to Yale, both as students and as parents. The children of former Yale presidents A. Bartlett Giamatti, Kingman Brewster and A. Whitney Griswold all attended Foote. Guido Calabresi, the dean of the Yale Law School, and his wife, Anne, went to Foote, as did their three children. And the son of Yale's new provost, Judith Rodin, is now enrolled, as are many of the children of Yale's faculty.
Popular with students, Mr. Perrine knows the name of every child in the school. They approach him openly, unafraid. In part, they respond to his modesty, his optimism, his sense of humor, his willingness to chat with them. He still teaches the seventh grade and views himself as an old-fashioned history teacher. He said he did not think students were hurt by memorizing certain things: the Gettysburg Address, multiplication tables, state capitals and the names of the Presidents.
At his final school assembly, the students presented him with a wooden chair, which they had all signed. They asked him to sit him in it, and then each grade recited his favorites, including "The Swing" by Robert Louis Stevenson and a section of the Declaration of Independence. They ended with a hymn with many chords, just the kind Mr. Perrine likes to play on the piano.
Given this adoring throng, why is he retiring at the age of 58? It is not for another job. Rather, he said he believed it was time for him to try something new, and for the school to have fresh leadership. The new headmaster is Jean Lamont, formerly of the Allen-Stevenson School in Manhattan. Mr. Perrine said that a good friend who had retired at 65 told him not to wait that long. "He said 'you've got another life in you; do something else.' I believe that. I think you're a long time dead. Life is short and you should try different things. So, I forced myself out."
The Perrines will spend a year at their home in Peru, Vt., before deciding what's next. Even so, they leave a personal legacy to education -- their three adult children, Charlotte, Bill and Katherine, are all in teaching.
Looking back, Mr. Perrine says students are different today from when he arrived in New Haven from the Harvey School in Katonah, N. Y., where "we shook hands with the kids every morning and shook hands with them every night." The year was 1967 and the Foote School was at half its current enrollment of 430 boys and girls.
"Kids today are much more sophisticated," he continued. "They have a lot more stuff -- Nintendos and games and after-school activities. I tell their parents slow 'em down, be their parents, tell them no, don't rush their childhood, don't over-program them. Stay with them. Take the afternoon off, go fishing. Don't have so many baby sitters. Enjoy your children, enjoy your life."
If anything, he confided, he has seen more change in parents than in children: "I get so mad at them, I want to spank the parents some days. They give those kids everything. The kids are crying for someone to set limits, they're not going to tell you that but they are dying for someone to structure them. Tell them what they're supposed to do. I tell the parents don't let these kids be making all their own decisions. Some parents are afraid to speak to them, to say stop it."
Mr. Perrine, a product of the public schools of Cranbury, N. J., learned from the easy style of his acknowledged mentor at the Harvey School, Headmaster Leverett T. Smith, and sought to transplant the coziness of boarding school to Foote. But shortly after he arrived he was caught up in the educational turbulence of the 1960's. "We were radical about education in those days." he recalled. "I got all stricken with the latest theories. The open classroom, the idea that kids can't learn sitting still. We had merged age groups. Then our board starting getting on my case because they didn't think the kids were learning."
Mr. Perrine credited several people including Mr. Giamatti, who was secretary of the Foote board and teaching at Yale at the time, with helping him rethink his educational philosophy. "Rather than lecture me, he had me talk it through," Mr. Perrine said. "And we started all over again. We developed a statement of purpose for the school, we got a committee together and we worked on Saturday mornings and slowly pulled it all back."
Mr. Perrine said he now thinks a classical education is best. "We have Latin in grades seven to nine and French from grades four to nine, and we emphasize writing and the arts and sports."
He said a major issue for Foote was how to keep a social and economic mixture in a good community school when tuition ranges from $8,350 to $9,250 a child each year. Foote draws from 30 communities in addition to New Haven, has a 16 percent minority enrollment and a strong scholarship fund.
The paradox in the departures of Mr. Perrine from Foote and Mr. Schmidt from Yale is that Mr. Schmidt, who is to lead a new private school venture for Whittle Communications, has had no experience in a world that has been Mr. Perrine's life. The Whittle concept does not appeal to Mr. Perrine because, he said, it is predicated on computer and television learning, and as important as technology is to education, it cannot replace the teacher.
"Education is the spark between the kid and the teacher," he said. "You've got to get inside a kid's heart. That's not what computers do."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Frank Perrine, headmaster at the Foote School in New Haven, handing out leather bookmarks, right, as going-away presents to students before attending gathering in auditorium, above. (Photographs by Gene Gorlick for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: DINING OUT;
Thai Food in the Heart of New Haven

BYLINE: By PATRICIA BROOKS

 BODY:
IN the late 1980's, Thai restaurants seemed to open with alacrity, but the pace has slowed over the last couple of years. That probably has as much to do with economics as with any possibility that the public is sated with the delicate yet fiery cuisine.
Nevertheless, a new Thai restaurant, Bangkok Gardens, has opened smack in the center of New Haven, near the Yale University campus and across the street from the Yale Repertory Theater. 
 While hardly a garden, the new restaurant strives for an airy, open feeling. A glass-roof addition on the front of an old brick building has two glassed-in sides that contribute to a feeling of space and cheerfulness. Inside, the restaurant proper (the smoking section) has buff-color walls (and one exposed brick one) decorated with ornate Thai embroideries and pictures.
The menu has the expected Thai curries -- red, green, yellow and massaman -- available with chicken, pork, beef, shrimp or tofu. There is also a listing "Exclusively Bangkok Gardens" of nine dishes available with the aforementioned ingredients, plus assorted vegetable combinations, many sauteed. This back-to-basics approach may be intended as a simplification for non-Thai diners, but it has the suggestion of fast-food preparation. The dish in this category that we tried -- pork with ginger, mushrooms, onions, scallions and bean sauce -- reminded us more of China than Thailand.
As starters, we began with satays of chicken: white meat threaded on matchstick skewers. The chicken was deep yellow from its curry marinade, with a mild but definitive curry flavor. The peanut dip with it had zip but was unnecessary with the curry. We used the dip with Bangkok rolls instead of the sweet sauce that came with them. The spring rolls, filled with minced meat and vegetables, tasted freshly made and the wrappers were thin and crunchy crisp. Goong-tad, a third appetizer, consisted of five whole shrimp, wrapped in a thick batter, deep-fried golden and served with a delicate sweet sauce.
Yum nua, a salad that did double duty as a starter, was a dish we have savored elsewhere. The Bangkok Gardens version was severely disappointing, partly because the strips of broiled beef were so tough and dried out they curled at the edges, while the rest of the dish -- red onion slices in an indifferent sauce -- wasn't interesting enough to compensate. Missing were the usual fresh mint, cilantro, lemon grass and lime. The "mild tangy sauce" lacked the hot chili-lime taste that would have provided some gusto. In fact, the fresh accents that normally give a lift to Thai dishes and provide the intoxicating balance of hot, tart, sweet and subtle -- lemon grass, fresh cilantro, lime and Thai ginger -- were missing from all our Bangkok Gardens dishes.
Miles ahead of other entrees was chaiya noodles, a mixture of thin yellow noodles, scallions, bean sprouts and carrots in a spicy peanut-flavored sauce. It had verve and veracity. Bangkok basil -- a combination of beef, fresh basil, red and green peppers, onions and mushrooms, with a hint of anise -- also had a modicum of sparkle.
Certain dishes seemed to be going through the motions. An entree of massaman curry, cited as containing "sweet potatoes," piqued our interest. To our disappointment "sweet" turned out to be plain old white potatoes, a somewhat ho-hum addition to the chicken, onions, peanuts and carrots in the dish. Furthermore, the mild coconut milk curry sauce lacked the garam masala-spice mixture that differentiates a massaman curry from other Thai curries. Tamarind duck, another entree, consisted of excessively fatty duck slices, scallions, mushrooms, slices of red pepper, onions and canned pineapple cubes; the sweet-sour tamarind tang was missing.
Desserts are minimal at Bangkok Gardens, but the ginger ice cream made a pleasing palate refresher. Other options were coconut ice cream and canned lichees.
On one visit four of us spent $65 (before tax, tip and beverages) for a dinner that consisted of three courses apiece. There is a minuscule wine list and several beer choices, including Singha, the Thai beer that makes a light, agreeable accompaniment to a Thai meal. The price is certainly right at Bangkok Gardens and so is the location. Now if only greater attention were paid to the cuisine, this garden could really bloom.
 
Bangkok Gardens
Satisfactory
172 York Street (between Chapel and Crown), New Haven. 789-8684.
Atmosphere: An ample dining space divided into smoking (inside) and nonsmoking (a three-sided glass porch with glass roof) sections, decorated with Thai brocade hangings and artifacts.
Service: Usually competent but can be a bit flip.
Recommended dishes: Satays, Bangkok rolls, goong tad, Bangkok basil, chaiya noodles, ginger ice cream.
Price range: Lunch entrees $4.50 to $6.50, dinner entrees $6.95 to $10.95.
Credit cards: American Express, Mastercard and Visa.
Hours: Lunch 11 A.M. to 3 P.M. daily, dinner 3 to 10 P.M. Sunday through Thursday and 3 to 11 P.M. Friday and Saturday.
Reservations: Accepted.
Wheelchair accessibility: Ground-level access.

HEADLINE: After Shoreham Fiasco, Break Up Lilco

 BODY:
If the chairman of Lilco ["New Style Emerges as Lilco Revives," June 18] thinks that the company suffered during the 80's with the Shoreham fiasco, what about the innocent ratepayers who have to pay the huge cost of that mess for the next decade?
When you add in the interest charges, the price of Shoreham will be an easy $10 billion, which is higher on a per capita basis than the cost of the greatest American financial scandal: the Federal S. & L. swindle. 
 Shoreham and Lilco, our own local S. & L. scandal, is costing the Island ratepayer (a k a the public) dearly, and it is the public that is and will suffer from Lilco's mismanagement, while the managers collect fat bonuses and golden parachutes after the Governor's "wonderful deal."
What nerve to tell the innocent victims about Lilco's suffering, especially when this scandal hurts the poor, the elderly on fixed incomes and the most productive part of our economy -- small business! We the public are being robbed, and Lilco should be ashamed to say that "it now wants to help cure the sick economy."
Lilco's chairman may claim that his "critics do not understand the situation," but the critics were right all along. We don't have a nuclear Shoreham, and we never had the massive power crisis, the off-shore oil wells or "the one or more coal plants" that Lilco supporters threatened. If Lilco listened to its critics (who are probably hundreds of thousands, instead of tens of thousands), and converted to a gas-fired plant when Shoreham's price doubled, our rates would be near the national average. And projections of the average U.S. electric rate actually show a slight decline by the year 2000. Instead of now having the highest rates in the country, rates that will increase again by 60 to 100 percent under the Governor's deal, our electric rates could be below average with the inclusion of the Island's share of New York State hydropower.
Hydro that keeps Greenport's rates low and that was built by an Islander, Robert Moses, should be a major part of our power mix, no matter what Lilco says. But the maldistribution of this cheap, reliable and safe power in New York State is another way in which the Island public is being robbed besides the Shoreham swindle. If New Jersey can successfully sue for a share of this hydro, why can't the Island? Instead of suing Suffolk, Lilco could copy the Jersey brief and try to get us our share. But I guess there's nothing in it for them. And Cuomo seems to be unwilling to get hydro from upstate or Quebec.
This Shoreham saga has been riddled with baloney from Lilco all along, but the claim from them that "Lilco is no longer a monopoly" is blatantly not true. Nor only are they a monopoly, they are a double monopoly, controlling two vital sources of power that should compete with each other. Edison may have copied the gas works when he started his electric companies during the 1880's, but there is no synergy involved in combining gas and electric power in one-company-only greed.
Economic competition works. The Sherman Antitrust Act is supposed to work, and the recent breakup of A.T. & T. is already paying off in reduced rates and better service. Now is the time to start on the road to breaking up Lilco. If a well-heeled, respected company like A.T. & T., with a national treasure like Bell Labs, can be broken up, so too can a nearly bankrupt, despised Lilco with a national disgrace like Shoreham.
 
JOHN HURLEY
Remsenburg

HEADLINE: An Epitaph for Shoreham From Cuomo

 BODY:
From a podium near the reactor core Gov. Mario M. Cuomo delivered a requiem for the Shoreham Nuclear Power Station on June 17 to start the two-year decommissioning of the plant. The project, which will be carried out by the Long Island and New York Power Authorities, is expected to cost $186 million. It involves a partial dismantling and the removal of radioactive parts from the $5.5 billion plant.
After off-the-cuff remarks, Mr. Cuomo pressed a button to make an underwater torch sever a pipe that would have been part of the emergency cooling system. Following are his remarks: 
 I don't think I have the words to describe the significance of this event. This is a nearly incredible situation, I dare say, anywhere in the world, certainly anywhere in the country, where you stop the population and say, "Let us tell you exactly what happened on Long Island."
They built a great new modern facility. It cost them $5 billion and more. It was only supposed to cost $500 million. And many of us shouted from the early stages that it oughtn't to be done.
But forces that wanted to build it were greater than the forces against it in the beginning, and so they went forward to the point where they have an almost inexplicably complicated structure, billions and billions of dollars.
And a lot of people, including the Governor, felt that in the end it was threatening people's lives in a way that you could not tolerate. And no amount of money, no amount of investment could be great enough to excuse the risk that it would impose on even a single human life.
 
'How Do You Value Life?'
 And this then was the test of society on Long Island as well as New York State. How do you value life? All this vast power, all these billions of dollars, all the major newspapers, all the big shots, most of the politicians, most of the Republicans, most of the Democrats, the entire Legislature's refusing to stop it, because they were afraid of the power of the thing.
Everybody refusing to take a position. Nobody on the side of what we thought was the truth. Nobody. Even the Governor, for all that he tried to do, did not have the power to stop it by himself. How is it, then, that the plant was stopped?
It was not the Governor. It was not [State Economic Development Director] Vincent Tese. It was not [Long Island Power Authority chairman] Richie Kessel. It wasn't Irving [Like, a lawyer and authority trustee opposed to the plant]. It wasn't Nora [Bredes, a leader of citizen opposition to the plant and now a Suffolk County Legislator]. It wasn't all the people that struggled in the streets. We didn't do it. We never passed a bill to do it. We didn't stop it physically.
Well then, what stopped it? You try explaining that. I do. I tried in Tokyo, I tried at The Wall Street Journal. I tried from time to time in Washington and elsewhere. There are media people in this place right now who don't understand.
It was not stopped by any act of the Legislature. It was not stopped by an executive order of the Governor. It was not stopped by any act of the Department of Economic Development.
 
Recalcitrance of the Legislature
 It was stopped by consent of Lilco and the shareholders and a lot of people who decided it did not make sense. What won here in the end was the inexorable force of common sense and intelligence.
No political power won. All the political power was against us. I could not get the Legislature to vote on it. You talk about disgraceful performances. I could not get them to vote on it. Think of that. Millions of human lives at stake, and they did not want to take a position. You try being the governor under those circumstances.
And so it wasn't politics. It was common sense. No. 1, the threat. No. 2, the practicality. This would have wasted money in the long run. It is easier to close it down, to decommission it, to convert it, than to go forward with it. Common sense.
Now there's a heck of a thought. Imagine if you can apply that kind of intelligence to all the other near disasters in this society. If you had the capacity as a state and as a nation to spend $5 billion on something like this and at the very last moment recognize intelligence, turn it all around and now convert it into something which I guarantee you is going to be better.
Because even if you do not find an alternative use for this very facility when they do the arithmetic 10 years from now, the shutting of this plant will have been the smart thing to do on the dollars, on the economics, on the numbers.
The way you are correcting this mistake, you can correct them all. All the mistakes you have made in the budget deficit and squandered money on military devices that you no longer need, all the failed investment in education and poor people and people who need an opportunity, all the inability of the Congress and the Presidency and governments everywhere to do the right thing that has put us now on the downward slide economically -- the way you turned around Shoreham you can turn around anything. With common sense, with intelligence.
If only some people are persistent enough to keep shouting it at the public, this country will recognize that in the end. That's what Shoreham means.
Common sense and intelligence closed it. And common sense and intelligence can bring us back to the top in this world economically, can reverse some of the stupid things we are doing. It can correct the foolish differences we have among one another, the blacks, the African-Americans, the rest of the population, constantly people's looking to divide us and put us against one another. Stupidity, lack of intelligence, lack of common sense. Shoreham is the reminder that in the end we can be smart, that in the end we can be good.
I am very pleased to have been a small part of it. Thank you.

HEADLINE: No-Frills Insurance Proposed for Cars

BYLINE: By JAY ROMANO

 BODY:
AFTER years of paying some of the highest automobile insurance premiums in the country, New Jersey motorists may soon be able to purchase bare-bones insurance for as little as $250 a year.
Two proposals about to work their way through the Legislature would dramatically change the state's compulsory-insurance law to allow drivers to buy greatly reduced coverage for a fraction of what they now pay, which can exceed $2,500 a year. 
 The no-frills policies, supporters say, are intended for New Jersey's estimated 500,000 uninsured motorists, many of whom are believed to need their cars for work but cannot afford to buy insurance at current rates.
Both proposals have influential legislators behind them. Senator Gerald Cardinale, Republican of Demarest, who is sponsoring one of the measures, is the chairman of the Commerce Committee, which presides over insurance matters in the Senate. The sponsor of the other proposal is Assemblywoman Clare M. Farragher, Republican of Freehold Township, who is chairwoman of the Assembly Insurance Committee.
Both proposals would reduce the amount of personal injury protection coverage required under the state's no-fault insurance law to $15,000 from $250,000. And under Mr. Cardinale's proposal, a person with a no-frills policy would not be required to have liability insurance if he or she gives up the right to sue for pain and suffering resulting from an accident caused by another driver. Currently every automobile must be covered by at least $15,000 of liability coverage, which is used to pay claims for pain and suffering of those injured in an accident caused by an insured driver.
Insurance industry officials said the proposals appeared to be a first step toward more affordable insurance. But some trial lawyers argued that as many as half the state's automobile owners might sign up for low-cost, bare-bones policies, leaving them with inadequate coverage.
And the possibility that drivers could waive their liability coverage was described by Gerald H. Baker, a Hoboken lawyer who specializes in automobile negligence cases, as "trading the legal rights of the innocent victim in order to protect the economic rights of the careless driver."
The average price in New Jersey for full- coverage insurance -- including comprehensive and collision -- for a person with a clean driving record is now about $825. That figure soars for drivers with bad records.
"We are currently forcing people to pay as much as $2,500 for insurance when they don't really need that much coverage," Mr. Cardinale said. He added that the no-frills policy he was proposing would sell for about $300.
Mrs. Farragher said the $15,000 minimum would be adequate to cover the insured person's medical expenses in 90 percent of cases. In those cases where medical costs exceed $15,000, the insured person would be responsible for the balance.
Both proposals also change the amount of insurance required to cover the cost of repairing vehicles involved in accidents. Mr. Cardinale's plan would reduce the minimum property damage coverage required to $2,000 from $5,000. Mrs. Farragher's proposal would increase the minimum coverage to $10,000.
But the two proposals differ most markedly in the area of liability insurance coverage.Under Mr. Cardinale's proposal, car owners could avoid buying liability coverage by relinquishing their right to sue for pain and suffering, but they would not be prohibited from suing for actual economic losses, like wages.
"It's cat and dog suits that really run up our insurance bills in New Jersey," Mr. Cardinale said, adding that under his proposal, "a lot of those suits will be eliminated."
Mr. Cardinale acknowledges that a no-frills policy is inappropriate for most drivers, particularly those with assets, since such a policy affords little or no protection against a lawsuit.
"We're going to be putting in a very dramatic disclosure statement saying, 'If you own a house, you're crazy to take this,' " he said.
Some trial lawyers, however, say no-frills policies provide inexpensive insurance at the expense of innocent accident victims. Allowing drivers to drive without liability coverage, Mr. Baker said, will make it nearly impossible for an accident victim to recover damages for pain and suffering from the party at fault.
"You can sue them and win and you get nothing," Mr. Baker said. "The purpose of compulsory liability insurance is not to protect the assets of the careless driver; it's to protect the innocent people who are injured in accidents."
 
Concern Over 'Bandwagon'
 Mr. Baker also expressed concern that many drivers other than those who were currently uninsured might opt to buy the bare-bones policies.
"I would bet you that 50 percent of the public jumps on the bandwagon because they think they're getting a bargain," he said. And that, Mr. Baker said, would result in more people's driving around with minimal coverage, and an increase in the cost of insurance for the responsible drivers who buy conventional insurance.
Thomas J. Vesper, an Atlantic City lawyer who specializes in personal injury cases, described Mr. Cardinale's proposal to require the purchasers of no-frills policies to give up their right to sue for pain and suffering as "offensive and unconscionable" because it would take advantage of a disadvantaged person's desperation.
"They might say 'great' today," he said, "but what happens tomorrow when they're in an accident and lose a limb or lose a fetus?"
 
A Different Approach
 Mrs. Farragher's version of no-frills insurance takes a different approach to the liability issue.
"We toyed with the idea of taking out the mandatory liability coverage," she said, "but settled on a $15,000 single-limit bodily injury liability per car."
A $15,000 single-limit policy means that $15,000 is the maximum amount that would be paid for pain and suffering for injuries sustained in an accident, regardless of how many people are injured. Current law requires a "$15,000/$30,000" minimum, which means that any one person can recover up to $15,000, with a maximum of $30,000 being available for each accident.
Mrs. Farragher said her proposal was the result of recommendations and suggestions that her committee received over the last six months from insurance experts across the state.
 
'Every Possible Interest Group'
 "We sent a letter to every possible interest group that had anything to do with auto insurance," she said. "We wanted to develop an affordable product that could be marketed to the working poor who sometimes have to choose between making their auto insurance payment and paying their rent or feeding their families."
Mrs. Farragher said the final cost of her proposed policy had not yet been determined. "We've had numbers from $250 a year to $500," she said.
Insurance industry officials say that while more radical changes will ultimately be necessary, the proposed no-frills policies represent a first step toward more sensible insurance requirements.
"We're saying that the no-frills policy is fine for the limited number of folks who don't need and can't afford anything else," said Jerry Lane, regional vice president of the State Farm Insurance Company in Wayne."It essentially makes a person street-legal."

HEADLINE: ABOUT NEW JERSEY

BYLINE: By Anthony DePalma

 BODY:
IT was a honey of a day, so sweet with sunshine and June skies that Newark looked scrubbed. Real nice.
In such light the three blocks from Pennsylvania Station to Broad Street make for an impressive show. Buildings there are tall, glassy and appropriately angular, the way they are expected to be in any modern city. The overhead walkway above Raymond Boulevard connecting the train station to the Legal and Communication Center even makes Newark look, at that angle, futuristic. 
 The home-bound rush hour was over, but there still was plenty of light left in the day. Light, but not life, for Newark's main streets had already been all but abandoned to the night. The only people around were a woman in a white uniform standing impatiently outside the Wilfred Academy, smoking a cigarette, and a knot of people near Military Park waiting for some way-late bus. A shirtless man sat on a bench asking for change.
There was one place, however, that hummed with both light and life. Above the door a telling sign: Free Public Library. Emphasis on free.
Most every community has a library; it is one of the elements that transforms a settlement into a community. But few places have great libraries. Usually these are reserved for universities and the odd cities that transcend the confusion of special interests to create institutions rooted in the forceful notion that unless all of us prosper, none of us do.
The building is a three-story limestone classic with a confident facade and an aura of rock-steadiness. It sits essentially unchanged in an unlucky city where so much else has been irreversibly altered. Approaching it from the sidewalk on Washington Street, one is struck by the notion that the Newark Public Library is a great institution in a city that once believed it too would be great.
Most New Jersey residents have never set foot inside the main branch of the library, but those who have understand it is a special place that, in a sense, belongs not just to the 275,000 people who now live in Newark but to the 7.7 million people in the state. It is a treasure, albeit something of a hidden one, that is now endangered by a shortage of dollars and the limits of vision.
Newark, like every city, is feeling the economic squeeze of rising costs and inadequate resources. Something had to be cut. And along with lots of other things, the administration of Mayor Sharpe James decreased the library's budget by 15 percent this year. That has meant the library is closed on Sundays, and on the three late nights it is open until 8:30, not 9 o'clock.
The book purchasing budget was slashed by more than 25 percent, which actually translates into a far worse cut because books and subscriptions are rising in cost so quickly that just staying even requires more money from one year to the next.
There have been other cuts, too. The staff is down to 195, 65 fewer than a year ago. At the same time the number of people using the library is up, as always happens when times get bad.
Or it means that while the library itself is open, some departments will be dark. On this night, the Sala Hispanoamericana, a new Spanish-language collection, had to be kept locked because there wasn't enough staff to man it.
The library's gentle-mannered director, Alex Boyd, does not complain much. He says he understands the predicament Newark is in, having to choose between one good purpose and another. Dressed in a neat gray suit, with a lavender shirt and tie, he is the picture of composure. But the strain is there.
"I don't know how long our fingernails will last," he said during an interview in his third-floor office, "but we're hanging in there."
Part of the problem is Dr. Boyd's doing. You see, he has pushed the library into a dilemma. When he came from the Chicago Public Library four years ago, he was determined to throw open long-stuck windows. He has kept all one million books on the shelves, and added thousands more, but his focus is setting up all kinds of activities to reach out to the community and bring back the library to what its most influential director, John Cotton Dana, had conceived of as "a people's university."
Dr. Boyd did everything he could to entice people into the library. He spruced it up with paint and carpet and user-friendly computers. He had his staff organize all kinds of hands-on activities for children, hoping that by inviting the little ones to puppet shows or barbecues he can whet their appetites for the wonders of knowledge.
It has worked, in a sense too well. Having hooked them, the library has found that the new patrons required more services, just when money has grown scarce.
Dr. Boyd doesn't like to retreat. The cuts made so far fall into the category of what he calls "selective pruning" which, he said, if done right, makes the main stem stronger without any permanent damage.
"We'll downsize somewhat, but we don't want to stop anything all together because if you let anything go you may never get it back," he said. "We don't want to lose the heart and soul of the library because of what we hope is a temporary financial difficulty."
By 8:25 that night the streets of Newark have cooled a bit, the shadows having gone to encroaching night. The library begins to empty, and it's like one of those old television routines where the clowns keep coming out of the Volkswagen.
Here comes Robert Pennick, a 29-year-old part-time student at Union County College, coming from a tutoring session that the college requires him to attend. Behind him, a group of Spanish-speaking people who had been closeted on the third floor practicing their English pronunciation: "Draw. Drew. Drawn. Difficult, eh?"
A man and a woman come out together with a videotape from the library's circulating collection. Benjamin Martinez and his son Damian come out into the night air, each holding a book.
"It's lovely to be here," said Mr. Martinez, who has lived in Newark's Ironbound section for more than 30 years and who says he comes to the library at least once a week to borrow books. He has checked out a Spanish-language history of Puerto Ricans in America.
Mr. Martinez said the shorter hours had made it somewhat more difficult for him to use the library. He firmly believes that such cuts are, in the end, counterproductive. "Let me put it to you this way," he said. "If they cut things that are going to educate people, what good is that?"
A man with crazed eyes stumbles out of the library, shouting threats and heading into an uncertain night. But this is a big city, and no one pays much attention.
Behind the man swarms a flock of teen-agers, mostly girls but with a few boys mixed in. They have been coming once a month since last September to participate in the Delta Sigma Theta Teen Lift program, run by volunteer sorority members who live in the Newark area.
The teen-agers come to hear women like Patricia Davis Adams and Beverly J. Evans, who graduated years ago and have successfully launched their own careers. They talk about how much more there is to life than the streets of Newark offer.
"People need the library," said Tameika Carter, a 16-year-old from Irvington who said she often came to the library to help her mother, Joyce, a lawyer, do research in the library's outstanding New Jersey Reference Room. She said she also used the library for her own book reports and papers, and she came for some of the cultural programs.
"You come here to read up on history," she said. "All your history is in library books."
A white-shirted security guard comes out finally to close the gate and lock the doors of the library for another night. But he can't.
Inside the library's elegant marble vestibule, two young children in plastic fireman's hats have found out they can make loud noises. They seem to like the sound. Given the obvious affection they've already developed for the place, chances are they will grow up to be two more of Dr. Boyd's converts, two more budget busters who have developed the notion that libraries are fine places to be, if only you can get in.

HEADLINE: ART;
Pictures of an Accessible Russia Capture a Fleeting Moment

BYLINE: By VIVIEN RAYNOR

DATELINE: SOMERS

 BODY:
ARTISTS have traditionally forgathered in countries not their own. Depending on the time, they went to Germany, Italy or France -- wherever the art action was. But since World War II their reasons for relocating have been more often political and economic than esthetic and, seemingly, most have come to the United States.
As a rule of thumb, political trouble abroad augurs a new influx into American galleries. Cuban artists have been among the most visible, Romanians among the most recent. Yet, the emphasis is still on the Russians, who first became noticeable in the 1970's. Not long ago, two large collections of Russian art were bestowed on Rutgers University's Zimmerli Art Museum in New Brunswick, N.J., prompting plans to establish a Russian studies center. 
 Into this grand context must be placed the latest Russian manifestation in Westchester, a modest show at the Somerstown Gallery here by four painters, a printmaker and three photographers, some of them Russians and some using Russian subjects.
To take the painters first, Marjorie Michaels, a Russian, focuses on the churches on the Golden Ring, a tourist route in and around Moscow. Washed on canvas in thin acrylic, the pictures look postcardy and, in fact, were based on illustrations for a book about the route, which was recently published in St. Petersburg.
All the same, the grandeur of these structures shows through. The Mongol Empire, the largest in the history of the world, has not had a good press but at least it bequeathed to Russia the incomparable onion-shaped dome. There is even one gracing the all-wood Church of the Transfiguration in the village of Hogliat Yevo.
Leonid Hushnatov paints men's heads, giving them titles like "Inspired Spiritual" and "Beaten, Hackneyed." Some of the subjects are bald and bearded while others are turbaned, but all look somewhat alike. He also shows a not-bad view of a landscape inundated by a flood.
Still, the best painting is the heavily impastoed study of a cherry tree in bloom by Dima Karabchievsky, while the most problematic pictures come from Sergei Polyakov. Inspired by Russian folk tales, these may prove too extravagant even for Americans partial to camp.
For example, "Mikula," a scantily clad peasant with Schwarzeneggerian muscles, charges toward the viewer pushing a hand plow; "Father Frost" has a white beard so long that it seems to drool across the landscape; "Veles" is an old man who sits among trees with reindeer antlers on his head and is encircled by a crowd of miniature horses, bears and rabbits. Mr. Polyakov's technique also leaves something to be desired.
Andre Shabunin gets the show back on track with his mezzotints of figures -- a family group and a woman at her toilette, which may be based on photographs, and a portrait of himself wearing a Rembrandtian tam-o'-shanter.
Then come the photographers, of whom Lisa Toto, an American, gets the most space. Most of her pictures are color prints of familiar types -- the old folk musician with the bifurcated beard, women with seamed faces in babushkas, flaxen-haired children, fashion plates looking not too different from their American contemporaries who affect the styles of the 1950's, and so on. Nudging the viewer along are such titles as "All Work and No Play," "Years of Pain" and "Generation to Generation."
But when visiting Chernobyl (a brave thing to do), Ms. Toto abandons journalistic cliches and produces grainy black and whites that are unnerving in their ordinariness. There is a thicket of pylons that could be in New Jersey, and a baby carriage abandoned in a rural cul-de-sac suggests poverty in the Appalachians. A solitary sandal could be one of hundreds that appear on New York City streets, raising the question of why one never sees people half shod.
A Russian photographer, Sergei Kuzmin, takes stark but effective black and whites of daily life. Two smiling Siamese twins joined at the hip pose in a corridor with one crutch apiece; a day starts in a communal kitchen with a tousled man scratching himself as he takes a coffee pot from the stove, and a woman sits on a folding chair making up her face. In another shot, the head of a theater company stands in his office before a wall scrawled with what seem to be congratulatory messages.
Also working in black and white is Anatoly Loshmanov, who deals mainly with children in all their ethnic diversity, but includes a shot of a masked surgeon at work.
These photographers, like many of those in the recent Russian exhibition at the Lehman College Art Gallery, suggest that now is a precious moment: a country that has been closed for about 70 years has much to show the world and does not yet have an abundance of gadgetry and color film with which to glamorize its findings.
The show is on view through July 5.
Regarding hardship, most museums may be having a bad time financially, but Somerstown has prospered enough to open the Gallery in the Schoolhouse in Croton Falls. Information about both spaces may be obtained by calling 277-3461.

GRAPHIC: Photo: "Emptiness," a scene photographed at Chernobyl by Lisa Toto, one of the photographers on display at the Somerstown Gallery. (The Somerstown Gallery)

HEADLINE: MARKET WATCH;
Rates Are Just One Piece of the Puzzle

BYLINE: By Michael Quint

 BODY:
President Bush has again urged the Federal Reserve to lower interest rates. It was still another reminder that the economic recovery remains sluggish despite short-term rates that have already been cut to their lowest level in 20 years.
Undoubtedly, a further move by the Fed would help the economy a bit by prompting cuts in the prime rate charged by banks, causing the banks to cut rates on deposits so low that consumers and businesses would have more incentive to spend or invest. 
 But the economy's muted response so far is a reminder that the willingness of banks to make loans is just as important as the level of interest rates. Bank loans to businesses have been declining the past year, one more reason for the slower than normal rebound in the economy.
According to an article soon to be published in the annual report of the Federal Reserve Bank of Boston, bank regulators' efforts the past few years to cure the banking system of the ills that resulted from lax supervision in the 1980's may have aggravated the lending squeeze -- and the economy's malaise.
Rather than blame legions of nameless bank examiners for being too tough, Richard F. Syron, president of the Boston Fed and author of the article, says regulators compounded their earlier mistakes when they imposed tougher capital standards on banks that were weakened by loan losses. With that change, he said, the banks' capital lost its traditional role as a buffer available to absorb losses in times of stress. That role allowed banks to continue their business without wrenching adjustments to lending practices and customers.
But as bank failures led to losses at the Federal deposit insurance fund, regulators began seeing capital as a pool of money to share those losses, Mr. Syron said. When regulators told the weakest banks to strengthen their capital quickly to 6 percent or 8 percent of their assets, well above the 3 percent minimum standard, Mr. Syron said, many New England banks could comply only by selling their best assets and shutting off the spigot for new loans.
The changes at banks went "well beyond any desirable backing away from overly liberal lending terms," Mr. Syron said, and caused severe harm to the local economy and many customers, especially small businesses. He said a gentler policy could have helped the economy, which would have done more to reduce losses in the bank insurance fund than any belated efforts to strengthen capital.
While regulators still say that banks are better off with more capital, they do not seem inclined to make standards tougher in the immediate future. Under the Fed's preliminary version of new capital rules for banks most sensitive to interest rate shifts, just a small part of the industry will find itself in need of additional amounts.
Meanwhile, the Fed can only work with the tools it has, and that suggests another cut in rates.

HEADLINE: World Markets;
The Rocky Road to a Global Market

BYLINE: By Jonathan Fuerbringer

 BODY:
"Our market will be the market where the action will take place," said Jean-Francois Theodore, the chief executive officer of the Bourse de Paris. It is the kind of comment expected of a man in his job, especially when he is visiting the United States to drum up business for the French stock market.
This is the message he brought to the pension funds, brokerage firms and investment banks that he met with: France is a market poised for explosive growth, especially as the Government moves to sell to the public many state-owned or -controlled blue-chip companies, like Rhone-Poulenc, Air France, Banque Nationale de Paris and Credit Lyonnais. It is a market that has been modernized, with a computer trading system driven totally by orders. 
 "The market is not going to be lively in Germany," Mr. Theodore said. "It is a cultural thing. There is no privatization." And in Britain, he added, "everything has already taken place."
What he did not explain on his tour, but did detail in an interview last week in New York, are his views on the broader future of the French Bourse, which is up 8.5 percent this year, and European stock markets as the political and economic unification of Europe approaches at the end of the century.
On this issue, he embodies all the parochial and national desires that will make this transition difficult, especially in the market for retail investors. He is caught between the market forces that are pushing change and the desire to protect his own turf. His comments on the future should be as much a warning to those who have chosen not to worry about the obstacles ahead as was the Danes' surprising rejection of the outline for political and monetary union in a referendum at the beginning of June.
In a new book, "The End of Geography," Richard O'Brien, chief economist at American Express Bank in London, also issues a warning. He sees market forces, like technological advances and the free flow of capital, pushing financial markets toward a global state in which the location of a company's headquarters or where financial business is conducted will no longer be important.
But the transition to that "seamless" market, he warns, will be grim. "A more sober look at the crystal ball," he writes, "suggests a rather bumpy road toward seamlessness: intense competition among financial firms, with major bankruptcies, forced mergers followed by massive shakeouts, crises for customers and producers alike, repeated losses from overambitious investment in new technologies, financial crises leading to the socialization of losses (a la S&Ls) and seemingly intractable problems in reconciling the fundamental differences between contrasting financial systems."
Mr. Theodore acknowledges that the hegemony of local stock markets in Europe will be challenged, likely by outside trading networks that will try to establish their own pan-European market for big blue-chip stocks. It is the kind of challenge that the London stock market has already offered with its concentration on the trading of foreign stocks. (Mr. Theodore dismisses claims by the London market and others that a major share of French stock trading takes place in London, arguing that the London market "is always counting trades two, three or four times.")
He says there should be more cooperation among Europe's stock exchanges on trading as a defense against the success of some outside network. He lamented the fact that a trading system called Euroquote, which would allow the trading of blue-chip stocks among exchanges, has been stalled because of opposition in Germany and Britain.
But, he still concludes, "I don't believe in a pan-European stock exchange. At the end of the 90's, there will be four or five or six stock exchanges, each having the lead in its own domestic stocks." He argues that despite the internationalization of many companies, like Nestle in Switzerland and Peugeot and Alcatel Alstholm in France, the country where the company is based will still determine where most of its stock is traded and where the stock will be most liquid.
"The internationalization of a company does not mean the internationalization of the stock," he said. "The home country will always mean something."
One reason he thinks a broader market will be difficult to achieve is that the merging of company and takeover laws throughout a unified Europe will be very difficult because of the desire to protect the home base. "It is more difficult to converge on company law," he said, than on inflation.
Despite the problems ahead, Mr. Theodore seems to be resisting the change that is coming. It may not be pan-European, but it seems more likely that there could be a two-tier system, with major blue-chip stocks trading as much outside their own countries as in them, while smaller stocks stay home.
Mr. O'Brien, like Mr. Theodore, thinks it will be much harder to move to a truly global equities market than it has been to create an international foreign exchange market. But he also doubts whether any stock market will be able to maintain a national base in a world where location is losing its importance.
The Bourse approach, Mr. O'Brien writes, "presupposes that in the future it will be easy to identify French versus Italian versus British stocks, when companies themselves are becoming more European and/or global in an end-of-geography world."
He also asks if it will be feasible for French companies to "confine trades in French stocks to one market if those companies could enjoy better conditions elsewhere, as a result of tapping a wider marketplace."

SECTION: Section 3; Page 2; Column 3; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 1651 words

HEADLINE: Business Diary/June 21-26

BYLINE: By Joel Kurtzman

 BODY:

THE ECONOMY
 
While the Other Guys Brawl, Clinton Sneaks In a Platform 

While Ross Perot and the Republicans dominated the airwaves with their slugfest over "opposition research," Gov. Bill Clinton and the Democrats released drafts of their party platform. The plan -- which they call the "third way" because they see it as between laissez-faire and welfare-state economics -- proposes increased taxes on the wealthy and on corporations and a 5 percent cut in middle-class taxes -- reduced from Mr. Clinton's original proposal of a 10 percent tax cut. It also calls for a cut in capital gains taxes, new tax credits for the working poor and heavy spending on schools and highways. The tax increases will enable the Democrats to chop the Federal deficit in half by 1996. "It's clearly time to reassess and change our policies and face reality," Mr. Clinton said. Predictably, the Republicans were not impressed. The Clinton plan "has been strictly for media consumption," said Richard G. Darman, President Bush's budget director.
 
A Presidential Prod at the Fed
 
While economists noodle over whether lower interest rates will add spark to the economy or just fuel inflation, President Bush made his views known last week. "I'd like to see another lowering of interest rates," he said, in a statement aimed at prodding Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve, to push down rates. But the Federal Reserve only controls short-term rates. Longer rates -- the ones mortgages and car payments are pegged to -- are determined by the market. "Long-term rates are about where they were in November, even though the Fed has eased and eased short-term rates," said Mickey D. Levy, chief economist at CRT Government Securities. When the Fed meets this week, Mr. Bush's urgings will still be in the air. "It's poor practice for the President to jawbone the Fed. There's value in an independent central bank," Mr. Levy said.
 
Clean Air on Endangered List?
 
It was a curious decision for the environmental President to make. But last week, the Bush Administration gave manufacturers authorization to increase the amount of hazardous pollutants they emit into the atmosphere. The rule allows them to increase by 245 tons the amount of pollution they pour into the air without public notice or hearings. The Environmental Protection Agency issued the rule at the behest of the President's Council on Competitiveness. The rule, "carves the heart out of the Clean Air Act," said Representative Henry A. Waxman, Democrat of California. President Bush supported passage of the act.

G.D.P. History Repeats Itself
 
When the Commerce Department said last week that gross domestic product grew by 2.7 percent in the first quarter, it was a trip down memory lane. Last year, the first quarter looked promising but the economy lost its oomph by spring. "The numbers we have for May indicate a slowdown," said Norman Robertson, chief economist at the Mellon Bank.
 
Full Disclosure on Executive Pay
 
To prevent executives from lining their pockets with stock options and fat paychecks at the expense of the shareholders, the Securities and Exchange Commission proposed new rules for disclosing how executives are paid. The rules require companies to publish what their top executives are paid in a series of tables, graphs and notes. The S.E.C. wants them to plot those figures -- including the value of stock options -- alongside shareholder's return on investment over a five-year period. It also wants charts comparing companies' rates of return with those of their competitors. Shareholder groups have applauded the changes, while the Bush Administration opposes them as meddling.

A Surprising Drop in Durables
 
Orders for durable goods, those big ticket items that are supposed to last three years or more, fell by 2.4 percent in May, the Commerce Department said. That was a surprise to most economists. The consensus forecast was that orders for durable goods would rise. "The goods orders mean we shouldn't expect employment growth in manufacturing in the near term," said Charles McMillion, president of MBG Group, a business information firm in Washington. "Services linked to manufacturing -- software, trucking, insurance -- will also see an absence of high wage growth," he said.
 
 
 
INTERNATIONAL

 
Israel's Shift in Direction
 
By voting into power a Labor Government headed by Yitzhak Rabin, Israel will be getting a Prime Minister who says his first priority is self-rule for Palestinians in the occupied territories. Israelis will also be getting a Prime Minister who opposes new Jewish settlements in those lands. Mr. Rabin's plans are likely to please Washington. The Bush Administration held up Israel's request for $10 billion in loan guarantees because of the previous Government's pro-settlement policy.

Minivans: Japan, 1; Detroit, Zip
 
Relativism may be the scourge of the White House, but it's still in favor at the International Trade Commission. The commission decided that although the Commerce Department determined Toyota and Mazda had been selling minivans here for less than in Japan, penalties should not be imposed because their sales were still too small to hurt Detroit. The commission said its decision was based on whether American car companies suffered "material injury." Detroit, which claims Japan's dumping has hurt its minivan sales, was incensed by the decision. In a statement, the Ford Motor Company said the commission signaled to "Japan that it's O.K. to defy the international rules of trade, and, what's more, that it doesn't hurt America."
 
Agriculture Is No Magic Kingdom
 
As protests go, this one was pretty Mickey Mouse. Five hundred French farmers drove to Euro Disneyland near Paris to protest reforms that cut farm subsidies. The farmers parked tractors at the park's entrance and set tires on fire. Why Disneyland? "You're on American territory." said Henri Pepitas, an organizer. "They're the ones who wrote the reform."
 
 
 
COMPANIES

 
A Joint Deal for Flash Chips
 
The deal-making mavens at the International Business Machines Corporation are at it again. Last week, they made an agreement with Japan's Toshiba Corporation to produce flash memory chips jointly. Those are the chips invented by Toshiba in 1984 that can store large amounts of data even when the power is switched off. Some analysts see them as a replacement for magnetic disk drives, especially in notebook and laptop computers. But Big Blue and Toshiba face pretty stiff competition. The Intel Corporation commercialized Toshiba's invention and now has 80 percent of the market. Dataquest, a research firm in San Jose, Calif., expects sales of flash chips to climb to $1.5 billion in 1995, from $130 million today.
 
I.B.M. is not the only computer company sweet on Toshiba. Last week Apple Computer Inc., said it would jointly develop products with Toshiba. This link-up would develop products like CD-ROMS's, which can store data, text, images and sound.

Mr. Murdoch Was Not Amused
 
When Stephen Chao, the 36-year old the one-time wunderkind of TV president of the Fox Network, addressed a group of company executives and guests last week in Aspen, Colo., he wanted to demonstrate his opposition to censorship. While speaking to the audience, which included Rupert Murdoch, chairman of the News Corporation, which owns Fox, Defense Secretary Dick Cheney and his wife, Lynne V. Cheney, chairwoman of the National Endowment of the Humanities, a male stripper wandered onto the stage. As Mr. Chao spoke, the stripper disrobed. Mr. Murdoch, who was not amused by the demonstration, dismissed Mr. Chao from the company on the spot. "Sometimes the hardest job a captain has is to terminate his best lieutenant," Mr. Murdoch said.
 
The Bangle With a Past
 
Marlene Dietrich, who died last month at the age of 90, was hardly the girl next door. She was an exotic and alluring beauty with singular taste. Last week, Sotheby's announced it was auctioning off one of Ms. Dietrich's favorite bracelets, which it described as "a dazzling important ruby diamond bracelet," estimated to be worth $300,000 to $400,000.

Merck's Prostate Drug Potential
 
Scientists at the nation's pharmaceutical companies are always on the quest for the elusive billion-dollar drug. Merck & Company received Federal approval on one of them last week. Merck's new concoction, Proscar, is used for treating enlarged prostates. "Proscar has greater commercial potential than any other new drug in pharmaceutical history," said Ronald Nordmann an analyst at Paine Webber. Analysts estimate Proscar will be a $1 billion drug by 1994.

Bat Redux Reaps Record Return
 
The caped crusader's career really took off when he dumped his comic-book pal, Robin, and made it to the movies. "Batman," was one of the highest grossing films ever made. "Batman Returns," the sequel which opened last week, is so far even more of a bonanza. It raked in $46.5 million in its first weekend, a Hollywood record. For Warner Brothers Inc., which produced the thriller, full of bizarre sets and high-priced actors, such boffo results are welcome. It spent between $55 million and $75 million to make the film (accounting in Tinseltown is no science).

A Less-Than-Remorseful Sears
 
At first, Edward A. Brennan, chairman of Sears, Roebuck & Company, denied wrongdoing when California's Consumer Affairs Department accused it of charging people for car repairs they did not need. Sears put pressure on employees, Consumer Affairs charged, to sell a certain number of repairs or services during each eight-hour shift whether they were needed or not. Last week, Mr. Brennan apologized. But he attributed the overcharges to the company's commission plan, not to pressuring employees.

HEADLINE: All About/The Gold Market;
The Good News About Gold: There Isn't a Lot

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
Yes, there is some good news about gold.
Gold fillings are making a comeback as prices have retreated. And in 1991, the amount of gold used to make jewelry rose to 2,111 metric tons, a 3.7 percent increase from the year before and a new record, according to Gold Fields Mineral Services, the London-based precious metals research firm owned by three gold mining companies. It was the fifth straight year that jewelry manufacturing had increased. 
 It appears, according to the same Gold Fields study, that the huge increase in the production and overall supply of gold that lead to the sharp price declines of the last six years is leveling off. The total world supply of gold declined last year for the first time since 1983, and mine production showed the smallest increase since 1980.
Less supply could mean stable, if not immediately higher, prices. But the bad news right now is that these facts, and all the others that are mined daily in the gold market, still may not mean much in the way of a stronger price.
The problem for gold is that no matter what you think moves the price -- supply and demand, inflation, the dollar, wars, ideological conflict or even the possibility of a renewed gold standard -- none of these factors is currently providing much of a plus signal. Communism has faded; inflation is at low levels around the world, and gold supplies are shrinking but not plummeting. So no matter how you track gold, its outlook at the moment is neutral at best.

 
 
A Raging Debate
What Causes Prices To Rise and Fall?
 From an average price in 1987 of $446.07 an ounce, gold fell to $362.26 in 1991. Adjusted for inflation, gold in dollar terms is at its lowest level since the late 1970's and in Japanese yen its lowest since 1972. On Friday gold closed at $343.50 an ounce in London, up $8.40 from its low for the year. But at its 1992 low, gold was at a level not seen since 1986.
Despite the disappointing performance of gold, the debate still rages over what moves its price.
David Poole, a senior vice president and economist at Van Eck Associates Corporation in New York, is in what might be called the traditionalist camp. He is in some ways a "gold bug," someone who believes strongly that gold is the best insurance against any currency's depreciation.
For Mr. Poole, who has followed gold for more than 30 years, the price is moved by expectations of inflation and the strength or weakness of the dollar. Using data for these forces and other proxies, like the interest rate on 30-year Treasuries, Mr. Poole has traced the performance of gold since it was allowed to trade freely 21 years ago.
Like most gold bugs, Mr. Pool is optimistic: He sees a rebound in gold prices over the next two years. Mr. Poole is bullish because he expects the economic recovery to continue and to be a little stronger than the consensus outlook. With growth, Mr. Poole expects the rate of inflation to rise to the 5 percent range. This should lead to a further weakening of the dollar, with gold rising to around $500 an ounce. The last two rallies to the $500 level came when inflation rose from below the current 3 percent range into the 4.5 percent to 5 percent range.
Mr. Poole acknowledges that his theory is not perfect. For example, in his view, the price of gold should be rising when the dollar is falling. But that is not the case at the moment. The dollar, compared with other major currencies like the Japanese yen and German mark, has fallen 3.5 percent since the end of May, but gold has managed to inch up just 1.8 percent.

 
 
Money or Commodity?
Supply and Demand In the Ascendancy
 At the other end of the spectrum is the view that gold is moved basically by supply and demand. This means that gold is a commodity, like soybeans, wheat or coffee.
James R. Steel, a metals analyst at Refco Inc., the big commodities trading firm based in Chicago, believes that gold is part money and part commodity. But because inflation is at such lows, the money part is getting no help. Mr. Steele thinks that "supply and demand is going to increase in relevance."
Looking at the Gold Fields report, which the industry often refers to as "The Bible," one feels that gold is behaving more like a commodity. This is the case because forces other than supply and demand that could have an impact on gold prices -- like inflation, war and financial catastrophe -- seem to be receding.
In addition, there are more alternative investments today than 10 years ago, so gold is less likely to be a preferred investment. And the supply and demand factors over the last 10 or 15 years really look like they have had an impact. The rise in supply in the 1980's helped lead to the fall in the price of gold, the Gold Fields analysis shows. The slowing of that supply might now lend some support to the price.
But the report also postulates what Gold Fields considers a more likely supply and demand scenario. With sluggish economic growth, the slowing supply of gold will not become a force boosting the price very quickly. This in turn will encourage a lot of investors to sell gold, adding to the pressure on price and maintaining "a depressed gold price for some time to come," the report says.

 
 
Fillings
Dental Patients are Going for the Gold
 One of the key supply factors is mine production. According to Gold Fields, the continuing fall in the price of gold has left around one third of the world's industry unprofitable, although this does not mean they have stopped producing. In 1991, mine production rose only 2.1 percent, down from 3.7 percent in 1990 and 8 percent in 1989. If the price of gold falls further, the pressure on production will increase.
South Africa is the world's largest producer of gold, but it also has the second-highest average cost of production. There are only four mines in South Africa, according to Gold Fields, that have production costs under $250 an ounce. Eighty percent of the mines in South Africa have costs per ounce of gold that range from $230 to $372. At the higher range, the cost of producing an ounce of gold is higher than the metal's current selling price.
One clear effect of the lower price of gold is that people are putting it back into their teeth again.
The biggest players in the dental gold market are the Japanese and the Germans. In 1982, they put 31.3 metric tons of gold in their mouths -- half of the world's dental goldplate. But then Germany -- and the rest of the world's dental patients, except for the Japanese -- turned to less expensive alternatives like compacted porcelain, titanium and palladium-based alloys. Use of gold dropped just over 28 percent from 1982 to 1987.
But in Germany last year, the use of gold rose 22 percent. The reason: the fall of the Berlin Wall and the reunification of Germany. The former West German government has subsidized 80 percent of the cost of dental treatment for former East Germans. But this subsidy will be halved on July 1. So there has been a rush to get the gold in.
 
 
 
A LITTLE TARNISH ON PLATINUM

Platinum, another precious metal that is not as precious as it used to be. Because its major industrial use is in catalytic converters, platinum is moved by economic slowdowns or recoveries, when auto production is falling or rising. It can also be moved by new demand, like that expected when Europe tightens its air-quality standards and thus demands catalytic converters.
But the price of platinum is moved most abruptly by scares that some other material can be substituted for it. The most recent scare came in May of last year, when Nissan, the giant Japanese auto maker, said it was developing a palladium-only catalytic converter. Even though it was obvious that this converter could be used only on small cars in Japan, the price of platinum tumbled $30 an ounce.
But while platinum is not a mystic metal like gold, many in the market believe it has an inverse connection to the price of gold. And this can make it a hedge against adverse moves in the price of gold.
The outlook for platinum, according to the annual review released last month by Johnson Matthey, a refiner and marketer of precious metals based in London, is a little unclear. The metal rallied from a low of $331 an ounce in January to $378 in June.. But recently, said James R. Steel, a metals analyst at Refco Inc., the commodities firm based in Chicago, it has gotten a "little bit of a bloody nose." It closed on Friday at $360.40.
And while the Johnson Matthey report points out that supply will shrink this year and demand in Europe will rise, the slowdown of the economy in Japan could turn off any major rally. The Japanese are the world's chief consumers of platinum jewelry -- they bought 86 percent of the world's total last year.

HEADLINE: Mutual Funds;
Bright Spots in a Dim First Half

BYLINE: By Carole Gould

 BODY:
This hasn't been a good year for stock funds, with the total return of the average fund off 3.3 percent through last Thursday, according to Lipper Analytical Services Inc. For some funds, the first half was extremely painful -- the health and biotechnology sector slid nearly 20 percent, while Japanese funds fell 17.8 percent.
But there were some pleasant surprises, too. Consider what happened to the natural resource funds, which invest in a mix of energy, forest products and metals stocks. Last year, the group lost nearly 3 percent in total return, which measures income plus changes in principal, compared with an average 7 percent gain for all stock funds. This year, many in the group turned in positive returns and the 23 funds as a whole almost broke even, off just 0.3 percent. 
 Fund managers attribute the recovery to several factors. Oil prices hit bottom in February at $17.50 a barrel, then rose as the United States economy began to pick up and worldwide demand increased while production from the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries remained flat. Last week, the price of crude for August delivery hovered around $22.50. More dramatic yet were moves in natural gas prices, which dipped below $1 for 1,000 cubic feet during the winter but bounced up to more than $1.50, helped by a cold spring in the Northeast.
Combine these prices with intensive industrywide cost-cutting measures and the positive trend for the funds could continue "through the second half of 1992 and into 1993," said Ned Shadeck, manager of the $120 million Putnam Energy Resources Trust, which is up 4.4 percent this year. "Because energy is not as tightly correlated with the stock market as food or drug stocks, energy provides a great inflation hedge," Mr. Shadeck said, adding, however, that he recommends investing no more than 5 to 10 percent of a portfolio in the group.
John C. Neff, who runs the $8.1 billion Vanguard Windsor Fund, one of the nation's largest mutual funds, has been even more bullish recently about the energy sector. "We built our energy position to 13.1 percent of assets during the first half," he wrote last month in a report to shareholders.
While most of the natural resource funds concentrate solely on energy, a few small ones have more eclectic mixes which have paid off handsomely so far this year. These include the $24 million Prudential Global Natural Resources Fund: A, up 7.8 percent, which has only one-third of its assets in energy, and the $6 million Fidelity Advisers Global Natural Resources, up 7.0 percent, whose biggest holdings are aluminum stocks.
Here is what some of the natural resource fund managers are doing these days.
Mr. Shadeck says that he is cutting back on Exxon, Mobil, Royal Dutch and other large international oil companies in favor of energy companies whose operations are largely based in the United States. That is because the United States, which led the world into recession, will also lead coming out, he said. His fund's three biggest holdings are Phillips Petroleum and Unocal, domestic integrated oil companies, and the Coastal Corporation, a Houston-based gas pipeline company.
At Prudential Global, the leader of the group, the biggest sectors now are energy (35 percent), gold (23 percent) and agricultural chemicals (13 percent). Leigh R. Goehring, the fund's manager, has built the gold position by moving out of forest products. His biggest holding is Freeport McMoran Copper and Gold Inc. Another favorite: the Potash Corporation of Saskatchewan, which is profiting from supply disruptions in Germany and Russia.
Ernst von Metzsch, who manages the $160 million Vanguard Specialized Portfolios: Energy, which is up 0.6 percent, sees "a pretty tight oil market by the fourth quarter." But that easily could change, he cautions, "if the economy stalls or we have another mild winter, especially if people build large inventories, as happened late last year." For now, the fund's largest holdings are Amerada Hess, Unocal, Kerr-McGee and Norsk Hydro, a Norwegian oil and gas producer.
The $4 million Eaton Vance Natural Resources Trust, which is up 5.8 percent, is a quirky little fund. About a third of its assets are invested in gold and about a quarter in forest products, with only 20 percent in energy-related stocks. That kind of mix, a hallmark of the fund's five-year existence, is the main reason for the fund's relatively good performance over time, said its manager, Thomas E. Faust.
Shifting among industry sectors is the mark of Malcolm W. MacNaught, who manages the Fidelity natural resources fund. The fund held up in 1991 due to its investments in copper, aluminum and paper; now Mr. MacNaught is moving back into energy and natural gas stocks. Still, his largest holdings are aluminum companies: Alcoa and Reynolds. After that come two natural gas companies: Louisiana Land Exploration and Apache.
One of the half's worst performers was the $20 million Financial Strategic Energy Fund, down 16.7 percent. Philip J. Dubuque, the fund's manager, is bearish on energy over the long run. Sooner or later, he said, Iraqi oil will no longer be embargoed and Kuwait will be back up to full production. "Oil prices will have a tough time doing well in the long term," Mr. Dubuque concludes.

SECTION: Section 4; Page 16; Column 4; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 369 words

HEADLINE: Low-Skill Immigrants Hurt Job Market for Low-Skill Natives

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Obfuscation seems to have become a hallmark of the debate on the economic impact of immigrants. "Immigrants Don't Take Jobs of the Poor" (letter, June 14) by Thomas J. Catapano, in response to Edward N. Luttwak's May 15 Op-Ed article, adds to the confusion. 
 There are two distinct but interrelated issues. One is the impact of immigration on the overall economy, and the other is the distribution of this impact on segments of the population. There is no doubt, as Assemblyman Catapano asserts, that the overall American economy has benefited from immigration. This is supported by sound economics research, as well as by the survey of "38 leading economists" he cites.
It is also true, however, that the United States has experienced a large increase in low-skilled immigration in the last two decades. This has arisen in part from the large illegal immigration, which has acquired permanent residence de jure or de facto. Also in part from a system for allocating most visas that ignores the applicant's skills.
Substantial research indicates that a large immigration of low-skilled workers depresses employment and wage opportunities for low-skilled workers, without regard to the race or ethnicity of the immigrant or native-born Americans. While immigration is not the cause of poverty in the United States, in recent years it has inadvertently made it more difficult for low-skilled Americans.
Mr. Catapano is selective in the evidence he cites. He is perhaps unaware of the research on the adverse effect of low-skilled immigration on low-skilled Americans. Nor does he indicate what the survey of economists would say about this issue.
I sympathize with Mr. Catapano's dilemma. He wishes to expand immigration opportunities for the poor living in other countries and he wishes to help Americans in economic hardship. Sometimes policy objectives come into conflict. To acknowledge the potential policy conflict can result in constructive public policies.
 
BARRY R. CHISWICK
Jerusalem, June 16, 1992
 
The writer, research professor and head of the department of economics at the University of Illinois at Chicago, is a visiting professor at the Hebrew University.
