HEADLINE: JUNE 21-27: 20-Day Deadline;
Congress Orders Binding Arbitration To End Railroad Strike

BYLINE: By JOHN H. CUSHMAN JR.

 BODY:
The railroad strike came and went last week like a summer thunderstorm, causing a spell of anxiety as it loomed ominously on the horizon, arrived uproariously, and then vanished before most people had time to comprehend the complex forces at work.
Congress, as usual in major rail disputes, intervened before the strike could have serious economic effects. This time, though, Congress introduced a new form of binding arbitration, hoping to appease labor and business alike. 
 The brief crisis involved three sets of negotiations: the International Association of Machinists, bargaining with most freight lines; the Brotherhood of Maintenance of Way Employees, seeking a new contract with Conrail; and several unionsconfronting Amtrak, the passenger railroad. Talks had reached an impasse months ago, and Presidential emergency boards had recommended compromises that the unions rejected. Finally, the mandatory no-strike period set by law expired at 12:01 A.M. Wednesday.
Had all the unions struck, passenger and freight lines would have been sidetracked, and commuter trains would have been unable to operate. But only the machinists struck, and they picketed only one freight line, CSX. The railroads, in a display of solidarity that the American labor movement could only envy, responded by shutting down virtually the whole freight system from coast to coast. Eyeing the already wobbly economy, Congress reluctantly imposed a truce.
Now the adversaries will negotiate for another 20 days, after which each side will present its best offer to neutral arbitrators. Congress dictated that the arbitrators may not split the difference, but must choose one offer or the other. This approach should encourage each side to moderate its demands in an attempt to win the arbitrators' approval. Conceivably, the two sides will come close enough to strike a deal before the choice is made for them.

HEADLINE: Israel's Vote Shows It Has Tired of Ideology

BYLINE: By CLYDE HABERMAN

DATELINE: JERUSALEM

 BODY:
FORGET for a moment about which parties landed on top and which on the bottom in Israel's national election last week. The real winner was pragmatism and the big loser uncompromising ideology.
In the search for reasons why Israelis said yes to a Labor-led Government and tossed long-governing Likud unceremoniously out on its ear, it is tempting to say that they voted, as if in a referendum, for an accelerated Middle East peace process, for territorial concessions to the Arabs and for a drastic reduction in settlement-building on occupied lands. Judging from the hosannas pouring in for Labor's Yitzhak Rabin, that seems to be a common view overseas. And in some respects it is correct, for if Mr. Rabin keeps his word he will push for a speedy peace agreement, give up some of the territories and put the brakes on new settlements there. 
 "It's a real victory for the peace camp," said Ephraim Sneh, a former general and a Rabin adviser on Labor's dovish side. "I think I can say with reasonable confidence that it was a vote for a substantial peace process, although not exclusively so, because people were fed up with Likud."
That final qualifier is important. It helps explain the complex combination of events behind the upheaval that ended 15 years of Likud governance, threatening that party's stability and dashing the conventional wisdom that Israel's political drift is inexorably rightward.
While the peace camp may have won, a true debate on peace never really got going in the campaign. And while the hard-core right wing clearly lost ground, the major gains were in the center and not on the left. In the end, Likud wore out its welcome, through an incremental erosion of public faith that it possesses the vision, cohesion, integrity and competence to keep the country running.
Likud's leaders say they are just as interested in peace as the next fellow. They point out that it was under Likud Governments that Israel signed its peace treaty with Egypt, gave up the Sinai Desert, and moved last fall to the peace table with other Arab states and the Palestinians.
But a majority of Israelis are apparently convinced that with Likud, as with Moses, Sinai is as far as they will get. Enough of them now believe they will have no peace with Prime Minister Yitzhak Shamir's unyielding insistence on retaining every square inch of occupied territory.
As for Mr. Shamir's ideological commitment to settlement-building in the West Bank and Gaza Strip, many are also persuaded that it translates into less money for their own schools and hospitals, and into unhelpful diplomatic fights such as the one that led the United States to withhold requested loan guarantees on $10 billion for resettling Russian emigres. Many Israelis have grown tired of it, not because they theologically reject either settlements or territories but because of a pragamatic conclusion that unbending ideology just doesn't work.
The point was acknowledged by Defense Minister Moshe Arens, who announced unexpectedly in the wake of Likud's defeat that he would retire from political life, a decision likely also to be made soon by Mr. Shamir.
"A part of the public," Mr. Arens said, "does not see the slogan 'Greater Land of Israel' as an adequate or sufficient response in grappling with the complexity of problems associated with Palestinians in the territories."
 
Too Many Fears
 Indeed not, many Israelis say. For them, ideology has brought years of rocks and gasoline bombs in the Palestinian uprising; endless fears that the Arab street sweeper in Tel Aviv is not the hard-working man he probably is but rather a terrorist ready to pull out a knife; haunting nightmares that this will be the week that one's son in the army does not come back from the West Bank. These weary people say that if some land must be given up for peace, fine. If it means stopping a new settlement in Gaza to build a drug rehabilitation clinic in Ashdod, also fine. And if that doesn't work, they are ready, they say, to keep on looking until they find whatever does.
These people found the electoral jump to Labor easy, especially since Likud had helped push them in that direction anyway. The governing party seemed intent on committing political suicide with its internal squabbles, its government scandals, its failure to breathe fresh life into the Israeli economy and, in Mr. Shamir, a leader who did not inspire.
Few Israelis were more unhappy than recent emigres from the former Soviet Union, who punished Likud for their high unemployment by voting for Labor in sufficient numbers to give it three or four extra parliamentary seats. Sephardic Jews in so-called development towns, a group that tended to view Likud not as a mere political organism but as a way of life, still kept the faith, but in dwindling numbers, according to opinion polls.
Likud suffered most in the election, but it was hardly alone. Tehiya, a small party of far-rightists, was wiped out. Moledet, which calls for transferring Arabs out of the occupied territories, had been expected to increase its parliamentary strength dramatically but instead picked up only one extra seat. Only Tsomet shone on the right, soaring from 2 seats in the old Parliament to 8 in the new. But its triumph was due only in part to its no-compromise territorial policies. Perhaps more important was its image as a clean-government advocate.
The left did not do all that well either. Hadash, an Arab-dominated Communist party, won 4 seats in 1988 but only 3 now. Meretz, a coalition of three small left-wing parties, garnered 12 seats, 2 more than in 1988 but not a huge leap. And Labor, while finishing in first place, did so by draping a veil over its many doves and highlighting its relatively hawkish leader, Mr. Rabin, a take-no-guff former general who had shown as Defense Minister that he knows a thing or two about breaking the bones of rock-throwing Palestinians.
It was, in the main, a centrist vote that did not significantly alter the prevailing left-right balance. "The Israeli voter showed prosaic cleverness," wrote Meron Benvenisti, the demographer and former deputy mayor of Jerusalem, in the newspaper Haaretz. "He turned his back on the groups that tried to mix myths with the political decision-making process."
 
Lessons for Rabin
 There are important lessons here for Mr. Rabin as he begins the process of building a working coalition, one that he wants to make as broad as possible, with himself right in the political center.
What his Government will be like is not quite clear, for he has talked only vaguely about what he plans for the economy, and about how close he would come to a total freeze on settlements, and about how his proposals for Palestinian self-rule would differ (if at all) from Likud's. Mostly, Israeli voters are accepting on faith for now that he will serve them well, even though they also know that his first term as Prime Minister in the 1970's was far from brilliant and that they had finally deserted Labor in 1977 because of its long, overbearing and even corrupt rule.
Mr. Rabin must sense that, in the present pragmatic, throw-the-rascals-out mood, he may have to produce quick results on peace, security and the economy. "These elections make it clear that the Israeli public doesn't want a government whose main talent is not to act," said Nahum Barnea, a political writer for the newspaper Yediot Ahronot. "Whoever gets caught up in passivity will be punished, and very quickly."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Yitzhak Rabin, left, leader of the Labor Party, celebrating last week after his party's victory over the ruling Likud Party in national elections. (Benami/Media/Sipa)

HEADLINE: THE WORLD;
Libyan Doubts About Qaddafi Are Growing

BYLINE: By CHRIS HEDGES

DATELINE: TRIPOLI, Libya

 BODY:
THE United Nations sanctions that went into effect against Libya last April are turning out to be more than a symbolic gesture. While they have not yet accomplished their purpose of compelling Libya to turn over two suspects wanted in the 1988 bombing of a Pan Am jumbo jet over Lockerbie, Scotland, they have been politically damaging to the mercurial Libyan leader, Col. Muammar el-Qaddafi.
The Libyan military is struggling with serious shortages. Many Libyans openly call for Colonel Qaddafi's removal and the official information media now admit that the Arab solidarity that was the cornerstone of Colonel Qaddafi's foreign policy was "a mirage." 
 The sanctions have succeeded in banning flights in and out of Libya and in prohibiting the sale of military equipment. They have also brought a reduction in the diplomatic staff Libya maintains abroad. Western diplomats say the departure of 1,700 Russian advisers and technicians has devastated the military's infrastructure, rendering the air defense system ineffective while much of Libya's military hardware rests idle.
One result is that the littered streets and back alleys in Tripoli, where young men once shied away from foreigners because they feared the pervasive security apparatus, are seething with open resentment.
If Colonel Qaddafi were to turn the suspects over, a subsequent lifting of the embargo might permit him to halt the deterioration of his popular support. But Arab and Western diplomats say the extradition of the two men is unacceptable to his security apparatus -- the organization that has held him in power for 23 years.
These diplomats also believe that if Libya was involved in an operation of the magnitude of the Lockerbie bombing, it could not have been carried out without Colonel Qaddafi's approval. "Colonel Qaddafi has no desire to see two of his intelligence agents describe the inner workings of his regime to the West and directly tie him to state terrorism," one Arab ambassador said.
The Libyan leader appears to be hoping to bargain his way out of his predicament; he has been trying to meet the sanctions requirements half-way by giving the West some satisfaction in hopes it will drop its demand for the two men. "The Libyans know little about how the outside world works," a senior diplomat said, "and so they are vainly trying to work out a compromise."
The United Nations, in addition to the extradition of the two suspects, has called on Libya to end support for international terrorism and assist in the investigation into the bombing of a French airliner over Africa in 1989. The two bombings killed 441 people. In response, Libyan officials have turned over information about the Irish Republican Army, for which they provided training and funds, to British officials. They have expelled the Palestinian terrorists Abu Nidal and Abul Abbas, and have closed several Palestinian training camps.
The Libyans are hoping that these actions will at least stave off the imposition of stiffer sanctions when the United Nations reviews the measures in August.
Diplomats say this tactic may work; a senior Egyptian official who travels frequently to Libya said that if Colonel Qaddafi can avoid further sanctions he will probably retain power. The Egyptians believe that despite the erosion of Colonel Qaddafi's grip on the country he does not yet have any serious rivals.
When Libyan officials are questioned about the extradition demand, they appear to be stalling for time. In a letter sent last month to the United Nations Secretary General, Boutros Boutros-Ghali, Foreign Minister Ibrahim M. al-Bishari promised that the Libyan parliament would "take an appropriate stand regarding the matter as soon as possible." But the 631-member body, which met for 10 days that ended last Tuesday, skirted the issue for most of the meeting. And at the conclusion, it reiterated the standard Libyan demand that the two suspects be turned over to the Arab League or the United Nations, rather than the United States or Britain. Similar offers were rejected before the sanctions went into place.
 
A Catalyst for Anger
 Within Libya, the sanctions have become a catalyst for popular outrage. After two decades in which efforts to follow bizarre economic and political theories have left many Libyans without basic services such as water or garbage collection, even some Libyan officials admit that they are in trouble.
The problems are evident in one of Colonel Qaddafi's most lavish schemes, a $25 billion effort called "the Great Man Made River Project." After spending $6 billion to channel water from aquifers to reservoirs built for the project, the Libyans have discovered that the desert heat is evaporating the stored water. Many Libyans, watching as planners scramble to build roofs over the reservoirs, have begun calling it "the Great Mad Man River Project."
Such feelings do not sit well with the older bureaucrats who dominated the recent session of the parliament. Most spent much of the nationally televised debate attacking the younger generation for advocating change. But younger delegates, while making sure never to attack Colonel Qaddafi by name, complained of shortages in everything from school desks to electricity.
While the sanctions have eroded Colonel Qaddafi's hold on power, his decision to hold onto the suspects while trying to give the West enough to keep the United Nations from imposing tougher measures might just work. "He has been weakened," said an Arab ambassador, "but if he can maintain the status quo, he might survive."

HEADLINE: THE WORLD;
Cuba Seeks Pain Relief: Shot of Western Capital

BYLINE: By HOWARD W. FRENCH

DATELINE: HAVANA

 BODY:
RECENT months have been filled with sour news for Fidel Castro. First came the sudden dissolution of the Soviet Union and the disappearance of the billions in aid it had furnished. Then came a near disastrous harvest of sugar, Cuba's main product.
Finally, even as supplies of oil and consumer goods from flour to bath soap began drying up, the leadership watched in scarcely disguised horror as an exile-led campaign to tighten the noose of a 31-year United States embargo seemed to gain serious momentum in Congress. 
 In the past, Havana responded to almost every adversity with a single reflex: blaming the United States with torrents of overcharged rhetoric. But this time the leadership has begun showing the kind of flexibility that may yet shorten the prohibitive odds against its long-term survival.
Since Havana's special relationship with the Soviet Union began to seriously wane two years ago, Cuban officials have made a high priority of building new economic and political ties with Western Europe and Latin America. Under this program, much of the economy, which was once entirely state controlled, has been rapidly opened up to private investment from abroad in the form of joint ventures, some with majority foreign ownership.
Under pressure from these new partners and growing disgruntlement at home, Cuba has also begun introducing political reforms such as multi-candidate elections to the National Assembly. Some experts see such changes as the beginnings of an effort at gradual reform in order to avoid an accumulation of explosive pressures for change.
Already, with the loss of trade ties with the former East Bloc, which represented more than 80 percent of Cuba's external commerce, the island is undergoing as jolting a transition as it had during the nationalizations of the early 1960's, when Washington, its overwhelmingly dominant foreign partner, imposed an embargo.
 
Courting Americans
 Recently, even as conservative Cuban-American groups in the United States were urging United States officials to do more to cut off the island from the outside world, Havana began a sophisticated campaign to court American businessmen in the hope that their thirst for opportunities would bring them to lobby in Washington for a change in relations.
This month, in the most spectacular recent effort, 100 American business executives, many from large companies like Phillip Morris, Squibb, Kodak and Boeing, were flown to Havana to assess the opportunities. Although few of them wished to be quoted, many said they were strongly impressed by the possibilities they saw.
Such overtures appear to be a decisive acknowledgement that only access to American capital and markets can fill the enormous gap left by the disappearance of the Soviet economic presence here; new ties with Europe and Latin America may help slow the country's decline, but they cannot alone replace what has been lost. Some students of Cuban affairs say, in fact, that such a realization gives the United States a singular opportunity to encourage change here -- as long as the present policy, built around an inflexible embargo, is changed.
 
The Key Actor
 "As has almost always been the case, the key international actor for Cuba has been the U.S.," said Richard R. Fagen, a professor of political science at Stanford University. "Now, with the Soviet Union gone, we are in a clear position of first place, not necessarily in directing Cuba, but at least strongly influencing it."
Cuban officials at the highest levels have themselves begun appealing to this sort of thinking; they are hoping (as they did in previous United States election years) that a new Administration in Washington, whether led by a newcomer or a second-term incumbent, will re-examine relations that have long been frozen in amber.
Cuban officials speculate, for example, that President Bush would be more free in a second term to set aside his past hostility, since he would not be hamstrung by electoral considerations. They remember Richard Nixon's opening to China and the way it cast the Republicans as the party whose hard-line anti-Communist credentials gave it a freer hand to offer detente.
On the other hand, the officials also hope that if Bill Clinton or Ross Perot is elected, policy toward Cuba would be a natural candidate for review.
Hoping to set the stage, articulate Cuban officials who now appear at conferences around the United States argue the case for new relations and express little acrimony.
In a recent interview here, President Castro himself set forth the argument.
The United States, Mr. Castro said, "has two paths: either the path of hostility, aggression, or the path of peace and influence on Cuba's political life through other means." Dialogue, he said, would facilitate "another political direction for our nation."
Although language like this is a departure for Mr. Castro, skeptics say it is not enough for for the United States to take seriously, since the Cuban leader still clings to a lot of his cold war rhetoric toward the United States and a hostility to dissent at home.
"From the Cuban point of view, they recognize that they have to live in a capitalist world, and they are trying to adapt by integrating into the world economy and subtly appealing to the United States," said William LeoGrande, a professor of government at American University in Washington. "But these kinds of gestures by themselves are not going to be enough to change U.S. policy.
"There are some issues, not the ones of the past, but serious issues that remain, like human rights, and there would have to be serious improvements before any Administration takes the risk at home of improving relations."

 Advocates of economic change appear to be gaining ground in China, with officials stepping up plans to privatize housing, decontrol prices and give foreign companies a role in the service industry. 1

HEADLINE: Sixth-Grade Graduation Ceremony: Moment for Pride on Treacherous Path

BYLINE: By FELICIA R. LEE

 BODY:
Lakeshea Hill, a graduating sixth grader, gave her teacher the best farewell gift she could afford. It was the message she scribbled across the blackboard of Room 412 last week. It said in part: "I know we gave you a hard time but I'm going to miss you. You taught me to believe in myself."
Believing in yourself is a victory at Public School 158, a five-story red-brick building in East New York, Brooklyn, surrounded by vacant lots, boarded-up buildings and dusty bodegas -- a school full of dreams in a neighborhood of grim statistics. 
 Born to parents from Alabama, Georgia, the Dominican Republic and Puerto Rico, the 19 black and Hispanic children in Sylvia Ruth's class know they want better lives than their parents had. Their ambitions are lofty: a doctor, a lawyer, an astronaut. How those goals are achieved is more vaguely answered. Few have real-life role models doing what they aspire to do. And for some those goals may be sidetracked by teen-age pregnancy or violent death.
Listen to Lakeshea, age 12, who came here with her family from Jamaica: "This is not a wonderful place. You can't let your child stay out late because there are crackheads. Other kids influence you to do things, like throw rocks at buses or stay out of school.
"Nothing bad has happened to me because I'm not afraid of anybody, but people get shot and hurt in other ways inside their hearts. They are sad because their friends die or people beat you up all the time. You might get shot."
In many ways, the struggles of Lakeshea and the other students who graduated Wednesday are shared all across the nation in neighborhoods like East New York. At the ages of 11 and 12 they are deciding between doing what society expects or succumbing to the temptations that beckon on every corner.
They are the most ordinary of children forced to run a gantlet of extraordinary obstacles. At a tender and awkward time when their suburban peers are fretting about pimples and puppy love, these children are dodging bullets and learning what corners are free from drug pushers.
For Lakeshea and the other students who graduated last week, the choices are complex and painful, like staying in school or dropping out, selling drugs or selling fast food, carrying a gun for protection or staying in the house. They ask themselves: Can I make it? How do I make it? Who can show me the way out? Is it safe to speak standard English, avoid fights, and not spend a lot of money on clothes and risk the disdain of peers?
Listen to Pedro Alicea, a 13-year-old known as "PP." He hangs out with a group called the Junior Links Mob for protection. His goal is to be a football player and earn a college degree, but he admits that his strongest allegiance right now is to his friends.
Pedro says he knows kids who sell drugs because it is the only way they know to make money. When you are hungry or desperate to be popular, he said, getting a regular job or waiting to finish school to make money just takes too long.
"If you don't wear name-brand sneakers they dis you," Pedro says. "You'll feel embarrassed. You'll feel even more embarrassed if there are girls there."
"They make a lot of money, $700 a week," Pedro said of children in the drug trade. "They say stay in school, get a job. Then you hear about the recession and no jobs. How do you even know you will live long enough to graduate? People do die."
Pedro is a tall, handsome boy who likes dying his dark hair various colors. His mother is a restaurant cook and his father is a disabled diabetic. When Pedro had the option of being a drug lookout he did not do it, he said, because, "My moms says it's committing a sin." He hopes he will continue to hold out when the next illegal opportunity rolls around.
 
Street Values Clash With Pupils' Dreams
 The teachers, the parents and even the children themselves concede that there will be many casualties in the battle between street values and old-fashioned success.
"I don't want to be around these neighborhoods when I grow up," said 11-year-old Kelly Samuels. Saying she has only "associates, not friends," at P.S. 158, she predicted that half the boys will end up in jail and half the girls will be teen-age mothers.
"I don't want to be like these girls who are 15 and 16 having babies. I don't want to be like that, not old enough to support my child. I want to be a lawyer before I get into all that.
"My mother has a good life but I don't want my life to be like hers," Kelly said. Her mother works in a school cafeteria. "She's got three kids. I want one. I want my own house. I want a better job.
"I never see my father, but my mother has a boyfriend. When I get married, I want my child to have a father."
Sharon Silcott came with her family to New York in 1989 from the Bahamas.
"I feel funny in East New York because of all the shooting," she said. "It's like no one cares, no one likes anybody. In class, I'm afraid the roof is going to fall on my head. When I grow up I want to leave New York and go someplace nice, someplace quiet."
 
A Big Difference: Believing in Success
 In conversations with some of the 100 P.S. 158 sixth graders, Mrs. Ruth's class in particular, it becomes clear that the children who appear to be on the road to success are those who believe they can be successful, have mentors and role models, understand what practical steps they must take to make it, and have mastered basic reading and math skills.
"I have always wanted to be an astronaut," said Raul Ortiz, a 12-year-old who will enter a special program for gifted children at I.S. 166 this September. "I read a lot, and my grandmother and my uncle and my mother always told me I could do it, I can do it. I just have to do well in school and stay away from bad people, from trouble."
Raul, who gave the class welcoming address to an auditorium of shining faces at last Wednesday's graduation, was born to a 14-year-old mother. But he was an only child and his mother went back to school to get a college degree. When Raul was sent to live with his grandmother in a neighborhood where junkies and men with guns ruled the streets, he stayed in the house to read.
" 'You have to have your eye on the future,' my uncle told me," Raul said. "People might make fun of me, but I'm going to be an astronaut."
For many of the children, the equation does not add up in their favor. They may have loving parents but fear violence to the point they carry a gun for protection and end up in trouble. Girls who are smart in school may end up pregnant and dropping out because of the strong pressure for sexual activity. Children lacking the money to dress well are teased by their classmates and can end up turning to crime because it is an easy way to make money fast.
Complicating every choice is East New York's isolation. The students rarely spend time with black and Hispanic professionals, and so emulate the speech, mannerisms and dress of people who have dropped out of mainstream society. They only have vague notions of what role racism will play in their lives because they have had few encounters with whites.
"I don't know, I think prejudice was a long time ago," Frederick Melendez, a 12-year-old said. "My parents told me there will be some people out there who won't like you -- not you, but your color, the way you speak. Now, Rodney King, that was racist."
 
High Aspirations Built on TV Images
 Many of the children are below grade level in math and reading, and have at various times seen school as a place that makes them feel bad about themselves. Most of Mrs. Ruth's students could not even name a college they had heard about. One student said she wanted to be a lawyer. Asked what she might do to accomplish that goal, she said, "definitely get a G.E.D." The general equivalency diploma is the equivalent of a high school diploma and is awarded to people who pass a test.
Listen to Arjene McCrae, a small 12-year-old boy with a lively sense of humor who wants to be a football player:
"Most of the people who are rich people, I would say it is because they are born that way. Many white people are rich even though they don't work that hard. They want black people to do all the work. If you be selling drugs, the cops don't do nothing and you can get rich. I'm not saying that it's right, for me it's not right, but it's the way for black people to make money. The main way, I would say. Maybe sports, too."
Listen to Yaslynn Perez, a 12-year-old who lives with an extended family that includes her mother, her grandmother, her sister and four cousins. Yaslynn, a pretty girl with long dark hair who likes to dress in black, decided to be a lawyer because she enjoys watching "Divorce Court" and "People's Court" on television. Her Aunt Alba is also a lawyer's assistant.
Yaslynn, whose mother is a shoe clerk and whose grandmother is on welfare, said she is most inspired by the characters on "Beverly Hills 90210," a television show about a group of upper-middle-class white teen-agers in Beverly Hills.
"I used to stay home and never go out," Yaslynn said. "My friends tried to make me go out. They tried to make me do bad stuff. People told me that I was too quiet, that I didn't fight enough.
"Nobody in my family has a high school diploma except my uncle. They're really looking up to me to go to college. I'm a Catholic and I go to church. My family wants me to be a good girl and that's what I'm trying to do."
 
Children Learning Ways of Violence
 Enough, however, has already happened in the lives of these children to let them know that things are not easy and life is not fair, even for good girls and boys.
Room 412 was so cold this winter that the children had to gather around a radiator in the hallway to keep warm, an experience they bitterly recall. And Mrs. Ruth, who calls the children her "babies," invites them to her church, and brings them books to read, has lost her job because of budget cuts.
Mostly, though, it is the violence around them that engenders a sense of hopelessness. The boys, especially, fear being shot or killed. Mrs. Ruth said almost all of the students have had a personal brush with violence; some with death.
Yaslynn's favorite cousin, Pablo, was fatally shot over the Memorial Day weekend. When she began crying in the middle of a reading lesson and her classmates asked what was wrong, Mrs. Ruth told them. Their only reaction was to ask matter-of-factly "was it a boy or girl?"
Eleven-year-old Cory Brown, his 9-year-old sister, Ebony, and his mother, Lucille, were standing at an ice-cream truck one Sunday afternoon when a bullet ripped into Mrs. Brown's left thigh.
Pedro fears the police because of a couple of incidents in which they roughed him up and even pointed a gun at his head. He says he was simply in the wrong place at the wrong time.
Maurice McClary, who at age 12 is about 6 feet tall and calls himself "a boy in a man's body" hears the gunfire outside the project where he lives and tries to imagine what it would be like to die. His uncle and several of his older friends have been shot.
Listen to Maurice, a soft-spoken, aspiring rap artist:
"I grew up rhyming. I write my own songs. Positive things. I don't use the word nigger and all that stuff. I write about a black man and everything that's going on in the world. There is too much violence and too much drugs.
"I'm moving out. I want to go someplace quiet. There's a lot of violence around my way. I be daydreaming that one day they'll get me, so I don't like to go outside. You hear pop, pop, pop. Popping. People be running, scattering. They're always shooting at somebody. I hope they don't get me one day. I'll be dead. I'm only 12."
 
Crumbling Walls And Vacant Lots
 And so on Wednesday, the children of Room 412 along with the other graduates -- the girls wearing white carnations, the boys red ones -- attended their commencement. They posed for pictures; they exchanged autographs. They looked forward to the long, lazy summer dividing childhood from puberty.
Principal Anita D. Harrison, a tiny dynamo of a woman who brought a host of special services and a new spirit into the school when her tenure began five years ago, wept openly as the children received their diplomas.
"Some of these kids, this is the only graduation they will have," she said. "Some of these kids, by some quirk of fate, might be killed. Some girls might have babies. They're good kids and the opportunities aren't there for them. The experiences aren't there for them."
Mrs. Ruth, who spent Wednesday clearing out her desk, was equally pensive. She said she hoped she had instilled in the children some sense of self-esteem, some emotional armor.
"No one in government comes to ask what I need," she said. "These children have so many problems. They need something more than vacant lots and crumbling walls."
As always, the children had the last word on her performance.
"Mrs. Ruth," said Lakeshea. "Can I go home with you?"

GRAPHIC: Photo: Sylvia Ruth, a sixth-grade teacher at Public School 158, giving a graduation-day hug Wednesday to Arjene McRae. Mrs. Ruth has tried to give her students some sense of self-esteem and some emotional armor to go along with their knowledge of adding mixed fractions and writing essays. (Nick Malter for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Mitterrand Leaves for Sarajevo, Hoping to Shock His Serbian Ally

BYLINE: By ROGER COHEN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PARIS, June 27

 BODY:
By embarking unexpectedly for Sarajevo tonight, President Francois Mitterrand appeared to be seeking to show that the patience of even Serbia's closest ally has run out and that a European leader does not need Washington's endorsement for a bold diplomatic gamble.
 Although it did not seem clear that the French leader's visit would significantly advance the quest for peace, the domestic effect might be considerable for a President facing an important and potentially close-fought autumn referendum on European integration. 
 Throughout the Yugoslav conflict, France, an ally of the Serbs since their shared opposition to the Austro-Hungarian Empire during World War I, has sought to head off drastic measures against Belgrade and distance itself from the German-led support of Croatia. Paris has done so in the belief that it could use its special relationship with Serbia to bring President Slobodan Milosevic to reason.
The French Government has also been increasingly critical of what officials close to Mr. Mitterrand have portrayed as recent attempts by Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d to take charge of the response to a conflict in which Washington long showed no real interest.
 
Reverses Course
 At the European Community meeting in Lisbon that ended today, Mr. Mitterrand, who had been alone among European leaders in refraining from explicitly blaming the Serbs for the war in Bosnia and Herzegovina, changed his stance.
He called for a tightening of United Nations sanctions against Mr. Milosevic's Government and declared that "Serbia is today the aggressor, even if the origins of the conflict go back a long way."
By a visit to a besieged Sarajevo, officials close to the French leader suggested tonight, he could send a powerful message to Serbia that even Belgrade's oldest friend in Europe could no longer tolerate the war.
"While the Germans rushed to recognize Croatia, the French alone have maintained a strong commitment to Serbia," said Nicholas Wahl, a professor of French political history at New York University. "So a visit to Sarajevo by President Mitterrand sends a new message to Belgrade."
The President may also be seeking a public relations coup. His Health Minister, Bernard Kouchner, visited Sarajevo recently, and it seemed likely that he had laid the groundwork for Mr. Mitterrand. Mr. Kouchner arrived in a plane full of food and medical supplies.
 
Inertia and Dithering
 Mr. Mitterrand has been the target of widespread criticism of European Community inertia in the face of the Yugoslav conflict, and he can use the sort of boost that a successful Sarajevo visit might bring him.
Support is particularly important to him because he faces a referendum, probably in September, on the Maastricht treaty on European union. Defeat in this vote could turn into a referendum on his 11-year rule and would seriously endanger the drive for European political, military and economic unity.
According to Mr. Mitterrand's spokesman, Jean Musitelli, the President feels it is essential to rapidly resolve the Yugoslav conflict because its persistence could strengthen an image of European dithering and hold back approval of the Maastricht treaty.
France sees European unity as an essential balance to American power at a time when it argues that Washington's influence in a Europe without the cold war is bound to recede.

HEADLINE: G.O.P. Vows Budget Fight With Florio

BYLINE: By WAYNE KING, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TRENTON, June 27

 BODY:
New Jersey's Republican leaders vowed today to override Gov. Jim Florio's veto of their $14.6 million budget and use their subpoena power to insure that their budget is carried out.
"The Legislature will oversee the implementation of this budget," said Assembly Speaker Garabed (Chuck) Haytaian, a Republican whose party controls two-thirds majorities in both houses. "Commissioners will be called before committees with subpoena power to explain how they are expending tax dollars." 
 Shortly before midnight Friday, Mr. Florio became the first Governor in state history to unconditionally veto a budget, maintaining that "it is wrong to hurt people for a penny," an allusion to the Legislature's vote -- over his veto -- to repeal the 1-cent sales-tax increase he and the Democratic Legislature imposed in 1990.
The veto sets up a showdown on Monday when the Republican leaders will set up a vote to override.
Today, Mr. Haytaian and Senate President Donald T. DiFrancesco accused the Governor and "his minions and hatchet men" of plotting to "see to it that pain and suffering is inflicted on the people of New Jersey because the Republicans had the audacity to cut state spending and lower taxes."
 
'Watch Them Carefully'
 Suggesting that the administration would use the Republican budget as an excuse to make cuts in programs and services that would inflict maximum discomfort on residents, Mr. DiFrancesco said: "Today, I issue a warning to the people of New Jersey: Watch this administration. Watch them carefully."
In his veto message to the Legislature Friday night, issued only hours after the Republicans had rejected the Governor's plea to extend the sales-tax increase for a minimum of three months in order to continue vital services, Mr. Florio said he could not sign "a budget that would force state government to renege on its responsibilities and turn its back on our people."
He accused the Republicans of ramming through a Draconian spending plan, which, in cutting $1.1 billion from his own recommended $15.7 billion budget, would sharply cut or eliminate financing for such programs as tuberculosis treatment, kidney dialysis and treatment of AIDS patients with the drug AZT. Other administration officials have said the cutbacks would require 7,000 layoffs out of a work force of 67,000, reduce the quality of education and drive up tuition at state-supported colleges. They have said the cuts would cut back air and water pollution monitoring that would threaten the Jersey Shore, curb snow removal this winter and force early closing of state parks.
 
'The Mongers of Fear'
 Today Mr. Haytaian said: "If you believe the mongers of fear, scores of workers will lose ther jobs, children will go hungry, the aged will suffer, crime will run rampant, beaches and state parks will close, prisoners will roam free and public transportation will come to a grinding halt. All of this chaos and turmoil from a 6.8 percent decrease in state spending."
Mr. Florio and administration officials, howewer, say that the cuts proposed by the Republicans come after the state Government has already been reduced by 4,000 jobs, and state revenues have been flattened by the national recession, which hit New Jersey particularly hard.
Mr. Florio, at a news conference this morning, also criticized what the Republicans called safeguards they wrote into the budget mandating certain job cuts and forbidding such things as park closings. The Governor said the restrictions were both impossible and an unconstitutional intrusion on the constutionally mandated authority of the administrative branch of government.
"They can require that the sun not shine, but that does not mean it will happen," the Governor said. He said, for example, that a requirement that 200 positions be cut in the Department of Education was impossible because only 126 people worked in the category slated for reduction, "to say nothing of the question of separation of powers" between the legislative and executive branch.
 
Aiming at Public Advocate
 But Mr. Haytaian and Mr. DiFrancesco insisted that the Legislature had the power to enforce its budget in detail. "This is a law," Mr. Haytaian said. "The budget is a law, and if the Governor tells us he is going to break the law, we will go to court."
The Republican leaders also insisted they had the authority to force department heads to follow their dictates and not make cuts in areas they have mandated are off limits.
The Public Advocate, Wilfredo Caraballo, for example, has said that reductions in his department will mean that some Public Defender postions will have to be eliminated, and thus shift the burden of providing constitutionally mandated lawyers for indigents to the counties.
Vowing to subpoena commissioners or other department heads to force obedience, Mr. Haytaian said, "The first to be called will be Public Advocate Wifredo Caraballo, who has already started the attack against the people of New Jersey."
Mr. Caraballo maintained that the reductions in his department -- including the total elimination of two sections, the Division of Public Interest Advocacy and Citizen Complaints -- were political retaliation. Several Republican lawmakers called for his resignation last year when he joined a suit that could force combining school districts to achieve racial integration.
 
Anger at Suits for Poor
 Those lawmakers have also objected to the broad powers the Public Advocate has, unique in the 50 states, to bring suit in the public interest, including suing the state itself, which it has often done in the past on behalf of the poor or others it deems to lack adequate legal representation.
Both Mr. Haytaian and Mr. DiFrancesco insisted they had enough votes to override the Governor's veto. The budget passed the Assembly with more than enough votes to override the veto. In the Senate, two Republicans did not vote, one was absent, and one, Leonard T. Connors Jr., voted against approving the budget.
Mr. Connors said he objected to cuts in the Homestead Rebate program that would hurt people in his district, and also to cuts in outlays for tourism promotion, pollution monitoring and Medevac helicopter service, all important in the shore area he represents.
Mr. Connors, who said after the vote that he had not made up his mind whether he would vote to override the veto, did not answer telephone calls to his office and home. But Mr. DiFrancesco said he had been personally assured by Mr. Connors and the three Republicans who did not vote on the budget Thursday that they would all support the motion to override on Monday, supplying the necessary 27 votes to make it successful.

HEADLINE: A Shimmer in the Kitchen

BYLINE: By CATHERINE S. MANEGOLD

 BODY:
Some time in the 1980's, the old stereotype of the brilliant chef as a florid, shouting and temperamental genius gave way to a new stereotype of the brilliant chef as a florid, shouting and temperamental genius who could attain fame on a par with, say, Luciano Pavarotti.
So it was that a tremor shook the faithful last week when Daniel Boulud, Le Cirque's executive chef since 1986, announced that he would leave that temple of gastronomy in September to open a Manhattan restaurant of his own. Within 48 hours the restaurant's owner, Sirio Maccioni, had calls from at least 10 renowned chefs. He put them off and left, on schedule, for a six-week vacation in Tuscany where his farm's kitchen is so big that it can comfortably seat 12 at a table near the stove. 
 Mr. Maccioni's equanimity stunned no one in the restaurant business. For like great theater, great kitchens are a collaborative effort led by the chef but sustained by a broad staff of talent from those unsung apprentices responsible for the cold appetizers to assistants like Le Cirque's patissier, Jacques Torres, a bit of a star in his own right.
More than 9,000 restaurants slake New York's varied appetites. All told, in 1991 they did $6 billion in business and kept 180,000 people employed, according to the New York State Restaurant Association. Yet out of that vast pool of talent, ambition and blind hope, only a handful of kitchens ever rise to star billing.
 
Gloss From the Dross
 The recession has trimmed away about 10 percent of New York's restaurants like so much unwanted fat. But the best of the city's kitchens have not suffered much despite prices that can easily soar to $300 for a dinner for two. Reservations for a Saturday night at Bouley, at 165 Duane Street, can still mean a four-month wait. And even then, the host may airily announce that the first opening is nearer to midnight than to dusk.
"We are very busy," said the chef and owner David Bouley (pronounced boo-LAY) by telephone, on vacation in Tucson. "It's amazing. People like Bernadette Peters come in and order nine courses. And their plates are wiped clean! Sometimes we have 15 to 20 tables of V.I.P.s."
Stars, of course, twinkle brighter in constellations than alone. "Years ago there was not this notoriety," said Marc Sarrazin, who has been described by New York chefs as a culinary power broker and is a member of the board at the Culinary Institute of America. "Now, it's glamorous. But most people don't understand that to be a great chef you have to be a great cook, a great gentleman, a great communicator and a great leader."
 
Shopping List for Glory
 In truth, for executive chefs, there is usually not much cooking. A complex mix of administration, art, sport and psychology, the life of New York's great chefs is more a matter of building and sustaining an institution than making the perfect pot-au-feu de canette salee aux racines de saison et huile d'echalote aux herbes (duck). Thus, a kitchen like Le Cirque's can live on in glory well beyond a chef's tenure.
At Le Cirque, at 58 East 65th Street, a staff of 26 prepares everything from the Scottish wild hare that might begin a dinner to the warm souffle with apricots and almond sauce that could end it. Mr. Boulud's contribution is to keep the pace and the performance consistent. His supervision -- from checking the aroma of a morrel airlifted in from Oregon to the towel swipe that obliterates an errant drop of consomme -- insures that the results are precisely as he would have them.
It is his stamp. And it is a 12- to 16-hour-a-day job.
"I have no life," Mr. Boulud (pronounced BOO-loo) lamented over a steaming cup of espresso away from the kitchen's lunch-time rush. "Everything is work."
At the same time, chefs share in the rewards that stardom confers. Salaries often exceed $100,000 a year. Mr. Boulud once rejected four times that amount because he feared the cynical manipulation of his name and reputation. Other offers, with varying inducements, have been made throughout his six years at Le Cirque. He stayed, because "after 23 years of being imposed upon by others" he knew that his next move would be solo.
Other great chefs have not been as cautious. Instead, they jump from kitchen to kitchen, staff to staff. "Everyone knows each other," said Karen Waltuck, who with her husband, David, runs the high-toned TriBeCa restaurant Chanterelle. "People will run over and say, 'Oh my God! We're out of foie gras!' and come to borrow some. As time goes on, you share employees. You hire people with talent and like children, people with talent eventually go off and leave you. But sometimes they come back."
That endless cycling through the better restaurants does not always work to a chef's advantage. "There is a lot of talent in New York," said Mr. Boulud. "But everybody should not try to be a vibrant star overnight. I've seen a lot of talented young people jump too often and drop as fast as they went up. Sometimes I feel that things are spinning out of control in New York."
 
Daylong Frenzy
 For those who near the city's culinary peak, the pressures are enormous. The day begins about 10:00 A.M. with the purchasing of everything from lettuce to rare truffles that can cost as much as $1,500 a pound. By mid-afternoon, a tormentingly hectic lunch culminates in "the hit," when every order seems to arrive at once. The break between meals is devoted to administration and perhaps a change of clothes. Dinner repeats the intensity, and the day finally ends in a sea of dirty dishes, near midnight.
Hour after hour, in kitchen temperatures that frequently exceed 100 degrees, the chef must coordinate a staff the size of a baseball team. "It's kind of like a pit crew," said Mr. Maccioni's 24-year-old son, Marco, who has been left in charge of Le Cirque for the summer. "Every guy has to do his share to build a car. And then, when the car is ready, you take it to the door and run it into the dining room."
Danny Meyer, owner of the popular and well-regarded Union Square Cafe, tries to keep the chaos to a minimum by easing the channels of communication. At a roundtable meeting held between meals last Wednesday, Michael Romano, the executive chef, turned to a waiter. "I know the kitchen for you all is a break," he said. "But sometimes, hearing chatter and singing, it drives you to the edge." The meeting ended like a locker-room session before a football game. Staffers joined hands above the table and yelled "Whooo!" before dispersing to their jobs.
A moment later, Mr. Romano was on the run. Taking the stairs two at a time, he burst from the cellar with a bunch of wild watercress. "This!" he said, pointing to its pin-sized flowers and crunching a leafy sprig between his teeth, "This is the sort of stuff that really turns a chef on. When the green market kicks in at this time of year, it's the most exciting moment." The restaurant, at 21 East 16th Street, is neighbor to a farmers' market in Union Square Park.
 
Home-Grown Talent
 Mr. Romano, an Italian-American who grew up in East Harlem, acquired his love of cooking from relatives and friends. "The women would get together on Sundays and make pasta," he said. "And our neighborhood had my uncle's butcher shop, a cheese shop, a fish shop. Even at school you could hear chickens getting slaughtered."
David Waltuck also developed his love of cooking in Manhattan. But his motivation was quite different. "My mother," he said somberly, "was not a good cook." He learned the art partly at the Culinary Institute, in Hyde Park, N.Y., where he studied for a time, and partly on his own.
The care and, of course, feeding of growing chefs is often at the core of their skills. Mr. Boulud grew up on a farm in Lyons, France, where the family made cheese, sausage and fresh bread and raised all the fowl, pork, beef and veal that they consumed. Mr. Bouley's French-born mother cooked for the table from the bounty of the fields of the family farm in Rhode Island.
Both men went on to work in some of the best kitchens in Europe. Mr. Boulud's career started at 13. "The first year I plucked birds," he said. "I learned how to chop parsley, how to peel asparagus and how to handle a knife. There was an old chef of 30 years there who was drunk every night but besides that could really cook like a master."
Before he was 20, Mr. Boulud had graduated to the kitchen of a three-star restaurant near Cannes, Le Moulin de Mougins. "The first year I had goose bumps," he said. Eventually, he moved to Washington to be the personal chef of the Frenchman in charge of the European Economic Community delegation. From there, he moved on to New York's Upper East Side and to Polo, in the Westbury Hotel, and Plaza Athenee.
 
Adding Breadth to Depth
 Mr. Bouley's path was less direct. He, too, cooked with such legends as Roger Verge at Le Moulin de Mougins. But he took time off now and again to study French civilization, painting, economics and other subjects at the Sorbonne.
That training have served him well. At Bouley, he has been the force behind everything from the foie gras to the flower arrangements and the furniture. "I had no idea what I was doing, but I was going crazy doing everything. I think I put down a plaster spatula one day and the next day picked up a kitchen spatula."
Other chefs are less wide-ranging but no less overworked. When Mr. Boulud goes home to his wife and young daughter, he taps away at a book about his cooking. Marta Pulini, the acclaimed chef at Le Madri, used to spend her one day off so tired that she could only sleep. Now, she has added to her responsibilities by teaching in Long Island.
All is not heat and sweat and long hours, though. Mr. Romano and his friends gather occasionally to eat and drink and laugh in a movable club they call "the Chefs from Hell." Ms. Pulini's friends gather the first Monday of each month as "les amis de cholesterol." The name hardly suits her cooking, though, which she keeps light.
"When I cook," she explained, "I don't want to kill my customers."

HEADLINE: Geneticists' Latest Discovery: Public Fear of 'Frankenfood'

BYLINE: By MOLLY O'NEILL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NEW ORLEANS, June 25

 BODY:
Exhibits of Pfizer's Chy-max, a genetically engineered coagulant, bloomed alongside displays for the Santa Cruz Valley Pecan Company and Sunsweet Prunes at the annual meeting of the Institute of Food Technologists this week in New Orleans.
From above, the displays looked like technological crops in a furrowed field. Instead of overalls, the 15,000 conventioneers who plied the 700,000-square-foot exhibition at the New Orleans Convention Center wore white lab coats or business suits. They carried calculators and notebooks, and when they spoke of "growth potential," they were more often than not referring to the possibilities in the genetic engineering of food products. 
 "The interest in genetic manipulation is unbelievable this year," said Alex Merolli, the marketing manager of Genencor International, which makes enzymes in South San Francisco, Calif. "It's all people are talking about."
 
The Boon and the Nightmare
 Industry analysts say that new genetically engineered food products will be rolled out in the next 18 months. It could be the biggest boon to corporate profits since frozen foods were introduced in the 1930's, and it could also be a marketing nightmare. Food technologists, the biologists and chemists who develop food-growing and processing techniques, are at the forefront of the image crisis that is kicking up around genetically altered food like a dust storm over an Idaho potato field.
In the three weeks since Vice President Dan Quayle announced that the Federal Government would not require specific testing or labeling for a number of genetically engineered food products, advocacy groups have questioned the long-term safety and impact of genetic technology.
Scientists have argued both for and against gene splicing in food production. And agricultural economists have discussed a wide range of altruistic applications: genetic technology could provide safeguards against certain food-borne diseases, it could curtail the dependence on chemical pesticides, and it could increase food production in an economical fashion, easing world hunger.
 
Doing What Nature Doesn't
 Biotechnologists have been developing gene-splicing techniques for almost a decade, hoping to bypass the vagaries of nature. Chy-max (the first syllable is pronounced to rhyme with eye), the only gene-altered product now on the market, is a replica of a natural coagulant used in cheese-making. But nature's coagulant is unpredictable, and Chy-max creates completely consistent cheese.
Similar technologies are being used to create more consistent yeasts for fermenting beer and wine. Fertilizer companies like Dow Chemical have inserted pest-resistant genes into some crops. Other food producers are experimenting with frost resistance and with genetically increasing crop yields.
But therein lies the marketing problem.
"The public is scared of the word genetic," said Dr. Jean-Marc Pernet, a biotechnologist for the Rouquette Corporation, a carbohydrate maker in Gurnee, Ill. "We are working on a number of products, but we know very well that developing the technology will be easier than marketing the technology."
 
A Sense of Menace
 Shortly after the Vice President's announcement, a cartoon in The New Yorker pictured a mad scientist rubbing his hands near his crop of leafy plants. The plants had menacing, feral faces on spindly stems. "That's splendid news from the F.D.A., my pretties," read the caption. In a letter to the editor of The New York Times, Paul Lewis of Newton Center, Mass., called genetically altered food "Frankenfood."
It is not only the public that is uneasy. After a seminar on the use of plant culture in genetic engineering at the New Orleans meeting, one food technologist dubbed such products "sci-fi food."
Coinages like these resonate a distrust of more than Chy-max or of the genetically engineered Flavor Saver, a tomato with an extended shelf life that Calgene Inc. of Davis, Calif., expects to introduce next year.
 
Worrying About Safety
 Indeed, the deconstruction and re-construction of DNA chains in edible plants and animals seem to tap a wellspring of modern misgiving.
"There's a distrust of technology, distrust of corporate profits, distrust of Government regulatory agencies and general fears about the safety of the food supply," said Dr. Susan K. Harlander, a leading researcher who is a professor of food science and nutrition at the University of Minnesota.
In January, the Food Marketing Institute, a trade group in Washington, found that consumer confidence in the safety of the national food supply had dipped 10 percent in the previous 12 months, the largest slide in nearly a decade.
 
Memories of Chernobyl
 Dr. Marshall Martin, a professor of agricultural economics at Purdue University in West Lafayette, Ind., said that the growing public distrust was not unfounded. "After World War II, the public was told that DDT was safe," he said. "The same thing with nuclear power. It was supposed to solve all our problems and be safe. And then we have Chernobyl and Three Mile Island."
Add the recent outbreaks of food-borne illness and the increasing evidence of the carcinogenic effects of a variety of food additives, and you begin to appreciate the breadth of public apprehension that greets any "New!" "Improved!" food product today.
"A public-relations approach is not going to sell biotechnology to the American public," Dr. Harlander said. But along with a number of scientists, she believes that the public needs to be educated about food science. Genetic technology is too promising, she said, to dismiss it out of a free-floating mistrust.
 
Irrational Reactions
 "If the public understood the technology, they would understand that part of their emotional reaction is irrational," she said.
Introducing genes that endow an organism with specific properties -- a piece of DNA that might make a chicken resistant to salmonella, another that might make wheat inherently pest-resistant -- is merely an extension of selective breeding techniques that farmers have been using safely for generations, Dr. Harlander said. This view was cited by the Bush Administration in announcing its policy on genetically engineered foods.
But in the public mind, using a flounder gene to make a tomato frost-resistant is a long way from crossing sweet corn with sweet corn to get sweeter corn. "Just think of splicing a few seconds of rap into a tape of a Beethoven symphony," said William E. Swartz, the director of technology for Rhone-Poulenc Food Ingredients in Cranbury, N.J.
 
Unease About Animal Genes
 Investigating public attitudes toward biotechnology in a telephone survey of 1,228 adults last month, Dr. Thomas J. Hoban, a professor of sociology at North Carolina State University in Raleigh, and Dr. Patricia Kendall, a professor of food and nutrition at Colorado State University in Fort Collins, found that people favored genetic tailoring of plants but that crossing species lines, particularly using genes from animals, "got people nervous," Dr. Kendall said.
Like most researchers, Dr. Harlander counters such concern by pointing to the altruistic applications of biotechnology. These promise to mitigate pollution by decreasing dependency on agricultural chemicals and by creating crops that require less processing, presumably cutting costs and curtailing the need for chemical additives.
More important, she said, "our natural resources simply cannot sustain a world population that is growing by 90 million people a year."
 
A Business, Not a Philanthropy
 World hunger seemed rather far removed from the food technologists' meeting in New Orleans, where the displays indicated more concern with such developments as low-fat kielbasa and dips, ice cream and burgers made with simulated fat. "They've been talking about famine for 50 years," Mr. Swartz said dismissively. He pointed out that his was a consumer industry, not a philanthrophy, a business driven by people who were willing to pay premium prices for low-fat hot dogs.
Certain innovations simultaneously offer potential corporate gains and potential famine relief. At the New Orleans meeting, the most talked-about genetic innovation -- a tomato that is frost-resistant because of the addition of a fish gene with antifreeze properties -- could significantly curtail crop loss.
But makers of tomato sauce and ketchup are more interested in pulp than they are in antifreeze, said Dr. Dietrich Knorr, a professor at the Berlin University of Technology who is an expert in the genetic engineering of plants. "By increasing the solids in tomatoes by 1 percent, a scientist could increase the profit in tomato processing worldwide by $100 million a year," he said.
The profit motivation worries Dr. Knorr. "Personally, I have to fear that people or companies will use the technology to overexpress certain properties," he said.
 
'Very Few Potential Allergens'
 Public outcry has been directed toward the possibility of allergic reactions to genetically altered food and the long-term health implications of ingesting genes that operate as pesticides. But most researchers and technologists at the convention agreed that these possibilities are less troublesome than other potential problems.
"Out of the hundreds of genes in any organism, there are very few potential allergens, and all are very well known," said Dr. Pernet of the Rouquette Corporation, who is experimenting with gene-splicing in carbohydrates. "But we have no idea what the long-range effect of limiting plant or animal diversity could be."
Of course, years of selective-breeding techniques have already affected the genetic diversity of plants, and some at the convention argued that genetic engineering was necessary to preserve desirable characteristics.
 
At Cross Purposes
 Dr. Herbert F. Pierson of Preventative Nutrition Consultants in Woodville, Wash., who has pioneered research in plant chemicals that appear to fight cancer, explained that years of developing garlic and onions that ship well and cheaply had affected the cancer-fighting phytochemicals in those foods. Nowadays, he said, it is necessary to extract the phytochemicals and then administer them, rather than simply advising people to eat more garlic and onions.
"The concentration of phytochemicals you find in the garlic or onions from the supermarket is completely random at this point," Dr. Pierson said. "We need technology to insure consistency and control over dosage."
The deconstruction and reconstruction of the gene chain made Dr. Pernet smile. "The point of most technology is control, cost control, quality control," he said.
The fact that recent public outcry over genetic engineering of food has centered on control as well -- consumers are asking for detailed labels that give them control over what they buy and eat -- was not lost on Dr. Pernet. To him the consumer's cry for control, like the food marketer's attempts to control the image of genetically altered food, echoes the underlying misgivings about genetic engineering in the scientific community.
"All of us know we can control several generations of genes," he said. "No one has any idea how many generations that control will work, or how long it will take for the genes to resist control. And that's scary."

HEADLINE: Sanctions Keep West Off the Road to Libya

BYLINE: By CHRIS HEDGES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TRIPOLI, Libya, June 21

 BODY:
Fauzi Ghnedi, the director of Libya's national tourist agency, sat in his empty office and tried to think of those who might want to take their holidays here.
"Perhaps reformed alcoholics," he said hopefully. "You know, we don't allow alcohol. And then there are those who like adventure." 
 Libya's nascent tourist industry, which in the best of times attracted only one or two thousand people a year, has suffered a body blow with the imposition of United Nations sanctions.
The sanctions, imposed in April after Libya refused to turn over suspects in the bombing of a Pan Am jet over Lockerbie, Scotland, in 1988 and a French plane over Africa in 1989, have halted flights in and out of the country.
 
U.S. Visits Restricted
 The State Department sharply restricts visits by Americans to Libya, which for years has been on the department's list of countries that sponsor terrorism. The American Embassy is closed.
"We go to the international tourist trade fairs in Europe," Mr. Ghnedi said, as his four assistants sat idly in front of their blank computer screens, "but we would need billions of dollars to counter the image we have abroad."
Tourism has never been high on the list of priorities for the Libyan leader, Col. Muammar el-Qaddafi. When he first took power in 1969 he nationalized hotels and restaurants, which led to a decline in service and food shortages.
The mores and culture of the West come under frequent attack from Government officials and in the state-controlled media. And when the sanctions were imposed, several Westerners working in the oil fields were temporarily unable to get exit visas.
 
Can't Buy Spare Parts
 Because of the sanctions the Libyan airline, which still flies internally, has lost its maintenance contract with Air France and cannot buy spare parts. Flights are no longer insured.
A group of German veterans from World War II recently canceled a trip to visit the war memorial at Tobruk. And the British have bowed out.
"It's not where you want to get sick in," a Western envoy said. "The hospitals are lousy and there is no way you can be flown out for treatment."
But Libyan officials, buoyed by the success Tunisia has had in bringing in Germans and Italian groups, say they are determined to lure vacationers.
 
'Libya Is Something Different'
 "People must be tired of going to the same old places," said Mr. Ghnedi, "and Libya is something different. Besides, it's not crowded."
Plans are under way to build a hotel on the island of Farwa, near the Tunisian border, and a string of motels along the coast.
"We realize there is a lot we have to do," said Ali el-Oammoudi, who is in charge of the Farwa project. "We have to do a lot of building and we have to train people. We hope to open a tourism institute."
Libya has a long and undeveloped coastline, although much of it is littered with garbage. It has some large Greek and Roman ruins. And for those who enjoy trekking in the desert, the south of the country has thousands of miles of open and unspoiled dunes.
But Libya is one of the most expensive countries in the world, if visitors change foreign currency at the official exchange rate. Most outsiders are required to declare the amount of money they bring into the country and are given a currency form to note each time they change money, making black market transactions difficult.
It takes three dollars to equal one dinar, according to the official rate, making a cup of coffee a $2 purchase. A modest hotel room costs $150, and credit cards aren't used.
On the black market the dinar is worth about 60 cents.

SECTION: Section 4; Page 16; Column 6; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 483 words

HEADLINE: In East Europe, the Hapsburgs Get Revenge;
Inside the New Croatia

 BODY:
To the Editor:
For eight days Zadar was a ghost town; the populace buried in underground shelters because of continuous shelling by Serbian terrorists camped in the surrounding hills. The city was under siege. At 6 A.M., May 27, the all-clear sounded and families slowly and timidly overcame their fear. Everyone needed a breath of fresh air. The local heroes here today are the bakers, who despite constant shelling, baked bread all night, and journalists and printers, who got the paper out every day. The favorite paper here is Slobodna Dalmacija (Free Dalmatia), whose journalists and editor are now under indictment. 
 Most space is given to war reporting, but journalists and other Croatians are carefully watching the new Government. A few articles have ridiculed and criticized its leaders. The indictment is based on "criticism that might undermine the stability of the new Government." Journalists wonder if the courts will protect their right to criticize, praise or ridicule the leadership of the new Croatia.
There is general euphoria over the acceptance of Croatia into the United Nations, but as some journalists noted, Slovenia was also accepted without so much pomp and at considerably less expense. Inflation is rampant, the war has slowed production and trade. An influx of refugee needs immediate care. Every available space is being turned over to house mothers and children; food and clothing come in from all over Europe. The war is not over, and the Government is slow to react to daily needs.
Journalists have dared to criticize the new regime because the country is hungry for democracy. They know what life is like in other European countries. The big question is what does democracy mean to the leadership of this country. The Government would like a stronger army: to protect the country or those in power? Journalists ask the same questions in all countries. Is the Government a public servant or is it there to meet the needs of a few? Journalists express the fears of a populace fighting for a truly representative government that worries about the price of a loaf of bread and whether or not a family can afford to buy it.
An article in Slobodna Dalmacija today was headlined "Ethnically Pure Onions." Slovenia no longer allows peasants from Croatia to sell vegetables in its market. Only a native Slovene has the right to sell vegetables. Of course, the price has doubled. The housewife's answer: I don't care who sells me onions, I just want to be able to buy them.
In 1991 I retired as a language teacher at the Bronx High School of Science. My parents were born in Dalmatia. My father went to the United States as an Austrian, my mother as a Yugoslav. I have close ties with this part of the world and like to spend a few months here every year. The shelling has started again now at 7 P.M.
 
SONJA GRGUREVIC MARTIN
Zadar, Croatia, May 28, 1992

HEADLINE: Quest for Zoning

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Congratulations to David W. Dunlap for his "The Quest for New Zoning" on April 12.. His description of the city's current zoning ordinance as "a cumbersome, bloated, 835-page patchwork that can be navigated only by a handful of specialists, leaving everyone else -- developers, architects, community leaders and neighbors -- at a baffled disadvantage" is right on the mark. 
 Sigurd Grava, director of the graduate planning program at Columbia University and current president of the New York chapter of the American Planning Association, has pinpointed the reason for inaction despite the obvious need: "While there are many of us in the trenches who think it should be done, we really don't have a very high official who'd take this on as a major political platform."
This is not a new issue nor is it one only for planning professionals. Three years ago the Women's City Club easily found more than 40 community groups from all parts of the city eager to sign on to its campaign for zoning reform.
Economic development is the key to the future of the city, and an up-to-date, comprehensible zoning resolution is critical to sound physical development. Is a new Zoning Resolution too difficult for a city that has just adopted a new Charter?
The Women's City Club urges the Mayor and the City Council to provide the funds, and instruct the Department of City Planning to give the city a Zoning Resolution for the 90's.
 
SUSAN ALT, BARBARA REACH
Manhattan
 
Ms. Alt is president of the Women's City Club of New York and Ms. Reach is chairwoman of its zoning committee.

SECTION: Section 10; Page 12; Column 6; Real Estate Desk

LENGTH: 211 words

HEADLINE: Quest for Zoning

 BODY:
To the Editor:
New York City does not need a fundamentally new Zoning Resolution or an initial "master plan" ["The Quest for a New Zoning Plan," April 12]. Rather, it needs an officially adopted set of objective master-planning principles to resolve any clash between private real estate interests and development regulations at a specific location. 
 Paramount among those master-planning principles should be a policy favoring economic development and employment growth, especially in areas served by rapid transit or major roadways, or bordering existing commercial or industrial sections.
Zoning changes that implement the economy-employment objective should generally be approved expeditiously by the city (unless offset by a compelling city interest, such as protecting an ecologically fragile spot or maintaining a long-established residential character).
In this increasingly competitive global setting, the city should embrace an entrepreneurial land-use policy. Current zoning-resolution controls (with ordinary amendments or clarifications) may then be applied in new areas or new ways to match this policy.
 
STEVEN D. KOWALOFF
 
Mr. Kowaloff, a Manhattan zoning lawyer, writes the "Zoning Advice" column in the Real Estate Weekly.
