SECTION: Section 1; Page 1; Column 1; National Desk

LENGTH: 1849 words

HEADLINE: CAMPAIGN 1992: Interview;
Clinton Seeking Forceful Image As a Leader in Foreign Affairs

BYLINE: By GWEN IFILL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LITTLE ROCK, Ark., June 25

 BODY:
In a wide-ranging discussion of the policies he would pursue if elected President, Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas supported strong action to help the besieged citizens of Sarajevo, criticized White House inaction on aid to the former Soviet republics and said Japan should be treated as "our most important bilateral relationship now."
Throughout a 90-minute interview at the Governor's Mansion here, Mr. Clinton sought to project himself as a more forceful leader than President Bush in foreign affairs, which is normally regarded as Mr. Bush's strong suit. 
 On domestic matters, Mr. Clinton, who was asked to paint a detailed picture of how he would govern, promised a 100-day legislative blitz intended to solve the problems he has accused Mr. Bush of ignoring.
 
Thinking Through Answers
 Mr. Clinton said he would propose an economic package that would increase financing for education and job training and emphasize tax breaks for business investment, research and development. He also said he would introduce health-care legislation to impose cost controls on hospitals and other providers and require universal insurance coverage.
Seated in a straight-backed chair, pausing to frame his answers and speaking in an introspective manner that captured both the accumulated frustration of a long campaign and the optimism that has at times rescued his Presidential bid, Mr. Clinton acknowledged the challenge of the general election tasks ahead. [Excerpts, page 17.]
"In order for a Democrat to win, you have to broaden the base of the Democratic party," he said. "You have to be able to do that. You have to prove that you can go beyond the little boxes that have characterized the Democrats for the last 20-plus years."
Mr. Clinton conceded the immensity of the task before him as he seeks to recover from the deep wounds of the primary campaign. But after a two-week stretch in which the spotlight of scrutiny turned to Ross Perot, Mr. Clinton suggested he now sensed an opening for the message he has been trying to deliver.
His fate, Mr. Clinton said, depended on his ability to overcome the fact that "people just feel there is nothing they can do" about the country's problems.
"So all I can do about it is run, is tell them what they can do about it and tell them what I'm trying to do about it, and trying to engage their ears and their interest," he said. "The result so far has been mixed, but I think there's a real shot that we can do it."
 
The Question of Trust
 Mr. Clinton, who will formally accept his party's nomination at the New York convention that starts July 13, outlined an election campaign designed to present him as a leader who would be more knowledgable than Mr. Perot, his likely independent challenger, and more engaged than Mr. Bush.
"I have been more detailed on how I think we should proceed in the future even than President Bush and certainly than Mr. Perot," he said. "So for all those reasons, I think I can be trusted to be Commander in Chief."
In providing a critique of Mr. Bush's foreign policy, Mr. Clinton said he would, if necessary, use military force, in concert with other nations, to bring humanitarian aid to the citizens of Bosnia and Herzegovina.
Mr. Bush "may have been a little slow on the uptake" in responding to the disintegration of Yugoslavia, Mr. Clinton said. But the United Nations should be consulted, he added, before multilateral military action is used to reopen the airport at Sarajevo, the Bosnian capital.
He also said the President had engaged in "foot dragging" on providing aid to the republics of the former Soviet Union and said Mr. Bush was wrong to link loan guarantees for Israel to freezing the building of Jewish settlements on the West Bank and the Gaza Strip.
 
'Inconsistency' in Bush
 Extending his critique to other areas of foreign policy, Mr. Clinton said the White House had been too cozy with China and too harsh in its treatment of Haitian refugees.
"I think there is an inconsistency in a lot of his actions," Mr. Clinton said of Mr. Bush, "because of the pull between the impulse, the deep American impulse, to support freedom and democracy and his own preference for stability in his relationships with foreign leaders in a given area." .
The Governor advocated closer ties with Japan and repeated his criticism of the President's foreign trade mission there last year, which he described as "perfectly absurd" because corporate executives whom Mr. Bush took with him were seeking only concessions from Japan.
 
Emphasis on Japan
 Acknowledging what he described as "serious differences of opinion between us about what constitutes fair and free trade," Mr. Clinton said the collapse of the Soviet Union had changed the diplomatic equation between Japan and the United States.
"It would not be in Japan's interest for America to continually struggle economically," he said. "It's not in Japan's interest for America to have 10 more years in which most Americans work harder for lower pay. It is in Japan's interest for this not to be a zero-sum game.
"I think that we ought to begin by saying this is an important bilateral relationship, perhaps our most important one. And we want it to work. And in order for it to work, both parties have to benefit."
Mr. Clinton spoke of an altered American relationship with other nations that would be based on strengthening the United States economy through job training, education financing, tax breaks for investment and a smaller military.
Only such a concerted economic effort at home, he said, will build confidence abroad.
 
A 'Fairly Daunting' Agenda
 He said he expected to accomplish his goals in part by relying on party loyalties to break the deadlocks between a Republican White House and a Democratically controlled Congress.
"I realize that's fairly daunting," he said of his plan. "But we have had bursts of activity in the first 100 days of Presidencies before, in times of national emergencies, and I think that people perceive this as a time of crisis, when we need to make a real turn in what we're doing."
Mr. Clinton said he expected that a revamped Congress, chastened and restructured by retirements and election defeats, would be willing to work quickly to enact his proposals. Mr. Bush and Mr. Perot, he said, would not be able to accomplish as much as quickly.
"Of the three choices, I am the best, most likely, to rebuild America's strength at home and therefore to give us the emotional and political freedom to engage the world," said Mr. Clinton, who has been searching for ways to prove that a Governor from a small state is equipped to handle global concerns.
"You know, if you look at the number of incumbents that are being defeated, that are retiring this year, I think that the key is whether Congress will be committed to working with the President," he said. "I do not believe that the permanent Government will resist the reforms if Congress and the President are in harness.
"I think it depends on having a Congress that's in sync with the mood of the people out in the country and that responds to the results of the election."
 
What Americans Want
 Mr. Clinton acknowledged that his challenge for the general election was to demonstrate to American voters that Democrats "were strong enough to manage the economy at home, stick up for the interests of the country abroad and articulate the broad public interest rather than a collection of particular interests."
"I think there is a yearning out there to have something that works again," he said. "You can see that in the Perot boom, which I hope is a boomlet."
Mr. Clinton has largely resisted attacking Mr. Perot and left squabbling about the Texan's background and exposition of his record to Republicans and the press.
Both Mr. Bush and Mr. Perot, the Governor said, have "been Republican insiders since the Nixon years."
Left on the sidelines in their growing war, Mr. Clinton has polished an argument that he, not Mr. Perot, could best manage the Government.
"I'm going to run this just the way the best companies do," he said. "We're not going to fire anybody. We're going to reduce Government by attrition."
Government employees, he maintained, "like a good shot in the arm every few years."
Mr. Clinton defended his optimistic budget projections, saying he intentionally did not include potential savings that could be derived from cost controls on the health-care industry and from economic growth.
He said economists and others were "right to be concerned" about the amount of the budget deficit that is caused by the rising cost of spending for guaranteed benefits programs like Medicare and Medicaid.
But he said that cutting the availability of those benefits was not necessarily the best way to rein in such costs. Advocates for the elderly, in particular, have objected to any proposals that would cut such entitlement spending.
"The entitlements are driving the deficit," Mr. Clinton said. "But most people have the wrong prescription for it."
 
Health Costs and Economy
 He said deficit-cutters should look instead to the source of the problem: the growth in poverty that forced more people to become dependent on the Government's social safety net.
"If you don't believe we can bring health costs in line with inflation, or at least pretty close to it, then a lot of what I'm proposing doesn't hang together," he said. "If you do believe it, it is the central key to getting control of the Government deficit and bringing about the billions of dollars in the private sector for reinvestment."
Mr. Clinton indicated he had left room for compromise on several of his other economic proposals, including his plan to create a $200 billion fund for rail, highway and bridge improvements.
He said he would not proceed with the rebuilding plan, which would be paid for in part with $115 billion in new and higher taxes on corporations and the wealthy, unless he could win Congressional approval for the tax increases.
"Would I do it totally through deficit financing?" he asked. "No."
Mr. Clinton said he would stand by the budget estimates that his staff devised for his proposal to increase taxes for the richest 2 percent of taxpayers.
He said he was convinced that his detailed plans would persuade voters that he should take over the reins from President Bush.
"What is killing us is people no longer believe that they're part of a larger community where they matter, and where they can make a difference," Mr. Clinton said. "That's why I've taken the unusual step of doing my best and exposing myself to considerable criticism in putting forward this plan and hammering it out.
"Roosevelt ran for President on a balanced budget," he said. "Kennedy ran for President on getting the country moving again. I've tried to do it, in part, to break throught the wall of cynicism that exists out there."
Mr. Clinton, some of the hoarseness from persistent allergies returning to color his voice, paused.
"I don't know," he said. "If it were easy, I'd have done it already."

HEADLINE: Focus: Spring Island, S.C.;
Development With an Environmental Bent

BYLINE: By LYN RIDDLE

DATELINE: SPRING ISLAND, S.C.

 BODY:
Here among moss-draped live oaks and unspoiled ponds, a new style of island development based on strict environmental standards is being tested, and developers around the nation are watching to see if it succeeds.
"When I came to this island, I knew I had to find a way to spend the rest of my life here," said Jim Chaffin, one of the owners. "Not having $17 million to spend on my personal island, I knew we would have to do some sort of development." 
 Mr. Chaffin said he wanted development to have as little impact as possible on the pristine environment. Thus, he has imposed such restrictions as deep setbacks for homes beside the water, transplanting trees rather than cutting them down, large buffers between houses and streets, small lots and no paved roads. A third of the 3,500-acre island is to be left untouched as a nature preserve.
The state's Beachfront Management Act, which sets strict waterfront setbacks for homes, is being challenged in the United States Supreme Court by a property owner on the Isle of Palms -- north of here and just off the coast of Charleston. Whatever the decision, it would change nothing here because of the tight controls imposed by the owners in their deeds.
"Spring Island represents a marked improvement over some of the other developments on South Carolina islands," said Dana Beach, who as head of the South Carolina Coastal Conservation League is frequently a harsh critic of developers. "There are abysmally few examples of good developments along the coast."
In the last decade, as more and more people have moved at least part-time to seaside houses, the dozens of islands along this state's 187-mile-long coast have undergone massive development. Among them are Hilton Head, which now has 15,000 housing units on 23,000 acres; Kiawah, with 2,000 units on 10,000 acres, and Seabrook, with 1,700 units on 3,000 acres.
Thirty-three major islands lie off the mainland here, with 11 owned by the state or the Federal Government. Of the remaining 22, which are privately owned, five are permanently protected by deed restrictions or conservation easements. The rest are either already developed or slated for some sort of housing development in the next few years.
A host of other islands, like Spring Island, are in the rivers and harbors of the state, and developers are looking at them, too. Spring Island, between the Colleton and the Chechessee Rivers just east of Beaufort, S.C., has been privately owned since 1706. Early owners grew cotton here, while the most recent owners used it as a hunting preserve.
Mr. Chaffin and his longtime business partner, Jim Light, bought the island in 1989 for $17 million. They cut their teeth in development more than two decades ago on Hilton Head Island, a few miles south of here. They moved from there to Snowmass Village, Colo., in 1977, and recently have built housing developments at Semiahmoo, Wash., and on Callawassie Island, a neighbor to Spring. All are typical resort developments and all have made money for the two partners.
But Spring Island is a major risk, the owners say. When they were devising their development plan, Mr. Chaffin and Mr. Light asked eight key developers to visit the island and tell them how it should be developed. The consensus was that it should have at least 3,000 units and three golf courses.
The owners said no. Despite the county government's earlier approval for 5,500 units, the developers said Spring Island would have no more than 500 units and only one golf course designed to follow already existing cornfields.
The previous owners had planted the cornfields to attract wildlife. Anticipating development, they had obtained the approval for 5,500 units.
"People we respect think we're flipping out, that we've gotten too radical," said Mr. Chaffin, who lives on the island with his wife, Betsy, a member of the board of the South Carolina Nature Conservancy and head of the Spring Island Trust, which oversees the nature preserve.
MR. CHAFFIN, 47 years old, was recently elected vice president of the Urban Land Institute, a 14,000-member organization based in Washington that studies land management. Its new executive director, Richard Rosan, said he sees a real and growing interest by developers in applying strict environmental controls.
"This is the 90's," Mr. Rosan said. "The ones in the black hats left in the 80's."
But the bottom line is still money, and the verdict is not in yet on whether there are 500 people willing to pay from $155,000 to more than $1 million for lots of three-quarters of an acre to 10 acres of Spring Island. So far, 80 lots have been sold, at an average price of $300,000.
At the Hilton Head Plantation development, the average price of a lot in the first quarter this year has been $144,000. On Dataw Island, the average has been $129,000 and on Kiawah Island -- which also has tight environmental rules -- it has been $252,000. On Seabrook Island, which is under receivership, it has been $23,000.
Spring Island buyers are doctors, lawyers, entrepreneurs and corporate executives. The head of a major national environmental organization, whom Mr. Chaffin declined to identify, has bought a lot here, he said. The average age of the buyers is 47; 30 percent will live here year-round, 40 percent seasonally and the rest will use Spring Island for a second home.
The first two houses are under construction now. Eight more will be begun by early fall. The houses range in size from 3,500 to 12,000 square feet to be spread over several buildings. There is no minimum size for homes, and the developers say they prefer that the houses be small.
"PEOPLE come here not to be seen, but to behold; not to be heard by others but to listen to that special wisdom which only nature and history can impart," said Mr. Chaffin.
When the partners started selling the lots in 1990, they sold 40 immediately, enabling them, with the approval of the Coast Guard and two state agencies, to build a bridge to the island from their own Callawassie Island.
The recession and Persian Gulf War brought business to a virtual halt, although sales have begun to pick up, Mr. Chaffin said. The partners -- Mr. Chaffin and Mr. Light own 20 percent each; Dr. Peter Lamont owns 10 percent and a Japanese company, Nippon Landis, owns 50 percent -- are under no serious financial pressure, thanks to Nippon Landis's infusion of cash, Mr. Chaffin said.
One of Spring Island's drawbacks is that there is no ocean frontage and, therefore, no beach. But it has a wealth of ponds, where a goodly number of alligators live. And the partners are counting on what they perceive to be the public's growing interest in marshes and rivers.
Oceanfront property, Mr. Chaffin said, is overcrowded and he wants Spring Island to be like a camp, with horseback riding, boating, walking, golf and bicycling available. A central dining facility and nature studies by a resident naturalist are provided as well.
"At this point in our careers, we risk not only money but also reputation," said Mr. Chaffin. "If you get too far out in front, you get arrows in your back."

HEADLINE: THEATER;
As Phoenix Opens 5th Season, Names Are Brighter

BYLINE: By ALVIN KLEIN

DATELINE: PURCHASE

 BODY:
"WE are the exception that tests the rule," said Bram Lewis, the founder and artistic director of the Phoenix Theater Company, which opened its fifth summer season last week. Mr. Lewis was referring to the company's resilience in Westchester, where professional nonprofit theater has so often failed.
"The structures that make nonprofit theaters possible have been under attack," he said, "beginning with a restructured tax code. Given Jesse Helms, who made things uncomfortable with the National Endowment for the Arts. Given the 46 percent the New York State Council on the Arts has cut. Given the most serious recession to hit the country since the 1930's. 
 "All those givens notwithstanding, we've grown in community support, and here we are celebrating our fifth anniversary season."
 
'Morning's at Seven'
 That season began with Tuesday's preview performance, at the Performing Arts Center at the State University of New York here, of "Morning's at Seven," the 1939 comedy by Paul Osborn that was memorably revived on Broadway in 1980. It is directed by Ellis Rabb and is to run through July 12.
An East Coast premiere for a revision of "The Old Boy" by A. R. Gurney is to run from July 15 through Aug. 2. The play was seen at Playwrights Horizons in Manhattan last year with Stephen Collins, who is now directing the Phoenix production, in the title role.
"Twelfth Night," in which Mr. Rabb is scheduled to play Malvolio in Mr. Lewis's staging, and the mythical island of Illyria is transported to South America in the 1840's, concludes the season. It is to run from Aug. 5 through Aug. 23.
Although the Phoenix is, philosophically, all about action and plays, not stars and shows, the inescapable value of names sets this season apart from preceding ones. To those well versed in theater, the roster of actors appearing in these plays is formidable.
In the lineup for the current play are Tom Aldredge, Jacqueline Brooks, Meg Mundy, Josef Sommer, Frances Sternhagen and Richard Woods. Then, Joan Copeland, Maryann Plunkett and Jay Sanders are to be in Mr. Gurney's play. The talent blitz began two weeks ago with a benefit performance of Mr. Gurney's "Love Letters" in which Jason Robards and Elaine Stritch were the readers.
 
'Best and the Brightest'
 For Mr. Rabb, the name Phoenix has a special resonance. In 1960, he founded the Association of Producing Artists of which he was the artistic director for 22 years. From 1964 through 1969, the company was known as A.P.A.-Phoenix, when it formed an association with Manhattan's Phoenix Theater Company -- an inspiration for Mr. Lewis's organization -- and played during those seasons at Broadway's Lyceum Theater.
"If Rabb is here, or a reworked Gurney play is being directed by its original star," Mr. Lewis said, "the theater's best and the brightest will come running in pursuit of excellence and to reinvent a tradition -- of transformation by live theater -- that too many people don't know about."
Although Broadway audiences "are used to the glitz, the shine, the dazzle," Mr. Lewis said, it is the actors' imagination that "for us is the spark."
In its fifth season, the Phoenix is playing in its third theater in Westchester. The company had three summers of rotating repertory at the Masters School in Dobbs Ferry. Last season, it moved to the 300-seat Abbot Kaplan at the Performing Arts Center, and the repertory, which had included classical and new works, became popularly oriented. The plays were not performed in alternation.
 
477 Seats to Fill
 Now the Kaplan Theater, a flexible "black box" space, is to be used for a series of experimental plays to be presented by the Phoenix later in the season. The major works are being staged in Theater B -- "a more sophisticated space," Mr. Lewis said, and a larger one, with 477 seats.
"We set ourselves up as a rotating repertory theater, and we hope we'll have the money to get back to that artistically ideal format," he said. "But we can't keep such actors here for 15 weeks at the laughable developmental theater salaries we're paying."
Mr. Lewis added: "Half of our original gang went off to make names and careers in Los Angeles. I bless them and miss them. Michael Patrick King has been discovered. He wrote five episodes of 'Murphy Brown' last season. And Peter Francis James and Harriet Harris have been appearing widely in theaters all over."
After the move from Dobbs Ferry to Purchase, "we lost half of our subscribers," Mr. Lewis said. "But then we gained others and came out even. And now we're gaining more."
He said the group started out to prove that Westchester could be hospitable to professional theater and is now proving "that Westchester is the place for important talent to come and work."
"Westchester has 19 symphony and chamber orchestras and 3 world-class museums," Mr. Lewis said. "We are establishing ourselves as another cultural linchpin in a community that can now find excellence in theatrical terms. We're finding our audiences -- and our angels. It's a challenge to convince them that we're going to stick around."

HEADLINE: Flexible Hours: A Tool to Keep Employees

BYLINE: By PENNY SINGER

 BODY:
FOR four days a week -- Mondays through Thursdays -- Caron Palder arrives at her office at Nynex corporate headquarters in White Plains shortly before 7:15 A.M. She takes a half hour for lunch and leaves for home in Stamford at 4:30 P.M. On Thursdays, she leaves at noon and works at home for the rest of the day. On Fridays, she is off and stays at home.
Ms. Palder, a manager in the Nynex corporate human resources department, said she chose a compressed 35-hour workweek in order to spend more time with her 2 1/2-year-old son, William. 
 "I would hate to miss his toddler years," said Ms. Palder, who is 35 years old. "That way I can be with him for half the week, which is good. Nynex's flexible work plan has made it possible for me to combine two important facets of my life, work and home."
 
Countering a Downturn
 Nynex, one of several major Westchester corporations that use work schedules, inherited its family-friendly policies from its Bell parent, said R. Michelle Green, who is the director of work/family initiatives. And during this recession, Nynex is increasing, not decreasing, these efforts to reduce the effects of the economic downturn.
"It is in our best interests to retain our employees," Ms. Green said. "Training new workers is a very expensive proposition. The flexible policies are also a good recruiting tool. They have made us known to the world as a company of choice, which means that prospective employees hear about us by word of mouth and seek us out."
Work-family policies have expanded or remained stable during the current recession, according to a survey by the Conference Board, a business research group based in Manhattan, which polled 138 organizations that deal with work issues.
 
Increased Productivity
 Nynex recognizes that the work force is changing, Ms. Green said. "There are more men and women working. There are more single parents, and there are more families in which both the husbands and wives work," she said. "We want to make it easier for them to work at Nynex and harder for them to leave."
The bottom line, she said, is that flexible scheduling not only attracts and keeps the best workers but also increases productivity significantly. Toward that end, Nynex, with the cooperation of its two unions -- the Communication Workers of America and the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers -- recently contributed $6 million to a fund to increase the supply and quality of child care and the care of the elderly in communities where Nynex employees live and work.
"The company has 85,000 employees in the Northeast," Ms. Green said. "Obviously, we have too many work sites to have child-care centers at each one. So what we do is make investments in existing facilities that are recommended to us by our employees."
The method, she explained, is that employees make formal applications for grants, and those agencies deemed to offer the most benefit get financing.
Paradoxically, while it is in the process of reducing its work force, I.B.M., at its corporate headquarters in Armonk, is in the middle of expanding several family programs.
"It's part of our long-term strategy to keep our valued employees," said John C. Childs Jr., director of the program of work-force diversity at I.B.M. in Purchase.
 
Optional Schedules Available
 "Over the last 10 years we have developed work-life programs to help employees come to work and let them work with a minimum of distractions that are brought on by family problems," he said. "In 1984, for example, we were the first company to make child-care referral service available for employees."
Last year, Mr. Childs said, flexible working hours at I.B.M. were extended to a four-hour period. "What it means is that on either side of our normal work day, which is from 8:30 A.M. to 4:30 P.M., our people have the option of working from either 6:30 A.M. to 2:30 P.M. or from 10:30 A.M. to 6:30 P.M.," Mr. Childs said.
"And we've extended our meal break, which means employees can take up to two hours for lunch. Some people use the time to visit an elderly relative or attend a child's school play, for example, or others choose to work out in a gym. They have to clear the time with their department manager, and it has to be made up the same day."
At Nynex, employees arrange flexible scheduling of work hours through their department heads. "We act as a clearinghouse for information on flexible scheduling," Ms. Green said. "Employees come to us with questions like, 'How do I approach my boss?' 'Will this harm my career?' We make clear to them that it is not seen as a negative occurrence."
Peter Benson, a manager of human resources at Nynex corporate headquarters, said that he not only makes other employees aware of the different facets of the program but also understands it firsthand, having participated in it himself for several years. By exercising his option to make his working hours 7:30 A.M. to 3:30 P.M., Mr. Benson said, he is able to accomplish much more on the job.
"It has been an advantage to get to the office early, before the telephones start ringing," Mr. Benson said. "I find I can get a lot of work done in that period. And when necessary, I can stay later than 3:30 in the afternoon without any impact on my home life."
Mr. Benson, 44, said he chose his schedule to have more time with his two children, who are now 11 and 15. "Personally, it has been a very satisfying arrangement for our family," he said. "I feel I've been able to be a positive influence in my children's growing-up years, which is something it's hard for an absentee father to do.
"Working for a company like Nynex that shows concern for family life means a lot to many people. We have very loyal employees."
Recently, I.B.M. expanded a three-year flexible work leave-of-absence program the company began in 1988, Mr. Childs said. The program allows employees to work part time for three years with full benefits paid by I.B.M. With the expansion of the program they can now continue to work part time by paying some of the benefits costs themselves.
"Several hundred employees have taken advantage of this program," Mr. Childs said. "They include mothers with small children and people who have obligations to care for older family members."
The company has also expanded its work-at-home program, he added, but only employees who have jobs that can be done at home are eligible. An employee in the financial area, for example, can prepare weekly reports at home and file them by computer modem linked to a telephone line. Someone using a word processor can write a training manual at home and keep in touch by computer with people at the office.
"All flexible work programs have to be worked out with department managers," Mr. Childs said.
And although companies with flexible work policies are viewed favorably by employees, it is more than altruism that motivates the companies that install them.
"Of course, employee morale is important to us," Mr. Childs said, "but equally significant is our investment we make in employees. To recruit a skilled person in categories such as a systems engineer, it costs us $12,000 to $14,000. And training will cost an additional $100,000. Is it any wonder we want to retain them?
"As our work force becomes smaller, it is even more important that we retain valuable employees and do what we can to make them even more productive."

(Photographs by Joyce Dopkeen/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: FOCUS;
Development With an Environmental Bent

BYLINE: By LYN RIDDLE

DATELINE: SPRING ISLAND, S.C.

 BODY:
Here among moss-draped live oaks and unspoiled ponds, a new style of island development based on strict environmental standards is being tested, and developers around the nation are watching to see if it succeeds.
 "When I came to this island, I knew I had to find a way to spend the rest of my life here," said Jim Chaffin, one of the owners. "Not having $17 million to spend on my personal island, I knew we would have to do some sort of development." 
Mr. Chaffin said he wanted development to have as little impact as possible on the pristine environment. Thus, he has imposed such restrictions as deep setbacks for homes beside the water, transplanting trees rather than cutting them down, large buffers between houses and streets, small lots and no paved roads. A third of the 3,500-acre island is to be left untouched as a nature preserve.
 The state's Beachfront Management Act, which sets strict waterfront setbacks for homes, is being challenged in the United States Supreme Court by a property owner on the Isle of Palms -- north of here and just off the coast of Charleston. Whatever the decision, it would change nothing here because of the tight controls imposed by the owners in their deeds.
 "Spring Island represents a marked improvement over some of the other developments on South Carolina islands," said Dana Beach, who as head of the South Carolina Coastal Conservation League is frequently a harsh critic of developers. "There are abysmally few examples of good developments along the coast."
 In the last decade, as more and more people have moved at least part-time to seaside houses, the dozens of islands along this state's 187-mile-long coast have undergone massive development. Among them are Hilton Head, which now has 15,000 housing units on 23,000 acres; Kiawah, with 2,000 units on 10,000 acres, and Seabrook, with 1,700 units on 3,000 acres.
 Thirty-three major islands lie off the mainland here, with 11 owned by the state or the Federal Government. Of the remaining 22, which are privately owned, five are permanently protected by deed restrictions or conservation easements. The rest are either already developed or slated for some sort of housing development in the next few years.
 A host of other islands, like Spring Island, are in the rivers and harbors of the state, and developers are looking at them, too. Spring Island, between the Colleton and the Chechessee Rivers just east of Beaufort, S.C., has been privately owned since 1706. Early owners grew cotton here, while the most recent owners used it as a hunting preserve.
 Mr. Chaffin and his longtime business partner, Jim Light, bought the island in 1989 for $17 million. They cut their teeth in development more than two decades ago on Hilton Head Island, a few miles south of here. They moved from there to Snowmass Village, Colo., in 1977, and recently have built housing developments at Semiahmoo, Wash., and on Callawassie Island, a neighbor to Spring. All are typical resort developments and all have made money for the two partners.
 But Spring Island is a major risk, the owners say. When they were devising their development plan, Mr. Chaffin and Mr. Light asked eight key developers to visit the island and tell them how it should be developed. The consensus was that it should have at least 3,000 units and three golf courses.
 The owners said no. Despite the county government's earlier approval for 5,500 units, the developers said Spring Island would have no more than 500 units and only one golf course designed to follow already existing cornfields.
 The previous owners had planted the cornfields to attract wildlife. Anticipating development, they had obtained the approval for 5,500 units.
 "People we respect think we're flipping out, that we've gotten too radical," said Mr. Chaffin, who lives on the island with his wife, Betsy, a member of the board of the South Carolina Nature Conservancy and head of the Spring Island Trust, which oversees the nature preserve.
 Mr. Chaffin, 47 years old, was recently elected vice president of the Urban Land Institute, a 14,000-member organization based in Washington that studies land management. Its new executive director, Richard Rosan, said he sees a real and growing interest by developers in applying strict environmental controls on their projects.
 "This is the 90's," Mr. Rosan said. "The ones in the black hats left in the 80's."
 But the bottom line is still money, and the verdict is not in yet on whether there are 500 people willing to pay from $155,000 to more than $1 million for lots of three-quarters of an acre to 10 acres of Spring Island. So far, 80 lots have been sold, at an average price of $300,000.
 At the Hilton Head Plantation development, the average price of a lot in the first quarter this year has been $144,000. On Dataw Island, the average has been $129,000 and on Kiawah Island -- which also has tight environmental rules -- it has been $252,000. On Seabrook Island, which is under receivership, it has been $23,000.
 Spring Island buyers are doctors, lawyers, entrepreneurs and corporate executives. The head of a major national environmental organization, whom Mr. Chaffin declined to identify, has bought a lot here, he said. The average age of the buyers is 47; 30 percent will live here year-round, 40 percent seasonally and the rest will use Spring Island for a second home.
 The first two houses are under construction now. Eight more will be begun by early fall. The houses range in size from 3,500 to 12,000 square feet to be spread over several buildings. There is no minimum size for homes, and the developers say they prefer that the houses be small.
 "People come here not to be seen, but to behold; not to be heard by others but to listen to that special wisdom which only nature and history can impart," said Mr. Chaffin.
 When the partners started selling the lots in 1990, they sold 40 immediately, enabling them, with the approval of the Coast Guard and two state agencies, to build a bridge to the island from their own Callawassie Island.
 The recession and the Persian Gulf War brought business to a virtual halt, although sales have begun to pick up, Mr. Chaffin said. The partners -- Mr. Chaffin and Mr. Light own 20 percent each; Dr. Peter Lamont owns 10 percent and a Japanese company, Nippon Landis, owns 50 percent -- are under no serious financial pressure, thanks to the infusion of cash from Nippon Landis, Mr. Chaffin said.
 One of Spring Island's drawbacks is that there is no ocean frontage and, therefore, no beach. But it has a wealth of ponds, where a goodly number of alligators live. And the partners are counting on what they perceive to be the public's growing interest in marshes and rivers.
 Oceanfront property, Mr. Chaffin said, is overcrowded and he wants Spring Island to be like a camp, with horseback riding, boating, walking, golf and bicycling available. A central dining facility and nature studies by a resident naturalist are provided as well.
 "At this point in our careers, we risk not only money but also reputation," said Mr. Chaffin. "If you get too far out in front, you get arrows in your back."

CORRECTION-DATE: June 28, 1992, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
An article on June 28 about development on Spring Island, S.C., misstated the financial condition of Seabrook Island, 45 miles to the north, and the cost of its lots. The island's homeowners' association is not in receivership. The first-quarter average price of its lots was $76,000, not $23,000.

HEADLINE: Sex Without Fear

BYLINE: By Daniel J. Kevles; Daniel J. Kevles is the Koepfli Professor of the Humanities at the California Institute of Technology. He is co-editor, with Leroy Hood, of "The Code of Codes: Scientific and Social Issues in the Human Genome Project."

 BODY:

WOMAN OF VALOR
Margaret Sanger and the Birth Control
Movement in America.
By Ellen Chesler.
Illustrated. 639 pp. New York:
Simon & Schuster. $27.50. 
 Ellen Chesler, in "Woman of Valor," brilliantly reveals and revitalizes Margaret Sanger in the context of the tempestuous public battles for birth control that she waged through the first half of this century. Sanger once remarked to a lover that she was "not a peaceful or restful person to know at all." Ms. Chesler has come to know her about as well as anyone could at this distance from her life -- she died in 1966 -- by interviewing friends, associates and relatives and by exploring a vast body of published and unpublished material, ranging from sizable caches of private papers to her file at the Department of Justice. The letters and diaries expose a great deal about Margaret Sanger that she did not want the public to learn during her lifetime, but that, as Ms. Chesler notes, she apparently wished history to see.
Ms. Chesler is an independent scholar whose many years of work on this biography were interrupted by extended service as chief of staff to former New York City Council President Carol Bellamy. She brings a keen sense of political nuance to the conflicts that surround issues of reproductive rights. She approaches her subject with critical sympathy and informed psychological insight. "Woman of Valor" is at once richly detailed and capaciously conceived -- authoritative, readable and rippling with the energy of the life it conveys.
Margaret Sanger, nee Higgins, grew up in Corning, N.Y., in an Irish-American family in which, Ms. Chesler contends, "one parent taught her to defy, the other to comport." The teacher of defiance was her father, Michael Higgins, a ne'er-do-well stonecutter who was an apostate Roman Catholic, militant freethinker, labor-rights advocate and social pariah. The model of comportment was her mother, who remained pious throughout her life, bearing 11 children and dying, worn out, at the age of 50.
MARGARET HIGGINS went to New York City to study nursing, and there, in 1902, she met and married William Sanger, a handsome young architect and aspiring painter. The product of an observant Jewish family, he was, like his new wife, a Socialist and a rebel against religious orthodoxy. In 1910, having been driven out of a suburban house following financial reverses, the Sangers returned to Manhattan, short on cash and with three young children to support. While Margaret Sanger worked as a visiting nurse and midwife, the couple got involved in the Socialist Party and with many of the day's radical reformers, including William (Big Bill) Haywood, the head of the Industrial Workers of the World, and the anarchist and feminist Emma Goldman.
Ms. Chesler emphasizes that Margaret Sanger's conversion to the cause of contraception owed a great deal to Goldman, who for some years had been insisting that much of the solution to what European Socialists called "the woman question" lay in birth control. Emancipated from the reproductive consequence of sex, Goldman reasoned, women would be freed from repressive forms of marriage and motherhood, liberated to realize themselves sexually and spiritually. Emma Goldman's doctrines moved Sanger toward an increasing impatience with the attitudes of male Socialists like Haywood, who spoke of an economic commonwealth where women could have "all the babies they pleased," and against the indifference of most radicals to Socialism's call for women "to revolt from sex slavery."
Sanger's sexually radical thinking was reinforced by her midwifery, which brought her into contact with impoverished women who resorted to self-induced abortions, risking death to end pregnancies that they did not know how to prevent. In 1914, Sanger began publishing a periodical entitled The Woman Rebel, declaring that women were enslaved by "sex conventions," "motherhood," and "middle-class morality" and promising that she would provide information on contraception to help emancipate them.
In August 1914, she was arrested and charged with violations of the Federal Comstock laws, which prohibited the dissemination of obscene materials, and -- since an article in her magazine had discussed political assassinations -- with incitement to murder. She fled to Europe to avoid prosecution, returning in 1915 as the public debate on the issue of birth control was mounting rapidly. In February 1916, after much of what she had published about contraception was now common magazine fare, all charges against her were dropped. Suddenly she was famous.
Sanger adjusted rapidly to celebrity, shedding her radical rhetoric to promulgate the doctrine of birth control as a means to achieve both sex without fear and social transformation through lowered birth rates, without class warfare. In 1916, she established the first birth control clinic in the United States in the Brownsville section of Brooklyn, advertising the facility in handbills written in English, Yiddish and Italian. During the early 1920's, she began publishing The Birth Control Review and organized the American Birth Control League. She fought opposition to birth control with persistence and flair. In 1929, when civic authorities forbade her to speak in Boston's Ford Hall, she stood silent on the stage, her mouth taped shut, while the Harvard University historian Arthur M. Schlesinger Sr. read her prepared statement.
All the while, in her private life she remained a rebel in both deed and belief, unwilling to repeat her mother's subordination or to let any man define her, least of all William Sanger, whose Socialist convictions regarding women stopped short even of Haywood's. She marched away from her marriage into multiple romances -- her numerous lovers included Havelock Ellis and H. G. Wells -- and sexually radical adventures (beds a trois or a quatre) with a circle of friends in England. Divorced from William Sanger in 1921, she confided that she would not remarry, except for money, describing herself as "no fit person for love or home or children or friends or anything which needs attention or consideration."
However, as Ms. Chesler points out, her instinct for comportment remained powerful. In 1922, she married Noah Slee, who was a Union Leaguer, an active Episcopalian 17 years her senior -- and a millionaire. Slee adored her enough to allow her independent life and liaisons, though at times he did so with considerable irritation. The marriage provided Margaret Sanger with the trappings of opulence, a financial angel for her activism and a respectability that was useful to her campaign.
Noah Slee was the largest individual supporter of the American Birth Control League, donating some $50,000 to the group by 1925, at least 10 times more than anyone else. In 1930, he bought a five-story town house for the birth control clinic on West 16th Street in Manhattan. Sanger also courted wealthy women for her cause, writing once that "little can be done without them." She allied herself with eugenicists against religious conservatives, contending that birth control was a powerful tool for limiting the reproduction of the unfit. American eugenicists claimed that a high degree of biologically determined unfitness characterized lower-income and minority groups, including not only people of color but also recent immigrants from eastern and southern Europe. Margaret Sanger publicly embraced such attitudes at times. However, Ms. Chesler contends that the alliance with eugenicists was principally one of political expediency, a misguided (and ultimately costly) bid for secular support. Weighing the evidence in the public and private record, she concludes that Sanger was neither racist nor anti-Semitic and that her championship of contraception was not primarily intended to check the proliferation of the lower classes.
The principal religious opposition to birth control came from the Roman Catholic Church. Spokesmen for the church hurled vituperations against Sanger and attacked her cause as introducing whoring into married life. Sanger was at first utterly shunned by the Administration of Franklin D. Roosevelt when she urged incorporation of birth control into the burgeoning public health programs of the New Deal (she was later given a serious hearing, thanks in part to the intervention of Eleanor Roosevelt). Ms. Chesler attributes the Administration's arm's-length treatment to its political dependence on -- and consequent fear of -- Catholic voters, and she notes that the birth control wars made Margaret Sanger an increasingly bitter anti-Catholic.
DURING the 1930's, Sanger and her allies lobbied unsuccessfully for Congressional repeal of the Comstock laws. But the courts, Ms. Chesler determines by tracking the cases, were steadily eroding Federal legal obstacles to the distribution of contraceptive information and devices by physicians. Moreover, public opinion polls showed increasing tolerance, even on the part of Catholic women, toward contraception. After World War II, Margaret Sanger busied herself with the issue of international population control until, in the 1950's, she was slowed and increasingly enfeebled by a series of heart attacks. Not only had she become respectable, but so had her cause -- at least in the socially sanitized guise of "family planning," stripped of its original radical aims for the reconstruction of society and the place of women in it.
Several pioneering historical scholars of birth control, writing from the perspective of the 1960's, criticized Sanger for trading social reform and women's public liberation for establishment alliances that merely helped women to enjoy sex without fear of pregnancy. Ellen Chesler vigorously challenges such ideas, contending that Margaret Sanger, wanting women "to have it all," saw birth control as necessary to that end, and pursued a strategy of "political accommodation" to make it widely available. Sanger had to deal with a society that was often conservative and always deeply divided about women's rights and reproductive freedom. In Ms. Chesler's view, Margaret Sanger sought in the birth control campaigns what her private life expressed -- freedom for women to work and to love as they might wish.
 
 
 
NEW TERMS FOR AN OLD DEBATE
 In the early 1970's, when women's studies was still in its infancy, Ellen Chesler was a graduate student in American history at Columbia University in New York. It was during her studies that she saw the need for a reappraisal of Margaret Sanger.
The existing Sanger biography by David M. Kennedy, Ms. Chesler said, argued that "Sanger turned women's concerns back to the personal, and that the personal is not political. I believed that the personal is political; this is the second stage of feminism."
Committed to the reinvestigation of Sanger's life, Ms. Chesler nonetheless interrupted her studies to work in local politics for Carol Bellamy, ultimately becoming Ms. Bellamy's chief of staff after a victorious 1977 New York City Council race. She didn't return to her graduate studies -- or to the biography -- until 1985.
In a telephone interview from her home in Manhattan, Ms. Chesler said that Sanger's work has resonance today, in light of the "family values" debate and the volatile issue of abortion rights. "I'd like to think that Margaret Sanger would get a group of women together in her drawing room to come up with a new term for abortion, as she did for birth control," she said. "They came up with the term 'family planning.' I'd like to think that she would recast the terms of the abortion debate to make a distinction between the first trimester, when 95 percent of abortions are done, and the second or third. She might have looked to Asia, where the evacuations in the first trimester are called menstrual regulation."
And on the future of the women's movement, Ms. Chesler says, "Margaret Sanger saw that history moves in fits and starts. Each generation pushes ahead. There is a tradition of using women for political purposes, a long tradition of making an issue of family values." -- NANCY SHARKEY

PUB-DATE: June 29, 1992

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 2; National Desk

LENGTH: 2572 words

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN;
On the Economy, Bush Followed Reagan's Lead, Not His Success

SERIES: The Bush Record -- Fourth article of a series.

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 28

 BODY:
By most gauges, the economy has performed worse under President Bush than under any other President since World War II.
Economic growth has been slower, more businesses have failed, fewer jobs have been created and the national debt has soared to a record high. 
 The poor have been affected most. One American in 10 is now on food stamps, the highest proportion ever. But the middle class has suffered, too.
The average salaries of college graduates and of managerial and professional workers -- once thought to be immune from the recession cycle -- have been falling since 1989. And for the first time since the Great Depression, there has been no appreciable increase in the number of white-collar jobs. Experts in labor and industry report that the job market for this year's high school and college graduates is the bleakest in memory.
If Mr. Bush does not win re-election, it is safe to say, his handling of the economy will have been the main cause of his undoing.
But the President's advisers say none of this is his fault. Michael J. Boskin, the chairman of the Council of Economic Advisers, blames "structural imbalances" over which Mr. Bush had no control. Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady blames Congress for blocking Bush initiatives and the Federal Reserve Board for keeping interest rates too high for too long.
Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, says the price is finally being paid for the overindulgences of the 1980's, when Mr. Bush was Vice President.
For his part, Mr. Bush said in an interview with The New York Times last week that the recession lasted longer than it should have because Congress refused to go along with his proposals "to selectively stimulate the economy."
Mr. Bush and his observers also say that some of the economic news is actually quite good. Inflation seems to be under control, and short-term interest rates, the province of the Federal Reserve and not the Administration, are now the lowest in 20 years, having been cut time and again to stimulate the economy. The trade deficit has been reduced substantially from its peak in the middle 1980's. And in the last few months, at long last, the overall economy has seemed to be turning slowly around.
"I happen to think the economy is better than most of the people in America think," the President said.
But the political damage has already been done. Whoever's fault the recession was, Mr. Bush appeared slow to recognize the agony the sour economy was causing many Americans. And whatever lies ahead, the public has been unwilling to excuse him and believes he has been indecisive and insensitive.
The latest New York Times/CBS News poll last week showed that only 34 percent of adults approved of the way Mr. Bush was handling his job, and that only 16 percent liked how he was dealing with the economy -- a lower rating than even President Carter got at his worst moment.
That may be because for more than a year, with the economy deteriorating, Mr. Bush seemed detached -- if not without principle, then certainly without program -- and focused almost entirely on foreign policy. He had never prepared the public for bad news. He had never mentioned, for example, that a hangover might follow the high living of the Reagan years, or that cutting the crippling budget deficit would require more than a few painless nips and tucks.
As a consequence, when the hard times hit and when taxes were increased despite his pledge, the public was taken by surprise, and many were left with the impression that the President was not in charge or didn't understand.
 
Intentions
A 'Soft Landing' With No Hands?
 Ronald Reagan led the country through its longest period of peacetime economic expansion, and George Bush promised to pick up where his predecessor left off.
He committed himself to policies that he said would lead to strong growth in the economy and generate 30 million new jobs over the following eight years, twice the number created in the Reagan years. In one of the most memorable lines from any political convention speech, he declared, "Read my lips: no new taxes."
But once in office, rather than working for economic expansion, he accepted the view of his main advisers that he should favor a "soft landing" -- a period of slow growth that would permit an economy overheated from the Reagan years to cool off without crashing into a recession. Such careful calibration proved impossible, and after months of stagnation, the economy actually began to shrink in the summer of 1990.
Then that fall, in perhaps the political turning point of his term, the President agreed to a tax increase as the price for striking a deficit-reduction agreement with Congress.
Through all this, Mr. Bush, an economics major at Yale, often seemed disengaged from economic affairs. His closest advisers say the appearance was deceptive and actually resulted from a deep-seated view that the best role the Government can play in economic matters is to keep its hands off.
John H. Sununu, the White House chief of staff until last December, described the Bush philosophy this way in early 1991 in the depths of the recession: "The President feels very strongly that the free-market system operates best when it does not have its hands tied by Government, is not shackled by a system that erroneously thinks it can improve it by command and control."
In the interview, Mr. Bush was asked whether the Government should have been more actively helping American industries that are losing out to those abroad.
"I don't think we ought to have industrial planning," the President replied. "I do not want to see the Government pick winners and losers."
But others in the Administration and elsewhere in the Government say Mr. Bush simply chose to focus on his main interest, foreign affairs, and to leave economic policy to others.
 
Illusions
Debts Come Due From Fat Years
 Looking back, the prevailing view of economists is that the prosperity of the 1980's was somewhat illusory, much of it borrowed from the 1990's. Companies, households and most of all the Federal Government went deeply into debt, and the borrowing was for the most part used to finance day-to-day consumption rather than investments that would pay off in the future. When businesses, households and the Government began paying off their debt in the last few years, the economy began to sag.
What Mr. Boskin calls "structural imbalances" also weakened the economy. Among them were more taxes and less spending by states and localities that must operate with balanced budgets, lower military budgets because of the collapse of Communism, restricted lending because of weak banks and the savings-and-loan disaster. The collapse of the commercial real estate market, in part because of tax-policy swings in the 1980's, also played a role.
Many economists predicted that the Reagan bubble would burst. But if Mr. Bush and his economic advisers recognized these problems in his first years in office, they never let on. The President's public statements about the economy were consistently upbeat. Budgets projected strong economic growth, if not at once then clearly in the near future. Budget deficits were vastly underestimated. The magnitude of the savings-and-loan losses was repeatedly misjudged.
"He was trapped by the Reagan legacy," said Robert D. Hormats, vice chairman of Goldman Sachs International, who has worked for several Presidents. "Most Presidents can make changes when they come into office by blaming their predecessor. He couldn't do that."
The President's senior advisers, including Mr. Brady and Mr. Boskin, say even today that they do not know of anything significant they could have done differently. But if promised anonymity, others say that the President dawdled too long without addressing the economy.
 
All Attention Was on Persian Gulf
 "From an economic perspective and I would guess from a political perspective as well," said an Administration official trained in economics, "we would be better off today if Bush had gone to the people early on and said: 'Look, the party's over. We're going to have to tighten our belt now, and things will get better soon.' "
Instead, from early 1989 until the middle of 1990, the economy stagnated. With the President and Congress doing nothing to get a handle on the budget deficit, the Federal Reserve kept interest rates unusually high as a hedge against inflation. By the time Iraq invaded Kuwait in August 1990, the economy was in recession. But all attention was on the Persian Gulf; the Administration and most outside economists did not immediately spot the turn for the worse.
Meantime, the White House was engaged in a titanic struggle with Congress over the budget. The President insisted on a reduction in the capital-gains tax, a measure Democrats portrayed as a windfall for the wealthy. Democrats demanded higher taxes on the wealthy, a step the White House said would stifle the economy.
The argument raged for weeks. Time and again the President changed course to the point where the term "flip-flop" became a staple of newscasts and editorial cartoons.
In the end, the President lost the fight, substantively and politically. The capital-gains tax remained essentially unchanged. The income tax rate the wealthiest Americans paid was raised to 31 percent from 28 percent at Congress's insistence. And Democrats managed to portray Mr. Bush as the patron of fat cats.
Even with all that, while the overall economy has performed poorly, some of Mr. Bush's policies have received high marks from economists for their boldness.
He addressed the savings-and-loan debacle from his first days in office, pushing through Congress a plan that now shows signs of resolving the worst disaster in public finance since the Great Depression. At some political risk, the President also stuck to a free-trade policy. And the budget agreement, for all the criticism it has received, did hold Federal spending below what it otherwise would have been. It forced Congress to find an offsetting spending reduction or tax increase every time lawmakers wanted to provide more money for some program or other.
But in other areas Mr. Bush declined to take risks. Early last year, for example, he announced that overhauling the country's anachronistic banking system was his chief legislative priority. Yet after sending his proposal to Congress, Mr. Bush never raised a finger on its behalf, and the measure died.
 
Consequences
Defining Moment Of the Worst Kind
 The President understood from the outset that the budget agreement would damage him politically, said Secretary Brady, perhaps Mr. Bush's closest friend. "He said, You know, 'It was nice while it lasted.' "
Two days after he signed the legislation, Mr. Bush told reporters that he regretted breaking his campaign pledge on taxes and that he would never accede to a tax increase again. But politically he never recovered.
In an article in the spring issue of Policy Review magazine, Christopher C. DeMuth, president of the American Enterprise Institute, a public policy research center, explained why the change in tax policy was so politically damaging: "President Bush's reneging on his 'no new taxes' campaign pledge was the bellwether event of his Presidency, comparable to President Reagan's handling of the air traffic controllers' strike in 1981. Both actions had consequences far beyond the issues at hand because they were convincing signals to political allies and foes, bureaucrats and private citizens of how the President would respond to political pressures in a multitude of other circumstances."
Yet the war against Iraq, and the political euphoria that followed, masked for a time the political consequences of the weak economy and the change in tax policy. By the spring of 1991, the recession seemed to have bottomed out, and polls showed the President's popularity rating at stratospheric levels.
Then disaster struck. To the astonishment of the White House, the Federal Reserve Board and almost all private economists, the economic recovery that seemed so promising right after the war ground to a halt as spring turned to summer and summer to fall. The unemployment rate continued to rise.
The nature of this recession had misled economists. In a normal recession, the economy plunges in and then vaults out. This time, the country slithered into recession and is still trying to claw its way out.
Month after month, Mr. Bush shrugged off the economic bad news. But his popularity rating, nearly 90 percent right after the war, went into a nose dive -- into the 80's, then the 70's and so on in the monthly polls, until last month it dropped below 35 percent.
Mr. Bush's casual approach to economic woes reinforced the view being spread by his political opponents that he was out of touch. "The feeling was that everything was out of control, and nobody was doing anything," said Stuart M. Butler, director of domestic and economic policy studies at the Heritage Foundation, a conservative research institute.
 
In Retrospect, Miscalculations
 Mr. Bush's political advisers say he seemed blase about the economy for so long because the people counseling him on the economic situation made two serious misjudgments.
The first was that the raw data they were looking at did not reflect the public's deep concern about the future. Mr. Greenspan, for instance, said the mood in the country was much worse than conditions warranted. "There's a deep-seated concern out there, which I must say to you I have not seen in my lifetime," the Fed chairman told Congress. People, he continued, are worried "about whether the current generation will live as well as the previous one."
The second miscalculation was that the economic advisers did not grasp how this recession was peculiarly damaging to a Republican President. Circumstances were especially severe in the Northeast and California, where the television networks and many of the leading newspapers and magazines are located, so the economy received an unusual level of news coverage. And the people who were struggling were not just blue-collar workers, people who mainly vote Democratic anyway, but managers and professionals, employees of banks, investment firms, military contractors and real estate agencies -- the kind of voters who should be mainstays of the Bush constituency.
On the other hand, Mr. Bush was not prepared to cut taxes or raise spending, the usual responses to recessions, in the face of a budget deficit approaching $400 billion. And those were really the only weapons at his command.
Treasury Secretary Brady argues that the President deserves special credit for not responding irresponsibly in the face of enormous political pressure to break the budget agreement. "I think what you should give Bush credit for," Mr. Brady said, "was his extraordinarily prudent management of the economy."
But what Mr. Brady sees as prudence, others, like Terry Eastland, a Reagan Administration policy strategist who is now writing a book about Presidents and the Presidency, calls timidity.
"Both as a political matter and as a public-policy matter," Mr. Eastland said of Mr. Bush, "a successful President simply must take more risks than he does."
