PUB-DATE: June 29, 1992

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 4; National Desk

LENGTH: 2753 words

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Shaking the Schools; When Perot Took On Texas -- A Special Report.;
Education Initiative Revealed A Savvy and Abrasive Perot

BYLINE: By SUSAN CHIRA, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: AUSTIN, Tex., June 23

 BODY:
It was a tale of Texan proportions, complete with epic name-calling, massive lobbying and political skulduggery. It was Ross Perot's foray into the turbulent world of education reform -- and it offers a rare chance to examine how he operates in a political arena.
Mr. Perot proved he could learn quickly, sell bold policies to the public and ram them through a reluctant Legislature. But he also showed a taste for attention-grabbing invective that alienated some of the very people whose help he needed to change the state's education system. 
 In 1984, Texas adopted one of the most sweeping education laws in its history, paid for by its first state tax increase in 13 years. Although he must share credit with top state officials, it was Mr. Perot who led the charge. It was he who barnstormed the state in his own jet convening public hearings on Texas's schools. It was his biting one-liners that sold many Texans on the changes. It was his money that paid for one of the biggest lobbying efforts this state capital had ever witnessed.
 
Measure of Leadership
 Mr. Perot touts the $2.8 billion education package as proof that business methods, plain speaking and listening to people are indeed what it takes to forge policies, make changes and lead a nation. The law established a state preschool program for children with disadvantages, shifted school aid from rich to poor districts, reduced class sizes in early grades, toughened academic standards, mandated testing for teachers and raised teachers' salaries but linked raises to performance. And in a state where football reigned supreme, the law required students to pass all their courses or give up extracurricular activities like team sports.
To shake up education in Texas, Mr. Perot used the very methods he says he would apply as President to all national policies: Convene experts; hold extensive public meetings; decide on a policy; and beat back every attempt to compromise it.
Mr. Perot did not answer messages left at his headquarters requesting
comment on his role in the education law.
It is clear that for all Mr. Perot's protestations about the evils of political action committees and political insiders, he knew how to leverage his considerable influence and money to railroad opponents.
"It was like being in a sword fight with a pocket knife," said Eddie Joseph, executive director of the Texas High School Coaches Association. The coaches fought Mr. Perot bitterly on the law, along with most Texas teachers unions, the state board of education and several powerful legislators.
It is less certain whether Mr. Perot could have succeeded had he actually been in government. He did not have to worry about angering those whose support might be crucial for re-election. Some of his opponents say his acid tongue and occasional strong-arm tactics alienated many school insiders.
Ridiculing the widespread practice of making coaches principals, for example, Mr. Perot would ask all administrators to stand up during the public hearings he held across the state, recalled Becky Brooks, a teachers union lobbyist. Then he would ask those who were coaches to sit down. Nearly everybody sat, proving Mr. Perot's point, but humiliating coaches and many administrators.
Nor do observers agree on the lasting impact of the education law, although most believe it marked a dramatic step forward for Texas schools.
 
Texas Whirlwind
More Than State Had Bargained For
 The education law that Mr. Perot helped shape and pass sought far-reaching changes. It gave Texas a preschool program for poor and non-English-speaking children. It transformed school finances, the state's first step toward redressing huge gaps in spending between rich and poor school districts.
Teachers received raises, but had to pass a competency test to keep their jobs. The law set maximum class sizes of 22 in early grades and required high-school students to pass a proficiency test to graduate. The infamous "no pass, no play" rule took on some of Texas's most sacred institutions, including football and county fairs, by forbidding students who failed a course to participate in extracurricular activities.
Texas officials had not planned on legislation so sweeping. But Gov. Mark White had promised teachers raises, and he decided he needed an influential businessman to help persuade the Legislature to raise the taxes to pay for them. In June 1983 he asked Mr. Perot to head a Select Committee on Public Education.
 
9-Month Investigation
 Governor White got more than he had bargained for. Mr. Perot set the committee's agenda from the start, plunging it into a nine-month investigation of Texas schools. He paid education experts to testify before his committee, commissioned surveys of Texas teachers, held committee meetings in plush Dallas hotels with catered meals, flew an entourage of about 50 people around the state for public hearings and paid Thomas W. Luce, a prominent attorney and now head of his petition drive, to manage the effort.
Mr. Perot did not like what he found, and he told Texans so in blunt, uncompromising terms. Texas had world-class education in only a few areas, he said: "drill team, band and football." The state would no longer be able to count on oil to stay prosperous; it's children would need solid educations to compete in a high-technology world.
Texans did not care enough about education, he said. Schools spent money on towel warmers and multimillion-dollar stadiums. "Kids who can barely read and write are leaving school at noon to cook hamburgers so they can make payments on their cars," he told audiences. His signature anecdote was a story about the boy who had been absent from school 35 days so that he could exhibit his pet chicken at livestock shows.
But what got people talking was one state icon, Mr. Perot, taking on another: football, a game that defines Friday nights in Texas.
"It was very difficult to tell during the heat of that battle whether he was a hero or Public Enemy No. 1," said George Christian, a lobbyist who later worked for Mr. Perot.
Texans are still divided. Many who worked with him, and even some who fought him on the education bill, said he was a sympathetic listener, an engaging and convincing talker.
 
Visionary or Demagogue?
 Some people complain that he didn't budge in the end. "When he talks about his town meetings, maybe Ross Perot has a vision of how things ought to be settled pre-town meeting, and maybe he uses the town meeting to solidify that," suggested Ms. Brooks, then president-elect of the Texas State Teachers Association, which fought the bill.
Sonia Hernandez, then a high-school principal and president of a grass-roots group working on school-finance issues, remembers a different Perot. After he had angered Hispanic parents by criticizing bilingual education, she asked him to reconsider and visit her school. "A couple of weeks later, I got this phone call out of the blue: Mr. Perot's in his plane, and he's going to land shortly, and he'd like to see what you're talking about." He came, listened and modified his position.
Others, however, saw him as dictatorial, simplistic and inflammatory. His cutting remarks made implacable enemies, like the longtime chairman of the State Board of Education, whose portrait, Mr. Perot once suggested, "may glow in the dark."
He attacked courses such as drivers' education: "That's where they house the assistant coaches who are too dumb to teach regular classes."
Mr. Joseph of the coaches association said, "A lot of it was demagoguery."
Demagoguery or no, Mr. Perot's sound bite transfixed Texans, convincing many that their schools had to change. "That's where he came through, drawing up the final recommendations and selling them," said Camilla Bordie, who worked on the committee. "What was sold was the four or five main thrusts. You can kill the public with details, and he knows better than to do that."
 
Lobbying Tactics
Suffer No Fools, Take No Prisoners
 At the opening of the June 1984 special legislative session convened to consider education reform, it appeared that Mr. Perot's bill was in serious trouble. He had angered many of the state's most powerful political groups: teachers unions, vocational educators, coaches, the State Board of Education and wealthy school districts that feared a drop in state aid.
A member of the State Board of Education hosted a lavish barbecue for legislators at a lakeside retreat, with swimming, water skiing and volleyball. The message got through; a survey of legislators by a Texas newspaper when the session began showed only a few willing to vote for the bill.
In response, Mr. Perot unleashed a massive lobbying effort and drew on his influence in his home state. Mr. Perot loaned five of his own lobbyists and lawyers from Electronic Data Systems, his computer services company, to the effort, sent Mr. Luce to Austin to lead the charge, and hired three of the state's most influential lobbyists. He "hired anybody who was warm," recalled Bill Hobby, then Lieutenant Governor.
 
Inspired a Verse
 Mr. Perot even sent female employees from Electronic Data Systems to talk to legislators and help host parties -- a common practice and master stroke, Ms. Brooks of the teachers association says.
Mr. Hobby commemorated the E.D.S. women in a poem he wrote about the battle:
 
What with taxes and all,
Lobbyists were thick as roaches.
So it took all the Luce women
To win the war against coaches.
Mr. Perot helped forge a new alliance between traditional business groups and the state's growing high-technology industries, which were already demanding an improved education system. In the months before the session, he crisscrossed Texas addressing influential business groups: chambers of commerce, the Houston Board of Realtors, the Texas and Southwest Cattle Raisers Association. He videotaped his speech and sent it to places he did not visit personally.
"Mr. Perot did essentially what Reagan did; he went beyond the system and appealed to the people and sold them on the need for reform," said Jack Vowell, a Republican state legislator from El Paso who opposed the bill. "If pressure came to me to support the movement, it did not come from Mr. Perot; it came from my constituents, who felt like this was a good idea."
In the fight to move the bill through the Legislature, Mr. Perot, or at least the lobbyists he hired, proved to be masterful coalition builders. To pay for the expensive education wish-list, lawmakers would have to raise taxes for the first time in 13 years. Mr. Perot's allies chose to increase gasoline taxes, assuring the help of the powerful highway lobby because most of the tax revenues would be used for roads.
 
All or Nothing
 The same stubborn refusal to compromise that makes some people wary of Mr. Perot proved invaluable in forcing fundamental educational changes after years of failed efforts, veterans of Texas political battles say. He insisted that lawmakers pass all his recommendations or none of them.
"He did not permit the Legislature to cut it up in little pieces," said Craig Foster, executive director of the Equity Center, an association of poor school districts. "It shortcut all the usual nonsense. Leadership is bringing things to closure and getting on with it. That's what Ross Perot does better than anyone I've ever seen."
As influential as Mr. Perot was, he did not single-handedly turn around Texas education. Some of the most important political support and maneuvering came from Governor White, Lieutenant Governor Hobby and Gib Lewis, Speaker of the Texas House of Representatives. When the House Education Committee gutted the school bill in a daylong session, Mr. Lewis reportedly threatened to strip the chairman of his post unless he introduced a bill that incorporated Mr. Perot's recommendations.
Mr. Perot got much of the credit for the new law, but it was Governor White who paid a political price. He lost his re-election bid in 1986 -- a defeat he and others attributed to the anger of the powerful teacher unions and the coaches association.
 
A Mixed Legacy
Time Has Dimmed Legislation's Glory
 At the time, the legislation was hailed by education experts as a quantum leap forward for Texas schools. The mid-1980's were a time when sweeping education acts were being enacted by several states, including Arkansas under Gov. Bill Clinton and Tennessee under Gov. Lamar Alexander, now Secretary of Education. Frank Newman, president of the Education Commission of the States, which monitors school reform around the country, called Texas's law a "reasonably good example," although he believes that South Carolina's changes were probably the best in the nation.
Yet even eight years later, some of the changes enacted in Texas's schools seem visionary -- particularly in early childhood education, school finance and public attitudes about education.
Texas was ahead of its time in creating a state version of the Federal Head Start program for preschoolers. Educators continue to call for more emphasis on early childhood education, and Ms. Hernandez, now director of education for Gov. Ann W. Richards, says the impact of these programs in Texas has been enormous.
The law overhauled school financing, marking Texas's first real effort to address huge gaps in spending between rich and poor school districts.
Mr. Perot's support for the last two provisions -- whose proponents at the time were generally liberals -- shows his political eclecticism.
And his fighting words forever changed the political landscape in education. "A lot of people in their very own school districts started asking a lot of questions," said Ms. Bordie, who was on the committee staff. "A lot of the reforms that occurred were not the result of the Legislature so much as Perot highlighting the issue and making people pay more attention to what was going on in their own school."
The "no pass, no play" law infuriated many parents who felt that their children were being unfairly penalized, and it spawned a rush of lawsuits. But it also drove coaches to insure that their students got tutoring help, Ms. Hernandez said.
Mr. Perot lost one major battle. He wanted a permanently appointed State Board of Education, reasoning that an elected group would not take on powerful vested interests. The law did establish an appointed board for four years, but voters turned down a referendum that would have made it permanent. Mr. Cole says that defeat allowed many who opposed further changes to sabotage the law effectively.
 
Mixed Record of Success
 And the 1984 law led to only modest improvements in student achievement. "We've had some changes in attitude, but, regrettably, many of the schools look the same as in 1983," said John Cole, president of the Texas Federation of Teachers, the only union that supported the bill.
Scores did climb steadily on the tests the law required, and the dropout rate fell from 34 percent to 21 percent. But that is still relatively high, and in the 1989-90 school year 23 percent of high-school seniors could not pass an English and math test required for graduation.
Some of the legislated changes brought unforeseen problems, particularly in light of the deep recession that hit Texas after the law was passed. Limiting class sizes to 22 in the lower grades is still widely praised, but it forced school districts to hire more teachers, and it strained budgets. The school-finance system has been ruled unconstitutional three times in the last four years, twice because, despite the 1984 law, huge inequities remained between rich and poor school districts, and once because the remedy was financed with an illegal tax. It is still being challenged in the courts.
Senator Parker, Governor White and nearly every teacher in Texas say that the teacher competency test, administered once in 1986, was a big mistake. Fewer than 1 percent of teachers failed, but the experience insulted and angered them.
"He got crossways with teachers, and that could have been avoided," Senator Parker said.
Education experts now say that the Texas law, like others of those years, focused too much on rule-making and too little on setting goals for students and letting teachers help them achieve those goals any way that works.
"In hindsight, they look very outmoded," Mr. Newman said. "But they said: 'Attention! The old world of half-baked schools isn't good enough.' "

HEADLINE: New Direction for the New President In Manila: Away From Washington

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MANILA, June 26

 BODY:
A few weeks before Fidel V. Ramos was proclaimed the winner of the Philippine presidential election, Washington delivered a message to this former colony that many here interpreted as another slap from America: with the forced closing of the giant Subic Bay naval base, aid would be slashed by more than half, with more cuts likely.
That should not have come as a surprise, because Manila, trying to overcome objections to the huge United States military presence, had long characterized the assistance as a form of rent. But to many Filipinos, the drastic reduction was a telling symbol. 
 Indeed, the conventional wisdom here is that when Mr. Ramos takes office on Tuesday, he will not only be the first freely elected President in a generation, but he will also be the first who is forced to survive without the United States to back him up.
Mr. Ramos is frequently termed an "American boy" in the press here -- he is a graduate of West Point and has strong ties to the Pentagon -- and many people around him say his first challenge will be to shift the country more toward Asia.
He is expected to seek from his neighbors, especially Japan, the kind of benefits that once flowed from the country's closeness to the United States. But that will mean a wrenching readjustment, forcing a country that is perpetually preoccupied by economic crises and overpopulation to compete with some of the most successful economies in Asia.
 
Seeing Benefits of New Ties
 "Philippine relations with the United States are at an all-time low," said Leticia Ramos Shahani, Mr. Ramos's sister and a former ambassador who is head of the Philippine Senate's foreign affairs committee. "I think many people feel that the special relationship is gone. But closing this chapter may be good. It will make us less obsessed with the American relationship, make us look more to Europe and Japan, and force us to be more competitive."
Mr. Ramos says that the turn in relations has him concerned. Within the first 100 days of his Administration, he said at a recent news conference, he plans a "complete review" of relations with the United States.
"The economic and trade side of our relations also needs to be reviewed," he said. But so far he has carefully avoided offering any details.
Elected with only 23.5 percent of the vote, the former general has spent the last few weeks bringing old rivals aboard: He had a much-publicized dinner the other night with Imelda Marcos, who received about 10 percent of the vote in the presidential race. Mr. Ramos agreed in principle to allow the body of her husband, President Ferdinand E. Marcos, to be returned to the Philippines for burial.
 
Political Problems at Home
 Mr. Ramos is also drafting a plan to offer amnesty to both the Communist insurgents who have fought a guerrilla war with the Government for decades and to the right-wing military splinter groups that tried seven times to overthrow his predecessor, President Corazon C. Aquino.
Filipinos complain that while they expected the restoration of democracy under President Aquino would strengthen ties with Washington, the result has been just the opposite. With the Communist insurgency in the countryside losing strength, one Government official lamented the other day, "even the C.I.A. doesn't pay attention to us anymore."
For those here who viewed the dependence on the United States as part of the reason that the Philippines is the laggard of Southeast Asia, some benign neglect from America is overdue.
"The act of standing up and saying 'no' to a superpower was very important psychologically to Filipinos," Carolina Hernandez, a professor of political science at the University of the Philippines, said recently.
But it had its price. The Inquirer, a local newspaper, noted the other day, "It is apparent that the Philippines can no longer run to the United States for help after ejecting the bases."
There has been a detectable move in the Philippines to discover anew the country's Asian roots, beneath the endless string of fast-food outlets and striptease bars that are the most visible remnants of Americanization.
The latest trends in art and music here are toward the indigenous. The man elected Vice President under Mr. Ramos, Joseph Estrada, an economic nationalist who ran on a rival ticket, is best known as a former movie actor who endeared himself to the electorate by playing the role of a tough-guy common man fighting against oppression, and against the American presence here. Mr. Estrada's popularity stems partly from the fact that he talks to his constituents almost entirely in a Philippine language rather than in English.
United States officials here say that some of the sense of abandonment is exaggerated -- especially since it was the Philippines that decided to oust the American military -- and that fundamental ties remain strong.
"There may never be a day when the Philippines is under the kind of focus in America that it had in the 1930's and before," said Frank Wisner, who departed as American Ambassador a few weeks ago. "But there is still a lot to make this relationship special. The history has a dynamic to it," a continuity based on the two million to three million Filipino-Americans and Filipinos living in the United States.
Nonetheless, United States aid declined to $219 million this year, from $567 million last year, with the steepest cuts in military and development assistance and food programs.
Equally important, United States direct investment has fallen short of Philippine hopes. Though United States companies have invested billions here since the country became an American possession, today most seem focused on Eastern Europe and more promising opportunities elsewhere in Asia. Many executives say that kidnapping threats and the daily failures of the electricity-generating system make expansion particularly problematic.
As a result, Mr. Ramos's best hopes seem to be Japan and South Korea, which he hopes will be lured by low wages here. Japan is now the largest aid-giver to the Philippines, but Japanese companies say that the same troubles that scare away others are making them reluctant to build more than relatively small-scale projects.
Mr. Ramos's closest political ally, Jose de Vencia, says that the new Administration's first move is likely to be to offer the United States access -- but not basing rights -- to an airfield at Subic. Other Southeast Asian nations have recently offered similar rights, and even a small presence on Subic would be a gesture that Manila still wants Washington to play an active role in the security of the area.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Media;
Networks Planning Cutbacks in Convention Time

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH KOLBERT

 BODY:
After receiving abysmally low ratings in 1984 and 1988, the television networks are putting their collective foot down this summer and planning to cut their coverage nearly in half.
Although their plans are still under discussion, at this point no network is committed to more than one hour of prime-time broadcasting each night from the Democratic convention, which begins July 13. Network executives say, however, that they expect to have somewhat more coverage on the final night, when the nominee gives his acceptance speech. 
 Four years ago, the three major networks were already restricting convention broadcasts, but each still carried at least two hours nightly in prime time. As recently as eight years ago, it was common for networks to pre-empt all programs from 8 P.M. to 11 P.M. on convention nights. In earlier decades, the networks broadcast as much as 12 hours of a convention in single day.
"We are driven by a desire to have the amount of coverage have some bearing on the news value," Lane Venardos, director of CBS News's special events unit, said, referring to the cuts planned for this year. "Increasingly, the parties have squeezed every ounce of controversy and potential news out of the conventions to provide a seamless event for their candidates. They've ceased to become news events, and have become theater."
 
Sign of New Era
 The cuts in network broadcasts of the conventions are another sign that the 1992 campaign, which in recent weeks has been waged mostly on the talk-show circuit, is ushering in a new era of political reporting. For more complete convention coverage this summer, viewers will be able to turn to C-Span and the Cable News Network, both of which were in their infancy 10 years ago.
"We're probably a throwback to what the networks always did," said Tom Hannon, CNN's political director, adding, "Certainly it's not unpleasant for us to think they've abandoned the field."
Underlying the networks' concerns about the conventions' newsworthiness is a concern about the bottom line; at a time of slumping profits, they can ill-afford to pre-empt money-making shows for expensive broadcasts of often tedious speeches.
In addition, ratings for the convention have been sagging. The 1980 Democratic convention in New York City received an average rating of 9 points for all three networks combined, while the 1988 Democratic convention in Atlanta received an average rating only 6.6, according to Nielsen Media Research. By comparison, the program that received the top Nielsen rating last week received 16.9 points. One rating point is equivalent to 1 percent of all the country's households that have televisions.
As the networks have cut convention broadcasts, the rise of cable television news has made it easier for them, removing any civic obligation they once felt to carry the conventions gavel to gavel.
"We've been able to take the networks off the hook," Brian Lamb, the chief executive of C-Span, said. "These conventions are very low-rated. There's no drama; we know exactly what's going to happen. They're made to order for what we do."
Indeed, C-Span viewers need not miss a minute of the coming conventions, with the cable network planning 18 hours of live reports each day. CNN is planning to start its reporting of the Democratic convention in New York City at 8 P.M., and to follow each nightly session until it ends.
In an unusual co-operative effort, the Public Broadcasting System is teaming with NBC to provide prime-time broadcasts of the conventions on PBS. NBC reporters will appear together with correspondents for "The MacNeil/Lehrer Newshour" on PBS stations from 8 P.M. to 10 P.M. Then the two teams will split up, with PBS continuing its broadcast, and NBC doing an hourlong prime-time program.
 
Plans Are Not Complete
 The arrangement will give PBS the benefit of NBC's resources and give NBC the chance to showcase its reporters without pre-empting prime time. The two plan to continue the arrangement for the Republican Convention, which starts on Aug. 17 in Houston.
"Given the realities of network economics, we weren't likely to get evening-long coverage on our own network," Bill Wheatley, executive producer of NBC's election unit, said. "So we thought it would be great if we were to have a partner."
Neither CBS nor ABC has announced its final plans for the conventions yet. But officials for both networks said that the prime time programs would be less than four years ago, and that they were certain only of broadcasting one hour of the convention proceedings each night. On Tuesday, July 14, CBS will be broadcasting the baseball All-Star Game.
Jeff Gralnick, executive producer of ABC's election reporting, said, "We'll be on at least from 10 to 11."
On the final night of the convention, all the networks are expected to begin broadcasting by at least 9:30 P.M.
Officials at the campaign of Gov. Bill Clinton insist that they are not concerned about the network cuts, saying they will be balanced by increases in local and cable television reports.
 
Some Loss, Some Gain
 "What you're losing on the network side, you're gaining on the local side," said Laura Quinn, the Clinton campaign's convention spokeswoman.
In anticipation of the networks' limited broadcasts, the Democratic National Committee has set up its own studio and editing rooms to provide tape of the convention by satellite to local stations. The Republicans have provided such satellite reports of its conventions since 1984.
Because of the restrictions on network time, Ms. Quinn said, the Democrats are trying to plan the convention program more painstakingly this year. She predicted, though, that if an important speaker took the podium when the networks were not planning to broadcast from the convention, they would change their plans.
"If Mario Cuomo starts talking at 9:30, it's hard to imagine them not tuning in," she said.
In the early years of television, the political conventions were a major broadcasting event that spurred sales of television sets and drew new viewers. But as the primaries grew in importance, the significance of the conventions waned, and by the early 1970's, many news executives say, the conventions had become dull viewing.
 
A Steady Decline
 "From '72 on, the networks tried to get rid of them and couldn't," said Reuven Frank, former president of NBC News. The national parties had become accustomed to the free television exposure, he said, and the networks, which are subject to licensing by Federal agencies, were loath to offend them.
"Licensed businesses," Mr. Frank said, "are gutless."
Since 1972, though, the number of hours the networks have devoted to convention reporting has steadily declined.
Twenty years ago, NBC and CBS each carried 36 hours of the Democratic convention, while ABC carried 19. By 1980, CBS and NBC's broadcasts of the Democratic gathering had dropped to 20 hours each, and ABC's to 10 hours. In 1988, the three networks each carried between 10 and 12 hours of the Democratic convention.
Following a similar pattern, average hours of viewership dropped from 5.8 hours per household during the 1972 Democratic convention to 2.8 hours during the 1988 convention.

HEADLINE: Ingeniero Jacobacci Journal;
Old Patagonian Express Puff, Puff, Puffs to Its End

BYLINE: NATHANIEL C. NASH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: INGENIERO JACOBACCI, Argen tina

 BODY:
The American travel writer Paul Theroux described it as "a kind of demented samovar on wheels, with iron patches on its boiler and leaking pipes on its underside and dribbling valves and metal elbows that shot jets of vapor sideways."
This rail relic, a steam engine that crosses 250 desolate miles of southern Argentina, is still running, a vital transportation link for a few thousand sheep farmers and Mapuche Indians, 12 years after the Theroux best seller, "The Old Patagonian Express," was published. It also employs several hundred workers 
 But the changing economic policies that have swept Latin American capitals, and a rush by Argentina to sell state companies, is about to doom this unusual piece of British technology. Its demise could mean the end also of the town of Ingeniero Jacobacci, named after one of the original railroad pioneers, and about a dozen other settlements from here to the town of Esquel.
The Government of President Carlos Saul Menem has asked for bids from investors who would like to buy the three state railroads that run through this province, Rio Negro. So far, it has received bids for only one of those lines, and not one for the Old Patagonian Express. And the Government has said that by the end of July it will shut the railroad down.
 
The Prospects Aren't Good
 Losses of $1 million a year may justify its passing in the minds of policy makers in Buenos Aires, some 1,000 miles to the north. But the tough Argentines who live along its track see this as just another in a series of blows to the economy and heritage of Patagonia, a land of stark landscapes that has inspired writers and attracted hardy British and South African settlers. Some outlaws have been drawn here, too, including Butch Cassidy and the Sundance Kid, who lived in Patagonia for years, out of the reach of United States justice.
"Without a doubt, this is an unprofitable railroad," said Jorge Ferreira, assistant to Governor Horacio Masaccessi. "We are trying to persuade Buenos Aires to give us more time to find a buyer. But the prospects are not good."
The truth is, the railroad is not called the Old Patagonian Express; the name was coined by Mr. Theroux. It is far from an express, making not only its 14 assigned stops but also numerous others as passengers jump out here and there.
Argentines call it the Trochita, a word that refers to the line's narrow gauge. This means that the engines, tank cars and passenger coaches are about one-quarter the size of normal railroad cars, making the Trochita seem more like a toy train.
On every trip, locals crowd the rickety wooden benches, drinking yerba mate, the hot Argentine drink, and huddling around the tiny wood-burning stoves in each car as the Trochita jolts on the 14-hour trip south to Esquel.
Though antiquated, it is a symbol of how important British technology was to the building of Patagonia's economy in the early part of this century. When work on the line started in 1922, it was decided that a full-scale railroad would be nearly impossible in this rugged area full of thorn bushes, sheep, foxes, guanaco -- the Argentine version of the llama -- and the ostrichlike rhea.
The railroad's golden days were after World War II, when the vast sheep ranches flourished, producing huge amounts of wool that the little boxcars would take up to Ingeniero Jacobacci, there to connect with the full-scale railroad line to the coast.
But as Buenos Aires became more and more a hub of economic activity and wool prices fell, the regional economy began to weaken. A paved road was put in along the northern border of Rio Negro, diverting large amounts of cargo. Soon the 14 hours it takes Trochita to go from Esquel to Ingeniero Jacobacci started to seem too long.
The industrial group owned by Amalia Fortabat, a close friend of Mr. Menem and perhaps Argentina's richest person after inheriting a vast fortune from her late husband, has offered to buy the profitable rail line that runs along Rio Negro's northern border. But efforts to have her buy the Trochita and the full-sized rail line running from San Carlos de Bariloche, a ski and lake resort, to Ingeniero Jacobacci and on to the coast have failed.
Ingeniero Jacobacci's Mayor, Edgardo Bujaryisqui, says that if this town of 6,000 inhabitants loses both railroad lines, there will be very little economic reason for its existence. He sees the Government's determination to shut down the little train as just another sign of its lack of concern for development outside of Buenos Aires.
"There is no one in Buenos Aires who talks of serious development in the interior of the country," Mr. Bujaryisqui said. "To them, we don't exist."
About the only hope he holds out is that investors will be persuaded that the Trochita has historical and tourist value. In the summer months, one station manager said, three out of four passengers are tourists, eager to ride the line before it dies.
Mr. Bujaryisqui's plan is to have tourists come from San Carlos de Bariloche by full-sized train, board the tiny train for Esquel, a town of 25,000, and return to San Carlos de Bariloche by bus.
"We think that is the only way to save the railroad," Mr. Bujaryisqui said. "This is one of the most unique train rides in the world in one of the most unique places in the world. People are always awed by Patagonia."

Crews prepared the train for its 14-hour, 250-mile run from Ingeniero Jacobacci to Esquel. (Nathaniel C. Nash/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: CONFLICT IN THE BALKANS;
Mitterrand Trip Welcomed Only in Paris and Sarajevo

BYLINE: By ROGER COHEN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PARIS, June 28

 BODY:
President Francois Mitterrand returned today from a daring six-hour visit to Sarajevo to face the dismay of Germany and his other European partners, the praise of most French people and the prospect of further friction in his country's strained relations with Washington.
Partly political theater and partly a statement that action is possible, the French leader's brief foray appeared less a major development in the search for peace in the Balkans than a boon to Mr. Mitterrand's standing at home. 
 President Mitterrand, who was whisked past bullet-riddled buildings to a meeting with the President of Bosnia and Herzegovina, Alija Izetbegovic, characterized his visit as a purely humanitarian gesture rather than a move to upstage the European Community or the United Nations in their efforts to end the war.
 
Broader Political Picture
 It seemed clear, however, that he was trying to make a point to Washington about Europe's capacity for action at a time when France believes that United States influence is destined to decline on a continent no longer divided by the cold war.
"There's a lot of emotion about Yugoslavia in the United States, and President Bush has echoed this," said European Affairs Minister Elisabeth Guigou. "But a lot has been said there about possible action, only to come to nothing. We in France prefer to be more sober and act."
Senior Foreign Ministry officials have been particularly dismissive of statements by Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d, suggesting they were too little too late.
 
Annoyed at Baker
 According to Le Monde, a letter sent by Mr. Baker to European leaders at their meeting in Lisbon this weekend particularly annoyed Paris. The letter expressed outrage at the "barbarity" in Sarajevo and called for Serbia's exclusion from the United Nations.
"France opposed this letter, judging its message ineffectual, even harmful, in that it is important to maintain some sort of dialogue with Serbia," Le Monde wrote in a passage it hinted had originated in the President's entourage.
Foreign Ministry officials have pressed the view that Europe should lead the search for a solution to a European problem, but Mr. Mitterrand's visit hardly seemed an advertisement for united European action. Other countries, particularly Germany, reacted testily to a trip of which they had been told nothing, despite the fact that Mr. Mitterrand left directly from the meeting in Lisbon.
 
Germany Is Annoyed
 Close French-German ties are increasingly at the heart of Europe's drive for unity in foreign and economic policy, and President Mitterrand may have sacrificed this special relationship for a spectacular gesture whose efficacy remains obscure.
German officials said they were particularly irked because France, an ally of Serbia since World War I, when they both opposed the Austro-Hungarian empire, has been the most reluctant to act against the Serb-dominated Government in Belgrade.
Only at Lisbon did Mr. Mitterrand explicitly refer to the Serbs as the aggressors and agree to a strong statement in which the community said it did not exclude recourse to military measures to insure that humanitarian aid reaches Sarajevo.
British officials also expressed outrage, but Foreign Secretary Douglas Hurd sought to put a good face on things, commenting that, "It's a brave act by an elderly president." Mr. Mitterrand is 75 years old.
With French television repeatedly showing films of the President being cheered in Sarajevo, it was clear that Mr. Mitterrand had gained a significant domestic triumph. His visit coincided with the 78th anniversary of the assassination of Archduke Franz Ferdinand of Austria, whose killing in Sarajevo contributed decisively to the start of World War I.
"Europe's honor had to be saved and the President achieved this," a political analyst, Gerard Carreyrou, said in summing up the reaction in France.
Among leading politicians, only former President Valery Giscard d'Estaing voiced criticism. "We cannot at one and the same time declare that we want a common foreign policy in Europe, and then undertake such isolated action without even telling our partners," he said.
With a crucial referendum on European union due in the autumn, President Mitterrand needed the boost his visit seems to have provided. Because he has made European integration the cornerstone of his second seven-year term, which is to end in 1995, a rejection of union could spell the end of Mr. Mitterrand's presidency.
 
But Was It Effective?
 Beyond such domestic considerations, however, it was not apparent what President Mitterrand had achieved. Officials suggested that he had at last opened the door to a relief effort.
In an apparent effort to underscore the point, two French Air Force transport planes, each loaded with 6.5 tons of food and medicine, took off for Sarajevo as Mr. Mitterrand left. But officials at the Defense Ministry said later that the planes would go to the Croatian port of Split before proceeding to Sarajevo on Monday.
Bernard-Henri Levy, the French philosopher, said the President's decision to undertake his mission stemmed from a meeting he had had with Mr. Mitterrand last week in which he had handed over a message from the Bosnian President.
Mr. Levy, who recently visited Sarajevo, said Mr. Izetbegovic compared the situation of the 300,000 people in the city to that of the Jews in the Warsaw ghetto.

HEADLINE: METRO DIGEST

 BODY:

METRO DIGEST
 
NEW YORK CITY
 
GAY PARADE BOTH FESTIVE AND SOMBER
Walking arm-in-arm, thousands of gay and lesbian marchers streamed down Fifth Avenue for the annual Gay Pride parade, a celebration of sexual freedom and tolerance. The mood was at once festive and somber. Some cheering and laughing marchers mocked themselves and stereotypes of their life styles. Others appealed for help in the fight against AIDS. B3.
 
CONVENTION CONTENTION
The Democratic Party, traditionally the party of organized labor, has been caught in an unexpected labor dispute as it prepares for the convention at Madison Square Garden. A four-day standoff kept power cables from being hooked into the television networks' trailers outside the Garden on schedule last week. B3.
 
20 YEARS AFTER THE KNAPP COMMISSION
As another scandal erupts over police corruption in New York City, the alumni of the Knapp Commission held a 20th reunion of the investigation aimed at killing graft and in many ways remaking an entire police department. One man who did not attend was David Durk, who persuaded a fellow detective, Frank Serpico, to go forward with the allegations that led to the creation of the Knapp Commission. But he has always been a loner anyway. Metro Matters. B3.
 
QUEENS CELEBRATES ITSELF
An estimated two million people gathered under turquoise skies and marshmallow clouds to celebrate the 15th annual Queens Festival in Flushing Meadows-Corona Park this weekend. As the smell of Ecuadorean empanadas, Italian zeppoli and Japanese bean-curd cakes were melded by the vibrating sounds of calypso, steel drums and Korean wedding music, it was not hard to tell that this year's theme was "Ethnic Pride and Cultural Diversity." B3.
 
DIPLOMAS DESPITE BARRIERS
Like most high school graduations, the one at the Upper Manhattan Outreach Center was a mix of elation over plans for the future and nostalgia for bygone days and friends. But there was something more. The 97 graduates of this alternative school shared a special pride: most of them had had problems in other schools and risked dropping out altogether. The school, started in 1980 to help young people who cannot make it elsewhere, is one of six such centers operated by the New York Board of Education. The centers have turned out 4,500 students over the past decade by adhering to the belief that "small is better." B2.

SUSPECT'S HELP CRACKS CASE
Investigators said they were finally able to find the body of Sidney J. Reso when one of the prime suspects in the kidnapping of the Exxon official, Irene Seale, agreed to cooperate. After 26 years of marriage, the influence of her husband, Arthur Seale, the other suspect being held, waned rapidly under the pressure of incarceration and the prospect of a lifetime behind bars. B5.
 
BUDGET SHOWDOWN IN NEW JERSEY
A Republican Senator said that he would not support his party's effort to override Governor Florio's veto of the Legislature's $14.6 billion budget, instead urging a compromise that the Governor had suggested. The decision by Senator Leonard T. Connors Jr. appears to leave the Republicans in the Senate one vote short of the two-thirds majority needed to override the veto. B5.

In the fight over his ambitious plan for a state takeover of all Medicaid costs, Gov. Mario M. B5.
 
SOARING AHEAD TO YESTERDAY
Thanks in part to its no-frills approach, Sussex Airport has weathered economic pressures that have shut down 5 of the state's 55 public airports in the last five years and pushed many others to the brink. Thanks are also due some of the airport's great characters, like its senior flight official, 79-year-old Wild Bill Voorhis. B4.

HEADLINE: Cuomo Is Tying L.I. Fiscal Bailouts to His Plan for Medicaid

BYLINE: By SAM HOWE VERHOVEK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, June 28

 BODY:
In the fight over his ambitious plan for a state takeover of all Medicaid costs, Gov. Mario M. Cuomo has added a major new weapon: he is increasingly linking the proposed fiscal bailouts for Nassau and Suffolk Counties to lawmakers' approval of the Medicaid plan.
The Republican-led State Senate is desperately seeking the local bailouts to avoid the likely alternative, huge property-tax increases that would enrage Long Island voters, who are crucial to the Republicans' continued hold on the Senate. 
 But the Senate majority leader, Ralph J. Marino, a Long Island Republican, has also been hostile to the Governor's Medicaid plan, which if fully put into effect would shift both billions of dollars in local tax revenues and billions of dollars in Medicaid costs to the state government.
The Democratic Governor has repeatedly made comments in recent days that suggest he would frown on the county bailouts, which involve new deficit-financing authority and increases in the county sales tax, as long as lawmakers refused to go along with an idea that he said would save the same counties money. The bailout plans are worth $91 million to help close the deficit in Suffolk County's $1.3 billion budget, and $100 million for Nassau's $1.8 billion budget.
In a telephone interview today, the Governor said, "How can you say to me that you're willing to let them borrow money because they're desperate, and then refuse our offer to give them millions and millions of dollars in Medicaid pickup?"
Mr. Cuomo has cast his Medicaid plan as a "win-win" program for local governments because, he says, New York City's and most counties' Medicaid costs are rising faster than the tax revenues they would give up.
The plan will be a plus for state leaders too, Mr. Cuomo argues, if they can succeed in working with him to drive down health-care costs once the state has full control of Medicaid. Republican leaders are skeptical, and many contend that the true cost of the takeover could be state tax increases in future years.
Mr. Cuomo vigorously denied that he would "start hostaging, or make it some quid pro quo" on the two issues, and he said he would never blanketly refuse to go along with the Long Island bailouts unless he got the Medicaid plan as well.
"I am generally opposed to hostaging," the Governor said. "I am generally in support of logical connections."
Whether the Governor will actually be willing to risk an all-out fight to secure his Medicaid plan remains to be seen, of course. After leading many lawmakers and lobbyists in Albany to believe that he would not sign redistricting bills this year unless they were accompanied by stringent new limits on campaign contributions and broad revisions of the state's election laws, Mr. Cuomo wound up settling for what was widely regarded as a tame package of changes.
But one official in the Democratic-controlled Assembly said today that on the bailout issue the Governor was trying to play the Senate's vulnerability for all it was worth.
"This is one of the very, very few times that the Senate wants something that the Governor can hold up and that doesn't hurt us," the official said. "Therefore, when the Senate says no to him on everything he cares about, when they say no to him six times in a row, he can say, 'Why should I give you this?' That's his game plan."
The Senate has said no to plenty of things the Governor wants, including a bond act that he says would help revive the state's economy and an environmental trust fund financed in part from taxes on beer and soda containers.
The Republican opposition stems in large part from the fears of several senators that they could get hammered by their opponents in this fall's elections for supporting "tax and borrow" policies. An unusually high number of state senators are facing potential primary challenges from Republican Assembly members who, because their party is out of power in that chamber, have always been able to vote against tax increases.
Now, however, the Republicans may have to weigh their desire for the bailout plans and reconsider their opposition to the Cuomo proposals.
The Governor said that he had serious concerns about the county borrowing in any event and that Long Island could slide down the same path that led to New York City's fiscal crisis in the 1970's.
"That is one of the most dangerous, and in New York City's case one of the most lethal, practices and should be shunned wherever possible," he said.
But Mr. Marino's spokesman, John E. McArdle, said Mr. Cuomo was holding the bailout plans "hostage to his entire political agenda."

HEADLINE: Japan's Top Party Agrees To Give Economy a Push

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Monday, June 29

 BODY:
Japan's ruling party agreed over the weekend on a broad plan to stimulate the nation's sagging economy through increased Government spending and other measures worth more than $45 billion for the current fiscal year.
The plan, agreed to on Saturday, represents in part a response to calls from the United States and other nations that Japan accelerate its economic growth to help the world recover from the recession. 
 Prime Minister Kiichi Miyazawa is expected to come under pressure to announce plans to jump-start Japan's economy when he meets with President Bush in Washington this week and with the leaders of the Group of Seven leading industrialized nations in early July in Munich.
The Prime Minister said this morning that he would take the plan seriously and had already discussed it briefly by telephone with President Bush, according to NHK, Japan's broadcasting company.
 
Tentative Measures
 An increase in Japan's public spending could also increase the nation's imports and cut into a bulging trade surplus that is again threatening to make Japan a target of world anger.
The plan put forth by the Liberal Democratic Party is not in final form and could change by fall, when the specific steps and monetary amounts are to be determined.
As it stands, the plan calls for more spending on projects like water and sewage and waste-disposal, increased Government support for science and technology, the establishment of a fund for environmental projects, and incentives for small and medium-size businesses.
Parts of the Japanese Government, particularly the Finance Ministry, have opposed increased spending. But because the Liberal Democrats have tight control over the Government, their plan is expected to have great influence over what finally emerges.
A spokesman for the Finance Ministry said that no final decision had been reached and that the ministry had no immediate comment on the Liberal Democratic Party plan. He said an important unresolved issue is how such extra spending would be financed.
 
Pressures From Business, Too
 Japan had been expected to announce extra spending for the current fiscal year, with estimates of the supplemental budget ranging from 2 trillion yen to 10 trillion yen. Liberal Democratic Party officials, while not specifying the size of the supplemental budget agreed to on Saturday, said the aim for the overall stimulative package should be 6 trillion to 7 trillion yen, or about $47 billion to $55 billion.
In addition to feeling pressure from abroad, the Government is also being urged by businesses here to spur growth and help the stock market, which is at a six-year low. The Liberal Democratic Party in particular wants to announce such pump-priming measures before the election in the upper house of Parliament a month from now.
Japanese officials have said they want to stimulate the nation's economy through domestic spending to avoid setting off trade tensions that would arise if Japanese companies tried to fight the slump by increasing exports.
 
Debate Over Methods
 "It is important to grow though expansion of domestic demand," Sozaburo Okamatsu, director-general for international trade policy at the Ministry of International Trade and Industry, said in a news conference on Thursday. Already, because of diminished imports, Japan's trade surplus nearly doubled in May from a year earlier and its surplus with the United States rose by about 50 percent.
Still, there has been tremendous debate within the Government about what to do. The powerful Finance Ministry has been resisting a big fiscal stimulus, worrying that increased spending would add to the national debt, especially since tax revenues are expected to drop this year because of weak economic conditions.
Before deciding what to do next, Mr. Okamatsu said, the Government will wait to see the results of a package of stimulative measures taken in late March and early April. At that time the Government decided to push most of the public works spending for the fiscal year into the first half of the year, and the Bank of Japan cut the official discount rate to 3.75 percent.
Given the internal debate, Mr. Okamatsu said Prime Minister Miyazawa was not planning to discuss specific figures for the pump-priming measures when he meets with Mr. Bush and the other world leaders.
But now, with the presentation of the plan by the Liberal Democratic Party, it is not clear whether Mr. Miyazawa may be more specific in his promises to other world leaders.

HEADLINE: Officials Say Body in Forest Is Sidney Reso

BYLINE: By ROBERT HANLEY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NEWARK, June 28

 BODY:
The decomposing body unearthed from a shallow grave in a southern New Jersey state forest Saturday night was identified today as that of Sidney J. Reso, the Exxon executive kidnapped outside his home two months ago.
The authorities said that Mr. Reso had been shot in one arm and that he appeared to have died shortly after being abducted on April 29. They said the cause of his death had not been officially determined, and they refused to speculate on whether the gunshot wound played a part. 
 Mr. Reso's body was found about 8 P.M. Saturday in a secluded corner of the Bass River State Forest, about 20 miles north of Atlantic City. It had been buried about three feet beneath dense underbrush.
 
Led by a Suspect
 The discovery ended an eight-week search that F.B.I. officials today described as the most intensive kidnapping investigation since the abduction of Patty Hearst in 1974. One of the suspects in the kidnapping, Irene J. Seale, told Federal authorities of Mr. Reso's death and then helped a team of 40 law-enforcement officials find the grave Saturday morning, an official close to the investigation said.
The Morris County Prosecutor, W. Michael Murphy, said at a news conference today that he planned to file a felony murder charge in the case on Monday. He refused to say whether he would charge both Mrs. Seale and the other suspect in the case, her husband, Arthur, or just one of them. Arthur Seale, an ex-police officer, is a former security official at Exxon.
Michael Chertoff, the United States Attorney for New Jersey, declined to speculate at the news conference when and where Mr. Reso died. In New Orleans, where Mr. Reso grew up, the wife of his cousin Jerome Reso said that Mrs. Seale had told the authorities that Sidney Reso died on May 3, four days after the abduction.
Mr. Chertoff also refused to discuss any negotiations or legal deals that might have prompted Mrs. Seale to tell authorities about the death and the body.
But he seemed incensed at news reports that the 57-year-old man may have died of natural causes. "Nobody can say that a man who dies in captivity -- having been abducted from his family and having been held against his will -- died of natural causes," he said. "I cannot imagine causes that are less natural than the causes that apply in this case."
New Jersey's Medical Examiner, Dr. Robert Goode, began an autopsy at the state morgue here this morning and was to continue the examination on Monday, officials said.
Law-enforcement officials speculated, pending completion of Dr. Goode's autopsy, that Mr. Reso may have had a heart attack, perhaps fatal, during captivity. He suffered a heart attack three years ago, said Gary Penrith, the head of the Federal Bureau of Investigation's New Jersey office. Mr. Penrith said he understood that Mr. Reso was not required to take prescribed medicine for his condition, but took a daily aspirin as a blood thinner.
The search team arrived at the forest with Mrs. Seale about 8 A.M. on Saturday and scoured the wooded terrain for several hours before locating the grave, with the assistance of a state police search dog, in the late afternoon, said the official close to the investigation. Mr. Reso's remains were removed about 8 P.M.
The body of Mr. Reso, who was president of Exxon's international division, was positively identified today, Mr. Penrith said. He called the death "unbelievably despicable."
In a statement today, Exxon's chairman, Lawrence G. Rawl, said Mr. Reso had made an "invaluable contribution" to the company in his 35-year career. "Sid was one of the finest men I have known who combined outstanding professional competence with humanity and warmth," Mr. Rawl said.
 
Describing Murder Charges
 Mr. Reso vanished about 7:30 A.M. on April 29, moments after leaving home for the 10-minute drive from his home in Morris Township to his office in Florham Park. His car, its engine idling, was found at the foot of his 250-foot driveway.
The Seales, both 45 and unemployed, were arrested about 1 A.M. on June 19, after an aborted attempt to collect an $18.5 million ransom for Mr. Reso. Through a series of telephone calls and letters, the kidnappers led an F.B.I. team on a four-hour drive through four towns in Morris and Somerset Counties. The string of communications broke off abruptly after a rented Oldsmobile that other agents had been following near Far Hills, N.J., encountered a roadblock -- which was not related to the Reso case -- and drove off, officials have said.
The Seales were arrested about an hour later at a car depot in Hackettstown, N.J., where Mrs. Seale had rented the Oldsmobile on June 18.
The Federal Government has charged the Seales with kidnapping, extortion, conspiracy, using the mail to transmit ransom letters and interstate travel in aid of extortion.
State charges of kidnapping, extortion and conspiracy have also been filed by Mr. Murphy's office. The felony murder charge Mr. Murphy described applies when a crime victim dies during the commission of a felony.
The indictment Mr. Chertoff filed against the Seales on Thursday asserted that others had joined the couple in the kidnapping. No other suspects have been identified. But Mr. Chertoff vowed today to press on with the investigation.
"I promise that we will not in any way abate or relax our efforts until we have successfully completed the investigation," he said.
For Mr. Reso's wife, Patricia, the discovery of his body ends an anguishing 59-day vigil she kept at their home in Morris Township. She has said she prayed daily for his safe return and kept a journal that, she said, enabled her to speak directly to her husband.
 
2 Televised Appeals
 Twice during the 59 days, Mrs. Reso made televised appeals for the kidnappers to free her husband. The first, on May 15, came in response to a message the kidnappers left a day earlier on a light pole at the Rockaway Townsquare Mall in Morris County, officials have said.
If the authorities are right, the kidnappers' demand came even though they knew that Mr. Reso was dead.
Mrs. Reso's second appeal came June 16, the same day the kidnappers directed F.B.I. agents to a letter in Morris County that gave instructions for bundling the ransom -- $18.5 million in old $100 bills -- into laundry bags, officials have said.
In this appeal, Mrs. Reso said she was praying that her husband would be freed in time for Father's Day, June 21.
Mrs. Reso remained secluded at her home today.

HEADLINE: SUSSEX AIRPORT JOURNAL;
Where Wild Blue Yonder Is Untamed

BYLINE: By JACQUES STEINBERG

DATELINE: SUSSEX, N.J.

 BODY:
TO imagine what this town's 47-year-old airport looks like, cast aside visions of sleek glass terminals, snarled parking lots and ominous jumbo jets.
This is a country airport.
The 150 small planes based here are arrayed in long rows on an open grass field near a meadow where horses graze. The "lounge" where recreational pilots spend hours swapping war stories, sipping coffee or just gossiping has no walls, just a soda machine, a ceiling bulging with exposed insulation and three fraying couches that spent their better years in private homes. 
 And then there's Wild Bill. A Navy flight trainer during World War II, Wild Bill Voorhis is Sussex Airport's senior flight instructor. He is 79 years old, and though the years have taken a few of his teeth, they have not diminished his enthusiasm --
or his sense of humor.
"We got a joke around here," he said on a recent Sunday afternoon between short hops with anyone who happened to drop by for a ride or lesson. "When I'm walking out to the plane, all the guys sitting around here yell, 'Hey Bill, did you take your heart pills today?' They do it just to scare the people."
Thanks in large part to its no-frills approach, Sussex Airport has weathered economic pressures that have shut down 5 of the state's 55 public airports in the last five years and pushed many others to the brink. Those stresses -- including high liability insurance, escalating property taxes and a fall-off in the production of small planes -- have tempted several airport owners to close down and sell their land to developers.
But not Paul Styger. Mr. Styger, an easygoing man with a deep tan and wavy white hair, bought part of Sussex Airport in 1946, a year after it opened, and has owned the whole thing since the mid-1950's. The small airport, in rural Sussex County 45 miles northeast of Manhattan, has not been lucrative, Mr. Styger says. His payback, he says, comes from sharing his passion for flying, which he calls "this good clean sport," with those who love it as much as he does.
To provide additional income, Mr. Styger has taken odd jobs over the years, once selling calendars, insurance and commercial refrigerators door to door. He has also postponed several renovations -- which he hopes to undertake soon -- including repaving the weathered, 3,500-foot runway and rebuilding the dilapidated main hangar.
"I don't say, 'My salary is X,' and take it every week," Mr. Styger says in the airport's narrow, cluttered office, which got its first computer recently. "Some weeks I don't take a salary at all."
Nevertheless, the airport has survived and even won an international reputation with the three-day air show in late August that it has staged for 20 years. The show -- which includes dozens of vintage planes, military fly-bys and demonstrations by stunt pilots -- drew 30,000 spectators last year, many of them children.
In the introduction to last year's program, Mr. Styger wrote: "To all the faithful friends, without whose support these shows would not be possible, we pledge always a friendly field, a helping hand, a place for hangar flying and old-fashioned camaraderie."
That spirit is apparent among the airport's several dozen regular pilots. On this Sunday morning, for example, a diverse group -- including the 61-year-old owner of a well-drilling company, a paralegal in her 30's and a 70-year-old retired American Airlines pilot -- flew together to an airport in the Catskills, just for breakfast.
Since it is a throwback to a simpler time before airports like Teterboro, about 40 miles to the south, became busy with corporate jets, Sussex attracts its share of elderly people.
Robert Templin, a 68-year-old retired ironworker, and his wife, Lorraine, 63, a former waitress, drive here almost every day from their home in Lake Hopatcong, N.J., about 40 minutes away. They don leather caps and fly for hours in a 50-year-old, open-cockpit World War II training plane that Mr. Templin assembled from pieces unearthed in an old hangar, a barn and someone else's cellar.
"There's always a thrill in doing a little acrobatic maneuver," he said. "It makes you feel really alive. And this airport is a friendly place. The hangar talk is as good as the flying."
Indeed, Mr. Styger has managed to imbue the airport with the feel of a general store, or, as he likes to call it, a bar without a liquor license.
"It's like a family," says Kathy Dylewski, the paralegal, from nearby Sparta. "Everyone fits in. You're just a number at other airports."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Sussex Airport in New Jersey is a throwback to a simpler time before small airports became busy with corporate jets. Robert Templin started his 50-year-old, open-cockpit World War II training plane recently as his wife, Lorraine, sat inside. The retired couple fly for hours almost every day. (Clifford Hausner for The New York Times)
 
Map of New Jersey showing location of Sussex Airport.

HEADLINE: Ocean Dumping Is Ending, but Not Problems;
New York Can't Ship, Bury or Burn Its Sludge, but No One Wants a Processing Plant

BYLINE: By MICHAEL SPECTER

 BODY:
Late this afternoon the ocean barge Spring Brook will slip quietly into the East River and head to sea, carrying for the last time one of America's least loved cargoes: 400 tons of New York City's processed sewage.
It has been four years since Congress voted to ban the common practice of using the ocean as a municipal chamber pot, and with the Federal deadline set for tomorrow, New York is the only city that still does it. For environmentalists and many politicians, the final barge journey will be a moment of triumph, one they say will make the planet a cleaner, healthier place. 
But behind the public celebrations lie a host of anxieties. For New York, the end of ocean dumping means the beginning of a new and troubling era: a city that is already struggling to recycle, burn, export, sell and bury nearly 30,000 tons of garbage every day will have to find ways to handle some more. And despite plans, promises and years of negotiations, it won't be easy or cheap.
 
'We Rushed Into This'
 "This ban has been portrayed as an accomplishment that has no flaws," said Representative Thomas J. Manton of Queens, whose district will be among those that will be forced to process the city's treated sewage, called sludge. "But I worry whether this makes sense economically or environmentally. We rushed into this for political reasons and now we will have to deal with some harsh truths."
Perhaps the most painful of those truths is that while it will be nearly impossible to export, bury or burn most of the city's sludge, it is even harder to find a New Yorker eager to live near a plant designed to process it, either by turning that waste into useful compost or by baking it into fertilizer pellets at tremendous heat.
Over the next decade the city, which has one composting plant on Wards Island, plans to build at least three composting plants, in Queens, Staten Island and Brooklyn, and one in the Bronx that will make fertilizer pellets. There are also plans for at least one chemical plant on Wards Island that would turn sludge into cover for landfills.
 
Needed but Unwanted
 Later, city environmental officials say, New York may need more sludge plants; plans for them, however, have proven as popular in city neighborhoods as incinerators, garbage transfer stations and recycling centers. Everybody may need them but nobody wants them. So besides battling for a new incinerator at the Brooklyn Navy Yard, the city faces the prospect of placing as many as 10 plants over the next 15 years to bake and compost its sludge.
And it will have to do it at great cost to the residents of New York City. In the fiscal year that starts on Wednesday, the city will pay more than $250 million to dispose of its sludge on land. Last year it cost less than $20 million to ferry it 106 miles to the Deepwater Municipal Sludge Site on the edge of the Continental Shelf, where at least 40 million tons of waste have been deposited since 1986.
City water and sewer rates rose nearly 20 percent in 1991, mostly to accommodate the anticipated increased costs of sludge processing. And the City Department of Environmental Protection expects the increases to continue.
"It's not really a water bill any more," said the department's Commissioner, Albert F. Appleton, about the combined sewer and water bills that city residents pay. "It's a sludge bill."
 
Priorities Are Doubted
 Mr. Appleton says he feels strongly that ocean dumping is wrong and he is pleased to see it end. But he also says: "I just don't know if this is how I'd choose to spend my next $250 million. On the list of pressing environmental problems faced by this city and this country, I really wonder if ocean dumping is that high."
Nearly every working day for most of this century -- here and in hundreds of coastal communities from Los Angeles to Miami -- barges made similar journeys, laden with millions of tons of sludge they dumped into the ocean.
Congress banned the practice in 1988, after a summer in which tons of medical waste washed up on beaches and thousands of dolphins died mysteriously along the Atlantic Coast. Although government officials insisted there was no connection between the medical debris and ocean dumping much farther out to sea, environmentalists had a strong election-year issue and few politicians were eager to oppose a ban.
Sewage sludge had been dumped 12 miles out in New York Harbor since 1924. Citing evidence that enormous expanses of the inner coast had been degraded by the practice, the Federal Environmental Protection Agency in 1984 designated a new site, 106 miles off the coast of Atlantic City, that was assumed to be much safer.
 
Pollutants at Deep Levels
 "The evidence is harder to gather at that depth," said J. Frederick Grassle, director of the Institute of Marine and Coastal Studies at Rutgers University. "The effects of ocean dumping that far out are not severe. But there are effects, and clearly pollutants are penetrating into the deepest layers of the sea."
For most smaller cities the shift to 106 miles made the cost and trouble of dumping too high. But some, including many communities in New Jersey and in Westchester and Nassau Counties, continued until last year. New York, by far the nation's biggest ocean dumper, took a little longer to stop.
Environmentalists who campaigned hard for an end to dumping -- in part by likening the assault on the oceans to the greenhouse effect gases are feared to have on the earth's atmosphere -- say that using the ocean as a dump site is never an acceptable alternative to finding safe ways to dispose of waste on land. Many scientists also worried about the long-term effects of dumping waste beyond the edge of the continent, where the ocean floor drops to a depth of more than 6,000 feet from 600 feet.
Opponents of dumping say that sophisticated new technology allows sludge to be treated so that it can be used in many beneficial ways. Among the recent innovations are giant centrifuges used in New York, which, like washing machines, rely on a spin cycle to reduce sewage to concentrated form.
 
'Sludge Has a Bad Name'
 "Just dumping this stuff in the ocean and pretending to forget about it is not an answer to any problem," said Nina Sankovitch, a senior project lawyer at the Natural Resources Defense Council. "Sewage contains nutrients that harm the ocean but that when properly treated can be beneficial to soil. Sludge has a bad name but it doesn't need to."
Good sludge, filled with nitrogen and phosphorus, is essential for plant growth, and when metals and other poisons are eliminated it can be better than most manure. The shrubs and grasses of the newly renovated Bryant Park in midtown Manhattan were planted on a bed of genuine New York City sludge and the flowers and lawns of Gracie Mansion have prospered with its use.
Under the administration of former Mayor Edward I. Koch, the city bitterly fought the Federal ban on ocean dumping and delayed its enforcement for years. But in the long run, the delays may have cost the city money because it was unable to establish joint sludge plants with neighbors like Westchester and New Jersey that also recently stopped dumping.
"We could have saved a lot of money and done the right thing here," said Sarah L. Clark, a scientist at the Environmental Defense Fund. "But at least, after years, we are finally getting out."
 
Few Like Landfills Either
 Mayor David N. Dinkins, who supported the ban on ocean dumping, will join environmentalists and William K. Reilly, administrator of the Environmental Protection Agency, at a celebration in New York on Tuesday.
Some of the city's sludge is now taken to southern Virginia and buried in a landfill, an alternative that few scientists consider any better than dumping at sea. And New York has contracted with several private companies to process its sludge and dispose of it. But by the end of the decade it plans to set up its own processing plants.
Where they will go remains a thorny issue. A Citizens Advisory Committee has been meeting regularly with the city environmental department for three years. Mr. Appleton has discussed with residents throughout the city the need for such plans, and most agree that they are essential. But neighborhood leaders often wonder why it is essential to put them where they live.
"This is going to be a real problem for the city," said City Councilman Stanley E. Michels of Manhattan. "We have to put the things somewhere. It's half of what our lives are consumed by. Where should we put this? Where should we put that? And the ban will put enormous pressure on the city. But we are doing the right thing and we will rise above the partisan problems. It'll just take time."

HEADLINE: An Economics of Reality Professed by Rising Stars

BYLINE: SYLVIA NASAR

 BODY:
A new brand of labor economics is emerging from two academic hubs: Princeton University, a school famed in the 1950's and 1960's for leading-edge research on the subject, and Harvard.
And three young economists at those universities are among the rising stars of this new economics. David Card and Alan B. Kreuger of Princeton and Lawrence H. Katz of Harvard have created a stir with their findings that a higher minimum wage has not led to cutbacks in jobs. 
 The three economists have often crossed paths as students, professors and, most of all, as research fellows of the National Bureau of Research, probably the most important sponsor of empirical economic research in the nation. All three were inspired by two older labor economists: Orley Aschenfelter at Princeton and Richard Freeman at Harvard.
What distinguishes the new labor economics is a credo that stresses reality-checking. "The economic model of the world seemed really useful to me," said Professor Kreuger, who is 31 years old. "But it also seemed to be largely untested."
Practitioners of this new discipline look for "natural" experiments where they can compare two real-life situations; they try to be credible, and they gather evidence as if they were creating a collage.
"The idea is to try to find sharp natural experiments where there are exogenous variations in the variables you're interested in," said Professor Aschenfelter, a 51-year-old Princeton professor who is one of the deans of the new labor economics.
For example, just when Americans were getting nervous that immigrants were grabbing jobs from natives, Professor Card, who is 35 years old, studied the impact of the Mariel boatlift on Miami's low-wage labor market. "No effect," was his finding.
 
Making Things Credible
 The new labor economists constantly talk about seeking results that are plausible. "You try to make the work credible, believable and straightforward," said Professor Katz, who is 33 and occasionally teams up with his Princeton colleagues. "The most important word is credible." That means describing how results are attained in a forthright, hands-above-the-table fashion, he said.
The new labor economists also pride themselves on casting a wide net to create a fabric of evidence drawn from as many different data, time periods and countries as possible. They weave in anecdotes, analogies and, when necessary, their own telephone surveys.
