HEADLINE: Forging New Insight on Minimum Wages and Jobs

BYLINE: By SYLVIA NASAR

 BODY:
Contrary to one of the most widely accepted orthodoxies of modern economics, raising the Federal minimum wage may not prompt employers to trim jobs, new studies suggest, and therefore may not be such a bad way to help America's working poor.
The findings are likely to influence the growing national debate -- re-ignited by the Los Angeles riots -- over how best to reverse the deteriorating fortunes of young, less-educated workers. 
 That is especially likely because the Federal purse is, for all practical purposes, empty, and because of the growing political appeal of taxing business to, in effect, provide employee benefits like health insurance.
"It's not ideal; it isn't going to solve the long-term, big problems," Lawrence H. Katz, a Harvard labor economist and co-author of one of the studies, said of the minimum wage. "But it can, in the short run, provide extra income to those who work."
 
Winners and Losers
 Both liberal and conservative economists have long been convinced that the minimum wage law priced bottom-tier workers out of jobs. They recognized that a more generous minimum wage was good for those who were not dismissed as wages were raised, but worried that the losers outnumbered the winners.
Now, two unorthodox studies by economists at Princeton University and Harvard University have concluded that the most-recent rise in the minimum wage to $4.25 an hour -- a 90-cent increase phased in from April 1990 to April 1991 -- did not prompt employers to chop jobs.
That finding makes the case for modest increases in the minimum wage as an ingredient in future anti-poverty measures. But liberal politicians are not likely to push for another increase soon.
Still, Gov. Bill Clinton, in his Presidential campaign, recently announced that he favored indexing the minimum wage for inflation, which is tantamount to increasing it annually. And a battle over minimum wage levels looms next spring, with the expiration of a provision in the law that permits employers to pay newly hired teen-agers less than the minimum.
The findings of the new studies, which ignited a controversy among economists and sparked criticism from other researchers, were borne out in interviews with executives employing minimum-wage workers.
"It really hasn't affected the number of jobs we have," said Jim Hennessey, operations manager for Andy Frain, a Chicago agency that provides 600 ushers for sports and entertainment events each week.
When the minimum wage was raised, the company increased its starting salaries even higher, from $3.90 an hour to $4.40, 15 cents more than the Federal minimum.
"Paying that extra 50 cents helped us recruit," Mr. Hennessey said. "We're getting better kids."
Tim Supples, manager of several Roy Rogers restaurants in Mercer County in rural Pennsylvania, reported a similar experience.
"Our head count didn't increase or decrease," he said. "You're just going to manage a lot more tightly to get more out of the people we've got."
The Princeton and Harvard researchers focused on teen-agers -- about a third of the low-wage workers affected by the increase in the minimum -- to get a handle on the entire low-wage labor market.
"Whatever you find for teen-agers, my guess is true for low-wage workers," said David Card, a Princeton economist and one of the authors of the studies.
About two million workers earn the minimum wage or less. They are hamburger flippers and garment workers, sales clerks and janitors. Two-thirds are adults, and though some of the remaining third are teen-agers from affluent families, most teen-agers earning minimum wages are members of low-income families for whom the wages are an important source of income.
It was concern for these workers that led Congress to raise the minimum wage. Until the 1990 and 1991 increases, the Federal minimum had been frozen since 1981 at $3.35 an hour. As other wages rose, the minimum wage fell to its lowest level relative to average hourly pay since the 1950's.
 
Isolating the Effects
 One problem economists face in studying how raising the minimum wage affects jobs is the difficulty in isolating the effect of a wage increase from that of the myriad other economic forces influencing the labor market.
But in the Princeton study, Mr. Card created a control group from states not affected by the Federal minimum wage increase because they already had higher or equal minimum wage levels of their own.
Comparing states where as many as 50 percent of the teen-agers earned less than the new Federal minimum with states where virtually all teens were already earning as much or more, Professor Card found that teen-age pay rose sharply in low-wage states where the higher Federal minimum wage had the biggest impact. But, contrary to expectations, teen employment did not suffer as a result.
 
Comparing the States
 Take, for example, what Mr. Card found in 3 of the 50 states he studied: In Georgia -- where teen-age wages are among the lowest -- the new Federal minimum increased wages 14 percent, but employment edged up by four-tenths of 1 percent.
In Minnesota, by contrast, where teen-agers were already earning more than the new Federal minimum, wages went up only 3.9 percent but employment fell 4.1 percent. For New York -- another high-wage state -- wages went up 2 percent but employment fell 3.2 percent.
By comparing fast-food outlets in Texas in the same way that Mr. Card compared states, Professor Katz at Harvard and Alan B. Kreuger at Princeton found more hiring in stores where wages were most affected by the new Federal minimum.
What is more, Professor Katz and Professor Kreuger found that only a small number of the Texas fast-food employers were using the sub-minimum "training" wage that lets them pay new teen-age employees less than the minimum for six months.
 
Aimed at First-Timers
 Before the passage of the 1989 minimum wage law, President Bush, supported by many economists and fast-food companies, fought to exempt newly hired teens from the full increase in the adult minimum. The hope was that employers would be more willing to train first-time workers if they could pay them less and that this would hold down the job losses from an increase in the minimum wage.
But Professors Katz and Kreuger found that just 6 percent of the Texas restaurants surveyed were using the training wage, despite the fact that most were aware of that option and many had been able to hire workers at wages below the new minimum before the change in the law. More than 95 percent of employers paid trainees the adult minimum or higher.
"If anyone was going to use the sub-minimum, you'd expect it to be these companies with high turnover," Professor Kreuger said. Roughly half of fast-food workers are teen-agers, turnover averages 200 percent in many stores and most teens can learn their jobs within a few weeks.
 
Some Unanswered Questions
 The standard economic model predicts that if something costs more, in this case labor, consumers, in this case employers, will buy less of it. Speculating about why businesses did not react as expected to the latest minimum wage increase, the authors of the studies said that the labor market may not work that simply.
"Some employers thought it was O.K. because they were already paying more," said Professor Card, "and others said they wouldn't mind paying more if everybody paid more because it made life easier for them."
He added: "You can have high wages and low turnover and less recruiting, or you can have low wages, high turnover and more recruiting."
The new studies have already circulated as National Bureau of Economic Research working papers. They will also appear in the October issue of the Industrial and Labor Relations Review along with critiques and earlier surveys of California and Texas by the same authors.
 
Position of Critics
 But critics contend that the new studies are flawed because they were conducted too soon after the increase in the minimum wage.
"It's not that everybody goes out and fires workers, but that job growth is slower than it would otherwise have been," said William Washer, an economist at the Federal Reserve whose own study of 50 states from 1977 to 1989 supports the established view that higher minimum wages lead to job losses.
He and others also argue that the recent contrary experience, even if it is verified by future studies, cannot necessarily be generalized.
But the studies' authors argue that the wage increase was well publicized in advance, and that restaurants had fully adjusted to it. They also note that the latest wage increases -- 12 percent or so in real terms -- were relatively modest.
 
A Blunt Instrument
 Professor Katz admits that the minimum wage is a blunt instrument for fighting poverty and that other ways of providing relief to the working poor are far more efficient.
"It's untargeted," he said. "A bunch of middle class teen-agers benefit, too."
But given the new evidence and the fact that the Federal budget is tied in knots by the high deficit, the idea of levying a hidden tax on employers to provide assistance to the poor in the form of higher wages is likely to gain appeal.

HEADLINE: Washington Talk;
After a Lull Capitol Gets In the Mood To Legislate

BYLINE: By ADAM CLYMER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 28

 BODY:
Congress seems to be easing into a law-making phase, dealing with legislation on issues from aid to the nations that used to be the Soviet Union to unemployment insurance and enterprise zones.
In the first three months of the year, about the only major bill to go through Congress was a middle-class tax cut that was more of a political statement than a serious policy proposal, let alone a measure whose advocates expected it to become law. Indeed, some supporters would not have voted for it without the certainty of a Presidential veto. 
 More recently, Congress has managed to pass legislation on campaign finance and fetal tissue research that it knew President Bush would veto, but that it still believed should become law. It has passed an urban aid measure that he could sign, and it is trying to work out an enterprise zone bill he will not reject.
The change is most striking in the House, which spent weeks on intramural backbiting over the House bank and bitter Republican charges that the Democratic majority behaves despotically. Those irritants have not vanished, but the tone of a backyard slanging match is decreasing and the resemblance to a legislative body is getting stronger.
This month alone, it has seriously debated a constitutional amendment requiring a balanced Federal budget, and then trimmed the first three appropriations bills before it. The House expects to deal with the jobless and enterprise zones this week, and may even get to legislation on health care and keeping families together the week after.
The Senate? Well, it was behaving like the Senate, with a filibuster over its effort to pass its balanced-budget amendment. But by Friday it seemed to have arranged to vote on aid to the ex-Soviet republics this week.
There are several reasons for this incomplete metamorphosis.
One is simply the calendar. This is the time of year when appropriations bills start commanding floor time. Moreover, if Congress is going to adjourn to campaign full time by early October, after time out for the party conventions and a little rest in August, there are only 9 or 10 weeks of scheduled legislative sessions left this year. If anything is to happen legislatively, it cannot wait much longer.
A second is that the distraction of the bank and other scandals seems finally to have run its course, and even lawmakers with check problems are now focusing on passing some bills so they can argue they deserve to be forgiven their overdrafts.
That approach corresponds with the argument that Speaker Thomas S. Foley has been making for months. The public's disdain for Congress, and by now for Mr. Bush too, is not really based on scandals or flubs, the Washington Democrat says, but on a national sense that the Government is failing to sense and address the urgency of the country's real problems.
Another reason, and one that matters more to Democratic leaders than to their followers, is the idea that by passing reasonable bills on important issues, the Democrats in Congress can help their party's all-but-nominated Presidential candidate, Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas.
Neither side of this awkward partnership is sure enough of the other to be pressing for a big, formal consensus. But if the Democrats can pass popular bills that Mr. Clinton favors and Mr. Bush opposes, or even vetoes, they can claim proof that Americans ought to vote for unified Democratic government. That sort of argument, made most insistently by Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, the House majority leader, is one major force behind the efforts on health care and supporting disintegrating families.
Finally, making laws is what most members of Congress came here to try to do. As Mr. Foley observed in a commencement address at the University of Washington this month "A strong dedication to legislating remains the driving force that inspires members of Congress," he said. "If it were not so, believe me, many fewer would be seeking re-election this year."
For all the apparent transformation, however, tempers are still raw, and offensive partisan hyperbole a basic fact of legislative life. The fact that the change is probably not permanent was demonstrated Wednesday when Representative Robert S. Walker, Republican of Pennsylvania, said the Democrats were either treating Republicans like Old South slaves or behaving like Nazis themselves.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN -- Voices: Miami;
On the Economy, Well-Being and Confidence

 BODY:
Political experts, local officials and ordinary voters in several cities were asked to appraise President Bush's handling of the economy.
 
ESTEBAN FERRER
65, law partner in the international division of the law firm Shutts & Bowen
The problem with the economy is not directly related with the President. It has to do with the overburdening of soft costs -- the mass of regulation and controls imposed on the small-business man -- that's kicked us out of the world market. We don't produce here anymore, just buy. There's nothing the President can do. I would give Bush a passing grade but not an excellent one. 
 He paid too much attention to world matters and basked in the glory in Saudi Arabia. World matters are serious and require a lot of time and energy; he could have done more pressing on the home front to incite business to action. Congress is the bad guy. All they do is burden the producing elements more.
There are no incentives to save. All you see here is spending, spending, spending. Somebody else is always to blame.
 
Matty Bloom
37, special projects coordinator for health care projects at the Stein Gerontological Institute
I'm a professional woman with a master's degree from a good college, and the job market is even frightening to someone like me. I'm having to think about ways to protect myself with other skills. Imagine what it's like for one of the secretaries at my institute. I've always felt a strong social responsibility. Now I find myself torn, having to give up some of my ideals to guarantee a certain level of economic security.
If I choose to become a parent, there's even more pressure on me. We've seen a feminization of poverty during the Bush Administration. Women have to go back to work to support the family and without adequate support it's very difficult to do. Bush has failed to back policies that support women, children and the family.
There hasn't been much difference between the Reagan and Bush Administrations. Reagan's "lie," that money would flow down to workers, they'd have more money and more money would then be spent, hasn't happened.
 
Donald M. Jones
40 years old, associate professor, University of Miami School of Law
President Bush suffers from a set of misplaced priorities. Under the Bush Administration we found money for the B-1 bomber and for the S.& L. bail-out, but didn't find money for our cities or the education of our children or jobs for black and brown people. This failure to develop priorities to rebuild the cities and revamp the crumbling education structure has a lot to do with why Los Angeles and Atlanta went up in flames.
Bush failed to take advantage of the peace dividends. Bush followed Reagan's failed domestic policy and continued the old Republican strategy, old slogans and rhetoric long past the point where it's clear they're not working. Bush still clings to the metaphor of "A Thousand Points of Light," as if these were just the private problems of individuals.
The economic problem is related to biracial harmony which has slipped farther away. The White House has at best ignored, and at times opposed, civil rights legislation. A large share of the economic problem can be traced to racial disharmony. If Bush is re-elected, any improvement we see in the economy will be limited unless there are fundamental changes in attitude and the creation of jobs. The mishandling of this particular Republican President has been nothing short of a minor disaster. I am black, but don't think I speak solely as a black person. We're in trouble if we don't make some changes.

HEADLINE: Tax Fight in Florida May Halt Services

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TALLAHASSEE, Fla., June 27

 BODY:
Having already vetoed two "no new taxes" budgets, Gov. Lawton Chiles of Florida has told the Legislature that he will begin shutting down the government at 12:01 A.M. on Wednesday unless lawmakers agree to increase taxes.
The state's budget crisis has come to a head after years of rapid population growth without a corresponding change in Florida's tax structure. 
 Since January, with an election looming, Mr. Chiles and the Legislature have been wrangling over how best to modify Florida's tax system.
Unless they can agree on a budget by July 1, when the new fiscal year begins, the government will have no choice but to suspend many of the state's most visible operations, starting with the lottery.
"The hour is late and the clock is ticking," said Mr. Chiles, a Democrat, at a news conference here on Friday. He called on legislators from both parties to "put aside partisan politics and do the right thing," and he expressed hope that they would not allow the state "to fall off the cliff."
 
A Test of Wills
 Both sides are vowing not to be the first to blink in a test of wills that could plunge the state into financial chaos if it is not resolved by Tuesday. But the dispute appears to have irritated an already restless electorate in a year when all 160 seats in both houses are up for re-election. Strategists for both branches of government and both parties acknowledge that no matter what the outcome, voters could take a "plague on both your houses" attitude.
Florida's population has more than doubled since 1970, to 14 million people, making it the fourth-most-populous state. But the state is saddled with a tax structure inherited from the early years of this century, when it was one of the most rural and sparsely settled states in the union. Florida is one of seven states that has no personal income tax, and no one in either party is proposing such a tax as a solution to the current budget crisis. In a further complication, Florida's Constitution requires that the Legislature adopt a balanced budget.
Mr. Chiles's insistence on a tax increase during this election year is part of a sweeping promise he made to overhaul the state's way of raising money. A plank in his populist platform was to reduce the tax burden that has been borne by lower- and moderate-income people.
Three-fifths of the state's revenue is derived from a 6 percent sales tax, which falls heavily on tourists and the state's poorest residents; regressive levies, like those on alcohol, cigarettes, gasoline and betting, account for most of the other sources of income.
 
Sales Tax Would Expand
 Ever since the Legislature convened in January, Mr. Chiles has been pushing for a repeal of some 100 sales-tax exemptions on goods and services and for an end to exemptions from the state's corporate income tax, which is limited and relatively low. If lawmakers would allow him to do that, he has argued, the state would receive up to $1.35 billion in new revenue that would allow it to maintain health, education, police, environment and social service programs at current levels.
On a television call-in program on Thursday, Mr. Chiles contended that his tax overhaul could be accomplished with virtually no inconvenience to the average Florida resident. He said state studies had found that though there were 1.25 million corporations in the state, a mere 800 of them accounted for two-thirds of all corporate tax revenue. Many companies paid no taxes at all, he said.
"We have to ask somebody else who is not paying now to pay a little money," he said. "I'm trying to broaden the base" for tax revenue, he added, because the existing system "just does not make sense to me." However, Mr. Chiles has said that he does not plan to introduce a state income tax.
Mr. Chiles said that he found it particularly difficult to justify what he characterized as elitist sales tax exemptions, like those that "special interests" have obtained for luxuries that include maid service, dry cleaning and tanning services. He has coupled his arguments about expanding tax revenue with a plan to cut the sales tax to 5 percent and to reduce property taxes on homes valued at $150,000 or less, an action that would benefit most of the state's homeowners.
 
New Taxes Are Resisted
 But many lawmakers, Republicans in particular, have been arguing that the state needs to tighten its belt instead, and they have demanded both cutbacks in spending and a ban on any new taxes. The Republican minority leader of the State Senate, Ander Crenshaw, has been particularly vocal, describing Mr. Chiles's plan as merely "a prelude to a state income tax" that would halt Florida's economic growth by discouraging workers and corporations from moving here.
Many Democrats who might ordinarily support Mr. Chiles out of party loyalty have been reluctant to come to his assistance, citing fear of a voter backlash at the polls in November. The Governor spent much of the spring traveling around the state to explain his tax plan at town meetings and on media talk shows, but many Democrats say that he has not been effective enough in selling the plan to the public and that, as a result, public support for it is not there.
The lower chamber, in which Democrats have a 74-46 majority, actually approved earlier this month a compromise budget plan that would have included $516 million in new taxes. But the Senate, where the Democrats have only a tenuous 21-19 majority, has simply refused to consider the House package, holding fast to a package that would limit spending to $30.65 billion and not raise taxes, a proposal that Mr. Chiles has described as "terrible." On Friday afternoon, the Senate failed to override Mr. Chiles's latest veto.
To override a veto, both houses have to muster a two-thirds majority. Leaders of both chambers planned to meet with the Governor's representatives throughout the weekend to discuss other compromise plans in an effort to avoid what they are describing as "a meltdown" of the government on Tuesday night.
 
Emergency Could Be Declared
 At his news conference here on Friday, Mr. Chiles announced that he would sign on Monday an executive order declaring a statewide "fiscal emergency" and ordering nonessential state services to cease operations starting Wednesday, July 1. He said the popular Florida Lottery would be the first state agency to close and said other nonessential public services would also be affected, but he declined to identify them and asked state employees to work on a volunteer basis if a shutdown was required.
However, members of the Legislature have said privately that they expect Mr. Chiles to close the state parks just days before the Fourth of July holiday and that the motor vehicle license bureau might also be shut down. Mr. Chiles has repeatedly said that no matter how bad the crisis gets, he would not allow law enforcement, medical care and other basic public services to be affected.
A few lawmakers have sought to make light of the situation. Some have worn buttons that read "Non-Essential State Employees" on the floor of the Legislature. But there are abundant signs that the 130,000 state employees who could be affected by a shutdown are not amused.
Across the street from the capital, one civil servant has been parading with a sign that reads, "State Employee -- Will Work for Food," on one side and on the other says, "If I did my job the way the Legislature has -- I would be fired."
 
Election Worries Abound
 Some legislators appear to be worried that they may be swept away in a tide of anti-incumbent sentiment and disgust with the political process that seems to be building around the state. Senator Rick Dantzler, a Democrat from Central Florida, warned his colleagues that "there is going to be a tremendous amount of chaos this fall" when voters go to the polls, and members of the House have been joking during their deliberations that up to half of the 120 members of their chamber may not return.
"I have had it," Mr. Dantzler complained. "I am sick and tired of the brinksmanship."
Senator Malcolm E. Beard, a Republican from the same area, said his constituents were telling him that if there were any bombs "left over from Desert Storm," they should "drop them right here," on the state Capitol.
Even though Mr. Chiles, who was elected in 1990, does not face re-election until 1994, he may also have been bruised by increasing voter dissatisfaction. In a recent statewide poll, a narrow majority of respondents said they approved of the tax proposals he has suggested, but support dropped once his name was associated with the package.
As Mr. Chiles has acknowledged, there is a certain irony in this turn of events. When he declined in 1988 to run for re-election to the United States Senate, where he had served for 18 years, he said one of the main reasons for his decision was the continual wrangling over the Federal budget between Congress and the President.

HEADLINE: Iran Allows Foreigners To Buy Its Companies

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TEHERAN, Iran, June 28

 BODY:
Government officials have recently signaled that they are serious about allowing foreign companies to control plants and businesses in Iran.
In the hope of increasing foreign investment, the High Council for Investments at the Finance Ministry approved a measure last month to allow full foreign ownership of Iranian companies. Foreign ownership was previously limited to 49 percent of an enterprise. 
 The controversial measure was intended to attract hard currency into the country's stagnant economy, but many people here were skeptical that officials would actually open the country to such an extent.
 
Guarantees to Investors
 There also are guarantees of free return of capital to investors' home countries and payments of hard-currency damages if property is nationalized or confiscated in the future.
Finance Minister Mohsen Nourbakhsh said the new laws "do not specify any restrictions, and the limits of foreign investment will be decided by the High Council for Investments, which is made up of several Cabinet members."
While the United States, Israel and South Africa have been legally prevented from investing in Iran since diplomatic relations were severed in the late 1970's, American companies have routinely been able to trade with Iran through subsidiaries based in Europe or Canada, or through Iranian partners.
There is no indication so far of whether the policy change has succeeded in attracting new interest from foreign companies. Officials of the Finance Ministry acknowledged that Iran's need for capital had not been met under the previous 49 percent limitation.
Despite a long political history of resistance to foreign ownership of natural resources and industry, which culminated in state confiscation of foreign interests after the 1979 revolution, senior Iranian officials now say that political and economic policy making have been merged into a comprehensive collective to serve investors and the Iranian economy.
Economic experts say that even though greater foreign investment has been made legal, the huge bureaucracy, Soviet-style labor laws and inefficiency of the system remain the main obstacles for prospective businesses.
Moreover, Islamic moral codes with restrictions on dress, food and some aspects of social behavior may further discourage companies and individuals from considering Iran as an investment opportunity.
"We have to begin to see ourselves as a contributor to the global economy," Mohammed Taghi Banki, president of Iran's National Steel Company, said in a recent interview. Mr. Banki's company is offering more than 20 plants and mines for sale to the private sector. "We must become productive and businesslike," he added, "because the actions and reactions of that global system affect us."

HEADLINE: U.N. Asserts World Growth Is Hindered by Budget Cuts

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, June 28

 BODY:
The United Nations said today that over-reliance on market forces could be self-defeating and that too much emphasis on reducing government budget deficits was hindering economic growth.
In a 227-page World Economic Survey, the organization forecast that the global economy might grow 1 percent this year and as much as 3 percent in 1993. 
 But it said this growth would do little to reduce unemployment.
The prediction is similar to some other major economic surveys, but the United Nations report is more pessimistic, saying the recovery is fragile because governments are putting too much emphasis on bringing down inflation rather than promoting investment.
 
Employment Projections
 By 1993, it said, unemployment should be about 7 percent worldwide and about 10 percent in Western Europe. Inflation should be held in check and budget deficits should come down sharply, particularly in the United States. Currently, the American unemployment rate is 7.5 percent.
The report said the collapse of Communism and the failure of central planning prompted people to overlook some important government functions.
"The pendulum may have swayed too far in the direction of market orientation, leading to a neglect of the role and responsibility of the state," it said.
The state, it said, had an "indispensable function" in insuring education and health, maintaining an acceptable distribution of income and safeguarding the environment.
Although the report backs medium-term goals to reduce government deficits, it says the United States, Germany and Japan would be better off with half the budget cuts they plan.
If the United States also restored a 10 percent tax credit to stimulate private investment by 1996, it could add $200 billion to the world's total investment, the report said.
 
Day-to-Day Fluctuations
 But it warned that too many governments were capitulating to day-to-day fluctuations of foreign-exchange markets.
"Financial markets react negatively to any announcement of a policy of economic stimulation that they suspect of being inflationary," the report said. "The result is likely to be a rise in the interest rates demanded.
"So governments anxious for market confidence press for wage repression and zero inflation."
But it said the social cost of driving down inflation was likely to be very heavy and discourage investment.
"There is in fact no other way to resolve the economic and political crises multiplying in the world community than to give priority to the restoration of growth," the report said.
Only then would the world be able to realize a peace dividend, since shifting resources from military to civilian uses was nearly impossible during years of economic decline.
 
Benefits 'Frittered Away'
 So far, the report said, the peace dividend had been "frittered away in budgetary squabbles in the West and engulfed by economic chaos in the former Soviet Union."
Specifically, the report said the economies of developed nations would grow about 2 percent in 1992, with inflationary pressures likely to ease in North America but to increase slightly in Europe.
Among developing nations, African economies barely kept up with population growth, Latin America seemed to stir after a decade of stagnation, and growth in Asia remained widespread despite the slowdown in world trade.

HEADLINE: Machine-Tool Orders Drop 27.5%

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Orders for American-made machine tools plunged in May, the second straight monthly drop, though they remain higher for the first five months of 1992 compared with depressed year-earlier levels, a trade group said yesterday.
Economists said the figures from the Association for Manufacturing Technology appeared to reflect a broader pattern of mixed signals that have characterized the recovery. 
 "The economy is expanding but not at a very impressive rate," said Richard Hoey, chief economist for the Dreyfus Corporation, a New York investment firm. "It's like when you start a car and it sputters along in the winter, not hitting on all cylinders."
The association, based in McLean, Va., said preliminary orders in May for machine tools totaled $159.1 million, a 27.5 percent drop from $219.5 million in April but 4 percent above the $153 million for May 1991. Year-to-date orders totaled $1.06 billion, a 5.8 increase from January-May 1991.
Machine tools represent a small industry, and the monthly numbers can swing sharply. But because orders are often placed months before shipments, they often are a signal of the capital spending plans and optimism of manufacturers. Some economists also consider machine-tool orders a useful indicator because they reflect the strength in industrial production. Machine tools are used to make products ranging from washing machines to pickup trucks.
Albert T. Moore, the association's president, said May has been a slow month for the last three years. Mr. Moore also said that other trends in manufacturing, notably improved corporate profits and higher factory utilization rates, were positive indicators for improved orders later.
The May decline came after a 9.6 percent decrease in April from a month earlier.
 
Fewer Foreign Orders
 Part of the weakness stems from a drop in export orders, a reflection of weakening economies abroad. Export orders in May totaled $25.1 million, down 14 percent from $29.2 million a month earlier. Year-to-date export orders totaled $129.6 million, down 43 percent from the January-May 1991 level of $227.6 million.
Last week, the first significant indicator of a long-predicted plunge in American exports surfaced when the Commerce Department reported a $6.97 billion trade deficit for April, up from $5.58 billion in March. A key reason for the increase was a sharp drop in sales of goods to Japan and Western Europe.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Timetable for Cutting Rates: Probably Later Than Sooner

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Most credit market analysts expect another cut in short-term interest rates, but probably not when the Federal Reserve Board's Open Market Committee meets tomorrow in Washington.
Nevertheless, many analysts argue that such a step would do little to cure the peculiar, perhaps unprecedented, problems on the economic landscape. And some suggest that another easing could do more harm than good. 
 The circumstances that prompted the Fed to cut rates many times in the past three years are in place today, analysts said.
 
Little Price Pressure
 After expanding in the first quarter at its most rapid rate since 1988, economic growth once again appears to be slowing, if not stalling. And business activity in Europe and Japan is far from robust.
Given that backdrop, analysts said it was difficult to imagine that inflationary pressures would be anything but benign for a year or two. Money-supply growth is weak, as is private credit demand. And labor costs are rising at a very slow pace.
"The Fed should ease," said Alan Sinai, president of the Boston Company Economic Advisers. "The economy did not snap back this spring, and there is almost no inflation risk for the next year to 18 months."
A drop in the overnight Federal funds rate of a quarter to a half a percentage point from its current level of 3.75 percent would almost immediately prompt commercial banks to cut their prime lending rates, now at 6.5 percent. That kind of reduction, Mr. Sinai said, would reduce costs on loans, increase profits and help to improve personal income.
 
Minimal Impact
 But a cut would probably not do much to spur demand even in sectors like housing and construction that are sensitive to interest rates, many economists argue. Such areas are beset with structural problems, not merely cyclical ones. Nor would it induce companies to take on big numbers of new workers or make consumers spend more freely.
"A cut in the prime wouldn't help much," said Donald J. Fine, chief market analyst at the Chase Manhattan Bank. "You have to have a need to borrow money."
Mickey D. Levy, chief economist at CRT Government Securities, said: "I think the Fed is perplexed and surprised the sharp decline in interest rates has not reinvigorated the economy. But this is a very unique period, and what is inhibiting stronger growth is not something they can fix."
Indeed, the Fed runs the risk of doing more harm than good if a cut in short-term rates causes bond yields to rise, analysts said.
"With the housing sector just sort of sitting there, you don't want to do anything to damage the chances that long-term rates might go lower," said Leonard J. Santow, a managing director at Griggs & Santow Inc., a New York financial consulting firm.
 
Expected Third Quarter
 Nevertheless, Mr. Santow said he expected the Fed to ease policy in the third quarter, because the economy will remain sluggish and political pressure from the White House to cut rates will probably intensify.
But unless important economic data to be released this week show the recovery is fading fast, President Bush's efforts to sway the central bank last week almost certainly mean that a move will come later rather than sooner.
Mr. Bush's remarks "were self-defeating," Mr. Levy said. "The best way to keep inflation expectations down and the bond market calm is not to challenge the Fed's independence."
As a way to defuse criticism that a rate cut may be politically motivated, Mr. Santow expects that rather than approve a cut at tomorrow's meeting, the Fed governors will instead give Alan Greenspan, the central bank chairman, the discretion to lower rates before Aug. 19, the next scheduled meeting of the Open Market Committee.
 
Await Job Figures
 The meeting and the economic data due this week will be the focal points of the holiday-shortened trading.
Important statistics will be released every day, through Thursday, when the Labor Department will report on the June employment situation.
According to most economists, nonfarm payroll employment probably rose by around 100,000 in June. That sort of gain is not normally associated with a vigorous recovery, but it would probably be sufficient to delay any easing. But a payroll increase of 50,000 or less would almost certainly lead to an easing, analysts said.
"Logic says the Fed should wait until after the payroll numbers come out," Mr. Fine said. "And if the numbers are weak, the Fed will go. But if they show signs of improvement, policy will remain on hold."

HEADLINE: WEDDINGS;
Diana Kaufman, Thomas M. Schutt

 BODY:
Diana Fern Kaufman, a daughter of Dr. and Mrs. Richard H. Kaufman of New Rochelle, N.Y., was married yesterday to Thomas Martin Schutt, a son of William M. Schutt and Margaret A. McKenzie, both of St. Petersburg, Fla. Rabbi David Blumenfeld officiated at the Mamaroneck (N.Y.) Beach and Yacht Club.
Mrs. Schutt, 23 years old, graduated from Emory University, as did the bridegroom. She is a candidate for a master's degree in special education at the College of New Rochelle. Her father is the president of Global Perspective, an economic consulting company in Greenwich, Conn. Her mother, Rosalind Fuchsberg Kaufman, is the managing partner in the Jacob D. Fuchsberg Law Firm, a New York practice that bears the name of the bride's grandfather, who is the firm's senior partner. The bride's grandmother Shirley G. Fuchsberg is the president of the Westchester Symphony Orchestra. 
 Mr. Schutt, also 23, is the sales manager of Price Signs Inc. in Florham Park, N.J. His mother is the billing manager for a medical practice in St. Petersburg. His father is the advertising director of Danka Industries, a distributor of office machines in St. Petersburg.

HEADLINE: Review/Film;
An American Jazz Master Through European Eyes

BYLINE: By JON PARELES

 BODY:
On June 2, 1964, Eric Dolphy played bass clarinet, alto saxophone and flute with a trio of Dutch musicians for a Netherlands radio show called "Jazz Magazine." Most of the time, the show's sessions were broadcast live and were not recorded; Dolphy's set was taped because he was leaving before the day of the show. It was fortuitous. On June 29 he died in Berlin after collapsing onstage; the doctors assumed that the black American jazz musician was on drugs. But Dolphy had slipped into a diabetic coma, and without treatment he never regained consciousness. The Dutch recordings became his album "Last Date."
The radio sessions were photographed but not filmed, so Hans Hylkema's documentary "Last Date -- Eric Dolphy," which will be shown today and tomorrow at the Film Forum 2 as part of the JVC Jazz Festival, becomes a tease. Although it is a kind of biography of Dolphy, its unstated and perhaps unintentional subject is the relation of Europeans to jazz, as perennial fans and inevitable outsiders, hearing a mythic America that they distrust and long for. The manager of the Berlin nightclub Tangente, who didn't understand until it was too late why Dolphy was wolfing down ice cream and Coca-Cola, says: "I thought he's a black musician, and so he'll be on drugs. I was tolerant, but. . . ." 
 The film follows Thierry Bruneau, who is writing a Dolphy biography, to the places Dolphy lived, where neighbors and colleagues are interviewed; it shows streets and bridges and black children playing as if congratulating itself on its authenticity. The film also returns repeatedly to the Dutch musicians who played with Dolphy. The pianist, Misha Mengelberg, apparently still bears a grudge that Dolphy called him lazy for the trouble he was having with the complex music; the drummer, Han Bennink, is still in awe.
A portrait of Dolphy does emerge. He was a "practiceaholic," one colleague said, and he wanted to be the first black musician in the Los Angeles Philharmonic. During a spell without work in New York, he lived on white beans, bags of them in his closet, but he'd also buy groceries for unemployed musicians. And he loved the sounds of birds, which turned up in his improvisations.
"Last Date" grows frustrating as the film details reactions to Dolphy -- like a Dutch bass clarinetist trying to recreate a Dolphy solo -- rather than showing the musician himself. In a few astonishing sequences, filmed the night that Dolphy told Charles Mingus he was leaving the bassist's group to stay in Europe, Mingus alternately abuses and commends Dolphy; the documentary then cuts away from the music. If that footage exists at greater length, it would be far more illuminating than the film's arty shots of hands taking apart a saxophone and nestling it in its case.
As it stands, "Last Date" only shows snippets of Dolphy performing until the closing credits. Behind the list of European and American names, the subject of the documentary finally has his own say.
 
Last Date
Eric Dolphy
Directed by Hans Hylkema; screenplay by Mr. Hylkema assisted by Thierry Bruneau; edited by Ot Louw; produced by Marian Brouwer; Deen van der Zaken, director of photography; Luk Brefeld, camera assistant; Piotr van Dijk and Lukas Boeke, sound. At Film Forum 2, 209 West Houston Street, SoHo. Running time: 92 minutes. This film has no rating.

HEADLINE: Challenging a Myth in Los Angeles

 BODY:
The young people of South-Central Los Angeles who try to make life work are almost invisible. News about their riot-torn community leaves the impression that black and Hispanic teen-agers are mostly gang members and welfare spongers. To learn how misleading that is, ask the Walt Disney Company.
Disneyland's employment recruiters visited South-Central and found themselves swamped by dynamic young applicants, many of whom will enter college this fall. The company wished it could have hired more than 200 of them. 
 That's a tiny fraction of the jobs needed. Congress has finally passed an aid program that will provide 350,000 summer jobs nationwide. And the city's own task force, Rebuild L.A., is working on long-term strategies. The Disney initiative provides an example of the rewards of private hiring programs for inner-city youth.
The rioting destroyed hundreds of small businesses, sources of many jobs for young people. Without work, they cannot help support their fragile families or save money for college this fall. The Los Angeles schools close for the summer tomorrow. Bernadette Graham, career counselor of Manual Arts High School, says three-quarters of her students are still seeking jobs. Marguerite LaMotte, principal of George Washington Preparatory High School, worries about what will happen to her 300 college-bound seniors.
The young men and women of South-Central competed hard for Disneyland's 200 jobs, despite a two-hour commute. Disney officials spoke glowingly, for instance, of 18-year-old LaRonda Jones, who hopes to become a police officer after college. They were happy to land Olivia Miles, headed for Grambling University.
Disney's surprise was evident: "They were wonderful kids, outstanding kids," a Disney spokesman told Sara Rimer of The New York Times. "We didn't know they were there."
But of course they are there. Initiatives like Disney's could pay big dividends -- not only in L.A., the point of greatest need, but in every big city.

HEADLINE: Bridge

BYLINE: By Alan Truscott

 BODY:
The modern one no-trump response, forcing for one round, is more heavily loaded than the old-fashioned non-forcing variety. This is workable provided players resist the temptation to use the bid when a clearer alternative is available. On the diagramed deal North should certainly have responded two diamonds to one spade. As it was, he was in difficulty when his partner followed with three clubs, showing a big hand.
North took a wild stab at six spades, which would have been a reasonable contract if South's spade suit had been strong. As it was South's chances were poor. Obviously six diamonds would have been easy, making an overtrick, and six no-trump would have been a desirable spot in a match-point game. 
 The declarer was Parthasaradhi Mallela of DeKalb, Illinois, a professor of economics at Northern Illinois University. He recognized that entries to dummy were likely to be crucial, and made a key play at the first trick. When dummy played low and East played low, he won with the ace instead of the ten, judging correctly that West held the queen.
He led a spade and West put up the queen and misjudged. Another heart would have allowed the defense to prevail, but he returned his remaining trump. It did not matter whether or not East played the king, and he chose to retain it. South cashed dummy's diamond winners and ruffed a diamond, reaching this ending:
 
[Diagram]
Thanks to his farsighted play at the first trick, South was able to reach the dummy by finessing the heart jack. He then cashed the heart king, throwing his club loser, and led diamonds. East was helpless in a coup position: whether he ruffed the 9th, 10th, 11th or 12th trick, his spade king was doomed and the slam was made.

HEADLINE: More Revenues, but Thinner Profits, for Law Firms

BYLINE: By BARNABY J. FEDER

 BODY:
Despite the recession, the nation's 100 largest law firms collectively took in a record $13.9 billion last year and suffered only a slight decline in profitability, according to a study to be released today by The American Lawyer, a legal magazine.
Five New York firms with a total of 377 partners -- Cravath, Swaine & Moore; Wachtell, Lipton, Rosen & Katz; Cahill Gordon & Reindel; Sullivan & Cromwell, and Davis Polk & Wardwell -- averaged at least $1 million per partner in profits. The least profitable firm on the list, Venable, Baetjer and Howard, of Baltimore, had an operating income of $20 million, or $155,000 a partner, on revenues of $72 million. 
 "For all the talk about this being a terrible time for law firms, it's just been a case of nipping at the margins," said Stephen A. Brill, the magazine's publisher. "It's no longer a situation where people can make as much money as they want to, but it's still extremely profitable."
Still, the revenue growth of 3 percent was the smallest gain since the first survey in 1985 and was not quite enough to compensate for the extra lawyers the top 100 hired. Revenues per lawyer fell to $371,000, down $1,000. And profits per partner declined for the second year in a row, falling 6 percent to $400,000.
The earnings slowdown reflects a variety of trends, including a decline in mergers, real estate transactions and other business deals that generate huge fees; a drive by many large corporations to do more of their legal work with staff attorneys, and pressures to limit or reduce fees for some services.
The total figures masked broader swings in the fortunes of individual firms. Vinson & Elkins, of Houston, saw total revenues plunge from $275.5 million, or 6th on the list in 1990, to $185 million, or 22nd, last year. Average profits per partner fell by 34 percent to $370,000. Gaston & Snow, a Boston firm that predated the Civil War, ranked 86th in 1990, went out of business last year.
By contrast, revenues for Baker & McKenzie, still No. 2 on the list, jumped 19 percent to $477.5 million, but were accompanied by a 21 percent increase in profits per partner to $375,000.
 
Smaller Firms Fare Differently
 Lawyers say that some of the same trends and the recession have probably had an even bigger impact on smaller law firms, whose performance is not covered by the survey.
But a survey of 650 law firms of all sizes released in April by Altman Weil Pensa Inc., a Philadelphia legal consulting company, showed that average revenue per lawyer was still rising for the profession as a whole, climbing five percent in 1991 to $231,000. Income figures also edged up, Altman Weil said.
The figures in both surveys may paint a very different picture from the experiences of individual lawyers in many law firms, especially the top 100. Most large law firms are acting more and more like the profit-minded businesses they serve. Lawyers are more likely to be fired or forced into early retirement than in the past. Those that stay are more likely to be paid based on how they perform.
 
Stability Among the Largest
 "But there's remarkable stability among the firms as a group," Mr. Brill said, noting that 90 of the top 100 were also on the list five years ago. Mr. Brill said that figures were also more reliable than ever, with about 90 percent of the firms giving the magazine "the equivalent of tax returns." In the other cases, which the magazine did not identify, it made estimates based on interviews with partners.
One of the firms that the magazine said declined to provide figures was Skadden, Arps, Slate, Meagher & Flom, which the survey has annually ranked as the top revenue producer. The American Lawyer estimated that Skadden, Arps's total revenue slipped to $490 million, from $503 million in 1990, with profits averaging $925,000 for each of 216 partners.
"These are changing times," said Peter Mullen, the firm's managing partner. "We are all trying to keep our fees down."
Mr. Mullen dismissed the survey as "of little interest," saying that Skadden, Arps preferred to participate in an annual Price Waterhouse survey that separates law firms by size, provides average performance figures within groups, and does not name any firms.
 
 
Closely Scrutinized Listing
 But others, including headhunters who specialize in the legal profession, say the American Lawyer survey is the most closely scrutinized in the industry.
"Clearly, law firms make their numbers as impressive as they can, but people believe they are accurate enough to make them useful," said Mitchell Berger, president of Howard-Sloan Legal Search Inc., a New York placement service. "Law firms use them in recruiting. Lawyers looking to move to another firm also use them."
The revenues of the top 100 are a rough measure of the economy in their regions, but many of the firms generate much of their business in Washington or other cities outside their home base. An extreme case is Baker & McKenzie, based in Chicago, which has 484 partners and nearly 1,100 associates, about 90 percent of whom are based in other cities.
Increasingly, the big firms' distant lawyers are overseas. Baker & McKenzie, a leader in the trend, has 44 foreign offices. Skadden, Arps has 12 foreign offices, less than five years after opening its first one, in Japan. Mr. Brill described legal services as a successful export.
 
Lawyers' Capital: New York
 No matter how the data are sliced, New York continues to be the nation's legal powerhouse. One third of the top 100 are based here. New York and Chicago, home of 14 of the top 100, account for nearly half the list.
Skadden and 14 other New York firms make up the top 15, as ranked by revenue per lawyer, a reflection of how active they are and how much they charge. New York firms also fill 14 of the top 15 slots in profit per partner, ranging from top-ranked Cravath's $1.46 million to Paul, Weiss, Rifkind, Wharton & Garrison's $580,000. The lone interloper at New York's profits banquet, Kirland & Ellis, of Chicago, ranked 10th at $710,000.
 
New York a Pro-Bono Laggard
 New York is an also-ran, though, when it comes to the survey's assessment of how much free work lawyers perform for clients who are unable to pay.
All lawyers, regardless of work load, are expected to participate in or support such "pro bono" work, according to the American Bar Association's Model Rules of Professional Conduct. Among the cities with more than one Top 100 firm, San Francisco and Washington stand out as leaders in pro-bono work, according to the survey.
The pro-bono rankings are in the form of grades, running A through F. Morrison & Foerster, a San Francisco firm that ranks 15th in revenue, is the biggest money producer to receive an A. Among the anomalies was Cravath, the most profitable firm in the nation, which went from an A in pro-bono ratings in 1990 to an F in 1991.
Mr. Brill conceded that the pro-bono ratings were the most speculative part of the survey, because many firms did not keep accurate records and there were disagreements about what kinds of activities qualified. As a result, some firms that have relatively good pro-bono records receive F's because they do not provide information on them.

CORRECTION-DATE: July 2, 1992, Thursday

 CORRECTION:

It came from Skadden, Arps itself, not from American Lawyer, which does not disclose whether firms respond.

HEADLINE: Lining Up to Inspect an Old Friend

BYLINE: By CAROL VOGEL

 BODY:
At 9:15 A.M. yesterday, 45 minutes before the Solomon R. Guggenheim Museum opened its newly expanded and refurbished Frank Lloyd Wright building to the public, only about a dozen people lolled around the entrance on Fifth Avenue, near 89th Street.
"When the line forms I'm No. 1!," shrieked Karen Wehman, a city planner from Chicago, who had flown to New York especially for the occasion and had been waiting since 6:15 A.M. "I grew up around Frank Lloyd Wright buildings, and this is an esthetic inspection. I got here early because I figured this would be a major New York happening." 
But most of the early arrivals sat quietly along the edge of the museum's planters, soaking up the sun, reading the Sunday paper and sipping coffee. After a week of glittering parties and nonstop media hype, the crowd to see the $45 million renovation -- a seven-year project that closed the museum for two and a half years -- took a while to grow.
"I'm surprised it's so quiet," said William Weiss, a New York advertising executive who said he was disappointed that he was only No. 2 in line. "When the Museum of Modern Art opened in June of '84 I was No. 6," he recalled. "But if the Whitney ever undergoes its renovation, when it reopens I'll make sure I'm No. 1."
 
'Our Favorite Museum'
 About 10:30 the line began snaking around both sides of the museum. By 5 P.M. museum officials estimated that about 5,000 people had visited the museum, and they were hoping for 1,000 more before the 8 P.M. close. People came from Hawaii and Canada, the Netherlands and Tokyo. For some, the opportunity to be one of the first to walk the famous spiral ramp again seemed irresistible. "It doesn't really matter what art they're showing, it's our favorite museum because of the architecture," said Ronald Richler, a lawyer from Toronto.
Even neighbors couldn't wait to get inside. "I've been watching the building change every day," said Mary Englander, who lives nearby and was in line with her husband, Alan. "I loved the building as it was and I'm anxious to see just how useful this new addition is."
Bernard Gross, a retired professor of economics from Baruch College, remembers when the Wright building first opened in 1959. "At the original opening the concept of modern art was still in the process of development," he said. "It was a great source of controversy. The lines went on for a mile, in part because the architecture itself created such a fuss."
But this time, the museum's officials are quick to point out, there were two entrances and six cashiers to accept the $7 admission fees, as compared with one entrance and one person handling admission in the old days. The museum has also doubled its capacity. Gail Harrity, the deputy director for finance and administration, said the building could hold about 3,500 people.
Inside the rotunda, some viewers were perplexed by the installation of fluorescent lights by the Minimalist artist Dan Flavin. "It seems kind of empty," said Lucette Selman, a New Yorker. Christof Hahn, a German musician who lives in Arizona, said he was surprised that there were no paintings in the famous rotunda space.
 
Starting at the Top
 Visitors approached the building's galleries in different ways. Some started on the ground floor and worked their way up. "We did it the old-fashioned way: we started at the top," said Alan Englander. "We were disappointed. Some of the new galleries seemed cramped. Even the sculpture terrace looked small. Still, it's nice to have the museum back."
Thomas Krens, the museum's director, chose not to mingle with the crowds, but was closeted in the new cafe, hanging historical photographs of the Guggenheim from the museum's archives. "I'm making the selection myself," Mr. Krens said. The director said the cafe would not open until tomorrow because of a delay in fabricating the large aluminum counter intended by Wright but only now being made for the museum.
The lack of the cafe may or may not have had something to do with the bustle around the hot dog stand at the corner of 89th Street and Fifth Avenue. "I have been here since 1976 and my father ran the business before that," said the vendor, Futios A. Velis. "When the museum closed I gave them $1,000 toward the renovation. The building is looking new and beautiful. Today I've made good money, so I'm happy."

CORRECTION-DATE: July 2, 1992, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
An article on Monday about opening-day visitors to the newly expanded Solomon R. Guggenheim Museum in Manhattan referred incorrectly to Karen Wehman, a visitor from Chicago. She is a writer who specializes in city and regional planning.

HEADLINE: Dow Up 37.45 on Expectations of Rate Cut

BYLINE: Reuters

 BODY:
Wall Street posted its sharpest gains since early May yesterday, as many investors bet that interest rates would drop soon.
The Dow Jones industrial average rose 37.45 points, to close at 3,319.86. Advancing issues led declining ones by a solid 1,221 to 581 on the New York Stock Exchange. 
 The rally was broad based and included secondary stocks as well as blue-chip issues. Over-the-counter stocks also surged, with the Nasdaq composite index gaining 10.96 points, or 2 percent, to close at 558.80. Big Board volume totaled 176.8 million shares, compared with 154.8 million on Friday.
 
Bonds Gain, Too
 Yesterday's strong rally was set off by speculation that the Federal Reserve would respond to continued signs of economic weakness by lowering interest rates, possibly as soon as its policy-making Open Market Committee begins its meetings today.
This speculation also pushed bond prices moderately higher, and short-term interest rates fell to their lowest level in more than two decades.
"People took a little comfort that bonds were up," said John Blair, head of trading at County Natwest. But he added, "I don't think it's the beginning of a rally."
But others disagreed. "The market tends to go up before a long weekend," said Alfred Goldman, chief market strategist at A. G. Edwards. "I think it will trade modestly higher over the next four to six weeks," he said, adding that recent high levels of pessimism suggested a rise was likely.
Hugh Johnson, chief investment officer at the First Albany Corporation, said that despite yesterday's surge, "the real fireworks for this week will have to wait" until after the Fed's Open Market Committee meeting and the employment report on Thursday.
Mr. Goldman said that while he was encouraged by the market's performance, "the only disappointment is that volume is moderate, which reduces the credibility of the lift."
Glaxo Holdings led the Big Board volume list, down 1, to 25 1/8. Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette cut its rating to neutral from buy.
Shares of DFSoutheastern jumped 9 1/2, to 28. It agreed to be acquired by First Union in an exchange of stock. First Union shares slipped 3/4, to 38.
Waste Management fell 2 3/4, to 34 3/8. Citing the weak economy, the company said it expected second-quarter earnings to be below analysts' expectations. Prudential Securities lowered its earnings estimates for the company.
 
Medical Issues
 Some medical-related stocks staged a comeback. Medtronic rose 3 5/8, to 80 1/4; United States Surgical added 5 1/4, to 96 3/8; Medical Care International added 2 1/2, to 50 1/4; United Health rose 3 7/8, to 81 3/4, and Critical Care added 2 1/4, to 36 7/8.
 Shares of Microsoft jumped 5 1/2, to 72 1/4. Shearson Lehman Brothers repeated a buy rating, citing continued strong fundamentals.
The Standard & Poor's composite index of 500 stocks rose 5.49 points, to 408.94. The American Stock Exchange index closed up 3.06, at 377.10.
The New York Stock Exchange composite index rose 2.76 points, to 224.44.
The Wilshire Associates Equity Index -- the market value of New York, American and over-the-counter issues -- was up 51.01 points, or 1.32 percent, to 3,926.61.

HEADLINE: Argentine Chief Suffers Setback as His Senate Candidate Loses

BYLINE: By NATHANIEL C. NASH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BUENOS AIRES, June 29

 BODY:
President Carlos Saul Menem suffered a political setback on Sunday when voters in the Argentine capital, apparently upset over continued scandal involving Government officials, gave his Peronist party's candidate for the Senate just 32 percent of the vote, while the opposition Radical Party candidate won with 50 percent.
At the same time, in the first municipal elections held in Chile since the military coup of 1973 that brought Gen. Augusto Pinochet to power, mayoral and city council candidates supported by the centrist President, Patricio Aylwin, won 53 percent of the vote on Sunday, compared with 30 percent for right-wing parties. 
 Political analysts said the outcome of the elections buttressed the two-year-old return to democracy in Chile, and that in Argentina it represented a warning to President Menem that there was growing unease about his apparent inability to stem corruption.
An elated Mr. Aylwin said his coalition Government now "appears to be the only solid alternative of government, and the electorate showed the tremendous inclination on the part of Chileans not to return to conservative positions."
 
Constitutional Changes Sought
 Analysts in Chile also said Mr. Aylwin was now likely to push Congress hard to change major parts of Chile's Constitution. He wants the authority to remove General Pinochet as head of the army, to dismiss eight Senators appointed for life by General Pinochet before he left office in 1990 and to restructure the National Security Council, which is a military stronghold.
The Constitution was written by General Pinochet in 1980 and accepted by opposition politicians as a condition for elections.
One indication that President Aylwin might be successful came from Andres Allamand, president of the National Renovation party, a center-right group that has defended some Pinochet policies. He said his party would be willing to discuss constitutional reforms.
But members of the Union for Democratic Independence, strong supporters of General Pinochet, said they would oppose such changes.
Of the 7.8 million eligible voters in Chile, 90 percent turned out. Voting is mandatory, and those who refused can be fined $25 to $125.
The country voted for 2,082 mayors and council members in 334 cities and towns. During the 17-year Pinochet rule, municipal officials were appointed.
When asked about the elections after he had voted, General Pinochet said, "I have fulfilled my civic duty, period."
In Argentina, while the vote involved only one Senate position, it was one of the first political setbacks for President Menem, who campaigned hard for his candidate, former Health Minister Avelino Porto.
But analysts said the vote was not against Mr. Menem's economic recovery plan. The Radical Party candidate and winner, Fernando de la Rua, campaigned on issues of corruption and morality, and never made Menem economic policies an issue. Those policies have been widely praised for reducing deficits and inflation and creating a stable economy that has attracted foreign investment.
In the last year, Argentina has re-established lines of credit with the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund, and also reached agreement with creditor banks to reduce its overall debt by more than 30 percent.
But corruption continues to dog Mr. Menem, with members of his Government repeatedly accused of wrongdoing. Several have been forced to resign.
Also, the public seems concerned about the degree of political power Mr. Menem now wields, in effect controlling Congress and the courts.
"The public likes the economic plan, but it wants more control over corruption and the overall political equilibrium," said Felipe Noguera, a political analyst and poll taker here.

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
Brazilian Stocks Plunge
 BRASILIA, June 29 (Reuters) -- Prices plunged by more than one-seventh of their total value today on Brazil's main stock exchange as leaders of the principal political party backing the beleaguered President, Fernando Collor de Mello, tried to quell rumors that they would leave the Cabinet over a corruption scandal.
Mr. Collor has been enmeshed in crisis over allegations that a former close aide set up a network of allies in the Government to steal money and sell favors.
Today, shares on the Sao Paulo Stock Exchange fell 14.7 percent. and the central bank was forced to intervene heavily to prevent the cruzeiro, the nation's fragile currency, from falling too precipitously against the dollar.
