HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN;
Long Fight for Local Support Hampers Bush on Education

SERIES: The Bush Record -- Fifth article of a series

BYLINE: By SUSAN CHIRA, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MEMPHIS

 BODY:
Four years after George Bush took office proclaiming himself to be the "education President," he can point to few tangible accomplishments that have improved the nation's schools.
But President Bush and his aides do claim credit for starting a movement called America 2000 that they say will force fundamental changes in American education. It has spawned local education reform drives like one here in Memphis and in more than 1,000 other communities across the country. 
 Memphis is a laboratory for Mr. Bush's contention that the answer to American educational ills lies not with Federal Government programs but with local initiative.
Asked in a recent interview to name his most significant domestic initiatives, the President's very first response was: "I think clearly the education initiative," and he pointed specifically to the "many America 2000 communities" across the country.
 
The Bedrock Assumptions
 His strategy assumes that American schools will be transformed, not with more money but through vouchers that allow parents to use public funds to pay private school tuitions, through local community efforts like Memphis's, and through the use of innovative new schools as well as national tests and curriculum standards.
Yet from educators and others on the right and the left come the same complaints that dog Mr. Bush in many other areas of domestic policy as well -- that he has articulated lofty conservative principles but has failed to rally the nation behind them or to follow through so his plans are actually put into action.
"The President has talked a good game," said Elsie Lewis Bailey, principal of Booker T. Washington High School in Memphis and a leading member of the drive to improve the city's schools. "But I can't applaud anything at this point that he has done to make a difference."
Mrs. Bailey's school stands amid housing projects where students fight daily battles with drugs and death. Washington High is a stark illustration of the policy rift between Mr. Bush and his critics. The President assumes that education problems are best solved through a process like the one under way here, in which parents and communities take the responsibility to encourage academic achievement and counter social breakdown.
But many educators believe the ills of American schools require a larger Federal plan, a detailed urban agenda and a great deal more money than Mr. Bush shows any sign of wanting to spend.
These educators say there is little hope that Washington High's students can conquer the poverty and despair that are the blight of so many inner-city lives until the Government also offers broader initiatives to counter the urban ills that so often lead to school failure. Chief among them are drugs, street violence, inadequate health care, glaring inequities between rich and poor school districts and broken families.
At Washington High, Fredderick Dupree, Sharon Page and Kalinda Garner had little trouble listing what their school does not have: a chemistry or biology laboratory, enough athletic equipment, up-to-date textbooks.
"We have a lot of honor students here, and they do want to be somebody," said Fredderick, who hopes for a career in math, science or medicine. "When you don't have the equipment, it just saddens you."
Even though all three students are excelling in school, in many ways they are exceptions. They watch as their neighbors and classmates struggle and, in some cases, succumb to poverty, teen-age pregnancy, parental neglect and violence.
"From what we see, people are dying every day," Fredderick said. "You don't know when you might go."
 
Vision
A 'Summit' Yields National Goals
 President Bush gets praise from educators for the first concrete action he took as education President: an "education summit" in Charlottesville, Va., in 1989 at which he and the nation's governors agreed on six broad, national goals. These include making sure all children are healthy and intellectually stimulated enough to start school; catapulting American students from near the bottom among industrialized nations to the top in world math and science achievement; raising the high school graduation rate from about 75 percent to 90 percent; erasing illiteracy and making schools safe and drug-free.
But educators waited nearly two years for clues about how President Bush thought Americans could achieve these goals. Then, in March 1991, Mr. Bush appointed Lamar Alexander as Secretary of Education. While Governor of Tennessee, he won praise for trying to improve Tennessee's schools. In Washington, Mr. Alexander quickly drew up the new education strategy.
The Federal Government has never spent much money on education -- it now pays only 6 percent of all public education costs, from kindergarten through high school, a drop of almost 40 percent from the level before President Ronald Reagan took office. But even without a White House pledge of major new spending, most educators welcomed Mr. Bush's emphasis on parental responsibility, local community action and curriculum standards.
At the same time, though, critics have attacked Mr. Bush's plan, saying it depends too heavily on model schools while neglecting inner-city issues. In fact, even his advisers concede that Mr. Bush's overall approach to education works best for his core Republican constituency -- white, middle-class, suburban families.
"Crisis one is middle-class kids who aren't learning much," said Chester E. Finn Jr., a key architect and still a backer of the Bush policy who is now helping to design a national network of private, for-profit schools organized by Christopher Whittle. "Crisis two is underclass kids for whom not learning much is just one of their problems. The same solutions don't apply to both. It may be that America 2000 over all is better tailored to crisis one, but that's probably two-thirds of American kids."
 
Behind the Voucher Plan
 Still, President Bush and his aides claim to be champions of the poor as well, asserting that their voucher proposal serves that end. Vouchers -- allowing parents to take their children out of public schools and use the money that would have been spent on them there to pay private school tuition -- is an idea dear to the Republican right wing.
In his current budget proposal, President Bush is asking for $500 million for vouchers of $1,000 that families with low or middle incomes could spend each year on public, private or parochial schools. The idea, Mr. Alexander says, is to give poor and middle-class families the same choices in schools that the well-off already have.
But Mr. Bush's endorsement of vouchers has also drawn vehement and well-organized opposition from those who believe it is nothing less than an attack on the very idea of public schooling. Critics believe it would skim off the best students with the savviest parents for private schools, leaving public schools to teach only the students who are handicapped, disruptive or neglected.
"This is a fundamental mistake, driven by ideological concerns," said Gov. Roy Romer of Colorado, a Democrat who has led the governors' efforts on education improvement and who has worked closely with the White House on education policy. "It's like saying we're going to shock you into change by taking your best students and resources out of here."
 
Reality
Needed: Cash And Consensus
 While President Bush and his aides say his greatest legacy will be the long-range education plan, they also point to some increases in spending. The Education Department's budget has increased by 22 percent after inflation during his term, and Federal spending has risen for college tuition grants as well as for math and science programs.
The biggest increases have gone to the Head Start preschool program. During his first campaign, President Bush pledged to finance Head Start so all eligible 4-year-olds could take part. He has asked for and got the largest increases in Head Start spending since the program was founded in 1965 -- the budget has risen by 78 percent since he took office -- and he is requesting another 27 percent increase for next year. By the Administration's estimate, this will create enough slots to serve all eligible children for one year, instead of the 60 percent the Administration says it now serves.
But critics say the program has always been designed to serve 3-year-olds as well as 4-year-olds. And so, they add, the program now serves only 28 percent of those eligible.
Mr. Bush's supporters also say the Government has made progress in some other areas of the President's overall education plan. Education specialists in and out of government, using $5 million in Government money, are drawing up broad standards in several subjects that will lay down what students should know by certain points in their academic careers.
But many of Mr. Bush's key proposals have died in Congress. Democratic majorities have refused to approve vouchers, and there seems to be little chance they will do so soon. Congress has turned down a request for $500 million to begin operating 535 new showcase schools because Democrats believe that creating such schools will not improve the 110,000 existing public schools.
Even conservative organizations, though they praise the President's ideas, say Mr. Bush has failed to do what leaders must: drum up enough support for his proposals.
"The stuff on paper is great," said Stuart M. Butler, director of domestic policy studies for the Heritage Foundation. "But there is absolutely no follow-through politically with any of these items. It's a deficiency of the White House in general in the domestic area."
Democratic aides on Capitol Hill who negotiated with the Administration on the President's education proposals say officials essentially gave up on Congress when they realized they would have to compromise. "I think they wanted 100 percent, and when they couldn't get 100 percent, they just sort of walked away from it," said one Democratic Senate aide.
Mr. Alexander, who is normally quite genial, stiffens when he hears such talk. He tells of deals that fell through when Democratic legislators got pressure from teachers' union leaders and other educators.
"By instinct, I'm a big coalition builder," he said. "Right now, with the current composition and leadership of Congress, it's almost a waste of my time."
Mr. Butler says the President should have joined the battle himself.
"There's a very stark alternative -- Lyndon Johnson," Mr. Butler said. "He put the political finger on people and said, 'These people are holding up a solution to these problems.' "
 
Commitment in Memphis
 Mr. Bush's defenders say the view from Washington is skewed, because the real results of his policies can be seen in places like Memphis.
As for the Bush plan, Mr. Finn said, "It seems to me in a year's time to have done much of what it was intended to do. It's had a consciousness-raising effect, a catalytic effect on a lot of states and communities to at least take the pledge."
At the same time, he added, "I don't know how much of an effect it's had so far on real practice."
By the Education Department's count, 44 states and 1,200 communities have agreed to draw up plans to improve their schools. Mr. Alexander says this is the vanguard of a "populist crusade" that will transform public education.
Others are more skeptical. Albert Shanker, president of the American Federation of Teachers, dismisses all of it as a "dog and pony show," a series of pep rallies that cost nothing but make people feel as if they are doing something.
Memphis shows both the possibilities and the limits of this approach. A bipartisan, multiracial coalition of 850 local leaders and citizens is analyzing what ails the city's schools and how to change them. Many, like Mrs. Bailey, refused to join unless the effort specifically repudiated the Bush voucher plan. They got that assurance and hope to have a final plan this fall.
Everyone who has worked on "Memphis 2000" praises it as serious and broad-based. The question is whether the well-meaning effort will actually improve the schools.
Mayor Willie Herenton, who served as Memphis's superintendent of schools for 12 years and was an early skeptic, said his test will be whether this coalition calls for higher taxes for education. Memphis spends $3,568 per pupil, well below the national average of $5,237. And leaders of the Memphis drive say they now have a powerful new coalition that would campaign for more money if the schools adopt the changes they want.
In Washington, Mr. Alexander has made no estimates or promises, other than to say that more money might come from the Federal Government if the America 2000 communities can present a good case.
 
Prospects
What Investment Beyond Money?
 In the end, the judgment on Mr. Bush's education record will rest on how to define what an education President should do.
Many educators believe part of the answer is more money. But President Bush has consistently denounced spending more on a system he sees as flawed.
"Putting money first -- we did all that in the 1980's, and it didn't work," Mr. Alexander said.
Overall spending on education at all levels of government has actually risen 33 percent in inflation-adjusted terms over the last decade, without dramatic improvements in American schools. But as the Federal share of that spending has shrunk, other cuts in Federal aid to cities and states for social programs have also left them less able to pay for education.
Mr. Bush claims that a President should provide leadership, not money. He does win wide praise for trying to shake Americans from their complacency, issuing a long-overdue call to parents and local communities to take their share of responsibility.
But others say vigorous leadership in education demands much more than President Bush has given. An education President, they say, should urge voters to approve local bond issues or new taxes if schools present a convincing reform plan. And they add that the crisis in American education demands a bolder, more ambitious Federal plan.
"I believe so much in the need to go to grass-roots America," said Governor Romer. "But it doesn't do anything for people to go out and get them all ginned up and then give them no design to proceed. There's not enough substance to it. You need to have a President out talking not about those generalities, but who's going to pay for what and who's going to do each piece of it, what's our time line and our priority, and I want to put that above a Sea Wolf and a bomber."
Critics as far apart politically as Governor Romer and Mr. Butler of the Heritage Foundation say that President Bush comes up short partly because he seems unable to galvanize the electorate the way Ronald Reagan did.
But his defenders say Mr. Bush just isn't being heard.
"If anyone will listen to President Bush on education," Mr. Alexander said, "he has a wonderful vision."

HEADLINE: U.S. to Ease Push for Policy Shifts by Germany and Japan

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
After long pressuring Germany and Japan to do more to stimulate world growth, Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady said today that the United States would not prod them so hard at the economic summit meeting in Munich because they seem ready to adopt policies that Washington has favored.
With the United States plagued by slow growth for more than two years, Mr. Brady had been pushing other major industrial nations to focus more on spurring growth, not only to help increase American exports but to help bolster the economies of the former Soviet bloc and the developing world. 
 In a news briefing today about the meeting of the seven largest industrial democracies in Munich next week, Mr. Brady said he was pleased that Japan's ruling party said it would stimulate that nation's faltering economy through increased Government spending and other measures worth more than $45 billion in the current fiscal year. Mr. Brady called this move "substantial" and said "it certainly addresses growth."
Mr. Brady also praised the German Government for indicating that it would hold spending growth to 2.5 percent or less next year. American officials have clashed with Germany in recent months after Washington called on Germany to reduce its budget deficit to give Germany's central bank some leeway to reduce its interest rates, which have helped push up rates elsewhere in Europe. German officials responded angrily to Washington's pressures, saying it was hypocritical for the United States to scold others about budget deficits.
Today, Mr. Brady talked as if his differences with Bonn were a thing of the past. He praised Bonn's promised spending discipline, saying "That is the first step to bringing down interest rates."
Mr. Brady said aid to Russia would be one of the main topics at the summit meeting, which will bring together the leaders of the Group of Seven industrial nations from July 6 to 8. President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia will be flying to Munich to meet with the Group of Seven leaders.
The seven nations are the United States, Britain, Canada, France, Germany, Italy and Japan.
In briefings today, American and Canadian officials said the leaders would also discuss the fighting in Yugoslavia, the environment, debt relief for developing nations and aid to the other former Soviet republics.
At the summit, the Group of Seven leaders are expected to approve a vast aid package to help the former Communist nations make their nuclear reactors safer.
Mr. Brady said he also hoped that the summit meeting would give some impetus to stalled world trade talks, known as the Uruguay Round. The talks are deadlocked because of a dispute between the United States and the European Community over how much to cut subsidies to farmers.
"If I could wave a magic wand over one problem" at the summit, Mr. Brady said. "I'd wave it over the Uruguay Round."

HEADLINE: Washington at Work;
Administration's Regulation Slayer Has Achieved a Perilous Prominence

BYLINE: By KEITH SCHNEIDER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
In an airy office down the hall from the Vice President's, an Indiana lawyer with dark eyes and dawn-to-dusk work habits basks nervously in the limelight of power.
At the age of 34, David M. McIntosh has amassed great influence in the loftiest councils of the White House, and his ideas, praised by industrialists and vilified by conservationists, have lifted the President's environmental policies to the top of the domestic and international agenda. 
 In Washington's power stakes, Mr. McIntosh is hot. As executive director of the President's Council on Competitiveness, chaired by Vice President Dan Quayle, he is the leader of the Bush Administration's sweeping assault on environmental regulations and on the public's authority to intervene in Government decisions on natural resources.
 
Regulation Slayer
 It was Mr. McIntosh who championed the Administration's move last week to give companies broad rights to increase their emissions of air pollution. The new rule was vintage McIntosh.
Last summer he wrote the Administration's proposal to open half the nation's wetlands to development. The rule has been held up because of disputes in the Administration over the provision.
It was he who developed the legal and philosophical basis for the President's decision in January to halt new regulations and rewrite old ones to make them friendlier to American industry. The impact has affected dozens of regulations to date.
Last month, the competitiveness council proposed easing restrictions on testing and marketing genetically engineered crops and organisms.
Mr. McIntosh was also at the center of a group of young White House aides who proposed removing restrictions on storing and disposing of 45 percent of the nation's hazardous wastes.
 
Enmeshed in Rio Leak
 But in the heave and surge that characterizes policy making in Washington, only the slimmest barrier separates being hot from getting burned. Though Mr. McIntosh is virtually unknown outside the Government, his prominence within it has generated resentment, envy, even fury among his enemies, who include prominent Democrats in Congress and the Administrator of the Environmental Protection Agency, William K. Reilly.
Earlier this month, Mr. McIntosh found himself at the center of an international furor that threatened, at least momentarily, to halt his ascent. Mr. Reilly, the chief United States delegate at the Earth Summit in Rio de Janeiro, sent a confidential message to Mr. Bush urging him to reverse his position and sign a treaty to protect wild species.
The President was deeply embarrassed when the message was made available to The New York Times by Mr. Reilly's opponents, who thought he might succeed. And Mr. Reilly accused the Vice President's office and the Council on Competitiveness of being the source.
Mr. McIntosh denied the allegation, and Bill Kristol, the Vice President's chief of staff, called it "totally false." But the blowup, which revealed the struggle between conservatives and moderates in the Administration, brought tension to the formerly confident council. Mr. Bush called the disclosure "insidious" and ordered the Department of Justice to investigate. Mr. McIntosh, who had freely expressed his views to reporters, was ordered not to grant interviews or make public statements.
Mr. Reilly made the accusation because Mr. McIntosh was the principal author of an April memorandum that persuaded President Bush not to sign the treaty because it would harm American biotechnology companies. The decision earned the United States international condemnation at the Earth Summit, isolating it from its closest allies.
 
'A Rational, Objective View'
 In an interview before the disclosure, Mr. McIntosh carefully chose his words. "We have a small staff, seven or eight people," he said. "What we are trying to do, to the extent possible, is looking at issues and deciding what to do based on a rational, objective view of the facts. Too often, policies have been undertaken that are not based on a rational view.
"You get a scare about cyclamates years ago, so saccharin is brought in as a replacement. That turns out to be worse for you, but you have a political backlash and can't do anything about it. To some extent you can't avoid that. Politics is bringing the public to a position to support your policy initiative. It's imprecise."
Mr. McIntosh's work is much admired among the executives, landowners and other conservative groups the White House is courting to shore up political support before the November election. But environmental leaders and consumer groups have loudly condemned the closed manner that characterizes the council, and they accuse Mr. McIntosh of being the Administration's chief lobbyist for big business.
Last week, the House Appropriations Committee voted 30 to 18 to shut down the council by preventing the White House from spending any money in fiscal 1993 on salaries for Mr. McIntosh and his staff. The full House is scheduled to vote on the issue, perhaps as early as Tuesday.
"The council has become an off-the-record, no-fingerprints operation for subverting necessary and appropriate regulatory activity of the Federal Government," said David E. Skaggs, a Democrat of Colorado, who proposed the measure.
 
Early Admirer of Quayle
 Mr. McIntosh says the spark that ignited his career was a 1976 speech in Kendallville, Ind., by a young Congressional candidate named Dan Quayle.
"There was this bright young man running against somebody older that I didn't really relate to as a young kid," said Mr. McIntosh. "I remember him focusing on the need to make Government more accountable to the people and not have unelected representatives here making decisions for people back in Indiana. That appealed to me because it meant more self control. It's what got me interested in politics."
But it hardly explains Mr. McIntosh's mastery of his job. He spends hours reading documents, gathering facts and pursuing a single goal: to loosen the environmental regulations enacted in the last 25 years.
In some cases, that means rewriting Federal laws, including a core provision of the Endangered Species Act and wetlands provisions of the Clean Water Act. In other cases, it means delaying new rules considered too costly for business.
 
Smart and a Little Mean
 Mr. McIntosh, the dutiful aide, said the council's influence is entirely owed to the support of the Vice President and the group's other members, especially Richard G. Darman, the Budget director, and Michael J. Boskin, the chairman of the Council of Economic Advisers.
To a considerable extent, though, the Council's influence is due to Mr. McIntosh. With his blue suits cut a touch too big for a thin frame, prematurely grey streaks in his hair, and a disarmingly polite manner, he hardly cuts an imposing figure. But allies and enemies in the Administration say that beneath the Middle Western reserve is a man with a prodigious intellect, keenly focused ideas and just enough of a mean streak to make life uncomfortable for those he considers disloyal to the Administration.
Equally important, say his friends and opponents in the Administration, is his careful, steady cultivation of like-minded aides. From his office overlooking the White House's West Wing, Mr. McIntosh has put himself at the center of a circle of conservative influence in the Federal buildings that ring the Washington Mall.
Among his allies are Teresa Gorman, special assistant for environment and energy policy development in the White House; Linda G. Stuntz, who was recently promoted to deputy secretary of energy; Tom Weimer, the chief of staff for Interior Secretary Manuel Lujan Jr.; and Arthur Fraas, chief of natural resources at the White House Office of Information and Regulatory Affairs.
 
Portrait of a Conservative
 Born in Oakland, Calif., the oldest of four children, Mr. McIntosh was 5 years old when he moved with his mother to her Indiana hometown of Kendallville after his father died of pancreatic cancer. After graduating from Yale University, he earned a law degree at the University of Chicago, where he studied under some of the nation's most prominent conservative scholars, including Antonin Scalia, the Supreme Court Justice.
In 1986, he joined the Justice Department briefly before being named a special assistant to President Ronald Reagan. In 1989 he was hired by Vice President Quayle as deputy counsel, then was named deputy director of the Council on Competitiveness. In January he became its executive director.
"What we are trying to do is what President Bush says he wants done: to get environmental protection and at the same time to encourage economic growth," said Mr. McIntosh. "It means that we need to change the debate about how we get the most environmental protection for the limited resources this society can spend on it."

Hobbies Hiking; skiing; sailing.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
A Busy '92 for Wall St. Underwriters

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON Jr.

 BODY:
Wall Street's money-raising bonanza extended through this year's second quarter, as the value of stocks and bonds taken to market again topped $200 billion and set a record.
Domestic underwriting in the three-month period that ends today reached $212.9 billion, up 53 percent from the $139.5 billion raised in 1991's second quarter, and just higher than the previous record of $210 billion in offerings that hit the market in the first three months this year, IDD Information Services reported. 
 With interest rates low, corporations across the country have been taking advantage of the seemingly endless public appetite for stocks and bonds. Initial public offerings dropped in the second quarter, but the demand for refinancing debt by companies did not, and may continue to keep bond underwriters busy for the rest of the year.
 
'Markets Aren't Always Logical'
 "Logic dictates that it has to drop off at some point," said John Keefe, a securities analyst and the president of Keefe Worldwide Information Services. "But markets aren't always logical. It almost seems a freak of nature that the numbers are so close" in the first and second quarters.
Over the first six months of the year, total domestic underwriting reached a new high of $422 billion, a stunning 69 percent increase over the $249 billion of securities issued in 1991's first half.
Underwriting fees earned by Wall Street in the second quarter reached $1.67 billion, only a small step down from the $1.78 billion earned in the first quarter.
Merrill Lynch clung to its position as Wall Street's dominant underwriter, with 17.1 percent of the market's domestic issues. Lehman Brothers was in second position with 14 percent, and Goldman Sachs was in third at 13.7 percent.
In underwriting fees, Goldman Sachs led Merrill Lynch, earning $308.3 million compared with $247.3 million. Lehman Brothers was third with $217.3 million.
Common stock offerings reached $19.2 million, compared with $21.2 billion in the year's first quarter. The recent drop in stock prices and in the market's receptivity to initial public offerings of stock may spell a slowdown in stock underwriting.
"June was certainly a difficult month for I.P.O's," said Thomas W. Davis, head of equity capital markets at Merrill Lynch. "But the quarter was strong over all. Volume will probably come off some in the third quarter."
For the quarter, initial public offerings totaled $7.9 billion, down from the $10.4 billion in 1992's first quarter and almost on a par with $8 billion in 1991's second quarter.
Debt markets, where the vast majority of cash is raised on Wall Street, still seem ready to swallow a lot more corporate issues.
"There is still a lot of refinancing to be done," said Edson Mitchell, head of Merrill Lynch's global debt financing division. "We think the second half of the year could be even stronger than the first."
Mortgage-backed securities reached $98.1 billion, only slightly lower than the first quarter's record $98.9 billion.
Junk bond issues grew sharply, raising $11.6 billion, compared with only $2.5 billion in the corresponding period a year ago, and $7.5 billion in 1992's first quarter.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
A Slower Flow Into Stock Funds

BYLINE: By Floyd Norris

 BODY:
THE stock market has gone nowhere fast this year, but only now are investors finally beginning to slow their headlong rush into stock mutual funds.
Several fund groups said yesterday that June had been the worst month of the year in terms of drawing cash into stock funds, and Fidelity Investments, the largest of the fund groups, reported seeing a significant reduction in investor demand in the last week. 
 During the first five months of 1992, a total of $34.7 billion poured into stock funds, nearly matching 1991's record annual total of $38.4 billion, according to figures released yesterday by the Investment Company Institute. The May figure of $6.6 billion was down from April's record inflow of $7.5 billion, but was still greater than any month prior to last December, when the flood of cash soared as share prices leaped.
For June, which ends today, some fund groups said yesterday they had sharp falls from the levels that had been prevailing through May. "Equity funds were flat, with almost no net inflows," said Gavin Quill, a spokesman for Scudder, Stevens & Clark.
At T. Rowe Price, the amount of cash taken in by stock funds fell by about 40 percent from May, said Steve Norwitz, a spokesman for the fund group. "It is not a bad month by historical standards," he said, "but it is a little bit of a letdown compared to what we've gotten used to."
In late June, however, some fund groups have seen cash moving out of stock funds. AMG Data in Arcata, Calif., a research organization that receives reports from many funds, said that in the week ended last Wednesday, $1.1 billion was removed from the stock funds it covers. Weekly figures are volatile, but the number could indicate greater caution on the part of investors.
The burst of cash into stock funds that began late last year was stirred in large part by aggressive easing by the Federal Reserve, which cut the discount rate in December, setting off a strong stock market rally. Both rising stock prices and falling yields on bank accounts and money market funds spurred investors to move money into stock funds.
The December rally proved short-lived; most major stock averages have lost ground in 1992. But the cash flows did not slacken until recent weeks, as short-term interest rates continued to slide.
That slide in rates has also prompted many investors to move cash into bond funds, a flow that has continued. In May, $8 billion came into bond funds, up from $7.3 billion in April, and many fund groups said flows continued in June.
But Neal Litvack, a senior vice president of Fidelity, said that the last week also saw a softening of demand in the bond area.
Much of the demand in bond funds has been for funds that provide relatively good yields at little principal risk. Both T. Rowe Price and the TNE Fund Group, a subsidiary of the New England, said that adjustable-rate mortgage funds were among their best sellers.
Among stock funds, the biggest falloff in investor interest has been in aggressive growth funds, particularly those devoted to small stocks, which have generally performed poorly this year. At the Janus Group, "we had a small outflow of cash from the Janus Venture Fund" in June, said Chrissy Snyder, a spokeswoman.
Over all, aggressive growth funds had a net cash inflow of $951 million in May, the smallest for any month this year. The cash-flow figures include new sales minus redemptions, as well as cash moved between funds in the same fund group. But they exclude reinvested dividends.
Despite waning investor interest, the aggressive growth funds have lots of money to invest, reflecting the reluctance of money managers to invest the cash available. At the end of May, 10.3 percent of such funds were in cash, up from 10.2 percent a month earlier and 6.5 percent at the end of January. That bulge of cash, coupled with the growing short interest in small stocks, could provide a surge of buying power to accelerate a rally, were one to begin.
As interest in American stock funds has waned, interest in foreign ones has grown. The weakness of the dollar has aided performance of such funds, but even funds invested in markets that have been plunging have done well. The Japan Fund, managed by Scudder, took in more than $20 million in each of the last three months.
Over all, funds that solely or largely invest in foreign stocks had a net cash flow of $1 billion in May, the highest for any month since July 1990. The flow into funds oriented to domestic stocks was $5.6 billion, the lowest of any month this year.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Short-Term Rates Fall Sharply

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Fresh signs of economic weakness sent short-term interest rates to their lowest level in more than two decades yesterday on expectations that the Federal Reserve Board would soon ease its monetary policy.
The catalyst behind the drop was a report from the Government showing that new-home sales fell by 5.6 percent in May, to their lowest level since September. 
 The report stimulated bidding at the weekly auction of new three-month and six-month Treasury bills, pushing yields to levels not seen since the first half of 1972.
At the auction, three-month bills were sold at an average discount rate of 3.59 percent, down from an average rate of 3.67 percent a week ago. It is the lowest rate on a three-month bill since the auction of May 8, 1972, when three-month bills were sold at a rate of 3.46 percent.
Bids on the new six-month bills generated an average discount rate of 3.66 percent, down from 3.77 percent at last week's auction. It is the lowest rate on a six-month Treasury bill since the auction of Feb. 18, 1972, when six-month bills were auctioned at a rate of 3.51 percent.
 
Decline in Home Sales
 The drop in home sales was the fourth in a row, and puts new-home sales 20 percent below their January peak. Given the rise in housing starts during May, most analysts had been expecting that sales would show an increase.
"The housing report was disappointing," said Stuart G. Hoffman, a senior vice president and senior economist at the PNC Financial Corporation in Pittsburgh. "These sales numbers seem to contradict what the starts figures showed, and lead one to ask what will happen to housing starts going forward."
The report reinforced the notion among traders that the Fed will answer continued signs of weakness in the economy by once again lowering the overnight Federal funds rate.
"Every number we have gotten since the durable-goods report last week has been weaker than consensus estimates," one government bond trader said. "This is just another one in the same direction, and it is tilting expectations about a Fed easing move."
Speculation about the Fed and its intentions will remain strong throughout the week, since the central bank's policy-making Open Market Committee is scheduled to begin two days of meetings today.
Most of the trading, and rise in prices, was concentrated among securities with maturities of five years or less, continuing a pattern that has been in place for the last few weeks. Bond prices rose only modestly. Consequently, the gap, or spread, between short-term and long-term interest rates widened further.
"The short end is now priced for better than a 50 percent probability of Fed easing over the near term," analysts at MMS International, an economic information and service company, noted in their daily market commentary.
In the secondary market for Treasury securities, the 8 percent 29 1/2 year bonds of 2021 were offered late yesterday at a price of 102 27/32, up 1/8 of a point, to yield 7.76 percent, compared with 7.77 percent late Friday.
 
Outstanding Treasury Issues
 Among outstanding Treasury note issues, the 7 1/2 percent 10-year notes were offered at 102 26/32, up 1/4 of a point, to yield 7.09 percent. And the 7 percent seven-year notes were offered at 101 22/32, up 6/32, to yield 6.68 percent.
Issues auctioned last week continued to gain in when-issued trading.
The new 5 percent two-year notes were offered late yesterday at a price to yield 4.80 percent, compared with 4.88 percent late Friday. And the new 6 3/8 percent five-year notes were offered at a price to yield 6.26 percent, down from 6.32 percent at the end of last week.
A number of corporations chose to price new debt issues as the second quarter nears a close.
Among the issuers was Wisconsin Power and Light, which offered $72 million worth of 13-year first mortgage bonds through underwriters led by Goldman, Sachs & Company. The 7.60 percent noncallable securities were priced at $100, or par, to yield 49 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point, over the prevailing yield on the Treasury's 7 1/2 percent 10-year notes at the time of pricing. The securities are rated AA-2 by Moody's Investors Service and AA by the Standard & Poor's Corporation.
Demand for outstanding securities in the tax-exempt municipal bond market remained strong in anticipation of an influx of cash from the huge volume of bonds scheduled to be redeemed on Wednesday.
Analysts have estimated that as much as $12 billion worth of tax-exempt issues will be called on July 1. Traders said that prices of most widely quoted tax-exempt issues rose by about 1/8 of a point yesterday.

HEADLINE: New-Home Sales Fell 5.6% in May

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 29

 BODY:
Sales of new homes fell in May for the fourth straight month, the Government reported today. Economists said the decline provided more evidence that the housing industry had lost its usual role of leading the economy out of recession.
Sales dropped 5.6 percent in May to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 501,000, according to the departments of Commerce and Housing and Urban Development. That was the lowest level since September, when home sales slowed to a 499,000 rate. 
 In addition, the median price of a new home fell to its lowest level in almost five years.
 
 
No Further Lift?
 "This is confirmation that housing has given us all that it's going to give," contended Daryl Delano, a housing analyst with Cahners Economics in Newton, Mass. "The recovery will have to be sustained by improvements in other sectors."
Lyle Gramley, a former Federal Reserve governor who is now a consulting economist with the Mortgage Bankers Association, agreed.
"There is every reason to expect gradual improvement in the second half, and that will spill over into other areas of the economy," Mr. Gramley said. But, he added, "Housing will not be strong enough to drag the economy along with it."
The May decline in home sales was led by a 34.4 percent plunge in the West, the largest drop since the Government began keeping track of sales in 1973. Sales also fell in the Midwest, although they rose in the Northeast and in the South.
 Analysts were unable to explain the big drop in the West, but they saw it as another sign that the region -- particularly California -- would be the last to emerge from the recession.
They said that since the West generally had the highest housing costs, its sales drop contributed strongly to the 11.6 percent drop in the national median price of a new home, to $106,000. That was the lowest level since July 1987, when homes sold for a median of $105,000. The median means half of homes cost more and half less.
The report also indicated that sales activity in April was weaker than first estimated. Last month's report had showed a 1.3 percent advance in April, but that was revised today to a 2.7 percent decline. The department also revised the 15.9 percent decline in March to just 12.9 percent.
Kent Colton, executive vice president of the National Association of Home Builders, said his organization still thought "there will be a recovery in 1992 in new-home sales and starts."
But he added, "It will be a modest recovery."
 The home builders group is forecasting that sales will rise 20 to 25 percent from the 509,000 reported in 1991, when the industry began its rebound. Mr. Colton said sales jumped more than 70 percent after the 1981-82 recession.
 
 
Permit Applications Fall
 The May sales activity left the seasonally adjusted estimate of new houses for sale at the end of the month at 274,000, down from 275,000.
Although housing starts jumped 11 percent, applications for building permits dropped seven-tenths of 1 percent. It was the third straight decline in permit applications.
Regionally, the 34.4 percent plunge in the West left home sales there at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 105,000. That was the lowest level since July 1982.
Sales fell 7.5 percent in the Midwest, to a 98,000 rate. But they jumped 28.3 percent in the Northeast, to a 68,000 rate. Sales were also up 8.5 percent in the South, to a 230,000 rate.

HEADLINE: FUTURES/OPTIONS;
South Africa Unrest Sends Platinum Prices Surging

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Unrest in South Africa and signs of freshening demand fed an explosive rally that lifted the prices of platinum futures to a four-week high yesterday on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Gold and silver futures rose moderately, while copper futures posted strong gains. 
 Platinum for July delivery soared $16.60, to $377 an ounce, in New York, the contract's highest daily settlement since June 2.
The more heavily traded October contract jumped $16.70, to $384.80 an ounce.
A platinum selling streak linked to the weak Japanese economy screeched to a halt last week amid worries that the June 17 massacre of 40 South African blacks would lead to new violence and strikes in the world's largest platinum-producing country.
As fears of supply disruptions have grown, so have hopes for increased platinum demand from auto makers as the world economy recovers. Platinum is a key ingredient in catalytic converters, which reduce harmful exhaust gases.
"Add physical demand to the possibility of a supply threat and that's like throwing fuel on the fire," said Martin Reichenberg, manager of trading services for the Pegasus Econometric Group in Hoboken, N.J.
 
Gold Rises to $345.10
 On the Commodity Exchange in New York, gold for August delivery rose $1.20, to $345.10 an ounce; July silver climbed 3.3 cents, to $4.008 an ounce.
July copper rose 3.10 cents, to $1.1045 a pound, on the Commodity Exchange, extending gains posted during last week's national rail shutdown.
Cocoa for July delivery climbed $36, to $897 a ton, on the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange in New York. It was the third straight day of a recovery from last week's 19-year low of $785.
Oil futures retreated on the New York Mercantile Excahnge in a sell- off that left gasoline more expensive than home heating oil.
Light crude oil for August delivery fell 20 cents, to $22.24 a barrel; July home heating oil dropped four-tenths of 1 cent, to 61.87 cents a gallon; July unleaded gasoline plunged 1.32 cents, to 61.58 cents a gallon; August natural gas fell 1.1 cents, to $1.508 for 1,000 cubic feet.
Cattle futures made strong gains on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, reflecting stronger cash markets and buying by speculative funds.
Hog futures were mixed and pork bellies fell sharply in light trading ahead of a quarterly Agriculture Department inventory of hogs and pigs, due out after today's close.
Grain futures ended mixed.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Loses Some Ground On Decline in Home Sales

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar was mixed in lackluster trading yesterday, as a new report showing weak home sales in the United States provoked limited selling.
The departments of Commerce and of Housing and Urban Development reported a 5.6 percent decrease in new-home sales in May. Many economists expected a 3 to 4 percent gain. 
 Jeff Mondschein, foreign exchange manager at the Continental Illinois National Bank and Trust Company in Chicago, said the housing report had not caused much excitement in the market. The weakness of the dollar is well known to traders, who shun it because of uncertainty before the November elections and higher interest rates abroad.
 
Some Traders Were Encouraged
 The fall of the dollar abated somewhat as it reached technical trading levels against other currencies, encouraging some traders to buy dollars, Mr. Mondschein said.
Some other traders disagreed with the buyers, saying the weak housing report supported the case that the Federal Reserve might cut interest rates again to revive the economy. Lower rates would weaken the dollar's value abroad.
The dollar traded at 1.5160 German marks late yesterday, down from 1.5385 marks late Friday. At the closing in New York, the dollar fetched 1.5215 marks, down from 1.5355 late Friday.
In Tokyo the dollar closed at 125.50 yen, down 0.10 yen from Friday's close. Later in London the dollar was quoted at 125.15 yen. The trend had reversed by the closing in New York, where the dollar fetched 125.80 yen, up from 125.70 yen late Friday.
In London, the British pound was quoted at $1.9070, up from $1.8925 late Friday. The pound was quoted at $1.9030 at closing in New York, up from $1.8945 late Friday.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Friday's rates included: 1.5215 German marks, down from 1.5355; 1.3730 Swiss francs, down from 1.3805; 5.1170 French francs, down from 5.1625; 1,151.00 Italian lire, down from 1,161.25, and 1.19575 Canadian dollars, up from 1.19385.

HEADLINE: Business Scene;
New Russian Car: Pretty Good Deal

BYLINE: By Louis Uchitelle

DATELINE: TOGLIATTI, Russia

 BODY:
OUTSIDE the Zhiguli Trade Center, the nation's largest new-car dealership, throngs of men loiter. They represent a growing phenomenon in Russia today: the car merchant. Their specialty is to collar people who have just bought new cars and to try to persuade them to sell their shiny vehicles on the spot. Sometimes new-car owners are offered double the amount they paid minutes before to the dealer.
Clearly, the free market has come to car sales in Russia today, but in a form not easily recognizable in the United States. In the United States, the supply of new cars is more than the American public will buy, so a new car's value begins to fall the minute it leaves the dealer's lot. Here, the Government has allowed dealers to raise new car prices twentyfold since Jan. 1. But even at the higher prices, long waiting lists exist for new cars and their value keeps rising after dealers sell them. 
 "The American car market may be satiated, but here it is like a hungry dog; you throw it a new car and it gobbles it up," said Aleksandr G. Zibarev, a vice general director of the Vaz Auto Works, which manufactures the Zhiguli in this Volga River city. Zhigulis, similar in appearance to Fiats, account for more than half the nearly 700,000 new cars sold annually in the former Soviet Union.
That helps to explain why car merchants gather outside Zhiguli dealerships in many cities, and particularly at the Zhiguli Trade Center here. It sells more cars than any other dealership, averaging 60 vehicles a day, all of them delivered straight from the Vaz factory two miles away. Many of the merchants are Azerbaijanis who bring fruit and other merchandise north from their home republic, sell it and then invest the earnings in cars.
New-car buyers who pick up their vehicles at the center often snub the merchants on the street outside. Nevertheless, the majority of new-car owners soon resell their cars, to a friend or to a friend of a friend. Whoever the buyer, Ismailov Yasin of Baku, Azerbaijan, one of the merchants outside the Trade Center, illustrates how the new- car resale market often works.
Because demand for new cars is so great, particularly for the Zhiguli, the Government in effect rations new vehicles. It does this by requiring dealers to sell new cars only to people with coupons that they have obtained from an employer, a trade union or by putting their names on a waiting list, as Mr. Yasin said he did.
The coupon entitled him to buy a new Zhiguli 2106 from a dealer at a fixed price, one that is almost always much lower than a new car's immediate resale value. In Mr. Yasin's case, the fixed price was 160,000 rubles, or $1,600, and the resale value today is 380,000 rubles, or $3,800.
Leaning against his month-old blue sedan in the street outside the trade center, Mr. Yasin said that he had sought for more than a week to exchange the car for a fancier Zhiguli 2199, which dealers sell for 500,000 rubles. Since the 2199's immediate resale value is nearly 800,000 rubles, Mr. Yasin says he has offered to pay more than 300,000 rubles in cash, in addition to his less valuable Zhiguli 2106. So far, he has had no takers.
While Mr. Yasin sought to trade up to a fancier car, one that he will almost certainly resell later for an even higher price, others are in the market for a different reason. Many buy the model 2199 to trade down. In a nation without auto loans, trading down is a way to finance the purchase of a car.
Trading down works this way: A consumer with, say, 100,000 rubles in savings gets a coupon that gives him the right to buy a model 2199 from a dealer for 500,000 rubles. After borrowing 400,000 rubles from family and friends, he buys the car and quickly resells it for 800,000 rubles. Once the debt is repaid, the consumer is left with his original 100,000 rubles in savings and the 300,000 profit. That is enough to buy a nearly new Zhiguli 2106. The 300,000 ruble profit becomes, in effect, an auto loan.
"Everyone wants the Zhiguli 2199 because the car is so easy to resell," said Zinaida R. Sankina, director of the Zhiguli Trade Center, owned by Vaz. "I have seen people beg and cry for a 2199."
The resale market for new cars is certain to endure as long as the Government maintains ceilings on dealer prices, a policy intended to keep new cars within the reach of low-income families. Inflation also feeds the marketplace. In a nation short of cars, their value tends to rise in lockstep with the inflation rate. That makes owning a car a hedge against inflation.
Whatever the lifespan of this market, some Russians say they find the car merchants too profit oriented and their sidewalk marketplace unseemly.
"It is somehow not ethical," said Pavel Ansonov, a retired Vaz worker who drives a 15-year-old Zhiguli. "It is based on mutual deceit; people trying to outwit each other."

HEADLINE: Ridden With Debt, U.S. Companies Cut Funds for Research

BYLINE: By WILLIAM J. BROAD

 BODY:
SCIENTIFIC research by private industry, the traditional powerhouse of innovation and technological leadership in the United States, is suffering deeper financial woes than previously disclosed, suggesting that America is slipping in the international race for discoveries that form the basis of new goods and services.
The National Science Foundation reported in February that industrial spending on research and development had begun to shrink after decades of growth. The most recent numbers showed that, in inflation-adjusted dollars, such financing peaked in 1989 at about $79 billion, dropped slightly in 1990 and was projected to fall further in 1991. Such declines worry analysts because foreign rivals are increasing their research investments at a brisk pace. 
 But it turns out that things are bleaker than the figures suggest. The science foundation, a Federal agency, now says it includes in such calculations the money spent by foreign companies doing research in America, for instance, by leading Japanese computer and electronics firms. Such work soared during the 1980's, going from 6 percent of the national total in 1980 to 13 percent in 1989, the most recent year available. Analysts fear this influx of foreign dollars is now masking the true size of the American research decline.
Whatever its magnitude, the drop is prompting hot debate among experts over its causes. A prime suspect is the mountain of corporate debt accumulated in the 1980's during the blitz of takeovers, mergers and acquisitions that swept American business. That debt, some experts say, is putting a squeeze on investments in the inherently risky realm of scientific research, where payoffs, if they materialize at all, may be years or decades in the making.
"It's a major factor -- and could be the major factor" behind the drop in research spending by business, Erich Bloch, former director of the National Science Foundation, said in an interview.
Charles F. Larson, executive director of the Industrial Research Institute, a trade group based in Washington, agreed that corporate restructurings had taken a hefty toll. "Just the threat of a takeover changes a company," he noted.
For instance, the Phillips Petroleum Research Laboratory, located on 300 acres of land in Bartlesville, Okla., in the early 1980's had more than 1,000 workers generating more patents than any other American oil company. The lab studied not only traditional topics like developing new products from gas and oil but also exotic ones like controlling the enormous forces of nuclear fusion to produce electrical power, a feat scientists hope to achieve sometime in the 21st century.
Then in 1984, T. Boone Pickens mounted a hostile takeover of Phillips. To protect itself, the company leveraged itself heavily with billions of dollars of debt in order to buy back stock. Scientific research was one of areas curtailed after the successful defense.
"We got cut in half," said Dr. Charles F. Cook, then head of research for Phillips and now retired. "We lost a lot of our experienced people, and in research that's devastating."
The jolt to scientific research from corporate restructuring in the 1980's "is much more severe than has been recognized," Dr. Cook said in an interview. "I think it's a real problem and a serious mistake," he said. "But Wall Street demands it."
Not all analysts agree, especially ones who realized big profits in the shake-ups. The largest and most aggressive acquirer of all, Kohlberg, Kravis, Roberts & Company, or KKR, as it is commonly known, in 1989 published a 34-page defense of its work, saying leveraged buyouts were beneficial to many aspects of corporate health, including research. Companies acquired by KKR, the study asserted, immediately after buyouts raised their spending on research and development by 19 percent.
"Managers trying to maximize value," the study asserted, "do not stop research and development, which generally does not constitute a large cash outlay, unless such spending is wasteful."
Experts agree that there are cases where research rose. For instance, Owens-Illinois, a leader in the glass-container industry, in 1987 underwent a KKR-sponsored buyout to dodge a hostile takeover. Kenneth H. Lemke, the company's technical director, later told Congress that Owens-Illinois research added 10 engineers and scientists just after the acquisition, for a force of 194.
 
 
R.& D. 'Prone to Suffer'
 But more typical, many experts say, is a drop wrought by debt or a bid to cut duplication. When the General Electric Company took over RCA Corporation in 1986, one of the first things to go was RCA's prestigious David Sarnoff Research Center, the last of the nation's major laboratories devoted to television research. In 1987 G.E. gave the center to SRI International, a contract research group. Its work force was quickly cut to 850 from 1,200.
Alarmed at such trends, the research subcommittee of the House Science Committee held a hearing in July 1989 on the impact of corporate restructuring. There the Congressional Office of Technology Assessment said it had studied the fate of 19 manufacturing firms involved in some kind of merger or acquisition, including Stauffer Chemical, Owens-Corning Fiberglas Corporation, Radian Corporation, Polaroid Corporation, Tenneco, G. D. Searle & Company, USG Corporation, Reliance Electric Company, Zenith Electronics Company and Caterpillar Inc.
"Our results support the contention that R.& D. is prone to suffer as a result of heavy debt loads," Dr. Julie Fox Gorte of the assessment office told the hearing.
The National Science Foundation also presented a study at the hearing that showed 24 companies after restructuring had, in inflation-adjusted dollars, an average decline in research financing of 8.3 percent. William L. Steward, director of the science agency's division of science resource studies, called the findings disturbing.
"It is worrisome to see our industrial research investment declining when we face increased competition from abroad," Mr. Steward said.
But other analysts at the hearing dismissed such sentiments, saying restructurings often increased the quality, if not the quantity, of industrial research.
 
 
Hostile Boardrooms
 In the 1990's, the topic has continued to spark debate among scholars and scientists, more so with the recent drops in the financing of industrial research. More quietly, it is also being discussed in corporate boardrooms, which still feel the heat of debt.
"All of you are a variable overhead expense," a Wall Street adviser told corporate research chiefs in 1990 at a meeting of the Industrial Research Institute. "Let me repeat," he said, wagging his finger at the audience, "you are all a variable overhead expense."
Dr. Alvin W. Trivelpiece, director of the Oak Ridge National Laboratory and former chief executive officer of the American Association for the Advancement of Science, bemoaned this type of pressure in "Science Under Stress," a round-table discussion of 10 experts published in the February issue of Physics Today, a professional journal.
"Industrial research of a basic nature got badly gored" in the corporate shake-ups, Dr. Trivelpiece said, adding that "those pressures are not going to go away very soon."
Trying to gauge the pressures has fallen to such scholars as Dr. Bronwyn H. Hall, an economist at the University of California at Berkeley. She recently wrote a lengthy study that carefully weighed a large body of evidence about corporate research, including most of the previous studies as well as her own research. "Massive changes in financial structure," she concluded, "do appear to be accompanied by reduced investment of all kinds."
A glimmer of hope seen by some analysts is that corporate indebtedness is in some cases being reduced in the 1990's. David J. Ravenscraft, a business professor at the University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill who is studying the fate of industrial research, said that within three or four years, a quarter to a third of the restructured companies plan a stock offering to swap equity for debt.
But more typically, Dr. Ravenscraft said, companies use their cash flow to pay off debt, a process that can take years to decades. As the burden lessens, he said, American corporations might become more competitive globally.
 
Japan's Ascendancy
 The United States once enjoyed a commanding lead in high-technology industries, with corporate scientists inventing items like nylon, Teflon, the transistor, the computer chip, fiber optics and instant color film. But now the nation faces stiff competition in many fields.
In 1991, all the top holders of United States patents were Japanese companies. Toshiba was granted 1,014 patents last year, Mitsubishi 936 and Hitachi 927. Eastman Kodak ranked fourth with 863 patents, Canon fifth with 823 patents and General Electric, which once dominated the top spot, dropped into sixth place with 809 patents.
America's recent retreat from its peak financing of industrial research was spelled out by the National Science Foundation in February with the release of "Science and Engineering Indicators," a biennial report on the health of the nation's research enterprise. After rising rapidly in the 1970's and early 80's, the report said, industrial research leveled off in the mid-to-late 80's and began to fall in 1990.
Bright spots fighting the trend include the pharmaceutical industry, where spending on research is rising fast. But overall, the drift is downward.
In recent interviews, analysts at the science agency disclosed that the national figures included industrial research done by foreign companies in America. The rationale for this, they said, was to show domestic activity. But analysts admitted that the rise of foreign research here, amounting to $10.3 billion as of 1989, or 13 percent of the national total, suggested they might have to separate such categories in the future.
 
Labs Around the Globe
 Behind the flood of foreign research dollars is the purchase of American companies and the hiring of some of the nation's most creative scientists for new research centers. For instance, the NEC Corporation recently opened a laboratory in Princeton, N.J., and Canon started one in Palo Alto, Calif.
Analysts at the science agency say American corporate research may be somewhat stronger than the gloomy numbers suggest because their national totals do not include research done by American business overseas. For instance, Minnesota Mining and Manufacturing Company, the St. Paul company that makes everything from Post-it notes to optical data disks, now has 2,500 technical people stationed abroad.
In general, however, this globalization of American research is too small to offset the foreign influx. Moreover, overseas research has been shrinking, according to the most recent numbers. Between 1988 and 1989, research done by American corporations in foreign countries dropped from $7.3 billion in inflation-adjusted dollars to $7.2 billion, about 9 percent of the national total.
Mr. Bloch, the former director of the National Science Foundation, said the empirical evidence was that so far the dust from the restructurings of the 1980's had yet to settle.
"Look at some of the big companies," he said. "They're still reducing their R.& D."
Mr. Larsen of the Industrial Research Institute said the downward trend had dark implications for the technological health of the United States.
"We're going to suffer later on," he said. "We're not looking to the long term."

HEADLINE: Books of The Times;
Snapshots From a Family Album

BYLINE: By MICHIKO KAKUTANI

 BODY:

Marine Life
By Linda Svendsen
165 pages. Farrar, Straus & Giroux. $17.
Images of water -- as a metaphor for the amniotic fluids of birth, the terrors of drowning and the fresh, clean promise of redemption -- surface again and again in the stories that make up "Marine Life," Linda Svendsen's astonishing debut book.
Adele Nordstrom, the narrator of these interlinked stories, remembers playing at the beach as a child, "listlessly filling a bright yellow pail with the waves" while waiting for her brother and his girlfriend to pay her some attention. Years later, she recalls a story about her boyfriend, Bill, how he and his brother watched a mother turtle struggle to make her way back to the water, after laying some eggs in the sand; if only she had been with him, she thinks, "witnessing this event might have made us kinder to each other." 
 An older Adele remembers accompanying her aging mother to a swimming lesson at the local recreation center, watching her "going away from me" in the pool, her breathing erratic and rapid. And she remembers receiving the news that her niece has jumped off a bridge and drowned: dead at the age of 16.
There's nothing heavy-handed or contrived about the way these images are made to echo one another. Indeed, "Marine Life" is as seamless as a fine piece of music, effortlessly held together by Ms. Svendsen's strong, clear voice, a voice capable of moving back and forth from the lyrical to the irreverent, the somber to the silly with complete fluency and ease.
Some of the descriptions Ms. Svendsen tosses off so cavalierly are poetic: Adele's boyfriend using a sparkler to write her name against the nighttime sky, each letter disappearing as soon as it's completed. Others are comic, in a ghastly sort of way: Adele's unhappy sister, Joyce, opening a birdcage and deliberately vacuuming up the small pink finch inside; or Adele removing all the fruit from the kitchen so her estranged boyfriend can't feed his next girlfriend a suggestive apple or banana.
Jumping about in time, Ms. Svendsen conjures up Adele's childhood in small-town Canada and her adult life in New York City with quick, sure strokes, giving us pictures of her family that possess all the immediacy of snapshots, all the lasting emotional resonance of more formal portraits. To begin with, there's Adele's mother, June, a cocktail pianist, who believes it's better to have a bad marriage than no marriage at all: she loses her first husband to another woman, and she spurns her second -- a nice, ineffectual man who is Adele's father -- for No. 3, a crass, foul-mouthed man named Robert, who is 10 years her junior. Robert makes Adele feel embarrassed in front of her friends; toward her own father, she feels a mixture of irritation and sorrow. She pictures him driving home after their Saturday afternoons together, alone and tired and discouraged; to make sure he's gotten home safely, she calls and calls until he answers the phone -- and hangs up the instant she hears his voice.
Adele is the family's baby -- "the afterthought," one of her sisters calls her -- and she grows up idolizing her older brother and sisters. Over the years, as she slowly loses her innocence, she will begin to see their lives shorn of the glamour she always imagined they possessed. Her brother, Ray, has earned his forklift operator certificate, and changes girlfriends as frequently as he changes jobs; he drinks too much and drives so recklessly that Adele is reluctant to get in his car. Her elder sister, Irene, marries a Belgian immigrant, and proceeds "to do boring Belgian things -- bake sour pastries, fuss with tulips, and raise a prim daughter."
Adele's other sister, Joyce, suffers a nervous breakdown in the wake of her divorce; though her former husband, an unemployed aviation freak named Eric, beats her black and blue, she still looks up at the sky whenever a plane flies overhead, remembering his passion. Joyce tries to rationalize the fights she and Eric used to have by citing "Princess Margaret and Lord Tony, and the bickering couple on 'Bewitched'."
As for Adele, she grows up, an observant, watchful child, protective of her lovelorn mother. As a teen-ager, she smears her face and arms with Sea & Ski self-tanning lotion so she'll look as if she's been somewhere warm. Years later, she will transform these fantasies of escape into a vocation: she majors in cultural anthropology and sets off for the Arctic to do research on her thesis. Eventually, she lands in New York City, where she acquires a husband and two children; the marriage, like so many others in her family, will end in divorce.
Elliptical yet compelling, Ms. Svendsen's use of a series of overlapping short stories to relate Adele's story recalls a similar technique used by Stephanie Vaughn in "Sweet Talk" and Tim O'Brien in "The Things They Carried." It's a highly effective device, resulting in a book that has all the cumulative narrative power of a novel, all the compacted energy of a volume of short stories. In the case of "Marine Life," it's also a device that showcases the talents of a remarkable new writer.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Linda Svendsen (James La Bonte/Farrar, Straus & Giroux)

HEADLINE: TV SPORTS;
Dream Team Routs Might Mean Lowly Ratings

BYLINE: By Richard Sandomir

 BODY:
No doubt about it, the men's Olympic basketball team proved it was the Dream Team on Sunday night. So soothing, so numbing was its 136-57 embarrassment of Team Cuba that I confess I slept, perchance to dream of competitive basketball -- and Fidel Castro posting up big-time in the paint.
My, oh, my, Jordan, Magic, Bird and Barkley lived up to all the Dream Team promotion we have been inundated with on NBC. Granted, there are other teams in the Tournament of the Americas who are better than Cuba and there will be teams in the Olympics who will give Team USA a bigger fight. 
 But what constitutes a bigger fight than losing by 79? A 42-point squeaker?
Can the Dream Team be a ratings magnet for NBC in Spain if competition-starved viewers would rather pull out tapes of luge from Albertville? Will hoops addicts reach into their money clips for as much as $125 for a pay-per-trouncing on the Olympics Triplecast? To be decided in Barcelona.
"We expected a blowout," said Marv Albert, NBC's basketball announcer, "but not to that extent. Brazil will be a little tougher, and they have Oscar Schmidt. Someone here said, 'Schmidt's just a shooter. He makes Kiki Vandeweghe look like Dennis Rodman.' They say Puerto Rico may be good."
Albert and his partner Mike Fratello were in super-fill mode most of the game, with plenty of time to vamp with the research compiled for them.
"There was still stuff I couldn't get in," said Albert, "like the Cuban economic situation is so bad they wash their uniforms in the hotel sink."
Anything else? "Guess who plays for Panama?" he said. "Stuart Gray! He was an Army brat who was born in Panama."
Team USA's vanquishing of the Cubans did not dim Albert's support of National Basketball Association players, who hold 11 of the 12 roster spots.
"It's easy to say what the U.S. is doing is ridiculous," he said. "Is this what they want to do, beat up teams by 75? You know that'll be written. But the rest of the world is moving closer to us in talent, and if they didn't do this now, the U.S.A. would be beaten again."
Albert is hoping for a better game Sunday when NBC airs the tournament championship game (TNT will air Dream Team games tomorrow and Friday).
"You can't judge things on the Cubans," he said. "I expect more sophisticated coaching from Brazil. The Cuban coach had nothing going."
Sunday's game earned a 9.3 rating in Nielsen's preliminary survey of 25 markets. It will be interesting to see if the ratings plummeted during the game, when it was apparent that the Cubans could not erase the inhuman deficit.
 
Hunt to Replace Wolf
 Warner Wolf has been gone from WCBS (Channel 2) since the end of May, and still there's no replacement for the longtime sports broadcaster who took his videotapes back home to Washington. The station's search for a successor is shaping up as the biggest since David O. Selznick casted a wide net for the actress to play Scarlett O'Hara in "Gone With the Wind."
Except right now there are no obvious beauties like Vivian Leigh. Rock Rote is the temporary regular. Dave Sims is the weekend fill-in. None of the nearly 100 suspects under any consideration has impressed Bud Carey, the general manager, or Dean Daniels, the news director. Candidates like CBS's Greg Gumbel and WFAN's Mike Francesa have had flirtations with the station.
"This isn't something you jump into," Carey said. "If you choose somebody, you do it for the long term. We're closer than we were a few weeks ago. Remember, Warner was a relatively easy hire. He was in the market."
Carey and Daniels may be debating the need to follow Wolf with a high-power big name, and how much that person could affect ratings in the last five minutes of the newscast.
But there is a possible solution, according to a station source, which would be to keep Rote indefinitely until WCBS's former No. 1 candidate, Bernie Smilovitz, of WDIV-TV, in Detroit, gets out of his contract in 1994.
Recently, the station wanted to hire Smilovitz, who uses a lot of video and humor in his sportscasts, but he wasn't allowed out of his deal.
 
AIRWAVES
 ESPN is making great use of super slow-motion replays of pitch releases, most notably on Sunday comparing White Sox knuckleballer CHARLIE HOUGH with Yankee right-hander MELIDO PEREZ . . . . Agents for New Jersey Nets Coach CHUCK DALY are shopping a weekly coach's show to WWOR-TV, SportsChannel and WNBC-TV. . . . SECTION: Section A; Page 23; Column 2; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 717 words

HEADLINE: On My Mind;
The Israeli Gamble

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL

 BODY:
Consider:
Later this summer, a free election will be held in an important Mideastern Muslim state, under the rules of parliamentary democracy.
The opposition will win on a platform of peace through major concessions to Israel and an end of enmity toward Jews. It will create a new government dedicated to those ends. The defeated government will step aside peacefully. 
 Well, of course -- the idea of a critical free election taking place in a democratic environment in the Muslim Mideast is absurd. And we do not dream of Muslim leaders telling their people, plain, the time has come for peace through concessions to Israel.
Yet the contrast is not only worth noting, but essential to an understanding of the Israeli elections, the future of the Mideast and the peculiar Western attitude toward Israel and her Arab neighbors.
Israelis want peace and, in their freedom, are willing to pay what could be a dangerous price for it -- full autonomy for Palestinians.
That was a concept of the Likud's own Menachem Begin. And without the stubborn determination of Yitzhak Shamir to get autonomy negotiations free of international interference the current talks would not be taking place.
But a plurality of Israelis decided to go with Yitzhak Rabin's promises of stronger, faster action toward autonomy. They trust him to do his best to get them both peace and security.
But both Mr. Rabin and Mr. Shamir recognize the risk in autonomy that neither has answered yet. Independence, not autonomy, is the Palestinian goal. Israelis hope that autonomy will not turn into the independent Palestine most of them see as a dagger. It is a gamble, taken in the hope of peace, without guarantees -- worthwhile and honorable, but a gamble.
The reality is that under any government Israelis survive in a jungle of dictators hunting for Israeli weaknesses. Economically, there are plenty. They could lead to military slippage for Israel -- an invitation to disaster.
Mr. Shamir was defeated in large part because Israelis correctly blamed him for failing to fix Israel's economy -- over-subsidized, over-politicized, under-privatized.
But Mr. Rabin and his Labor Party also failed to deal with that in their three decades in office. In fact, the corrupting system of favors-to-favorites was part of Labor's socialist roots. Mr. Rabin will have to control strong Labor leftists who will keep fighting for socialism.
The U.S. has interests in Israel. Mostly they are in the pragmatic advantages of a shared democracy. But they seemed more vivid to Washington when the U.S. also saw Israel as a bulwark against Soviet intervention in the Mideast.
And the U.S. has interests with the Arabs -- chiefly oil flow and oil profits. They are important but no excuse for moral equivalency -- the political perversion that ignores the greater American interests that lie in the difference between democracy and dictatorship.
Democracy makes for peace, dictatorship for war. American apologists for the Soviet Union suffered from that disease, as do American appeasers of Communist China, and of Mideast dictators.
One day, after the honeymoon, Mr. Rabin will have to confront Western moral equivalency, Israel's great enemy. But I hope he will not have to bear one burden carried by Mr. Shamir: the incessant merciless attempt of some American and European "friends" of Israel, Jews among them, to destroy him or pressure their governments to do so.
Foreigners' support of and friendship to Israel never involved obedience -- that was always part of the vicious "double allegiance" lie. But any friendship does involve a certain constancy, a lovely word.
To me, it rules out attempts by foreigners to destroy an Israeli government, Rabin or Shamir, because it is not submissive to their own vision of what is right for the country. Choosing or defeating their government is a job for Israelis.
At City Hall a few months ago, Mayor David Dinkins gave a breakfast for Mayor Teddy Kollek of Jerusalem. They like each other. Mr. Dinkins read from the Bible:
"Pray for the peace of Jerusalem: They shall prosper who love thee." In my office, I read in the Revised American Version the lines that come next:
"Peace be within thy walls, and security within thy palaces."
Both. Psalm 122.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Op-Ed
